# 太上感应篇

**Preface to the Memorial Presenting the *Taishang Ganying Pian***

Your servant, in his humble words, reveres the profound and venerable Dao, deeply admiring the sagely maxims of the Most High. With the carving of the woodblocks now complete, this long-held wish of your servant is fortunately fulfilled. Presumptuously, I dare to present this before Your August Ears, humbly repaying a fraction of Your boundless grace.

With trepidation and awe, I bow my head again and again.

I have observed that among the treasured scriptures of the sacred canon, there exists a great teaching of jade-like elegance—rooted in compassion to save all beings, it repeatedly instructs humanity with earnestness. It declares that the causes of good and evil responses arise like the pivot of a mechanism, while the principles of fortune and misfortune manifest more swiftly than shadow follows form. Its twelve hundred earnest words and countless ages of luminous admonitions require the elucidation of wise rulers to guide the multitude toward return and devotion.

Our August Emperor, in serene governance and morning audience, has illuminated the classics with diligent study. Though his mastery is devoted to the Six Classics, his inquiry extends broadly to all texts. He seeks the subtle and profound, following in the footsteps of his sagely ancestors’ quests; his transformative influence flows with purity, surpassing even the standards of the Han emperors of old. In the leisure between state affairs, he delights in pondering the interplay of Heaven and humanity, intending to propagate and transmit this work for posterity. Thus, he has compiled and refined it with care. Its solemn admonitions, like eight characters of black pearls, crown the text; its sweet and measured praises, wielded by a master’s brush, distinguish it as a singular achievement. How radiant is the splendor of this treasury! How honored is the favor bestowed upon cap and robe!

Your servant, insignificant and unrefined, is unworthy—how could I presume to serve in the bamboo palace or allow my name to sully the imperial court? Yet, having once been elevated within the teaching’s ranks, I now dare to presume upon Your Sagacious Decree. Commanded with the honor of receiving imperial gold, I was entrusted with overseeing the engraving of the plates. I vow to exhaust my meager strength in humble repayment of this private devotion.

Reflecting upon my late father, I have ever feared the neglect of this text; yet, by some twist of fate, the fire that spared it has only further illuminated the Dao. With the transcription and carving now complete, I reverently seal and present it.

I humbly beseech Your Imperial Majesty to deign to glance upon it, then widely promulgate it to the four directions. May all the people turn toward goodness and joyfully follow its teachings, so that this book may serve as an aid to uphold the world—thus fulfilling the role of both sovereign and teacher.

Your servant respectfully presents the printed edition of the *Taishang Ganying Pian*, inscribed with Your Majesty’s title, in eight volumes, accompanying this memorial.

With trepidation and awe, I bow my head again and again, and respectfully submit these words.

On a day in the eighth month of the sixth year of the Shaoding era,
Your servant Hu Yingwei, Supervisor of the Teaching on the Right Street, and Attendant for Ritual Offerings at the Taiyi Palace, respectfully memorializes.

**The *Taishang Ganying Pian*, Volume Two**
Transmitted by Li Changling, with Eulogies by Zheng Qingzhi.

Loyalty and Filial Piety.
The *Tradition* states: The Eastern Minister of Destiny declares: Some possess talents surpassing Xiao and Miao, their aspirations towering above the multitude, nurturing their vast and unyielding qi while scorning wealth and rank. Others attain the utmost integrity and incorruptibility, refusing food or clothing not rightfully theirs—neither ostentation nor the allure of ten thousand chariots can sway their steadfast virtue or tarnish their noble conduct. Still others inherit blessings from ancestors of merit, their legacy flowing to descendants, refined through generations until their lineage is transformed and reborn. All such individuals meet the criteria for transcendence and are destined to ascend among the ranks of immortals. Yet they must pass through many years before gradually advancing.

As for those of perfect loyalty and perfect filial piety, should they depart this world today, they shall be appointed as Earthly Governors by tomorrow, and from that office, they shall swiftly ascend to the celestial ranks. Generally speaking, loyalty is the cardinal virtue of a minister, just as filial piety is the fundamental duty of a child. If all ministers were disloyal, what could a ruler expect of them? If all children were unfilial, what could a father hope for from them? In such a world, the bonds of ruler and minister, father and child, would utterly collapse—humanity would differ little from beasts or barbarians.

How lamentable that they fail to recognize loyalty and filial piety as the very foundation of transcendence, the swiftest path to liberation from the mortal realm! Have you not heard the tales of Master Lü’s instruction or the covenant of Master Lan? Long ago, Master Lü served as Censor-in-Chief, his stern countenance and forthright speech shaking the court and the realm. One day, as he sat alone, a green-clad attendant appeared and presented him with an elixir, saying: “This is the Cooling Elixir. The August Emperor will soon journey south to the fiery lands, and He has charged you with rectifying the ranks of immortals there. As those regions are scorching, He first bestows this upon you.” Master Lü bowed twice and swallowed the pill, its coolness like ice and snow sliding down his throat. Soon after, he indeed departed this world.

At that time, Zhu Mingfu had just passed the imperial examination and, while by the Xiang River, encountered the Master. The Master rode a deer with jade-like antlers, flanked by young attendants in green, with hundreds of officials and soldiers clearing the way before and behind. Mingfu stepped forward and bowed, asking: "Have you already attained immortality?" The Master replied: "I now accompany the August Emperor on His southern journey. With no time for leisurely discourse, I shall recite a poem:

*By chance my merits were inscribed in jade towers high,*
*While countless robed ones lie buried ‘neath the dust.*
*Now following the Emperor as inspector of the ranks,*
*Who else remains to stand guard at the Cypress Terrace?*

With these words, he vanished from sight.

The Lan family was rooted in filial piety and fraternal love, with over a hundred mouths to feed, yet all lived in harmony, never exchanging harsh words. One evening, a perfected being descended from the Dipper and declared: "I am the Filial Piety King of the Northern Dipper. When filial devotion reaches Heaven, the sun and moon shine brighter; when it reaches Earth, grasses and trees flourish; when it reaches mankind, the kingly Way is fulfilled. Your filial piety has drawn my gaze—having met me, you are certain to attain the Dao." And so it came to pass.

Thus, utmost loyalty and filial devotion—how swiftly they hasten one’s transcendence of the mortal realm! Alas, if loyalty and filial piety yield such rewards, what fate awaits those who lack them?

**Eulogy:**
Without loyalty, there is no lord;
Without filial piety, there are no kin.
Abandon these two, and one is but a beast, not human.
Zhuang Zhou roamed free, yet still honored duty;
The Śākya school embraced emptiness, yet diligently repaid kindness.
Beneath Heaven and upon Earth, who can escape these bonds?
*Fraternal love.*

**Tradition states:** The *Analects* records: "The noble person attends to the root; when the root is established, the Dao arises. Is filial piety not the very root of benevolence?" The Envoys of the Controller of Destiny regard father and son, elder and younger brothers, and husband and wife as the Three Bonds. Root and bond—though the terms differ, their meaning is one. If one discards the root and abandons the bonds, how can human relations stand?

In general, those who practice these virtues in truth ensure harmony between high and low, unchanging and steadfast. But those who merely pay lip service invite regret and offense, leading inevitably to transgression.

Long ago, Yao Qiyun’s family was not wealthy, relying solely on farming and sericulture. When he was three, his father, having no son, took his elder brother’s place in distant garrison duty and died at the frontier. After his father’s death, Qiyun retrieved his spirit for burial, built a hut by the grave, and mourned for the rest of his life. The county magistrate, Su Che, donated his salary to buy land, opened paths, and erected a stele to commemorate the deed. The prefect, Hun Jian, reported the matter to the throne. An imperial edict praised and rewarded him, honoring his gate, naming his village "Filial Piety," its altar "Loyalty and Righteousness," and its lane "Love and Respect." From Qiyun’s time onward, for fifteen generations, hundreds lived together for over three hundred years, never uttering a discordant word. Even through the upheavals of the Five Dynasties, descendants guarded the ancestral tombs, never scattering. There was also Zhang Cheng, a farmer whose family lived together for generations, numbering over a hundred and seventy. Within and without, all lived in harmony, never exchanging harsh words. Clothing and the like had no fixed owners. Each morning, the family elder sat in the hall while descendants lined up below, receiving their duties in turn, all diligent and reverent. From his ancestor Guan to Cheng, six generations lived together for nearly two hundred years, ever constant. Alas! These two families were truly what is meant by "practicing in truth."

Could those who merely value empty literary forms ever achieve the like?

In praise it is said:
The bodies of father and mother
divide into brothers.
From one root they emerge—
how could there be different bodies?
Cherish the branches and leaves
to protect the root and trunk.
History mocks the bean and winnowing basket,
while the *Book of Songs* sings of the *tangdi* flower.
Like panpipes and flutes in harmony,
the spirit fosters kindness and joy.
Rectify yourself to transform others.

The *Tradition* records Confucius saying:
“The noble person rectifies his robes and cap.”
And again:
“If the mat is not straight, he does not sit.”
Alas! If even robes, caps, mats, and seats
must be made correct,
how much more one’s own conduct?

It further says:
“If the leader acts with rectitude,
who would dare not follow?”
And:
“If one cannot rectify oneself,
how can one rectify others?”
These are the great principles
by which the sages governed their conduct.
Thus, in governing oneself,
how could one not be rectified?
Only when rectified
do all people respect one.
Where people know respect,
their hearts are already transformed—
what need is there for petty efforts to change them?

In former times, Sima Wengong
was a man of upright integrity,
his name renowned throughout the land.
When he resided in Luoyang,
the customs of the place were wholly transformed.
All esteemed the teachings of names and propriety,
no longer urgently pursuing wealth and profit.
People, whether rich or poor,
were content with their lot.
The young and latecomers knew awe for moral integrity.
When they wished to do something,
they would warn one another, saying:
“Do no evil, lest Sima Duanming learn of it.”

Lü Shengong was upright and outspoken,
his influence shaking the court and the realm.
At the time, Wang Anshi was appointed governor of Changzhou.
He sent a letter to Shengong, saying:
“I have been a guest in the capital for two years,
and in this time, my faults and flaws
have accumulated in my heart,
often overwhelming me.
But when I visit elders,
I am suddenly calmed and return to myself.
The bearing of a virtuous person
dissolves all ill intent.”

Now, in Huishu, I encountered Master Lü of Yingyang, whose heart and Dao were profoundly aligned, his mind serene and unclouded. When he served as lecturer at Chongzheng Hall, he daily expounded the teachings of rectifying the heart and cultivating the self, exhorting and guiding the sovereign. He often declared: “If one’s self is cultivated and heart upright, no other arts are needed, and the realm will naturally transform.” Thus, wherever he resided, the restless were transformed, and those who heard his influence, though shallow, became steadfast.

When Hu Gongyuan served as professor in the two prefectures of Su and Hu, each time he explained the classics and reached a crucial principle, he earnestly urged his students to first govern themselves before governing others. “Then, gentlemen,” he would ask, “how do you rectify yourselves? By this, who would not be transformed?”

**Eulogy:**
When the standard is upright, its shadow is straight;
when the source is pure, its streams run clear.
To bend oneself while straightening others—
there is no such principle in all the world.
What art do sages and worthies possess?
They simply apply to others what they first apply to themselves.
To punish others for one’s own flaws—
even an army would gnash its teeth in scorn.

*The Great Learning* teaches: cultivate the self, order the household, govern the state.
**Pity the orphan.**
As the *Record* says: “The orphan is one who has not yet achieved maturity when parents pass early. Some lose their mother while the father remarries, or lose their father while the mother takes another husband. Worst of all are those who lose both parents, left alone and exposed—how unbearable their plight! Though a royal decree commands care, with fosterers providing food, clothing, and shelter, even the most compassionate guardian cannot match the tender love of true parents. How can one not pity them?”

Yet the world sees only the lowly status of orphans and scorns them, unaware that some, though orphaned, grow into greatness. Of old, Zhang Denggong Shixun lost both parents in infancy. As he grew, he endured poverty, studying tirelessly without respite, and ultimately passed the imperial examinations, rising to the rank of grand councilor, renowned for his virtue. Was this not an orphan of the poorest means who forged his own path and became a vessel of greatness?

Lü Zhongcheng lost his father in childhood, yet roused himself to study, refusing frivolous friendships. Few in Luoyang knew him. Only after passing the examinations did people learn he was the grandson of Lü Zhengdui, and he later held high office, famed for his integrity. Was this not an orphan of noble birth who forged his own path and became a vessel of greatness?

If so, how can orphans be scorned? Though this section is titled “Pity the Orphan,” I have not merely written of pity but recorded these examples to show that those unfortunate enough to be orphaned may take them as models and strive for self-improvement.

The foregoing speaks of pitying orphans, yet the elders of old regarded such conduct as ordinary, never deeming it extraordinary. When Scholar Cha of the Dragon Diagram Bureau was impoverished, he gathered and supported the lonely and destitute among his kin, spending all his salary and stipends as soon as he received them. In his dealings with others, he was deeply sincere, and toward the abandoned and exposed, he showed even greater kindness. Once, a colleague’s daughter, reduced to servitude by poverty...

Lord Cha heard of her plight and redeemed her, then married her to a scholarly clan.

Minister Lü Duān the Upright and Loyal, along with the late Chancellor Feng Dao and General Zhao Wendu, were all old friends from the same hometown. Feng Dao’s son was disabled by illness, so Lü shared his salary to support him. He also recommended Zhao Wendu’s son, Shaozong, for an official post. If not for genuine compassion for the orphaned, could one act thus?

**Eulogy:**
Young and orphaned—what to rely on, what to depend upon?
No one to embrace, no one to tend when illness strikes.
I was young once, and so are others’ children.
The fawn disobeys its mother’s command; the young deer weeps, vowing its loyalty.
To extend this heart is to align with Heaven’s principle.
**Pity the widow.**

**Account:**
A widow—still in the prime of life, when her husband suddenly perishes.
Alone in form, solitary in shadow, every glance brings desolation.
How much worse when women are fragile and men foolish, with a hundred affairs left unfinished?
This is precisely what Mencius meant by “the destitute with none to turn to.”
Such misfortune—is this not the depths of hardship?
To whom can she speak? Is this not utter helplessness?
Though her husband is dead, in the unseen realm, how could he bear no thought for her?

Formerly, when Xu Zezhi served as magistrate of Beijing, Zhao Shihuang arrived from Renxing and was retained as an administrative officer. Shihuang and Xu’s three sons all got along well. Before long, Shihuang suddenly died of illness. Xu and his sons arranged the funeral, provided ample provisions, and generously assisted his wife and children. They also dispatched the minor official Zhao Yi to escort them to the capital. After Yi completed the task and returned to report, he suddenly spoke in Shihuang’s voice to express gratitude: “Shihuang failed to care for his health and, in the prime of life, was abruptly entered into the register of the dead. I am deeply indebted to Your Lordship and your sons for your boundless kindness—not only did my remains return home, but my aged mother, wife, and children all safely reached our village, spared from wandering and homelessness. My humble heart is overwhelmed with gratitude. The Lord of the Underworld also admires this and will surely bestow abundant rewards upon you.”

The eunuch official Huang Shu was dispatched by imperial decree to deliver a fan and handkerchief to Pengze, where he was to welcome and present them to the northern envoy. Soon after, the boatman Wang Gui fell ill and died en route. His vessel belonged to the official transport bureau of the Two Zhe provinces. Upon Gui’s death, the boat was to be returned to the authorities. His wife, weeping, pleaded, “If the boat is reclaimed, my family of ten will starve to death. I beg for a single word of favor, that my eldest son may inherit his father’s livelihood.” Moved, Shu spoke of this in Zhenjiang, and the transport commissioner immediately enlisted the son as a soldier and entrusted him with his father’s boat.

That night, Shu lodged in Danyang. At dawn, he saw Gui maneuvering a small boat toward him from the misty waves. Gui prostrated himself in tears and said, “Though I am dead, my eldest son has inherited my work, and my family of ten need no longer fear starvation. I am deeply indebted to your kindness.” With that, he vanished.

Alas, such manifest events may be set aside for now. Yet I observe that widows, though standing alone among men, often resolve with a will far surpassing others—this cannot go unrecorded.

Counselor Zhao Gai was orphaned at seven. His mother, steadfast in her devotion, raised him with rigorous instruction, and he eventually ranked third in the imperial examination of the fifth year of Tiansheng. When the court later granted the privilege of official appointment to meritorious families, Gai requested that the honor be bestowed upon his mother as a commandery grand lady. The prime minister said, “As a scholar-official, your own rank is not far off.” Gai replied, “My mother is eighty-two, and her days are uncertain. I wish to see her honored in her lifetime.” His request was granted.

Prefect Li Jun was orphaned at three. His mother, Lady Qin, was comely. When suitors urged her to remarry, she rebuked them angrily: “The bond of husband and wife is ordained by heaven, and life and death are decreed by fate. I would sooner starve than remarry.” She devoted herself to Jun’s education, and he eventually passed the examinations and rose to the rank of military commissioner of Yaozhou.

Can the widowed be taken lightly? I record these accounts beneath my reflections on aiding widows precisely to show all widows the path to emulate.

Alas! For those who uphold such principles, heaven’s reward is certain, for they honor the weight of human bonds—how could their children’s success be theirs alone?

**In Praise:**
Widows who hold fast to virtue
Are revered by the wise and worthy.

The steadfast widow of Zhejiang and Lujin,
Her resolve more valiant than the bravest knight.
The tower of Lady Qing stood firm,
Defying the might of Qin’s tyranny.
How much more, then, those without recourse—
Could one not be moved by their plight?
Who would dare trample them,
Unshaken, undaunted?

**Revering the Aged.**
The *Records* state: The aged have witnessed the passage of time,
Endured the weight of countless affairs.
The young, in their heedless throng,
Are lost in untold numbers,
While the silver-browed, white-haired elders
Alone preserve their vigor and strength—
How could they not be revered?

In the early years of the *Kaibao* era, there was a certain Xu Yong, a county magistrate of Yun Prefecture in Yunzhou, who submitted a petition to the imperial complaint drum, stating: “My father, Qiong, is ninety-seven years old; my eldest brother, eighty-one; my second brother, seventy-nine; and I myself, seventy-five. I humbly request an appointment near my home to attend to their care and honor them.” Taizu summoned his father, and upon his arrival, Qiong presented his case with clarity and acuity, his speech and bearing undiminished by age. Taizu, deeply impressed, declared: “Such a household as yours must possess extraordinary virtue.” Qiong replied: “There is nothing extraordinary—only that for generations, we have lived in harmony, undivided.” Taizu said: “That *is* your extraordinary virtue.” He immediately ordered the bestowal of a robe and sash, a silver-inlaid saddle and bridle, a horse, thirty *jin* of imperial tea, thirty bolts of assorted silks, and an appointment to a post near their home, fulfilling their wish.

Wu Yuansi likewise enjoyed great longevity. When the court, during a sacred festival, sought out the elderly, Yuansi’s household alone included those of eighty, ninety, and over a hundred years—eight in total. Among them was Wu Wan, aged one hundred and fourteen, who walked with light, steady steps and whose *qi* showed no sign of decline. When asked the secret of his vitality, he replied: “For generations, we have lived in harmony, undivided.” The emperor issued an edict granting him an official title and commending his household with an inscribed gateway.

Beyond the gate, two platforms of righteous harmony were erected, each crowned with a pair of ornate *hua-biao* pillars topped with crane finials—a mark of exceptional honor.

If even the Most Exalted holds such reverence, how much more should others?

I have examined the annals of our dynasty and found that among those who held high office and attained great longevity, only the following stand out: Zhang Tuichuan, who lived to ninety-one; Duke Wen of Lu, ninety-two; Zhao Kangjing, eighty-six; Zhang Wending, eighty-five; Chen Wenhui, eighty-two; Zeng Xuanjing and Fu Wenzhong, both over eighty. Next in rank were Zhao Qingxian, Pang Zhuangmin, Xiang Wenjian, and Li Wenzheng—all above seventy, yet still falling short. Further below were Cao Wuhui, Xue Jiansu, Wang Yimin, Lü Wenmu, Lü Wenhui, and Kou Wenmin—each surpassing sixty, but again not matching the first group. Then came Shen the Remonstrator, who reached precisely sixty; Liu the Academician, fifty; Wang the Hanlin Academician, Yin the Drafter, Wang the Grand Master for Consultation, and Qian Xuanjing—all above forty, yet far from the mark. Others, like Shi Culai, died at forty; Su the Hanlin Academician, thirty-nine; Zhao the Drafter, thirty-six; and Wang the Censor, thirty-eight. These lives were especially lamentable. In the arena of fame and profit, time slips away in the blink of an eye—how much of it is truly spent cultivating one’s own destiny? If not, then one’s passage through this world is but a fleeting, aimless journey. The words of the Buddhas—*“May my lifespan be long, that I may diligently practice all good; may my virtue and fortune flourish, that I may widely aid all beings”*—are entirely missed.

Alas! Youth, so quick to flaunt their talents and scorn their elders, fail to grasp that longevity stands foremost among the Five Blessings. Even with abundant talent, if one does not reach that stage, what use is it? Long ago, Yang Danian, Zhou Han, and Zhu Ang served together in the imperial court. The latter two were already advanced in years, while Yang, barely twenty, often belittled them. Zhou, deeply offended, once retorted in jest: “Do not mock my age—old age will one day be yours as well.” Zhu, from the side, waved his hand and said: “Do not say such things, do not say such things.” Later, Yang Danian died at forty-nine. Thus, to attain old age—is it not a rare and difficult achievement? Should it not be revered?

In praise I say:

The cultivation of the Three Elders is learned and nurtured; rites honor those of advanced years. This is as close as kinship, the foremost duty of family and state. Heaven grants them longevity, allowing their crowns to flourish. Those who fail to revere them profane Heaven itself. How much more should those of venerable virtue be accorded respect? Surely none dare neglect their due rites.

**Cherishing the Young.**
The *Traditions* record Confucius’s words: *“The young should be cherished.”* Mencius likewise said: *“Cherish your own young, and extend that care to the young of others.”* For the young are but children, their understanding yet unformed. Those who are elder must naturally show them compassion and nurture them. To treat them with cruelty is no human way. Thus, parents with young children must exhaust their loving diligence; elder brothers with younger siblings must extend their fraternal affection. Even toward the young of others, one should extend the same heart of kindness, regarding them equally, without distinction of self and other. To love only one’s own children and kin while neglecting others’ is no true fulfillment of the Way of cherishing the young.

Moreover, the young hold lowly station and feeble strength—lowliness invites contempt, weakness invites oppression. Without a heart of compassion, elders may exploit their position to indulge their whims. The young, lacking eloquence to discern right from wrong or strength to contest victory, suffer as the lame endure the rod, unable to flee; as the mute endure slander, unable to defend themselves. Who could fully recount their hardships? How grievous is the thinness of custom!

Women’s nature often inclines toward jealousy and harshness. When a mother’s virtue is uneven, her love for her children becomes partial. She cherishes her own offspring but treats the children of concubines with coldness. Stepmothers, even more so, often neglect the children of their predecessors. As with Min Ziqian’s mother, who in winter clad her own son in silk floss but dressed Ziqian in reed catkins, then sent him to drive the carriage—such was her cruelty. If even Ziqian’s mother acted thus, what can be said of others? Countless orphaned and young stepchildren suffer cruel fates.

Among common folk, when disputes over property arise, elders and elder siblings—even those who have married into the family—may act without kindness or righteousness, oppressing their juniors. They craft false petitions, forge contracts, instruct brokers to bear false witness, and bribe clerks to twist the law, leaving the young helpless and stifled, with no recourse for grievance. Worse still, some resort to dark schemes and treachery, even harming their lives. Alas! How can one bear to speak of it?

In former times, the Li Zhiben household numbered over a hundred descendants, old and young living in harmony. The Yang brothers, orphaned and alone, relied upon one another. When the ancients sought to relieve famine, they made the care of orphans and the young their first priority. Such conduct truly fulfills the Way of cherishing the young.

**In praise I say:**
Yao cherished the young child;
Laozi longed for infancy.
Confucius’s aspiration:
*“The young should be cherished.”*
In their earliest years,
their virtue remains pure, undiluted.
Easily oppressed,
they must be protected and upheld.
Thus, for orphans and the young,
benevolence should come first.

*Here ends Scroll Two of the* Taishang Ganying Pian.
*Scroll Three of the* Taishang Ganying Pian *begins.*

**The Transmission of Li Changling**
*With a Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi*

Even insects and plants must not be harmed.

As the transmission states: According to the scriptures, all sentient beings possess within their single bodies the full array of forms and substances, taking shape as they are created. Ignorance gives rise to egg-born life; the womb of afflictions envelops the womb-born; the moisture of craving soaks the moisture-born; and sudden surges of vexation produce the transformation-born. Among these four modes of birth, humanity further distinguishes twelve classes of beings. Within each of these twelve classes, there are in turn eighty-four thousand distinct kinds. Taken together, the twelve classes thus comprise one million and eighty thousand varieties. How vast, then, is the diversity of sentient life!

Yet when we observe our daily conduct, we see that the eye, upon beholding rare treasures, immediately gives rise to thoughts of greed; the tongue, upon tasting rich and savory flavors, immediately gives rise to thoughts of killing; and the mind, upon grasping at splendor, immediately gives rise to thoughts of desire. Not only do the eye, tongue, and mind act thus—so too do the ear, nose, and body. Such deluded thoughts, such inverted perceptions, arise without cease. Even at the moment of death, when the eyes close, all remains in chaos, and one cannot take control. On the thirtieth day of the twelfth month, when feet scurry and hands flail, can one truly be master of oneself? It is from this that the Buddha’s twelve classes of beings and one million eighty thousand varieties arise.

In former times, when the Buddha wandered and taught among the kingdoms, there was one land that resisted his transformation. He sent Maudgalyāyana, and the entire nation came before him. The assembly asked the Buddha why. The Buddha replied: “The people of this land were once a swarm of bees. Maudgalyāyana was then a woodcutter who, while gathering firewood, struck a branch and disturbed the hive. The bees swarmed in fury, seeking to sting him. Maudgalyāyana said to them: ‘You all possess the Buddha-nature, yet through evil karma you have drifted into this state. Even now, you harbor petty resentment and wish to vent your venom. May I swiftly attain enlightenment and first deliver you.’ At that moment, the bees seemed to awaken and took his words to heart. Thus, when they now appear before us, they all follow in submission.”

Elsewhere, by the side of the Karanda Pond in Jambudvīpa, he expounded the Dharma for the assembly.

At that time, a clam, overjoyed upon hearing the Dharma, leapt from the pond and concealed itself among the grasses, listening intently with

Thus, how does the Most High show compassion for the wicked?

In former times, the residents of Chaozhou, beyond their daily sustenance, vied in consuming aquatic creatures. They knew not that on the twenty-fifth day of the twelfth month each year, the Jade Emperor presents his report to the Three Pure Ones, traverses the heavens, descends to observe the world, and determines the judgment of sins and blessings. On that day, the Envoys of the Nine Heavens, the sun, moon, and stars, the spirits of heaven and earth, the sacred mountains and rivers, and the deities of altars and temples all submit their detailed accounts.

One day, the Jade Emperor summoned the Central Heaven’s North Pole Purple Tenuity Great Emperor and asked: “In the southern continent of Jambudvīpa, within the astrological division of Wu, lies the land called Chaozhou. What is the state of its goodness and evil?”

The North Pole Emperor replied: “The inhabitants of this region are truly wicked, slaughtering living beings without restraint. I fear that as time accumulates, their vengeful spirits will grow ever more entangled. Unless the land is utterly submerged to form a great lake, it will not suffice to cleanse the years of cruel killings and the noxious, defiling qi of their savagery.”

The Jade Emperor said, “Let it be so.”

After the North Pole Emperor withdrew, he summoned Zhenwu and said: “As the patroller of the Three Realms, charged with examining good and evil, how could you not know of the wickedness of Chaozhou’s people? Their noxious, defiling qi has ascended to disrupt the Jade Emperor’s celestial procession, causing the sun and moon to lose their light and the stars to fall from their courses. The Jade Emperor has decreed that the entire city be sunk and destroyed. This task falls to you—proceed swiftly.”

Zhenwu replied: “A land spanning a thousand *li* is not uniformly corrupt. If all are sunk without distinction, I fear many will suffer unjustly. Allow me to descend and further inquire of the local earth deities before reporting back.”

The North Pole Emperor said: “The deities of that region have already received their edicts and been relocated elsewhere.”

Zhenwu then sighed deeply and said: “Alas! Even a single creature’s life remains bound in the cycle of rebirth, not easily liberated. How much more so when a thousand *li* become a watery expanse, where beings turn upon one another in harm, their great grievances ever more entangled—how can they be saved?”

He again pleaded earnestly, dispatching his subordinate Celestial Stem generals to conduct another investigation. Thereupon, one of the generals transformed into a giant soft-shelled turtle, floating upon the Han River. First, the local officials and their attendants carved its flesh for consumption, followed by the common people, who did likewise.

The Celestial Stem general returned and reported. Zhenwu once more requested to descend himself. This time, he transformed into a wandering Daoist, begging for water in the streets. For seven days, every household’s water reeked of filth. Only in the last home, belonging to an old woman called Jiao, was the water pure and clean.

The Perfected Lord questioned her, and the old woman replied: “Our family of three has long detested the stench of flesh. We eat only vegetables.”

The Perfected Lord said: “I am no mortal. This region will soon be submerged to form a great lake. Though I wished to save it, it is no longer possible. You must flee to another place at once.”

Before long, a torrential rain indeed fell, and a great flood surged forth. In an instant, the thousand-*li* expanse of the region became towering waves, vast and boundless.

On that day, the Perfected Lord descended again and said to Jiao: “You shall preside over this lake.” Several days later, the old woman died, followed by her son and daughter-in-law. They thus became spirits, and the lake was named *Jiao* after her surname.

Thus, how does the Heavenly Perfected One show compassion for the wicked? The scripture says: “Do not harbor resentment toward the stubborn”—this is its meaning.

A Eulogy says:
The wickedness of men—
is it truly their nature?
Seeing it is like plunging hands into boiling water,
and pity already takes shape.
“Do not harbor resentment toward the stubborn”—
the scripture’s admonition is clear.
Death, ruin, punishment, and execution—
these are but groping in the dark.
Yet there are those who rescue the lost,
a lamp in the dark chamber.
They delight in the goodness of others.

The tradition says: “My single word
most perfectly fulfills all vows and practices.”
When a person utters a good vow,
heaven hears it like the crack of firecrackers.
All buddhas without exception protect and cherish it;
the Perfected of the Highest Heaven without exception uphold it—
for the heart is in accord.

According to the *Tang Literary Anthology*:
Long ago, Gao Mai, seeing the eminent talents of his village,
took charge of the long-burning lamp in the sutra repository of Baorong Monastery.
Through diligent cultivation, he achieved success and, overjoyed, made a vow,
inscribing it in verse to praise it.
Later, pressed by many affairs,
he was unable to compose the text.
Suddenly, he contracted an eye ailment, his vision blurred,
as though gazing through layered silk.
He often sighed in regret,
unsure of the cause of his offense.
One night, he dreamed a spirit told him:
“Have you failed in your duty to the long-burning lamp?”
Mai awoke at once, rose, and composed the verse.
The next day, his eyes were healed.

Thus, how do the buddhas protect and cherish the good?

According to the *Records of Divine Responses*:
Cheng Sichang often stayed in Mizhou,
where he saw that the people of the western outskirts were fond of eating birds and sparrows.
Several hunters daily trapped and killed them,
some crushed head or breast beneath the talons and beaks of hawks and falcons,
others broken of leg or wing in nets and snares,
some pierced by arrows, others struck by pellets—
their piteous cries were unbearable to hear.
Thereupon, he rose at night and stood beneath the stars,
gazing heavenward, and declared:
“I wish to take all the living beings my family has consumed,
together with a *Precept Against Killing* chart for the karmic bonds of my seven generations of parents,
carve blocks, and print copies for distribution,
that this region may all know repentance.”
That day happened to be the descent of the Perfected Warrior Lord.
He prayed, entrusting the task to the sage’s power,
that it might be widely circulated.
After the blocks were carved and printing completed,
a man named Peng Jing, who lived at the barrier, also requested a copy
and took it home to show his wife.
His wife, Hua-shi the Third, flew into a rage upon seeing it,
tore it up, and cast it into a filthy place.
Soon after, she bought a fish and took up a knife to slice it.
The fish suddenly leapt and struck her eye,
causing blood to gush forth.
Instantly, it transformed into worms,
which crawled over her body, biting everywhere.
As the commotion spread,
the garrison commander rushed over,
only to see a spirit standing before him,
who declared: “I am the Perfected Warrior.
I have observed that in this place there is one great act of goodness—
Cheng Sichang of Xinghua Commandery, who printed and distributed the *Precept Against Killing* chart.
Yet this woman, Hua-shi, dared to tear it up and cast it into filth.
Her crime is grave; she should die at once.
Others who do not repent shall also incur punishment.
Wait until my descent on the twenty-seventh day to see what transpires.”

Then how do the Highest Perfected Ones uphold and sustain goodness? Alas! The Highest Perfected and the Buddhas, having long abided in liberation, still take such delight in the goodness of others. How much more should humans, who remain in the sea of suffering, do likewise? In the world, such people are not absent. Hu Wending, upon seeing Li Wenjing—serene and free from desire, and further enriched by learning—exclaimed: “This man stands at the eight- or nine-tenths mark of attainment.” When he beheld Wang Suogong, steadfast and unshaken, coupled with earnest study, he again declared: “This man, too, stands at the eight- or nine-tenths mark.” Shao Kangjie, observing Sima Wengong’s loyalty, kindness, brotherly respect, reverence, frugality, and integrity, rejoiced and said: “This man walks with both feet firmly on the ground—an eight- or nine-tenths man indeed.” When he saw Han Qinzhong argue matters before his superiors without evasion, he remarked: “This man is utterly pure yet unostentatious, utterly upright yet unprovocative, utterly courageous yet gentle—truly rare and admirable.” Likewise, Du Zhengxian, upon hearing of another’s goodness, rejoiced as if it were his own. Liu Jixian, upon hearing of another’s goodness, extolled it without end. Wen Lugong, upon seeing another’s goodness, feared he could not match it. Han Zhongxian, upon hearing of even a small act of goodness, would say: “This is something I have not yet achieved.” Could such men as these, unless they truly delighted in goodness—reaching that state where delight precludes all aversion—have acted thus?

In praise it is said:
Goodness is innate in all,
Flowing from heaven’s nature.
Why then envy and malice,
The triumph of self-interest?
Aggression, contention, resentment, desire—
These afflict one another.
Only when the self is forgotten
Does this heart cease to vie.
Seeing another’s goodness,
Like the moon reflected in a mirror.
Aiding another in distress—

As the *Yunji Qiqian* states: “The Venerable Nuojuluo said: ‘One of great wisdom is like a steadfast lamp within a basket. One of great merit and virtue is like a bubble floating upon the sea. One of great skillful means is like rain falling through empty space.’ Now, when one aids another in distress with the strength to do so, this is precisely what is meant by great skillful means. Like rain that nourishes all, its benefit to living beings is beyond words.”

Distress takes many forms. In illness, the urgent need is for medicine. In death and mourning, it is for funeral arrangements. In hunger and want, it is for food and drink. In marriage, it is for dowries and provisions. Beyond these, there are countless other urgencies that cannot all be enumerated. Yet if one responds to each according to its urgency, aiding with skillful means, this is truly to relieve another’s distress.

Long ago, Xu Shuwei, in his youth, prayed for success in the imperial examinations. One night, he dreamed a spirit told him: “If you wish to pass, you must rely on hidden virtue.” Xu reflected that, being poor and without means, only medicine could serve this purpose. Thus, he devoted himself to medical texts, eventually mastering them. Regardless of rank, he hastened to aid all who called. As the number of lives he saved grew, so too did his renown. Later, the spirit appeared to him again in a dream and bestowed a poem:
*“Medicine bears hidden merit,
In the hall of Chen and Lou.
At the gaming table, ‘Six!’ is called,
Yet ‘Five!’ is what they count.”*
That year, he placed sixth in the imperial examinations. The candidate above him was named Chen Zuyan, and the one below, Lou Cai. When official appointments were later adjusted, he was promoted to the fifth rank, returning home as a commissioned official—every word of the poem proved true. This was one who aided others in their distress.

Fan Wenzheng, while governing Binzhou, once took leisure to ascend a tower with wine and his subordinates for a view. As they raised their first cups, he suddenly saw several mourners in hempen garments moving about, preparing funeral rites. He sent someone to inquire and learned that a low-ranking official, residing away from home, had just died, leaving no provisions for his burial. Fan immediately canceled the feast and generously provided for the deceased. This was one who aided another in the distress of death and mourning.

Fan Zhongxuan, governing Qingzhou during a great famine, saw corpses of the starved lining the roads, yet had no grain to distribute. He requested the release of stored grain from the Ever-Normal Granary, but the local officials refused, saying: “A memorial must first be submitted.” Fan replied: “A person cannot survive seven days without food. If we wait for approval, how can we act in time?” That very day, he released the grain, adding: “Let none of you share responsibility—I shall bear it alone.” This was one who aided others in the distress of hunger and want.

Zha Dao, before passing the examinations, once traveled through the Xiang and Han regions, borrowing money from others to the sum of one hundred thousand coins. On the road, he encountered an old friend urgently preparing for his daughter’s marriage but lacking funds. Without hesitation, Zha emptied his purse and gave it all, showing no reluctance. This was one who aided another in the distress of marriage.

With such devotion of heart, how could they not be rewarded in the end? Xu Shuwei ultimately passed the examinations; Fan Wenzheng rose to the rank of vice-minister; Fan Zhongxuan became chancellor; and Zha Dao attained the position of attendant-in-ordinary. Were these not their rewards?

In general, when one dwells in the world, one ought to act thus in the face of another’s distress.

In praise it is said:
The noble one aids in urgency,
Not for private gain.
As one trapped in a well,
If you withhold the rope,
It is as if you killed them.
To bow and yield while another drowns—
Is this not self-deception?
Judging others by oneself,
Their urgency is plain.
Rescuing another in peril—

As the *Yunji Qiqian* states: “One who saves a life extends their own years by twelve. If the one saved is a good person, the extension doubles.” Then how can one fail to rescue another in peril? Peril here means the very threshold of life and death.

When Wang Jun rebelled in Shu, the court, enraged by Shu’s repeated uprisings, ordered Lei Youzhong to lead troops to suppress it, intending to massacre the city. At the time, the Shu scholars Fan Can, known for his integrity; Fan Xun, devoted to study; and the eminent monk Wenjian, all respected figures in Shu, joined together to secretly welcome the imperial army. Kneeling, they pleaded: “The people of Shu are weak. Those in the city are all common folk, and those who joined the rebellion did so only out of fear. They are not truly guilty.”

When the city was about to fall, they entreated: “We beg that slaughter not be wantonly inflicted; rooting out the ringleaders will suffice.” These three were all men of towering stature, and their arguments, delivered with fervor, were made selflessly for the sake of others, springing from utmost sincerity. Lei Youzhong was moved by their words and replied: “Had I not heard the words of such elders, the massacre of the city would have been certain.” Thus, the entire populace of the city was spared from death—through the efforts of Fan and Wenjian.

Zhang Xun, the great-grandfather of Zhang Dexiang, the Duke of Xuān, once served the Min kingdom as prefect of Jianzhou. His wife, Lady Lian, was a woman of wisdom and discernment. Zhang Xun once dispatched two generals on a military campaign; when they failed to return on time, he intended to execute them. Lady Lian interceded, and the two generals were spared. Later, they served as generals under the Southern Tang and led the attack to retake Jianzhou. By then, Zhang Xun had already passed away. Lady Lian still resided in Jianzhou, and the two generals sent envoys bearing generous gifts of gold and silk, along with a white banner, which they presented to her, saying: “When we sack the city, plant this banner at your gate, and we will command our soldiers not to violate your household.” Lady Lian returned their gold, silk, and banner, refusing them, and said: “I am grateful for your remembrance of past kindness and your wish to spare this city. If you insist on slaughter, my family will perish alongside the rest—I do not wish to survive alone.” Moved by her words, the two generals abandoned their plan to massacre the city—through the efforts of Lady Lian.

The Fan family has since become a prominent clan in Shu, with descendants flourishing and officials numbering in the hundreds. The Zhang family, too, has grown into a great lineage in Jian, with successive generations attaining high ministerial ranks—all descendants of Lady Lian.

Alas! The lives these two families saved were so numerous, and the recompense bestowed by Heaven was equally abundant. Such cases are indeed rare in the world. Yet in all matters of peril—whether from flood and fire, bandits and thieves, famine and cold, illness and plague, judicial coercion, or distress in travel—if one maintains such a heart of compassion, though the circumstances may differ, the act of rescuing those in danger remains the same.

**Eulogy:**
When a child falls into a well,
All who witness it feel alarm.
When a vessel is about to shatter,
Onlookers reach out to catch it.
Turning peril into safety,
Reviving the crippled to walk—
Once the other’s distress is averted,
My joy is profound.
With a heart of benevolence,
Regret and blame are no more.

*This concludes Chapter Three of the* Taishang Ganying Pian. *Chapter Four of the* Taishang Ganying Pian *begins.*

**The Biography of Li Changling**

**Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi:**
To see another’s gain as one’s own gain,
To see another’s loss as one’s own loss.

The *Biography* states: Wenju asked Vimalakīrti at the bodhimanda, “What is the bodhimanda?”
Vimalakīrti replied, “An even mind is the bodhimanda.”

Zhaozhou asked Nanquan, “What is the Dao?”
Nanquan replied, “An even mind is the Dao.”

Thus, is an even mind the Dao, or is it the bodhimanda?
Ouyang Wenzhong cultivated goodwill and delighted in virtue, yet he often found maintaining an even mind difficult.
Prefect Peng Siyong of Peng studied tirelessly and would tell others, “I do not study for others; since youth, I have studied only to even my mind.”
Is an even mind truly so difficult? Must one learn it for oneself?

Generally, the mind as a thing has no inherent substance, yet it is not absent when put to use. If one wishes to even the mind, it lies within oneself—what difficulty could there be? Those among the elders who found it difficult did so lamenting the lack of others to guide them, offering instruction to later learners.

Those in the world who cannot regard another’s gains and losses as their own surely do not understand this. Precisely because they do not understand, the distinction between self and others always prevails, and transgressions accumulate upon them like water flowing to the sea, growing deeper and broader day by day. How then could they be fit to receive the teachings of the bodhimanda or the Dao?

In ancient times, Xue Yuan served as minister to the state of Yan. He could not maintain an even mind: when he saw others gain, he felt as though he had lost; when he saw others lose, he felt as though he had gained. He never recommended a single scholar or promoted a single worthy. If there was anything praiseworthy, he envied it like an enemy, suppressing it to prevent advancement. In his later years, one son died in prison, and the rest were afflicted with blindness, deafness, muteness, hunchbackedness, madness, or idiocy.

At that time, Gongming Zigao saw him and took pity, rebuking him: “All this stems from your misguided thoughts and actions. Your crimes warrant the extermination of your entire family. Though your sons suffer thus, it is not yet enough to exhaust your transgressions.” Terrified, Xue Yuan earnestly begged for salvation. Zigao then transmitted to him the *Central Admonitions* of Master Red Pine. Yuan vowed to reform and practiced diligently, barely preserving one son.

What, then, is this *Central Admonition*? I have humbly reflected upon the *Book of Songs*, and it differs not at all from this text. In essence, both point out the errors in how people employ their minds. Their power to avert misfortune is most potent.

Xue Yuan reverently received the *Central Admonition* and thus escaped the extermination of his family. Zhou Chi propagated this text and thereby avoided starvation—is this not proof of its power?

Alas! All things in the world turn to emptiness in an instant; only evil karma remains a lasting burden upon the self. The purpose of annotating this book is precisely to elucidate this truth. Those who know of this book cannot but seek to read it; having read it, they cannot but put it into practice. If one truly acts thus, in the future, when facing the path of life and death, one will receive great power.

**Eulogy:**
Though a hundred chapters fill this book,
Mu’s oath concludes it best.
When others rise, regard it as your own;
If the people stray from the mean, blame has not yet departed.
The lord of *Hong Fan* grants blessings equally—
Not for others’ sake, but for one’s own good.
Do not expose the faults of others.

The Tradition states: Ma Fubo admonished his descendants, saying, "When you hear of another’s faults, it should be as if you heard the names of your parents—your ears may hear them, but your mouth must not speak of them." This fully embodies the teaching of the Highest Sages: "Do not expose the faults of others." As for Master Guishan, he further refined this saying: "Not only must the mouth not speak of them, the ears must not even hear of them." This surpasses Ma Fubo’s teaching, ascending to a yet higher realm.

Generally, those entangled in worldly toil cannot be without faults. Yet their faults are their own—what concern are they to me? For me, it suffices to remain in a state of not hearing, not knowing; thus, their faults will never be exposed through me. If, however, one utters even a single word to expose them, those faults will inevitably spread. Reputation will diminish from that moment, and one’s long-held integrity will collapse. If one traces the cause of this, who shall bear the blame? Know this: a noble person does not engage in exposing others’ faults, while the petty do so without scruple.

In ancient times, among the people of Guicheng, there was a man named Zhu Qisheng. He was narrow-minded and shallow, delighting in exposing others’ faults. If someone had a physical imperfection, he mocked them; if they were beautiful, he slandered them with envy; if they were foolish, he belittled them; if they were wise, he critiqued and ranked them; if they were poor, he despised them; if they were wealthy, he defamed them. With officials, he exposed their hidden deceit; with scholars and friends, he revealed their private affairs. If anyone’s lineage, talent, or reputation surpassed his own, leaving no room for criticism, he would cunningly seek out their flaws, saying: "Their grandfather was a commoner," "Their father was base," "Their mother’s family were merchants," "Their wife’s family were brokers," "Their brother is unprincipled," "Their son is worthless." Even the slightest fault would be exaggerated and embellished to magnify their shortcomings. No matter how trivial the matter, he acted thus—turning nothing into something, one into a hundred, suspicion into certainty, coincidence into deliberate offense, necessity into willful misconduct, and error into malice. If he judged someone gullible, he would confront them to their face, humiliate them before others, and slander them excessively. He treated strangers this way, and his own kin no differently. All his acquaintances fell under his censure. In extreme cases, he called his parents obstinate and unruly, his brothers like Guan and Cai. He would incite others to litigation, then mediate the dispute himself; urge others to curse their horses, then testify against them. Over time, such habits became second nature, and he saw no wrong in them.

In his later years, he suddenly fell ill, his tongue often turning yellow, requiring bloodletting by lancet—several *sheng* of blood would be drawn before it ceased. Yet soon after, the affliction would recur, demanding further needling. Within a single year, this occurred five to seven times, the agony so excruciating as to defy description, until at last his tongue withered and he died. This case is identical to that of Daoist Zhang Qiyi, who delighted in mocking others and ultimately died from a tongue-chewing affliction.

Is it then permissible to magnify others’ shortcomings?

**Eulogy:**
What I establish for myself, I aspire to sagehood and worthiness.
They, too, are but human—why should they be different?
If by chance they err, their faults may yet be washed clean.
But once their misdeeds are broadcast, it becomes a lifelong stain.
Thus, the gentleman values concealing others’ faults,
And does not flaunt his own strengths.

As the *Tradition* says:
Laozi declared: *"A skilled merchant stores deeply as if empty; a man of great virtue appears dull."*
Confucius said: *"The Way of the gentleman is obscure yet daily grows clearer; the way of the petty man is glaring yet daily fades."*

Generally, as humans, can any of us be entirely without merit?
If one possesses merit, it should be so by nature.
The key lies in further veiling one’s light and nurturing one’s character,
Ensuring daily renewal—only then can virtue be perfected.
Otherwise, one risks becoming frivolous and ostentatious,
Revealing one’s pettiness and shallowness.
How does this align with *"a man of great virtue appears dull"* or *"the Way of the gentleman is obscure yet daily grows clearer"*?

Thus, when Ouyang Wenzhong (Ouyang Xiu) excelled in literature, he would discuss governance with guests, never touching on writing.
When Cai Duanning (Cai Xiang) excelled in governance, he would discuss literature with guests, never touching on affairs of state.
Both were masters of self-effacement.
Alas, how few understand this—most instead indulge in self-display.

In ancient times, Li Bi was exceptionally intelligent. At the age of seven, he was already skilled in composition. Once, he wrote a *Ballad of Endless Singing*, which went:

"Heaven covers me, earth bears me,
Heaven and earth gave me life—was it with intent or none?
Else I’d fast and ascend the celestial thoroughfare,
Else I’d jingle my jade pendants and roam the imperial capital.
How could I fail to rise, fail to depart,
Wasting my towering stature as a true man?
O true man, ah, true man,
My lifelong aspirations brim with grand designs.
I beg you to survey the affairs of a hundred years—
Once my work is done, I’ll take a small boat and drift upon the five lakes."

When the song was finished, none failed to praise it. Only Zhang Jiuling sternly warned him: *"To conceal one’s talents within is what the ancients valued. Now that you’ve gained fame so early, you will surely face setbacks. You must veil your light and nurture your character—only then may you perfect your virtue. How much more so for a child? If you compose poetry, simply admire the scenery and sing of past and present; never exalt yourself, for that is the wiser course."* Li Bi seemed to awaken. He wept and thanked Zhang repeatedly, and from then on never again displayed his talents. Had he not ceased, how could he have perfected his virtue and become a renowned minister of his age?

One must know: if a person has strengths, they must never flaunt them.

**Eulogy:**
The Three Powers stand as one: Heaven, Earth, and Man.
The warp and weft of the world rest upon a single person.
From worthy to sage, from sage to spirit—
Yet in an instant, if one borrows without renewal, their virtue does not grow.
Yan Yuan appeared dull, yet his example is worthy of inscription on one’s sash.
To suppress evil and exalt goodness—

The *Commentary* states: *"To suppress evil and exalt goodness is called a gentleman in the *Book of Changes*, and a good person in the *Book of Songs*."*
The *Changes* says: *"Fire above heaven—Great Possession. The image of greatness and inclusiveness. The gentleman suppresses evil and exalts goodness, aligning with Heaven’s blessed mandate."*
Thus, to suppress evil and exalt goodness is to follow Heaven’s virtue and fulfill the mandate of all things. Is Heaven’s blessed mandate truly found in this?

Generally, unless one is a Yao or Shun, how can every deed be perfectly good? What is not good is called evil. If I can suppress it, preventing one from abandoning themselves, and instead encourage them to turn toward goodness—who does not value goodness? In every word and deed, if there is anything worthy, it is called good. If I can exalt it, making it known to all so that people strive to emulate it—is this not Heaven’s blessed mandate?

Heaven’s blessed mandate is thus;
My treatment of others is thus.
Is this not aligning with Heaven’s blessed mandate?

The *Doctrine of the Mean* says: *"Shun—was he not greatly wise? He concealed others’ faults and exalted their goodness."* If even Shun acted thus, how much more should others?

Therefore, Zhao Kangjing (Zhao Pu) made it his duty to conceal faults and exalt goodness. Shao Kangjie (Shao Yong) delighted in speaking of others’ virtues and never mentioned their vices. Both grasped the principle of suppressing evil and exalting goodness.

Alas, how few understand this—yet the making or breaking of a person often lies in a single word. Once spoken, how can one not be cautious?

When Kou Laigong (Kou Zhun) was in office, he built a mountain pavilion for his birthday, which was reported by the supervising officials. The emperor was furious, his imperial voice severe, and he said to Wang Wenzheng (Wang Dan): *"Kou Zhun imitates me in every matter—why?"* Wang replied: *"Kou Zhun is truly capable, but he is simple-minded."* The emperor said: *"Only simple-minded?"* And so he did not pursue the matter further. Had it not been for Wang’s single remark, how could the emperor’s anger over the mountain pavilion have dissipated so quickly? How could the praise of Kou’s ability have reached the throne?

When Wang Wenkang (Wang Zeng) governed Shu, he sentenced all thieves, regardless of the severity of their crimes, to death. Some criticized his excessive harshness. Later, when Liu Ye was summoned back, the emperor asked: *"Between Ling Ce and Wang Zeng, who governed Shu better?"* Liu said: *"Ling Ce served during years of abundance, so he could govern with leniency. Wang Zeng faced years of scarcity, fearing the people would turn to banditry, so he had to enforce the law strictly. In each other’s place, they would have done the same."* The emperor approved of his words. Had it not been for Liu’s single remark, how could the suspicion of excessive harshness have been so swiftly resolved?

How could such words of mutual understanding have reached the throne? This is the suppression of evil and the promotion of good—what the Highest Dao calls a good person, and what the *Book of Changes* praises as "following Heaven’s blessed mandate."

**Eulogy:**
The seeds of good and evil
share the same beginning.
To lift or suppress, advance or retreat—
like a turning windlass.
To curb one and exalt the other
depends on the pivot of the mechanism.
As spring’s breath stirs,
the hundred dormant creatures awaken.
To follow the blessed mandate of Heaven—
is this not the heart of Heaven itself?

**To yield much and take little.**
As the tradition states: *"To yield much and take little"* seems to refer to the bonds between brothers. Generally, when righteousness pertains to the great relationships, one ought to act thus in matters of wealth. If one were to do the opposite, where would righteousness remain? Moreover, wealth is especially valued for its connection to righteousness. Heaven’s judgment of men often hinges on this. Does taking more today truly mean one gains more? Does taking less today truly mean one gains less?

In ancient times, when Xue Bao divided property with his brothers, he took the elderly slaves, the barren fields, and the broken utensils. When Xu Ji divided property with his two uncles, he first asked them to take whatever they desired, leaving only a chest of books and two dilapidated rooms, which he accepted with joy, showing no resentment. Likewise: Wei Gong in the Xianxi era took only one or two divine artifacts; Dong Rong Shiheng took only books; Du Zhengxian Yan gave everything to his brothers; and Du Shentong Chun gave all to his youngest brother. These were all cases of yielding much and taking little.

As for official ranks, some have yielded them without accepting, such as Shen Jizhong, who twice declined imperial favors from suburban sacrifices, instead conferring the honors upon his two younger brothers. Xu Xi, after receiving an imperial appointment, deferred it to his brother’s son. Those who haggle over every penny and fight over every inch—how can their swift ruin be compared to such examples?

**Eulogy:**

To know the masculine yet guard the feminine—great whiteness appears as black. Not to be poor is the treasure; the Old Master’s thrift. To broaden oneself by narrowing others—how can this be called abundant virtue? The mind must guard against excess, the body must practice restraint. Yet to speak only of wealth is to fall short of Heaven’s principle.

To endure humiliation without resentment: as the *Nirvana Sutra* relates, there was once a man who praised the Buddha as one of great blessed countenance. A listener grew furious and said, "He lost his mother seven days after birth—how can he have great blessed countenance?" The praiser replied, "In the prime of his years and strength, he did not die prematurely; though violently beaten, he felt no anger; though his horse was struck, he did not retaliate—is this not great blessed countenance?" The angry man’s heart was subdued. Generally, only one with blessed virtue can act thus, and only one who acts thus possesses blessed virtue. Such depth is not within the grasp of those with narrow and shallow capacities. Have you not heard of those in our dynasty with great blessed virtue?

Long ago, when Lü Mengzheng was appointed Vice Minister, he was about to enter the court when a courtier behind a curtain pointed and said, "Is this little fellow also a Vice Minister?" Mengzheng feigned ignorance. Later, his colleagues insisted on learning the man’s name, but Mengzheng firmly refused, saying, "If I knew his name, I would never forget it for the rest of my life—better not to hear it at all."

When Fu Bi, Duke Wenzhong, was young, someone reviled him, and he heard it as if he had not. When told of it, he said, "Perhaps he was cursing someone else." They replied, "He clearly called out your name, Bi." He said, "Are there not others in the world with the same name?" And so he never inquired further.

These were scholars, and some might manage such restraint. But when even military men can do the same, it is truly admirable. Di Qing, the Vice Commander of Zhen Ding, once feasted with Sun Mian. A certain Liu Yi, also present, was known for his bluntness. When an actor mocked a scholar in jest, Liu flew into a rage and cried, "How dare a branded convict behave so!" He cursed Di Qing without cease, even hurling cups and dishes before storming off. Sun Mian watched Di Qing, whose composure remained unshaken, his smile unwavering, his words ever gentle. Only upon returning did Sun regret his own conduct—by then, Di Qing had already come to his door to apologize.

Wang Ji, serving as an auxiliary commander, joined the campaign against Liu Gan. Wang was cautious, dutiful, and sparing of words, appearing as if he had no ability. His peers constantly berated and scorned him, but he paid no heed, devoting himself entirely to his duties. He charged fearlessly into enemy lines, breaking through their ranks, and was later promoted to Commander. Could one without extraordinary forbearance have done the same? These men all had futures ahead of them, enjoying the fruits of blessed virtue. Who can say the Buddha’s words are untrue?

**Eulogy:**

Yanzi did not contend; Sui Hui could endure lowliness.
Xiahui bared his chest; Master De endured being spat upon.
Praised in their time, their fragrant names endure in records and biographies.
With hearts harmonious and qi at peace, they smiled as they observed the world’s changes.
Like an empty boat or drifting tiles—what cause had they for resentment?

"Receiving favor as if startled."

The *Scripture* states in the *Chapter on Favor and Disgrace*: "Favor is lowly; gaining it as if startled, losing it as if startled—this is called favor and disgrace as if startled." Yet in this passage, it is also said: "Receiving favor as if startled." Then is receiving favor truly like being startled?

Generally, fortune harbors misfortune within.
When receiving favor, one cannot avoid excess and fullness.
Should fullness reach its peak, then boundless calamities lie concealed within.
Once favor is lost, disaster inevitably erupts—how could one not truly be startled?

Thus the noble person receives it ever as if startled.
Formerly, when Wang Wenzhenggong Dan first passed the imperial examination, he was appointed magistrate of Pingjiang, then vice-prefect of Zhengzhou. Soon after, he was named Right Rectifier of Remonstrance and Drafter of Edicts, then Academician of the Jixian Hall, and promoted to Hanlin Academician. He was appointed Vice Minister of Works, then Participant in Government Affairs, and elevated to Grand Councilor. He accompanied the emperor on the Feng and Shan sacrifices, the rites at Fenyin, and served as commissioner for the Jade Pure Luminous Response Palace, welcoming the sacred image and the celestial writings carved in jade. At the Taiji Temple in Yanzhou, he presented the imperial regalia and was enfeoffed as Grand Preceptor, concurrently Minister of State. In his later years, his rank grew ever more exalted. Whenever his family offered congratulations, he would stop them, saying: "With such fortune, my anxiety only deepens—what is there to celebrate?" When he served as envoy for the court’s rites and departed the capital in the imperial carriage, all the officials gathered to see him off, unanimously praising his illustrious favor. Yet he said otherwise: "I only feel restless and uneasy."

Could others in his place have done the same?
When Zhang Zhongdinggong Yong was appointed Vice Minister of Personnel, he said to his confidant Li Tian: "Now I have received the sagely grace to be promoted to Minor Steward of the Celestial Office—how fearful, how fearful! If one reflects on the descent, the self remains secure; if one reflects on the ascent, then ruin is surely near."

With a trembling countenance as though gripped by fear—could others in his place have done the same? Precisely because he was thus, he was able to retain his favor. Had he been otherwise, he would not have escaped swift calamity and ruin. Have you not heard of Lu Duoxun and Zhong Fang?

When Lu Duoxun was first appointed Vice Director of the Chancellery, his attire and possessions grew increasingly lavish. His father, Yi, grew solemn and displeased, saying: "Our family has always been modest scholars—now, with sudden wealth and honor, we have come to this. I know not where we shall finally rest our carriage." Duoxun paid no heed to his father’s words and ultimately met with disaster.

When Zhong Fang was first summoned by the court, his reputation soared. His mother grew furious and rebuked him: "I warned you five times over not to gather disciples or seek fame—now you are indeed known, and no longer at peace." Fang disregarded his mother’s words and was ultimately undone by his renown. Both failed to treat favor with the awe it demands.

**Eulogy:**
When the sacrificial vessel gleams with azure and yellow,
it is the tree’s doom.
When the lone calf is clad in embroidered silk,
can it live in the wilds?
Rank and eminence are but borrowed things,
fleeting as passing shadows.
To covet glory while seeking self-preservation
is the womb of disaster.
Those who know the Dao—
how could they not tremble?

*This concludes Scroll Four of the* **Supreme Scripture on Retribution and Response**.
*Scroll Five of the* **Supreme Scripture on Retribution and Response** *begins.*

**The Account of Li Changling, with a Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

**On Bestowing Kindness Without Seeking Recompense**

The *Tradition* states: The noble person acts with intention in bestowing kindness, but without intention in seeking recompense. If one acts with intention in seeking recompense, then self-interest lies in the reward. Such an act is not truly for another. Recompense may not be certain, but resentment will surely precede it—how can this be called kindness? Thus, recompense unattained is ever present in kindness given without intent.

Long ago, Shang Lin served as magistrate of Wushan. The county commandant, Li Zhu, was most fierce and unruly. Without warning, he fell gravely ill. Lin took pity on him and asked if he had any last wishes. The commandant wiped his tears and spoke of his aged mother and young daughter. When he died, Lin set aside a portion of his salary to send the mother and her son’s coffin back to Hedong, and he arranged for the daughter’s marriage into a respectable family. One night, Lin dreamed of the commandant as if alive, bowing and weeping: "You have no son. Moved by your kindness, I have petitioned the Emperor with all my strength. Now I am permitted to become your son." That very month, Lin’s wife conceived. The following year, as Lin resigned his post and traveled downstream, whenever he passed treacherous rapids, he glimpsed the commandant standing faintly on the shore, as if pointing the way. Nearing Jingzhou, he dreamed again of the commandant, who said: "Tomorrow I shall be born in your residence. You must send a small box to the birthing chamber." Lin prepared a small box filled with millet, ready for gruel, and called it *he*. The child was named Ying. As he grew, he was exceedingly generous, quick to benevolence, and deeply filial. He rose to the rank of secretary in the Court of Judicial Review.

Yuwen Yingdi initially oversaw the prisons of Qianjiang. A local man, Fu Ai, had often lodged complaints against the officials of the Transport Bureau. When Fu was later imprisoned on unrelated charges, the officials, resentful, sought to fabricate evidence against him. Yingdi, indignant, vigorously defended him, and the sentence was ultimately reduced. Three years later, Yingdi traveled home along the official route, sailing downstream from Nanbin. It was the sixth month when suddenly a great rain fell, and the waters surged violently, waves towering like mountains. The towline snapped midstream, and the boatmen clasped their hands, resigned to capsizing. Then, a small boat emerged faintly through the mist and waves, drawing near to rescue them. Once ashore, they saw it was Fu Ai. Gazing at one another in astonishment, they exclaimed: "When I once adjudicated the case in Qian, I had no particular intent toward you. Today, you risked your life to come—did you know the drowning man was I? Was this heaven’s design to make the matter known?" They sighed together for a long while.

Alas! When Lin acted as he did for the commandant, he thought only, *We are colleagues; this is how it should be*—how could he have known of the recompense of a son today? When Yingdi acted as he did for Fu Ai, he thought only, *This is how a case should be judged*—how could he have known of the recompense of rescue from drowning today? These were both acts of kindness without intent, recompense unattained. Had there been intent, the outcome would not have been the same.

The ancient saying goes: "Kindness, though bestowed indiscriminately, may meet with unexpected reciprocity; resentment must not be provoked, lest one encounter an adversary on a narrow path." Truly, these are words of profound wisdom.

In praise it is said:
Fear and compassion arise spontaneously from the seven emotions of the heart.
When one seeks reputation through private connections, the mind of profit takes root.
Benevolence is the natural principle of heaven, flourishing when it encounters the world.
Profit, however, is human desire—its end inevitably leads to strife.
A hair’s breadth may span a thousand miles; one must weigh matters carefully.
To give to others without regret is the way.

The *Tradition* says: Laozi declared, "Having acted for others, one finds oneself ever more abundant; having given to others, one finds oneself ever more enriched." Generally, when people confront wealth, most become miserly—some refuse to part with even a single hair, while others give only to regret it later. Yet here are those who give without remorse. How could this be, if not for true joy in generosity? From this, fortune and virtue grow ever greater—is this not to be "ever more abundant, ever more enriched"?

In his youth, Liu Zhongtu once drank at a tavern where he saw a scholar about to call upon the Prefect of Wei to seek funds for his parents’ burial. Zhongtu asked, "How much do you need?" The scholar replied, "Two hundred thousand cash." That very day, Zhongtu returned home, emptied all he possessed—one hundred taels of silver and tens of thousands in coin—and gave it all to the scholar without the slightest reluctance.

When Master Fan Zhongyan sent his son Yaofu back to Gusuzhou to fetch wheat, their boat stopped at Danyang. There, Yaofu saw his old friend Shi Manqing, who had been unable to return home due to three unburied family members. Without hesitation, Yaofu gave him the entire five hundred *shi* of wheat on the boat. Upon returning home, before he could speak, Zhongyan asked, "Did you see your old friend?" Yaofu replied that Manqing had three unburied dead. Zhongyan said, "Why did you not give him the wheat on the boat?" Yaofu answered, "I already did." His face lit up with joy. Could such men as these ever regret their actions? These were indeed great men, and such deeds were only fitting. Yet even among those of lesser stature, such people are not absent.

Consider Li Qian, a wealthy man. During a year of famine, he lent a thousand *shi* of grain to his fellow villagers. The following year, famine struck again, and the people had nothing to repay him.

Li Qian then gathered the villagers and burned the debt records before them, declaring, "The debts are settled. No repayment is needed." The following year brought a bountiful harvest, and the people rushed to repay him, but he accepted nothing. The year after, famine struck again, and Li Qian exhausted his family’s wealth to set up gruel stations, saving tens of thousands of lives. Those who perished, he buried. Someone remarked, "Your hidden virtue is vast." Li Qian replied, "Hidden virtue is like ringing in the ears—only the self perceives it. If others now know, how can it still be called virtue?"

Another man, Li Haoran, also wealthy, spent over forty years aiding the poor and distressed without the slightest slackening. Could such men as these ever regret their actions?

Later, Liu Fan rose to prominence, and the grandsons of both Lis passed the imperial examinations. As the saying goes, "The more one gives, the more one gains"—was this not so?

**Eulogy:**
To begin virtue and not see it through—this the ancients despised.
Better to incur one’s own resentment than to demand fulfillment of promises.
The noble man is not stingy; he acts decisively in doing good.
Wealth despised and cast aside is shallow when hoarded.
To act within one’s means—this is true convenience.
Such are the so-called good men.

The *Traditions* states: Confucius said, "Good men I have never seen. If I could see those who are constant in virtue, that would suffice." But how then does one cultivate and adorn oneself to reach such a state? There is no other way—only by practicing the words in this text can one attain it.

The text says: As a minister, be loyal; as a child, be filial; among siblings, be harmonious; between husband and wife, be concordant. Rectify yourself to transform others, and extend compassion to all things. Do not deceive in darkness, nor tread crooked paths. Advance and retreat with the Dao, accumulate merit through deeds. Console the widowed, pity the orphaned, respect the aged, cherish the young. Grieve at misfortune, rejoice in goodness. Aid the urgent, rescue the imperiled.
