Do not flaunt your strengths nor expose others’ faults. Suppress evil, praise good. Take little, give much. Bear insult without resentment, receive favor with awe. Bestow kindness without seeking repayment, give to others without regret. Rejoice in others’ gains as your own, grieve at their losses as your own. Extend your benevolence even to insects, your kindness even to plants. Those who walk this path—are they not good men?

Formerly, when the retired Grand Councilor Li Fang was honored during the Lantern Festival, Emperor Taizong ordered a palanquin to welcome him, seated him beside the imperial throne, and personally poured him wine from the imperial goblet. Selecting the finest fruits and delicacies, the emperor bestowed them upon him, saying, "Men like you are truly good men and noble gentlemen. Though you held the highest offices, you never harbored thoughts of harming others—this is why I hold you in esteem."

Upon returning home, Li Fang told his sons, "Though I have no extraordinary achievements to astonish the world, I have never obscured others’ virtues, envied others’ advancement, deceived in darkness, or overstepped my bounds. In these four matters, I can say I have walked the path faithfully. Now, the Son of Heaven has honored me before the assembled ministers, calling me a good man and noble gentleman. Such titles are the highest praise for a man. Even Confucius said, ‘If I do not see such a person, how dare I claim the title?’ You must remember the emperor’s words of praise and my lifelong practice of these four principles. Be loyal to your lord, filial to your parents, cultivate yourself with care, and perhaps you may avoid disgrace."

His son, Li Zong’e, faithfully upheld his father’s teachings and became a renowned figure of his time.

When Minister Li Mu passed away, the emperor personally attended the funeral, weeping bitterly. Turning to his attendants, he said, "Does Heaven not wish good men to assist me? Why take him so soon?"

Li Zong’e asked Li Fang, "What did my uncle do to move the emperor to such grief?" Li Fang replied, "Minister Li was pure, upright, and unassuming, free from desire. He was forthright without offending others, yielding without compromise. His learning was comprehensive, his judgment profound and refined—truly a man of great elegance. Moreover, he excelled in governance and was kind in his dealings with others. Thus, on the day of his passing, all who knew him—and even those who did not—lamented in unison. How else could he have moved the emperor, the lord of ten thousand chariots, to attend his funeral and weep so deeply?"

Alas! The path these two men walked truly embodied the text’s definition of a good man. To be called a good man—was this not fitting?

**Eulogy:**

The *Qian* hexagram’s six yang lines—with the *yuan* as the chief of goodness—admit not even an inch of shadow, pure as the celestial image. To receive the center of Heaven, one must cultivate it within. Extend this and act upon it: ease and benefit come through diligence. As yang grows and yin wanes, this heart expands to the breadth of Heaven. All people revere such a one.

As the tradition records: When Juyuan served as county magistrate, he once purchased silk in the public hall, personally unrolling the bolts himself. His maidservant, observing from behind the screen, grew indignant and said, “Who would have thought that today I would be made to serve a silk merchant’s clerk?” She demanded to leave, and none could dissuade her.

When Fan Feng was Imperial Censor, there was a gatekeeper who had served in the Censorate for over forty years, having attended more than twenty censors. He could always discern a censor’s worth: when the censor was virtuous, the staff he held would lie horizontally as he bowed; otherwise, it stood upright. This saying had long been known. Those who became censors feared nothing more than his staff standing straight. One day, when it did, Fan Feng asked in alarm what was wrong. The gatekeeper replied, “Lately, I have seen the Censor repeatedly summon guests to instruct the kitchen—four or five times already. I thought to myself: if he ever becomes chief minister, will he not be just as fussy, meddling in every trifle? My heart truly disdains such pettiness, and so, unthinkingly, my staff stood straight.” Fan Feng, deeply ashamed, apologized again and again.

Alas! Even a single misstep in conduct may cost one the respect of servants and subordinates—how much more so the reverence of all people? Know then that those whom all revere must truly be worthy of reverence. Have you not heard of the cases of Lord Wen and Grand Remonstrator Liu?

When Lord Wen journeyed to the capital, the imperial guards, upon seeing him, could not help but raise their hands to their brows in salute. Even the common folk blocked his path, pleading, “Do not return home yet—stay and assist the Son of Heaven, that the people may live.” When Wang Bizhi passed through Qingzhou, he encountered hundreds of villagers who cheered and leapt for joy, crying out, “If Sima becomes chief minister, we shall all rejoice!” When Lord Wen passed away, the people of the capital closed their markets to mourn, selling their clothes to offer libations. Those who gathered for his funeral numbered tens of thousands. Even the elders of Fengzhou in Lingnan came in procession, over nine hundred of them burning incense upon their heads. Could such things occur if all did not revere him?

When Grand Remonstrator Liu served in the Song court, people said: “To pass through Nanjing without seeing Attendant Liu is like visiting Sizhou without paying homage to the Great Sage.” When he died, the elderly, scholars, commoners—even women, children, farmers, and marketplace vendors—all vied to bring incense and recite Buddhist scriptures as they wept. The crowd at his gate was so dense that none could enter; thus, a great brazier was set in the hall to receive their offerings, and the price of incense soared. Could such things occur if all did not revere him?

Another example: When Lord Yi served in the Grand Secretariat, Yang Wengong, known for his jesting nature, would tease and mock all his colleagues without exception—save for Lord Yi, of whom he said, “I dare not jest with the Fourth Hall Academician.” Chen Xiang, Chen Lie, Zhou Ximeng, and Zheng Mu, all devoted to understanding Heaven and exhausting human nature, took the weight of the world as their own duty. Scholars revered them as the Four Masters. Even the most unruly and insolent dared not show disrespect at their gates.

Lord Fu Wenzhong once rode a small boat past Tianjin Bridge.

The crowd following to observe him emptied the marketplace. Xu Xiaojie built a hut beside his father’s grave, and the city’s people came daily to pay their respects, likewise emptying the marketplace. Shen Yan’s independent conduct and singular integrity caused merchants to rise when he passed through the market. Whenever Shao Kangjie went out, the city’s scholars and officials would all turn their shoes backward to welcome him. Could such things occur if all did not revere them? Yet are they truly worthy of reverence, or is it simply that reverence arises wherever it finds a place?

In praise it is said:
The heart of reverence arises
before any act is done.
As with Lin Zong’s headscarf
or the purple fungus on Zi’s brows—
even in the case of Junshi,
known even to the lowest servant.
Not sought from others,
others come to it of themselves.
Were it not so,
why would the Uyghurs bow to Zi Yi?
The Dao of Heaven protects them.

The *Tradition* says: The saying that “the Dao of Heaven protects them” is precisely what the *Book of Changes* means by “aided by Heaven, nothing is not auspicious.” Generally, the Dao of Heaven shows no partiality but constantly favors the good. Without speech, it responds with goodness; without summons, it comes of itself. If we truly possess it, we align with it; if we do not, we are not protected by it.

In former times, Liu Qizhi, having passed the imperial examination, did not immediately assume office but returned to his studies. He often took Lacquered Carving Kai’s inability to trust fully as a warning, which greatly impressed Master Wen. When first appointed as a remonstrance official, he submitted twenty-four memorials in defiance, discerning the uprightness and depravity of court ministers. He also presented nineteen memorials opposing the employment of the petty man Zhang Zihou, causing others to shrink back in fear—yet he remained undeterred. When Zihou gained power, the minister fled into exile amid slanderous rumors, his path fraught with peril like plum blossoms in early spring or the perilous edge of death. The thunderous clamor of gossip made even mention of his name a cause for dread. Of the eight provinces he traversed, he governed seven, and though the summer heat made the roads treacherous, he ensured his subordinates maintained strict oversight. Each day, he advanced one stage, sometimes braving the dangers of sea voyages. All believed he would surely perish, yet he remained unharmed, living to eighty without ever falling ill.

During his exile, a grain merchant, discerning Zihou’s intent, volunteered to assassinate him. Zihou promptly promoted the man from a minor post to transport commissioner of the region. The assassin rushed to within twenty *li* of the minister’s place of exile, intending to carry out his deed the following day. His attendants trembled with fear. Yet at midnight, a sudden clamor arose—it seemed the commissioner had been struck by some unseen force, vomiting blood and dying instantly. Had he not perished, the minister would have met his end. If not for the Dao of Heaven protecting him, could such a thing have come to pass?

Geng Ziyu was likewise a man of virtue. When his father, serving as prefect of Baxi, was transferred to Ning Shu and died there, Ziyu escorted the coffin home. Autumn floods swelled the rivers, with only the tips of submerged rocks breaking the surface. The currents of Qutang Gorge were especially fierce, and as rain threatened, the boatmen grew terrified. Ziyu lifted his gaze to the heavens and wept aloud. Before his lament ended, the waters receded by over twenty *zhang*. Once they had passed, the river returned to its former state. Had the waters not subsided, who can say what might have befallen them? If not for the Dao of Heaven protecting him, could such a thing have come to pass?

Know this: the Dao of Heaven’s protection lies within oneself. How much more so for those of loyalty and filial piety, the crown of all virtues? Such goodness stands foremost.

In praise it is said:
Heaven is impartial principle,
acting as it must.
Without preference or aversion,
who wields its power?
It aids the good, upholds virtue,
supports the upright, exalts the worthy.
Following the auspicious path,
it responds in kind.
Who protects me?
Heaven within myself.
Fortune and emolument follow.

The *Tradition* says: As the *Book of Songs* declares,
*"Joyful is the noble man—
fortune and emolument extend him;
joyful is the noble man—
fortune and emolument enrich him."*
This is the meaning of "follow" in the preceding passage. Generally, good and noble men, through their auspicious kindness, naturally attract fortune and emolument. Thus, wherever they go, these come unbidden, as if in pursuit.

Formerly, Minister Fu Zhenggong, for his forthright remonstrance, incurred the displeasure of the chief minister, who recommended him for a mission to the unpredictable barbarians. At that time, Minister Ouyang Wenzhong submitted a memorial citing the case of Lu Qi recommending Yan Zhenqing to Li Xilie, warning that the chief minister sought to harm Zhenggong. Though the memorial went unanswered, by the time Zhenggong reached the barbarians, their ruler and ministers were reciting his words, seeking peace with the Middle Kingdom and eschewing war for nearly a hundred years. Upon his return, he was appointed Academician of the Bureau of Military Affairs, then Hanlin Academician, then Vice Director of the Bureau—each post he firmly declined. A month later, he was named Director of the Bureau, and again he refused. The emperor insisted on waiting for him before proceeding with court. Was this not fortune and emolument following him as a matter of course?

Such great fortune and emolument are not the sort that ordinary achievers dare aspire to. Yet speaking of each in their own circumstances, any attainment is fortune and emolument.

The physician Zhen Baili was originally a coarse and common man, yet he applied his mind with diligence. Regardless of the illness—great or small—he rushed to attend to it. One day, when the emperor fell ill, someone recommended his skill. After a single dose, the emperor recovered. Thereafter, he was favored above all other physicians, entering and leaving the palace for half a year. The gold he amassed was piled like ripe grain, totaling over twenty thousand pieces. The wine he stored in fine vessels exceeded three thousand *hu*. Though this may not be deemed fortune and emolument in the grandest sense, for Zhen himself, compared to his former days, was it not fortune and emolument?

Know this: fortune and emolument following one also lie within oneself.

In praise it is said:
Yan Yuan dwelt in a humble lane,
Boyi and Shuqi starved on Shouyang.
Poverty befell Yuan Xian,
punishment claimed Fan Pang.
The response of fortune and emolument
seems almost perverse.
Yet know these men
rival the sun and moon in splendor.
This fortune, this emolument,
shines bright for a thousand ages.
The multitude of evils shuns them.

The *Tradition* says: The multitude of evils refers to all that is unrighteous, for their kinds are manifold. Generally, the paths of right and wrong diverge and cannot coexist. Where right prevails, wrong cannot abide. It is like the sun shining upon an iceberg—the sun remains unchanged, while the ice must melt. The "shunning" is seen in ancient examples: spells could not touch Tan Xian, and the moon goddess dared not appear before Liang Gong.

Formerly, the Daoist Lu Xiujing and his followers were skilled in incantations. They could cause monks’ robes and palace beams to tremble and rise, boasting of their prowess as if they were immortals. When the Dharma Master Tan Xian heard of this, he rebuked them: "You have not yet met a true adversary—how dare you indulge in such petty tricks?" He placed the robe of Master Zhou the Meditator on the ground and bade them cast their spells. The Daoists exerted all their power, but the robe would not move. Xian then took it himself and placed it upon a beam, commanding them to enchant the beam—yet it too remained unmoved. Lu Xiujing and his followers shrank back, their faces drained of color.

As for Wu Sansi, he once acquired a concubine named Su E, a woman of unparalleled beauty.

At every banquet with court officials, he would bring her out to boast of her beauty. One day, he invited Di Renjie. As Renjie was about to arrive, Su E hid herself away and refused to emerge. Wu entered and called for her, but she retreated into the innermost recesses of the hall, her voice as faint as a silken thread: "I am but a spirit of flowers and moonlight. The Duke is a man of true integrity—how dare I appear before him?" From that day forth, she never emerged again.

Those like Master Fang and his peers are such men. As for Fang Qianzhi, he dwelt in purity and stillness, delighting in the savor of the Dao. In the region where he resided, mountain miasma and malaria were rampant. When Qianzhi inscribed his name for the afflicted, none failed to recover at once. Some begged him for his method, and Qianzhi replied: "I have heard that a rectified heart can dispel evil. I live in poverty and have no wealth to offer, so I daily recite the *Dharma-Wheel Scripture of the Dao and Its Virtue*, rejoice in all good deeds, and cultivate compassion to quell wickedness. I believe this suffices to heal myself and others—nothing more."

Such are those whose names alone keep evil at bay. As for Zhang Guaiya, he remained steadfast from beginning to end, unyielding in any circumstance. He was close friends with the reclusive scholar Fu Lin. One day, they met in the outskirts, drinking deep into the night as their conversation flowed. At the time, a great epidemic raged in that region, and many in the neighboring households fell ill.

That very night, the afflicted awoke as if cleansed, all instantly cured, and many beheld ghostly apparitions departing. Such was the power that fled at the mere presence of these two gentlemen—this is what it means to make evil retreat at the sound of one’s name.

As for Lord Peng Da, he was a man of serene detachment, few desires, and scrupulous integrity. Once, when he served as prefect of Huzhou, the official residence was long notorious for its hauntings; none of his predecessors had ever dwelled there in peace. Yet from the moment he took office until his departure, he never once inquired whether the spirits were present or not. The spirits, for their part, dared not display their tricks, and all remained perfectly calm throughout—never causing the slightest disturbance. This is what it means to make evil retreat at the mere sight of one’s person. Was it not because his inner power subdued them?

In contrast, when Zhang Xuangan took up his post as Transport Commissioner of Huainan, matters unfolded quite differently. Soon after his arrival, he was daily harassed by malevolent spirits. There happened to be a military officer skilled in exorcism, so Zhang summoned him to deal with the problem. Upon arrival, the officer rebuked the spirits: “The Commissioner is a high-ranking official entrusted with grave imperial duties. What manner of ghosts are you, to dare disturb him?” The spirits burst into laughter and replied: “Do you take us for one of your bureaucratic underlings, scraping by with hollow flattery just to secure a promotion?” From that point on, they grew even more brazen—smearing filth everywhere, darting about wildly, utterly uncontrollable. Was this not because neither man possessed the power to subdue them?

In praise it is said: In an age where the Dao prevails, even ghosts lose their efficacy.

When clarity and brightness reside within, nothing can conceal its true nature. With thoughts free from deceit, what remains is sincerity. Once the great character is firmly established, even thunder cannot startle. When this qi is never depleted, what being would dare to oppose it? The spirits and divinities protect such a one.

The tradition states: "Spirits and divinities are those who are perceptive, enlightened, upright, and just—they exist everywhere. What they revere is the Dao and virtue; what they accompany is loyalty and filial piety; what they align with is uprightness; what they favor is hidden virtue. How then can a person lack these four qualities? If one truly possesses them, the spirits and divinities will invariably offer protection."

Formerly, when Han Wei Gong was transferred from Chengde to assume command in Zhongshan, he was approaching the Sha River. His vanguard returned to report: "The waters have risen violently; we cannot proceed." The Duke said: "We have already come this far—what use is it to avoid it now?" He ordered boats to be prepared at once. No sooner had he crossed than the baggage train was still passing when waves surged like mountains, and some of the rear cavalry had yet to ford. At that moment, the people of Xujizhi all witnessed that as the Duke was crossing, a divine dragon coiled upstream, causing the waters to stand still and shallow.

Mei Gongyi once governed Huazhou. One night, word came that the Yellow River had breached its banks. He immediately led his officials and personally directed the soldiers and laborers to rush to the river, reinforcing the dikes wherever needed. Just as they were in dire straits, they clearly saw an old man with white whiskers, ferrying a boatload of straw, coming and going to assist them. Thinking him a local magnate, the Duke’s heart rejoiced. But when the waters subsided and dawn broke, he sent an officer to summon the man—only to find that the white-whiskered elder had vanished. These were all instances of spirits and divinities offering protection.

In praise it is said:

The Way of the divine is wisdom, clarity, and uprightness.
Vast and boundless above, it observes the minute and the hidden.
It rebukes and restrains misfortune,
to serve those of virtue and power.
Not that the divine seeks anything—
it simply fulfills its duty.
The wicked, by contrast, are punished by the divine.

*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Five (conclusion)
*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Six

**Biography of Li Changqi**
**Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

Whatever he undertook was sure to succeed.

It is said: In the world, there is no task that cannot be accomplished, no person who cannot achieve great things. If one possesses the will, nothing is beyond attainment. In former times, the grandfather of Zhang Zhongbin was originally a man who schemed for profit, but he abruptly changed his ways and devoted himself to teaching his sons. His sons, Zhongrong and Zhongbin, both passed the imperial examinations. The following year, Zhong’an also passed. Yu Lingyi, originally a commoner of the marketplace, likewise transformed his ways and devoted himself to teaching his sons. His second son, Jie, and his nephew, Xiao, both passed the examinations as well. Thus, for those of determination who aspire to great deeds, is there anything that cannot be achieved? How much more so for those who strive to practice manifold good works?

The story of Duke Dou makes this clear. Dou Yujun of Yanshan had a dream in which his grandfather said to him: “You will have no sons and will not live long. Why not cultivate hidden virtue early to reverse your fate?” Terrified, Yujun thereafter assisted his relatives, both inner and outer, in holding funerals for the unburied and marrying off unwed daughters. He burned debt records to free a servant’s daughter, returned gold to one who had lost it, and undertook every possible act of virtue. He also built forty rooms in his residence, amassed ten thousand volumes of books, and invited scholars of letters and conduct to reside there as teachers. Aspiring students came of their own accord, and from this, talented men gathered from all directions. Those who rose to prominence through his gate followed one after another.

In his own lifetime, Duke Dou rose to the position of Left Remonstrating Grand Master and died at the age of eighty without illness. Now a Perfected Immortal of the Grotto-Heavens, he later had five sons, all of whom passed the imperial examinations: Yi, Minister of Rites; Yan, Vice Minister of Rites; and Kan, Left Rectifier of Omissions.

Cheng served in the Grand Council; Xi became Gentleman for Court Ceremonies. Alas! He desired longevity and attained it; he desired sons and had five; he desired rank and received it; he cultivated virtue and ascended as a Perfected Immortal. Then can anything one undertakes in this world fail to come to fruition?

In praise it is said:
Seeking benevolence, one attains benevolence—such were Shuqi and Boyi.
Diligently pursuing profit, one meets the fate of Robber Zhi.
Heaven rewards the good, while evil ripens in due course.
Greed, deceit, and cunning—each act bears its consequence.
Success or failure, good or ill—its end is ever foreseeable.
Yet the Way of Immortals may still be hoped for.

As the tradition records: Zhang Yang perused the *Daoist Canon* and saw a wall-fish with a five-colored body, resplendent and dazzling. When he opened the scripture, he found that the characters for "immortal" had been entirely eaten away by bookworms. Thus he knew that the wall-fish, having consumed the characters for "immortal," had gained its five-colored form. He Ruo once acquired a scroll of the *Daoist Scripture of Rites* and found within it an object resembling a coiled lock of hair, about four inches in circumference, endless in its loop. When he cut it, water flowed from both ends, filling more than a liter. He asked many, but none could identify it. One day, he encountered a Daoist adept and inquired. The adept said, "That you met this thing yet failed to ascend—such is your fate. This is a *maiwang*, born of a wall-fish that has thrice consumed the characters for 'immortal.' When held to the light of the stars, it summons celestial messengers, and one may obtain the Elixir of Return. Drinking its water transforms the bones." He Ruo returned home, read the scroll in full, and found that every instance of the characters for "immortal" had indeed been eaten away. Thus he knew that a wall-fish, having thrice consumed the characters for "immortal," could transform into a *maiwang*.

Alas! Even an insect may achieve this—how much more so a human who aspires to immortality! There is nothing unattainable in such hope. Generally, humans and immortals share the same true nature; it is only that emotions prevail, and one loses this truth. Once one returns to truth and sheds worldly attachments entirely, one becomes an immortal. Moreover, if one aids this with goodness, seeking examples from antiquity—such as Zhang Liang’s loyalty, Wu Meng’s filial piety, Wang Jinxian’s unwavering chastity, Lan Qi’s fraternal devotion, Liu Yi’s selfless generosity, Zhao Sutai’s relief of the poor and burial of the dead, Master Xu’s healing with talismans and water, Yan Pingjun’s guidance through virtue, Zhou Bochi’s burial of abandoned remains, Li Wulang’s honesty in measurement, Chen Anshi’s refusal to take life, Li Xizi’s rescue of starving birds, Yang Jingzhi’s tranquil meditation, Tang Ruoshan’s freedom from anger, Huang Wanyou’s few faults, Jing Xiang’s passion for liberating living creatures—even Liu Pinga, originally a physician; Wu Mu, a county clerk; Liu Yan, a courtesan; Bao Jing, an innkeeper; He Sheng, a butcher; Ding Yue, a soldier; Zhu, a bandit; Li Zhengxuan, a hunter—all ascended from mortal to immortal.

In our own time, Chao Gongxiong became the Heavenly Lord of Tranquil Abode; Master Zhang Wenqi, the Perfected Lord of Life and Destiny; Wang Gongsu, Attendant Gentleman of the Jade Capital; Lü Gonghui, Censor of the Celestial Court; Han Gongqi, Master of the Purple Palace; Fu Gongbi, Overseer of Kun Terrace; Wang Gongsou, Keeper of the Iron Wheel of the Sagely Protector; Jin Sansan, Wind Earl of the Sagely Assistant; Zhang Xiaoji, Master of Mount Song; Dou Yujun, Perfected Immortal of the Grotto-Heavens; Ouyang Xiu, Master of Divine Clarity; Wang Anguo, Master of Lingzhi; Lü Qin, Master of the Jade Assembly; Shi Yannian, Master of Hibiscus; Chen Jing, Judge of the Bureau of Merit; Tian Chengjun, Master of Weiyang—these, too, ascended from mortal to immortal.

The scriptures state: In the Left Grotto-Yang Palace of the Second Grade of the Middle Primordial, which governs the Nine Imperial Mounds of Earth, the Four Corners, and the Eight Extremities, the divine officials and their subordinates number ninety-nine million, nine hundred ninety-nine thousand. All were persons of merit in the world who, upon receiving the Daoist precepts, were promoted to their posts. For example, He Xizhi of our prefecture, who annotated the *Diamond Sutra* to benefit the world, was posthumously appointed Inspector of the Western Marchmount and held various offices. Though this was a position in the Marchmount’s administration, it was still a promotion through the Daoist precepts. Who then can say that immortality is unattainable?

Yet some aspire to it and ultimately fail—not because immortality is unattainable, but because their cultivation was insufficient. Have you not heard the words of the Two Perfected? Zhongli Quan said, "Immortals seek humans more earnestly than humans seek immortals." Lü Dongbin said, "People lament not seeing me, yet even if they saw me daily and did not follow my words, what benefit would it bring?" Both lamented that humans cannot attain immortality.

In praise it is said:
The Way of Immortals is often called remote and vague.
Seeking yet not finding—Emperor Wu of Han was shamed.
Like the Buddhist teachings of hell and heaven:
If heaven truly exists, it must be the abode of the loyal and good.
If hell truly exists, it must be the domain of the petty and base.
Those who seek celestial immortality must perform one thousand three hundred good deeds;
Those who seek earthly immortality must perform three hundred.

As the tradition states: The distinction between celestial and earthly immortals lies in the difference in the number of good deeds performed. This has already been explained in the text and need not be discussed further. Let us speak instead of advancing beyond the hundred-foot pole—those who have already attained immortality will not fall but ascend further; those who have not yet attained need not seek elsewhere, for they may reach it this very day.

Śākyamuni said: There are ten kinds of immortals:
1. Earth-Walking Immortals,
2. Flying Immortals,
3. Wandering Immortals,
4. Void-Traveling Immortals,
5. Heaven-Walking Immortals,
6. All-Pervading Immortals,
7. Dao-Practicing Immortals,
8. Radiant Immortals,
9. Essence-Preserving Immortals,
10. Transcendent Immortals.

It is further stated: All these follow not the true awakening but pursue deluded thoughts, fixating on preserving form and wandering in mountain forests where men do not tread. Though they live for thousands or ten thousand years, they remain bound to the cycle of rebirth—deluded, inverted, and confused. When their retribution is exhausted, they return, scattered into various destinies.

Those who steadfastly ingest elixirs without ceasing, perfecting the Way of ingestion, are called Earth-Walking Immortals.
Those who steadfastly consume herbs and plants without ceasing, perfecting the Way of medicine, are called Flying Immortals.
Those who steadfastly refine metals and minerals without ceasing, perfecting the Way of transformation, are called Wandering Immortals.
Those who steadfastly regulate movement and stillness without ceasing, perfecting the purity of qi, are called Void-Traveling Immortals.
Those who steadfastly preserve seminal essence without ceasing, perfecting the nourishment of virtue, are called Heaven-Walking Immortals.
Those who steadfastly refine essence and form without ceasing, perfecting the absorption of purity, are called All-Pervading Immortals.
Those who steadfastly uphold incantations and prohibitions without ceasing, perfecting the arts of method, are called Dao-Practicing Immortals.
Those who steadfastly cultivate contemplation without ceasing, perfecting the clarity of thought, are called Radiant Immortals.
Those who steadfastly engage in union without ceasing, perfecting the resonance of awakening, are called Essence-Preserving Immortals.
Those who steadfastly master transformation without ceasing, perfecting the nature of awakening, are called Transcendent Immortals.

Zhongli Quan said: There are five kinds of immortals:
1. Ghost Immortals,
2. Human Immortals,
3. Earthly Immortals,
4. Divine Immortals,
5. Celestial Immortals.

In response to a question on the Great Dao, he further stated: The Dao originally has no questions; questions originally have no answers. Among all things, the most intelligent and noble is man. If one can exhaust the principles of all things and fulfill one’s own nature, exhausting principle and fulfilling nature to the utmost of destiny—preserving life in its entirety to harmonize with the Dao—then one naturally becomes as steadfast as heaven and earth and shares in their longevity.

Those who do not awaken to the Great Dao but seek only swift attainment, making their minds like dead ashes and their forms like withered wood, inwardly guarding their spirit and unwavering in their resolve, may project their yin spirit in deep concentration. Yet in the end, they are but ethereal ghosts, not pure yang immortals, and are called Ghost Immortals.

Those who briefly encounter the Great Dao among men, yet bear heavy karma and meager fortune, may retreat due to demonic obstacles or cling to one-sided practices without change—abstaining from the five flavors without knowing the six qi, forgetting the seven emotions without knowing the ten precepts, practicing swallowing and rinsing while mocking breath cultivation as misguided, dismissing stillness as foolish, delighting in seizing qi from heaven and earth without ceasing their grain intake, or fixating on visualization to absorb the essence of the sun and moon without practicing daoyin, sitting idly in solitude without recognizing natural movement, or laboring the body without understanding non-action—can only prolong their years in ease and comfort, and are called Human Immortals.

Those who emulate the ascending and descending principles of heaven and earth, grasp the generative and consummative numbers of the sun and moon, employ years and months within the body and hours within the day, recognize the dragon and tiger, pair Kan and Li, discern the clarity and turbidity of the water source, distinguish the earliness and lateness of qi, gather the True One, observe the Two Modes, differentiate the Three Powers, divide the Four Images, arrange the Five Phases, stabilize the Six Qi, assemble the Seven Treasures, sequence the Eight Trigrams, practice the Five Phases, invert the Nine Provinces, transmit qi from mother to child, circulate fluids between husband and wife, reverse the Three Fields, refine the elixir to joy, permanently stabilize the lower field, refine the form to abide in the world, and attain longevity without death, are called Earthly Immortals.

Those who, having attained Earthly Immortality, continue their practice without cease, transcend the ordinary and enter the sagely, and return to the Three Mountains, are called Divine Immortals.

Those who, having attained Divine Immortality, transmit the Dao to the world, contribute to the Dao and benefit others, fulfill their merit and conduct, and ascend to the Grotto-Heavens to assume office as immortal officials—if they further render great service to heaven and earth and achieve great deeds across past and present—progress through the ranks, ascending through the thirty-six Grotto-Heavens, returning to the eighty-one Yang Heavens, and from there transcending into the realm of emptiness and spontaneity, are called Celestial Immortals.

Thus, what Śākyamuni called true awakening and Zhongli Quan called the Great Dao—what do they truly signify? True awakening is the Great Dao; the Great Dao is true awakening. In general, if one wishes to recognize one’s own mind without falling into conceptual understanding, have you not heard the teaching of *seng-nā*? *Seng-nā* said: Illuminate your own mind by constantly examining and reflecting in the midst of daily activities—walking, standing, sitting, and lying down. When encountering forms or sounds, before thoughts arise, where does the mind go? Is it existent? Is it nonexistent? If it does not fall into existence or nonexistence, the mind’s pearl shines alone, perpetually illuminating the world without the slightest obstruction, interruption, or discontinuity—this is true awakening, this is the Great Dao.

Thus, Master Lü, though unlearned in alchemy, attained deliverance into the world. Later, upon hearing the teaching “Do not abide anywhere, yet give rise to the mind,” his heart-mind became thoroughly enlightened. Having attained this enlightenment, he once offered meals to monks at Xinghua in Tan. Master Sun, though devoted to medical texts, attained deliverance into the world. Later, he sought instruction from the Vinaya Master Xuan of the Tang. Having received this instruction, he once listened to the *Treasure Pagoda Sutra* from an unnamed wandering monk in Chengdu. This is what it means to advance beyond the hundred-foot pole.

As for performing good deeds, it is naturally so; even without seeking immortality, it is naturally so.

In praise it is said:
Three hundred rituals, three thousand deportments—
Only through interaction with others is the human way fulfilled.
Accumulating merit and piling up good deeds,
There must be sequence and priority.
With strict and detailed regulations,
Not a hair’s breadth is amiss.

Thus is the immortal’s Dao: to harmonize humanity with Heaven. If one acts contrary to righteousness or moves against principle, the saying goes—Mencius said: "Principle and righteousness are what all human hearts naturally affirm." What Mencius called righteousness is principle; in essence, principle and righteousness reside within humanity and must not be violated. Should one transgress them, one cannot avoid being swept away into turbid obscurity, sinking into mediocrity and base dealings, descending to every sort of degradation. How then could one dwell in purity and stillness, delighting in the savor of the Dao?

Let me speak of one or two exemplary gentlemen, that others may know shame. Of old, Guo Yanqing was renowned for his literary refinement and conduct. Disdaining the shallowness of the age, he built a garden on the south bank of the river and dwelt there for over twenty years, never once setting foot in the city. In his youth, he kept company with Zhang Wending and Lü Wenmu. When Zhang and Lü successively became chief ministers, they recommended him to the court, but though he was offered an official post, he refused to accept it. At the time, Qian Weiyan, the cultured and refined, served as military governor; Xie Jiang was judicial administrator; Yin Zhu was secretary; and Ouyang Xiu was assistant magistrate—all men of literary and moral distinction who admired his character. One day, they dismissed their retinues and went together to visit Yanqing. After a long conversation, Yanqing served them wine in a pottery vessel with fruit and nuts. Weiyan, charmed by his rustic simplicity, drank deeply without restraint. As evening fell, an officer arrived to report that the hour of *shen* had passed, and the governor’s clerks and armed attendants were arrayed in the courtyard. Yanqing calmly asked, "What offices do you gentlemen hold, that you travel with such a large retinue?" Zhu pointed to Weiyan and said, "This is the governor, the chief minister." Yanqing replied, "I never imagined that today you would deign to visit a rustic like me." They all laughed heartily and drank several more cups. When the sun set, the officials took their leave. Yanqing saw them out, saying, "I am old and infirm and cannot return your visit—pray do not be surprised." Weiyan mounted his carriage in a daze, as though he had entered the realm of immortals. Later, he sighed and said, "How lightly this man regards wealth and honor!" Such was a scholar-official who dwelt in purity and stillness, delighting in the savor of the Dao.

As for Du Wulang, he was originally a farmer, yet he too held firmly to his principles. His home lay thirty *li* from the county seat, consisting of only two small rooms—one for himself, the other for his son. There was nothing but an empty bed, with no other possessions.

At that time, Sun Zhen, the magistrate of Fanyang, admired his character and, seizing a moment of leisure, paid him a visit. After a long while, Du Wulang said, "I am but a rustic with no ability—why have you come to see me?" Sun asked why he never went out. He laughed and replied, "I am of no use to the times and seek nothing from others—it is merely that I do not go out by chance."

Sun then asked how he made a living. Du said, "Formerly, I lived south of the county, where I had half a *qing* of land, which I farmed together with my brother’s son. After my brother’s son married, I judged that the land would not suffice to support us both, so I gave it all to him. I then brought my wife and children here, where a villager had two vacant rooms, and I borrowed them to live in. At first, I sold medicines and selected auspicious days to support myself. When my son grew up, a villager gave me thirty *mu* of land to tenant. With surplus strength from farming, I also hired myself out to others. From then on, I had just enough, and as I did not wish to contend with others for profit, I abandoned all practices like medicine and divination."

Sun asked, "What do you do all day?" Du replied, "I simply sit upright." Sun asked, "Have you ever read scriptures?" Du said, "Twenty years ago, I once read a copy of the *Vimalakīrti Sutra*. At first, I found its discussions apt and quite admired them, but now I have forgotten it all, and the sutra itself is lost as well."

As they spoke, the hours passed, and Du’s bearing was free and unconstrained, his words precise and spare. Sun was deeply moved and sighed in admiration, "Truly, he is a man of the Dao."

Since then, I have often sought him out—a man who dwells in quietude among the fields and wilds, delighting in the savor of the Dao. Are not those who turn their backs on righteousness and reason, stopping at nothing, put to shame by such a one?

In praise I say:
Principle is the essence of the Dharma,
Righteousness its guiding rule.
Judgment follows the truth of the matter,
Not a hair’s breadth awry.
Weighing each grain with caution,
Still one may err by a stone’s weight—
How much more those who overstep the plumb line and square,
Indulging in perversity and bias?
If such a one goes unpunished,
What becomes of Heaven’s justice?
To take evil as ability,
To steel oneself to cruelty and harm—
As the saying goes: “The Dao of Heaven ever befriends the good,”
Yet you make evil your ability.
“The great virtue of Heaven and Earth is life,”
Yet you steel yourself to cruelty and harm.
To defy Heaven and turn your back on Earth—
If not you, then who?
The so-called “able” do not see their own wrong;
The so-called “hardened” lack all pity.
Among all evils, none is worse than this;
Among all cruelties, none more grievous.
The hells were established precisely for such people.
When the time comes, will the minions of Ox-Head and Horse-Face spare you their merciless hands?
I urge you, by the teaching of compassion,
To swiftly save them,
Lest they reach the point where deeds are done and cannot be undone.

Long ago, a monk from the Western Regions named Juecheng came to China. Yang Yi, Duke Wengong, upon meeting him, treated him with great courtesy. Later, when the monk sought to depart, he could not be detained.

The translator was then instructed to ask the monk, who replied: “In my homeland, no one eats meat. Now that I have entered China, everywhere I see the slaughter of pigs and sheep, their flesh hung in the markets—I cannot bear the sight. I do not wish to stay here long; I will make a brief pilgrimage to Mount Wutai to pay homage to Mañjuśrī, then return home.”

There is a line in a poem by the mountain recluse: *“Its fangs are already clenched like a child’s fist.”* When Zhang Ge first read this, he frowned in displeasure, calling the poet a cruel man. Yet this was merely the poet’s description of a thing—hardly an act of cruelty. Zhang, then serving as judicial officer in Henei, had followed the example of his superior, Ge Fan, who ate only vegetables and chanted scriptures; Zhang, too, had given up meat, hence his reaction.

If all people could awaken as Master Jue did, or as Zhang did, and always regard all creatures with compassion, I am certain they would never dare to take evil for ability or steel themselves to cruelty and harm. For this reason, I first set forth these examples before briefly recounting the consequences of such acts.

Long ago, Lu Xiaozheng sought to gather a swarm of bees but failed. Enraged, he poured boiling water over them, leaving none alive. Soon after, in the same place, he was suddenly stung to death by a giant bee.

Zhou Ang once napped during the day. On the door above him was a swallow’s nest, where three fledglings chirped, waiting to be fed. Ang, in a fury, stuffed thorns into their mouths, and all three died with their chests split open. Later, his three sons were all born mute.

At Jinhua Temple stood several cypress trees, which suddenly became infested with yellow caterpillars nearly two inches long. They devoured the branches and leaves without end. The monk Xiu Rong, repulsed, ordered them all swept away, buried, or discarded in a pile of firewood. His disciple, Xiu Ren, then spread the firewood in the sun to kill the remaining caterpillars before using it for fuel.

Years later, when Xiu Rong died, another monk in the temple also passed away but later revived. He told Xiu Ren: “I saw Xiu Rong in the underworld, bearing an iron yoke beneath the scorching sun. Countless caterpillars swarmed over him, biting and gnawing—his suffering was unbearable.” Upon hearing this, Xiu Ren was seized with terror. Within days, he developed a carbuncle on his back and died.

The monk Xiu Zhun of Daci Temple had newly built a pavilion surrounded by bamboo. Ants crawled all over the railings. Enraged, Xiu Zhun immediately had the bamboo cut down, gathering all the ants and casting them into the fire. Soon after, he developed a scabies-like sore, worst on his face and head. A physician examined him and said: “This is called ‘ant-leakage sore’—it cannot be cured.” Not long after, he died.

Then there was Wang Yu, who, angered by the cawing of magpies, cut out their tongues. Later, he died of a tongue ulcer. Zhang Lin, enraged by the croaking of frogs, poured hot ashes over them. Soon after, his body suddenly rotted as if scalded.

If this is how men treat creatures—taking evil for ability, steeling themselves to cruelty and harm—can they escape retribution for their deeds?

**Eulogy:**
Zhi Bo’s wisdom, Pan Cheng’s talent,
Ming’s promotion of Dan Zhu, Jie’s praise of E Lai—
All were endowed by Heaven with rare gifts,
Like trees bearing fruit in the wild.
Had they used them for good,
Who could have stopped them?
To wield such gifts for evil—
Is this not lamentable?
To secretly harm the good and virtuous—

As the saying goes: *“Chen Ping was full of secret schemes; he knew he would have no descendants.”* Gongsun Hong favored open dealings, and his later generations faded into obscurity. Generally, those who rely on secret schemes cannot avoid open dealings, while those who favor open dealings may still harbor secret schemes. It is like shooting pellets at a target—most effective when done in darkness. Such methods cannot be used against others, and least of all against the good and virtuous.

For the good and virtuous are the people’s hope.
In a kingdom, they are its foundation;
In a village, they are its pillar.

How could one secretly harm them?

Long ago, before Li Linfu rose to prominence, he encountered a Daoist at the Locust Altar who warned him: *"Your name is already recorded in the registers of immortals. Even if you do not ascend to heaven in broad daylight, you are destined to become a grand councilor for twenty years of peace. When power is in your hands, remember my words—do not recklessly engage in secret harm."* Once Linfu attained high office, he no longer recalled the warning. Instead, he clung to favor and wrought much destruction. After some time, he dreamed of the Daoist reproaching him: *"Have you forgotten my words? Now you have truly incurred guilt."* Thereupon, an underling led him to a place where Linfu heard only the rushing sound of wind and water in his ears. Upon arrival, he saw an imposing government office, its curtains and bedding lavishly adorned. Linfu rejoiced, saying: *"Even if I were to dwell here, it would not be so bad."* The Daoist laughed: *"This is the abode of scaled and shelled creatures. The suffering here is most cruel and bitter. If you still find it not bad, what then would you call evil?"* Linfu was struck with terror, his sweat soaking through as he awoke. This is the fate of one who secretly harms the good and virtuous—reborn among aquatic creatures.

Xu Wengui was once summoned to the netherworld. For having saved a life, he was granted an extension of twelve years before being sent back. Upon his return, he saw a gate heavily guarded. When Xu asked the attendant about it, the official replied: *"This is the capital prison. All those in the world who slander the loyal and good, ruin the virtuous, and habitually engage in secret harm—regardless of rank—are sentenced here after death."* This is the fate of one who secretly harms the good and virtuous—condemned to the underworld’s hells.

Who would claim such deeds bear no guilt?

To be clear, open, and easily understood is to incline toward the yang. To be treacherous, ruthless, and deeply concealed is to incline toward the yin. One who dwells in the yin in life will return to the yin in death—this is the inevitable principle, and nothing strange. What are aquatic creatures, what are hells, if not realms of the yin?

In praise it is said:
*To violate the law or manipulate it—*
*Both are equally transgressions.*

Brawling and premeditated murder alike are acts of killing. Yet when it comes to sentencing, their severity differs. Those who scheme with cunning and plot in secrecy—invoking even spirits and gods—may strike with deadly precision, but the harm they intend shall rebound upon themselves.

To secretly insult one’s lord or parent:
As the saying goes, for a minister, it is to indulge in deceitful flattery, spreading falsehoods—this is secret insult. For a child, it is to perform empty rituals without true devotion—this too is secret insult. In short, any minister or child who fails to fulfill their duty with utmost sincerity toward lord or parent commits secret insult. If all ministers secretly insult their lords, and all children secretly insult their parents, what then can lords and parents expect from their ministers and children? No greater harm to teaching and betrayal of the Way exists—this is why the Most High speaks of it.

Formerly, Cao Bin, the Martial and Benevolent, even in moments of private leisure, always wore his official robes and sat with solemn dignity, as if in the presence of his lord and father. Upon returning to his private residence, he would shut his doors and remain in quiet seclusion, never recklessly receiving guests. At the first stir of the fifth watch, he would hasten to the palace gates to await the court bell. For eight years, he maintained this discipline, never once relaxing his conduct, even in the bitterest frost. Wang Dan, the Cultured and Upright Duke, even in his private quarters, conducted himself as if still at court. One day, returning from the palace, he donned his robes and entered his quiet chamber, sitting in silence without emerging. His family, alarmed, hastily sent his younger brother to inquire of Minister Zhao Anren. Anren replied: *"Just now, a matter was discussed, and the Duke opposed its implementation. The decision was unresolved when he left—he must be troubled for the state."* Those who secretly insult their lords—do they not feel shame before such examples?

Guo Cong lost his father in youth and lived alone with his mother. For thirty years, he sighed without ceasing, never eating meat or drinking wine, praying morning and night to extend his mother’s life. His mother, Lady Zhang, indeed lived to one hundred and four, her senses sharp, her appetite undiminished. Meng Xi was a common market vendor who sold fruit daily to support his parents. Though unlearned, he fulfilled the duties of warmth, care, attendance, and daily greetings with such perfect devotion that even the learned often fell short. When his father died, he slept on the ground, abstained from salt and dairy, and mourned to the point of near self-destruction. For three years, his grief remained unwavering. Those who secretly insult their parents—do they not feel shame before such examples?

Only by daring no hidden disrespect did the Cao and Wang families flourish so remarkably. Guo Cong was honored by imperial decree, his gate and lane adorned with banners of commendation; Meng Xi, while digging the earth, discovered gold beneath his hoe and thus became a wealthy household. Does Heaven ever fail to reward loyalty and filial piety? In truth, it never errs.

**Eulogy:**
Reverence spares no disrespect,
Deceit bears no true weight.
In serving father, in serving lord,
No reverence surpasses this.
To withhold one’s heart
Is but deceit.
As face and form belong to man,
So too the role of minister and child.
Though one claim ignorance,
The gods and spirits make it plain.

*The Sixth Scroll of the Supreme Scripture on Stimulus and Response concludes here.*

*The Seventh Scroll of the Supreme Scripture on Stimulus and Response begins.*

**Biography of Li Changling, with a Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

*On Teachers*
A teacher is one who transmits the Dao, imparts knowledge, and resolves doubts. Even a single word of enlightenment, a single character passed down, originates from them—how could one dare treat them with disdain?

In former times, when Hu Yuandi served as a teacher, his disciples—such as Qian Zao, steadfast and profound; Sun Jue, pure and bright; Fan Chunren, upright and gentle; and Qian Gongfu, simple and honest—were all renowned scholars of their age. Yet in their teacher’s presence, they conducted themselves no differently than children and younger brothers attending their father and elder brothers, never once showing disrespect.

When Sun Mingfu served as a teacher, his disciples—such as Shi Jie, broadly learned and versatile, and Zhang Dong, widely read and erudite—were among the most virtuous in Lu. Even those of great virtue, in their teacher’s presence, followed in step when ascending or descending, carried his staff and sandals when bowing, and maintained solemn reverence—never was disrespect heard of.

Take, for instance, Peng Ruli, a Grand Master for Court Consultation, who in his youth studied under Ni Tianyin of Silu. When Peng later held the office of Protector of Trust, he welcomed Tianyin to his residence and treated him with the utmost reverence as a disciple. After Tianyin’s death, his mother remained unburied, and the following year, his wife also passed, leaving no son. Peng arranged the burials of all three, then married Tianyin’s daughter to Song Huan, a successful candidate in the same imperial examination. Minister Wang Cun, in his youth, studied under Chen Jun of Yingchuan. When Jun died without an heir, Wang appointed Jun’s brother’s son to an official post, ensuring Jun’s wife had someone to rely on. Such men never forgot their teachers even after death—how much less would they dare show disrespect while they lived?

Those who do treat teachers with disdain are but frivolous and petty men. They fail to recognize that teachers possess profound qualities not to be slighted. Their guidance may yet enlighten, their hidden virtues may be immense, and their descendants may flourish in ways yet unseen. The deeds of the Wang family serve as proof.

Long ago, there was a teacher named Wang, whose family was exceedingly poor, and he made his living by instructing village children. In his old age, he had a son at eight years old but could not afford to raise him, so he entrusted the boy to a monk at the Western Temple. The monk, upon seeing the child, recognized his exceptional nature and immediately allowed him to partake in offerings, sparing no expense in supporting him. During the Xianping era, the boy was recommended as a worthy and upright candidate, later rising to the rank of Participant in Determining Governmental Matters, then Military Affairs Commissioner. His name and position were illustrious, his official achievements surpassing others—this was Wang Wenkang. Xu was his given name. Wenkang’s son, Yirou, advanced to the rank of Dragon Diagram Academician. Yirou’s sons, Jinxing and Jinyan, both attained the rank of Grand Master, known for their virtue, and often associated with scholars like Kangjie. How, then, could one dare treat teachers with disdain?

**Eulogy:**
Father gives life, teacher imparts teaching,
Both are spoken of as one.
Transmitting the Dao, imparting knowledge—
Which is the root, which the source?
In mourning garb, the heart mourns three years.
This is the weight of human relations,
And the Way of Heaven as well.
To disdain what one should revere—
No evil surpasses this.
To betray what one serves.

*On Betrayal*
Subordinates to their superiors, soldiers to their commanders, servants and concubines to their masters—all serve. Betrayal need not be outright defiance; even in times of urgency, failing to rely on them, or in matters of profit and harm, showing no concern—this is betrayal. Let me speak of a few servants and soldiers, that those above them may feel some shame.

Long ago, a servant named Zhao Yansi served Wang Linji as a retainer. Linji, who held the office of Drafter of Imperial Decrees, died within a year along with his son, leaving his family impoverished and unable to support themselves. Yansi, bound by righteousness, refused to leave. He exhausted his strength to raise Linji’s three daughters for over a decade, never once seeing their faces. When the girls came of age, he journeyed to the capital to seek out Linji’s former colleagues, Academician Bai and Vice Director Yang Huizhi, weeping loudly as he recounted his story. The two officials, astonished, said, “What you have done surpasses us.” They pooled their resources to bring the three daughters to the capital, arranged good marriages for them, and found them reputable husbands. At the time, Master Shi Shoudao of Culai wrote an account of this to shame those who lacked steadfastness in righteousness.

Wang Kui initially served as a servant to Luntian Langzhong Li Tan, then enlisted as a soldier and was selected for the Bangri Army. When Tan and his son were implicated in a case and imprisoned in the Censorate, none of their former relatives or friends dared visit or send provisions. Only Kui stood guard at the prison gate daily, supplying them with food and drink for over forty days without the slightest negligence. When the case was concluded, Tan was exiled to Nannen Prefecture. As the entourage prepared to depart, all others took their leave, but Kui alone accompanied them. Tan, suddenly overcome with grief and indignation, took his own life. Kui arranged his funeral and mourned him as if he were his own father and son, moving all who witnessed it to tears. Only after all matters were settled did he dare depart. At the time, Minister Sima Wengong also wrote an account of this to admonish those who treat righteousness lightly.

How, then, can those who receive kindness from others, yet abandon them at the first sign of profit or harm, compare even to servants like Zhao Yansi and Wang Kui?

**Eulogy:**
Rulers, teachers, elders, and superiors—
Those who receive their grace...

All speak of their duties—
Each role bears its proper reverence.
Bonds of affection and righteousness endure,
Passed down to children and grandchildren.
Servants repay their masters,
As dogs and horses remain loyal to their halls.
I recall Ying of old,
Whose deeds live on in the Nine Springs.
Yet how deceitful are those without discernment!

The *Śūraṅgama Sūtra* states: "Delusion and ignorance mislead sentient beings, and after death, they shall fall into the Avīci Hell." If this is so, how can the sin of deception be considered light?

Long ago, at the Taiqing Palace in Bozhou, there lived a Daoist priest of striking appearance. He would carry a small furnace to the hall of Lord Lao, where he forged and sold medicines. Whenever a crowd gathered, he would point at the statue of Lord Lao and declare loudly, "I am his master!" Many were deceived, all eager to obtain his medicines. One day, as he again pointed at the statue and boasted, fire suddenly leapt from the furnace and engulfed him. In an instant, flames consumed his body. Though the crowd poured water upon him, the fire could not be quenched. He shrieked and writhed in agony, until at last, facing the statue of Lord Lao, he prostrated himself as if begging forgiveness. When they looked again, he was already dead. Yang Kedong later confirmed this account with the coroner.

There was also Zhang Huaisu, a man of imposing presence. He claimed mastery of Daoist arts, able to summon spirits and command flight. He boasted that when Confucius executed Shaozheng Mao, he had been present to advise against haste. He further claimed to have witnessed the Battle of Chenggao between Han and Chu from a mountaintop. Even the treacherous Cai Bian was deceived, praising him extravagantly in conversation. Only the loyal and upright Chen Guan refused to believe him. Later, when Zhang’s schemes unraveled and he was punished, many scholars were implicated—only Chen Guan escaped the web of false accusations.

Can those who deceive the ignorant ever escape disaster in the end?

Deceivers, in general, know only greed and fear no sin. As monks, they wield empty talk to deceive; as alchemists, they use elixirs to deceive; as spirit-mediums, they invoke gods and ghosts to deceive; as physicians or diviners, they exploit their arts to deceive. How can they not know that deception brings not only worldly ruin but also condemnation to the great hells?

**Eulogy:**

Laughter cuts deeper than rage, resolve strikes harder than war.
To mock, deceive, and delude—is this not against human nature?
Fish in the depths may leap, birds on the foam startle at shadows.
How much more so for us, of the same kind, the most enlightened of beings?
To treat deception as skill is to cover one’s ears while stealing a bell.
Slandering fellow students—

The *Records* say: Among the five human relationships are ruler and minister, father and son, elder and younger brothers, husband and wife, and friends—one of these five. If this is so, can the Way of friendship be taken lightly? How much more for fellow students, who daily share the same desk and mat, exchanging kind words and laughter in close companionship—can one rashly harbor jealousy and spread slander?

Long ago, when Minister Guo Zhi first composed his rhapsodies and gained fame, his classmate Li Mian deeply envied his talent and secretly slandered him. As a result, Guo repeatedly failed the imperial examinations, while Li later passed first. When Guo was later appointed examiner, Li—having only qualified through the lesser *mingjing* degree—was among the candidates. On the day the edict was announced, Li deeply regretted his past actions and returned home. Upon hearing this, Guo immediately sent a trusted friend to summon him back, allowing him to retake the examination and pass. Yet could Guo’s excellence ever truly be suppressed? Now, looking back, does Li feel no shame? It is lamentable that he did not understand how elders and classmates should treat one another with kindness.

In another case, Han Yi and Li Ruogu studied together in their youth and once traveled the same road to take the examinations, sharing a single quilt and felt mat. Whenever they went out, they took turns serving as each other’s attendants. That year, Li passed first and was appointed registrar of Changshe in Xuzhou. As he prepared to take office, Li personally led the mule for his wife, while Han followed behind, carrying a basket. When they reached the border, only eight hundred coins remained, which they divided equally before parting in tears. The next year, Han also passed, and both later rose to the rank of vice-minister. Their families intermarried, and they never slandered one another—did they?

Then there was Zhao Anren, who in his youth studied with Song Yuanxing. After Yuanxing died young, his descendants fell into decline. Once Zhao attained high office, he repeatedly used his salary to aid them and personally tutored their sons, hand-copying the *Book of Changes*, the *Analects*, the *Daodejing*, and the *Classic of Filial Piety*—seven texts in all, bestowing one upon each child. This is how one does not forget a classmate even after death. Those who lack loyalty and kindness—how could they ever match this?

**Eulogy:**

Fair-weather friendships crumble at the first sign of misfortune. Sun Quan’s envy of Pang Tong led to his downfall; Sun Bin’s rivalry with Pang Juan cost him his legs. Yet when friends observe and emulate one another’s virtues, the Dao flourishes and transformation ripens. Even if one’s talent is lacking, like stone that polishes jade, with the right companionship, one attains high office and a hundred blessings.

But falsehood, slander, deceit, and pretense—these are the four poisons. As the saying goes: *Falsehood* is reckless and groundless; *slander* is baseless defamation; *deceit* is a web of cunning schemes; *pretense* is exaggerated artifice. Though distinct in name, they share the same root: dishonesty.

Broadly speaking, sincerity is the Way of Heaven; cultivating sincerity is the Way of Man. Yet now, abandoning sincerity to practice falsehood, slander, deceit, and pretense is to rebel against Heaven’s Way and forsake the human path. Having defied both, where can one escape the Three Evil Realms?

Thus Master Yaoshan said: "To recognize the path of hungry ghosts, look to those today who are full of emptiness and short on substance, who make others distrust them." Again he said: "Do you wish to know the path of hell? It is those now boiling in cauldrons of scalding oil. Do you wish to know the path of hungry ghosts? It is those now full of emptiness and short on substance, who make others distrust them. Do you wish to know the path of beasts? It is those now who recognize neither benevolence nor duty, who cannot distinguish kin from stranger. Must they grow fur and horns, or be hacked and hung upside down? Do you wish to know the paths of humans and gods? It is those now who purify their bearing and hold offerings with reverence."

Of the four poisons, "full of emptiness and short on substance" is but one. Yet even this alone leads to such swift descent—how much worse when all four are committed? The consequences of such a fall need not be discussed. Let us instead speak of one or two sincere men, that all may learn self-restraint.

Long ago, Li Ji served as a regional inspector. When his term ended, he was summoned for an audience. Emperor Shenzong asked, "What is the price of wheat?" Li replied, "In my duties, I have been impartial in all matters—only the price of wheat, I do not know." Later, when a vacancy arose for a circuit inspector, the chief ministers repeatedly recommended candidates, but none met the emperor’s approval. One day, the emperor himself remarked, "Who was it who did not know the price of wheat?" The chief ministers asked why. The emperor said, "I wish to understand the conditions of the realm, so I need men of loyalty, sincerity, and integrity. If one does not even know the price of wheat, what else could he fabricate?" Thereupon Li Ji was summoned and gained the emperor’s trust.

A certain Wang the Recluse was originally a sturdy foot soldier of the Imperial City Bureau. At the time, the Yellow River had burst its banks in a great flood, and laborers were everywhere engaged in building dikes to contain it. The emperor dispatched several sturdy foot soldiers to travel back and forth as scouts. Two groups had already arrived and reported, "The breach has been sealed, and the waters have returned to their original course." Shortly thereafter, Wang arrived and gave the same report. Then he added, "When I first set out, I sensed that the flow at the ford had not yet been fully cut off—I fear this may still trouble Your Majesty’s thoughts." The emperor, enraged, had him detained. Soon after, a report arrived: the waters had surged with violent force, breaching the great dike once more and flooding several commanderies.

Thereupon the emperor summoned Wang, offered him comfort and praise, and immediately promoted him to the rank of junior officer. From then on, whenever appointments to the officer ranks were made, he often received exceptional promotions. When the Eastern Palace sought to select a personal guard commander, Liu Qian had already been chosen, but one position remained vacant. The emperor said, "Wang the Recluse is honest and trustworthy—he shall be appointed at once." Later, he rose to the position of Commander of the Palace Infantry Guard, Military Commissioner of Baoning, and was granted the privilege of hereditary office, benefiting seventeen of his descendants in all.

Thus, the integrity of these two gentlemen—how could it not surpass the hollow deceit of falsehood, which swiftly leads one into evil destinies?

In praise, it is said:

Without sincerity, nothing exists—this is called emptiness.
Words once spoken cannot be retracted, for their substance is false.
Deceit usurps truth; fraud verges on folly.
Who would partake of what remains when one conducts oneself thus?
Even flesh and blood become estranged—how much more so the capital and the state?
To assail and expose one’s kin—

As the *Record* states: The *Book of Rites* says, "Cherish your kin, and your uncles and brothers will harbor no resentment." What is meant by "cherishing one’s kin"? It is that each should cherish their own kin. Once one is counted among the clan and shares common ancestors above, how can they not cherish one another? Looking to those who came before, none expressed this more aptly than Master Fan Wenzheng.

Fan Wenzheng said: "Many of my clan dwell in Wu. To me, there are degrees of closeness, but in the eyes of our ancestors, all are equally their descendants, without distinction. Moreover, for over a hundred years, our ancestors accumulated virtue, and it is only in my generation that it has borne fruit. Now that I hold high office, if I were to enjoy wealth and honor alone without the slightest care for my kin, how could I face my ancestors in the underworld? What face would I have to enter the ancestral temple?" Thus, he ensured that all favors, salaries, and gifts were distributed equally among his kin. Furthermore, he purchased forty *qing* of fertile land near the outskirts of Suzhou to establish a charitable estate, providing for the poor among his extended family. He selected one virtuous elder to oversee its administration, giving each person a daily ration of one *sheng* of rice and an annual allotment of one bolt of silk. For weddings, funerals, and other needs, he provided additional support. His son, Chunren, was especially able to carry on his father’s aspirations. He maintained frugality and integrity, using his entire salary to expand the charitable estate. When granted the privilege of hereditary office, he always prioritized distant relatives. On the day of his death, five of his grandsons had yet to receive official appointments.

Such were these father and son—they truly embodied the principle of cherishing one’s kin. Others, too, exemplified this virtue: Minister Peng Siyong showed great kindness in nurturing his clan; Duke Zhang Kangjie often shared his salary with his nine generations of kin; Zhao Fuyou Shikai donated gold to relieve the uncountable poor among his relatives; and Gong’an, my revered ancestor, took in orphaned and widowed members of the clan, arranging marriages for them. These, too, were those who cherished their kin. If one can truly cherish their kin, how could they ever commit the error of mutual assault?

What is meant by "mutual assault"? It is the insistence on contention over every "you" and "me," the nitpicking over every thread and hair, the harboring of resentment and jealousy, the scheming to undermine and seize from one another—all of this is it. One need not wait for some great transgression to call it "mutual assault." Beware!

In praise, it is said:
Attack what is wrong in oneself,
but treat others with forbearance.
To mistake assault for uprightness
is what the sages abhor.
To be thick with outsiders
yet thin with those close—
this is truly shameful and fearful.
If even the roots of kinship,
those one sees daily,
are treated like strangers on the road,
and no effort is made to foster harmony,
then the constant norms of human relations are undermined.

**Hymn to the Heavenly Master of Emptiness and Stillness**
The nature of man
is profound, perfect, and quiescent.
When it engages the world and responds to movement,
all becomes delusion.
A single thought straying from rectitude
is already hell.
To reverently recite these words
is to break out in a sweat of realization.
The embers of the heart’s fire are banked,
the qi-steed is tamed.
Having thus examined oneself,
one also warns those who come after.

*This concludes the seventh scroll of the* Supreme Scripture on Responses and Retributions.
*Here begins the eighth scroll of the* Supreme Scripture on Responses and Retributions.

**Biography of Li Changling, with a Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

Strength without benevolence.
The *Transmission* says: Confucius regarded strength as akin to benevolence, while the Most High deemed strength devoid of benevolence. How could the words of the sages contradict one another? The strength Confucius commended was resolute fortitude—unyielding within, the strength of a noble man, who would even sacrifice himself to fulfill benevolence, hence "akin to benevolence." It is also said: "The root, if it desires, how could it not be strong?"

The strength the Most High cautioned against was brute force—wrath ever manifest without, the strength of a petty man, who bullies others and tramples all things. How could this be benevolence? Thus, it is called "without benevolence." It is also said: "The violent do not meet a natural death." And again: "The rigid and unyielding are companions of death." Generally, brute force, if one cannot restrain oneself, readily leads to the fault of lacking benevolence. This is why the Most High declared it in writing: "without benevolence."

In former times, Huan Fan, bearing the imperial tally, oversaw the military affairs of Qing and Xu provinces. Upon first arriving in Xuzhou, he quarreled with the prefectural governor, Xu Qi, over a residence and sought to invoke his authority to execute Qi. Qi reported this to the court, and Fan, having no just cause, was dismissed from office. Later, when he governed Jizhou, Lü Zhaocai served as General of the Northern Garrison, and Jizhou fell under his command. Fan, considering that Zhaocai had risen in office after him, held him in contempt and said to his wife: "I would rather kneel before the Three Dukes than bow to Lü Zizhan." His wife replied: "In Xuzhou, you sought to invoke your authority to execute Qi. Everyone said you would be difficult to serve under. Now in Jizhou, you refuse to bow to Lü Zizhan. People will say you are difficult to serve above." Enraged by her words, Fan struck her abdomen with a sword hilt, causing her to miscarry and die. Such violence—was this not lacking in benevolence?

Bao Xiaosu was inwardly resolute and unyielding. When his subordinates presented matters to him, he often rebuked them to their faces. Yet if their words accorded with reason, he would cheerfully amend his ways. Thus, all admired his strength without finding him obstinate. This was resolute fortitude, distinct from brute force. Hence, the differing views of the Most High and Confucius.

I have often reflected: even if one avoids brute force, excessive reliance on strength still risks error. Yang Wengong Yi, unmatched in literary prowess, was deeply favored by the emperor, yet his unyielding nature alienated others. Though many spoke in his favor, the emperor’s regard waned. Wang Neihan Yuchen, also unmatched in literary skill, was considered for high office, but his unyielding nature provoked opposition, and the imperial decree never came. Thus, unchecked strength—does it not bring harm to oneself? If even these two gentlemen met such ends, what of others?

**Eulogy:**
Strength that does not overcome strength,
violent and without kinship.
Strength that does not strengthen the self
must lord over others.
This is called a baleful virtue—
how could it be benevolence?
Good thoughts thus severed,
what evil would not follow?
Seek your manifold blessings,
for a withered tree knows no spring.

**Obstinate and Self-Willed**
The *Transmission* says: Obstinate and self-willed—according to the Buddha’s analogy, like a vicious horse, difficult to tame. If one is as untamable as a horse, should one not be counted among the lowest of men? Thus, in the *Dharma Wheel Sutra*, the Most High praised those who do not cling to their own ways and exhorted others to follow. Here, in this text, he again warns against obstinacy and self-will. Our Master Confucius also said: "The foolish who delight in their own ways, the lowly who delight in their own authority—such men bring disaster upon themselves." The sages of the Three Teachings all spoke thus—how could one not take heed?

Generally, the obstinate and self-willed distance themselves from friends and acquaintances, and take no pleasure in the discourse of life and the Dao. In daily conduct, they are perverse and unyielding—how could they avoid error? It is lamentable that they do not flow with goodness as water flows downward.

What the noble man practices, how could one cling to one’s own ways—let alone be obstinate? Formerly, Lord Li Wenzheng Fang often said: "At the end of the Xiande reign, I served as Hanlin Academician. When my mother, the Great Lady Ding, passed away, I resumed my duties after the mourning period. At the time, the Vice Minister of Rites, Dou Yan, admonished me: 'The fish-shaped tally, signifying diligent service to the ruler day and night, is adorned with gold—an ornament of the body. You, having been compelled by imperial decree to curtail your mourning, should not wear gold or jade.' I immediately acknowledged my oversight. From then on, I resolved: 'As a son, though mourning rites are not prearranged, if one fails to observe the three-year mourning with propriety, not only does one fall short of the norms of proper conduct, but how could one face one’s peers among the gentry?' Through this, I came to recognize Dou’s words as those of a true elder."

Master Zhengshu, passing through Fan Yaofu’s administrative post, said to Yaofu: "I heard that when the former commander died, you, having succeeded him only days before, held a banquet with music to entertain and reward the officers. Is this true?" Yaofu replied: "It is." The master said: "At first, you should have dispersed the gathering, making them understand that the lack of provisions was due to the former commander’s death." Yaofu’s face flushed with heat, and sweat drenched him as shame welled within. The next day, he escorted the master to the border, saying: "Had you not traveled far, I would not have heard such wise words."

These two gentlemen, when faced with matters, followed goodness thus. Is it any wonder their names resounded in their time and their blessings flowed to their descendants? Could the obstinate and self-willed ever hope for the same?

**Eulogy:**

"Zhong Hui possessed the proper way—self-reliance makes one small.
Goodness is hard to change; madness cannot be corrected.
A stingy heart seals itself off, private brows fret in turmoil.
Obstinate in rejecting counsel, domineering toward others,
evil accumulates until disaster’s omen appears.
Calamity befalls one’s person—it is self-summoned.
Right and wrong are not properly judged.

As the tradition says: Zhuangzi said, *'That is one right and wrong, this is another right and wrong—is there truly a that-and-this? Or is there truly no that-and-this?'* This is to view right and wrong from beyond right and wrong, where they seem as if they do not exist.
