Yet if one still engages with the world, one cannot avoid matters of right and wrong. Each right and each wrong is weighed by gain and harm. In one state, one weighs the state’s gain and harm; in one household, one weighs the household’s gain and harm. Who would say otherwise?

Thus Xunzi said: *'To affirm right and negate wrong is called wisdom; to negate right and affirm wrong is called folly.'* Folly means disregarding gain and harm, recklessly affirming wrong and negating right. To ruin another’s affairs—is this not folly? How lamentable that one does not know to keep the mind balanced and upright, daring not recklessly affirm wrong and negate right, and thus each day should transcend the world.

Have you not heard of Yin Zou’s affair? Long ago, Yin Shiru debated right and wrong with others, concealing nothing, neither stopping out of convenience nor following blindly, but only striving to exhaust principle to the fullest. On the day of his death, he first wrote a letter by hand to bid Wen Zheng farewell. When Wen Zheng rushed to him, the master had already bathed, dressed in his robes and cap, and sat upright in passing. Wen Zheng wept bitterly for him, but he opened his eyes and said: *'I have already bid you farewell—why come again? Life and death are common principles—does Xiwen not understand?'* With these words, he bowed once more and passed away. Had he not, in daily life, affirmed right and negated wrong without concealment, could he have faced death’s lord with such composure?

Zou Su served as *Chongban* of the Inner Palace, upright and unwavering, affirming right and negating wrong without reckless utterance. He never acted against righteousness, nor was he greedy or cruel, nor did he indulge in taking lives. In the end, he was received by the Perfected Warrior Lord and appointed to his retinue."

In the early years of the Qingli era, he was appointed as the wine inspector of Zhengzhou. One day, a sudden storm of wind and hail arose, and a copper coffin without a lid descended. The master immediately straightened his robe and cap, entered, and lay within it. In an instant, a lid descended and sealed the coffin. With a thunderous crash, the coffin rose into midair and drifted slowly northward. Today, he serves as the Judge of the Northern Dipper’s Lifespan Bureau, accompanying the Perfected Warrior Lord in his annual descent to assess the good and evil deeds of mortals, measuring the length of their allotted lifespans. Had he not, in daily life, affirmed right and negated wrong without reckless utterance, would the Perfected Warrior Lord have entrusted him with such a weighty office? These two gentlemen, in their transcendence, achieved such an end—what fate, then, awaits those who fail to discern right from wrong?

**Eulogy:**
Who slanders, who praises—such was the upright Way of the Three Dynasties.
The impartiality of right and wrong is as clear as black and white.
Yet petty minds, once deluded, invert praise and blame,
Confusing the upright with the wicked, the virtuous with the thieving.
Heaven’s judgment is never misled; the sun shines bright and clear.
To turn toward or away—this is where propriety is breached.

**Commentary:**
"Turning toward" means aligning with what is proper; "turning away" means opposing it. To turn away when one should turn toward, or turn toward when one should turn away—this is what the Most High calls "breaching propriety." The gentleman refrains from such acts, but the petty man commits them without scruple.

In former times, Minister Wang Zun maintained fair and impartial judgments, never swaying toward or against any side. His resolve was steadfast and unshakable. As Master Wen once praised him: *"Amidst ten thousand galloping horses, only Wang Zun could stand firm."* Thus, from his youth to old age, serving five reigns with unwavering devotion, he held fast to one principle through all changes and upheavals.

When Minister Liu Zhisu enumerated the ten harms of the corvée assistance policy, those in power demanded an account of his stance. Summoned to the Ministry of Revenue, he was ordered to explain himself. He replied: *"What I turn toward is loyalty and uprightness; what I turn away from is deceit and flattery. What I turn toward is righteousness; what I turn away from is self-interest. What I turn toward is the sovereign and the father; what I turn away from is the powerful and the corrupt. If governance follows this path, punishment is my due. Yet the corvée remains a scourge upon the realm—I beg Your Majesty not to forget my words."* Though others recoiled in fear, he stood unmoved. Could such men as these have breached propriety in their turning toward or away?

Yet others, like Li Shizhong and Wu Xiaozong, acted otherwise. Li Shizhong’s views had long clashed with those of Duke Jing. When the duke’s power grew, Li sought to curry favor, erecting a pavilion in Shuzhou named *Chuanyan*, alluding to the duke’s past tenure there and his enfeoffment in Shu. Wu Xiaozong, in his initial policy response, vehemently opposed the Xining reforms. But when Duke Jing gained the emperor’s favor, Wu composed *Ten Chapters of Alleyway Discourse*, claiming that even common folk praised the new laws. The duke scorned him. Such breaches of propriety—were these the acts of a gentleman or a petty man?

**Eulogy:**
To act with propriety is called righteousness.
To perceive truth clearly, to hold fast without wavering—
Once the path of compliance or defiance is chosen,
Fortune and misfortune diverge sharply.
Do not distance yourself from the wise and virtuous,
Nor draw near to the deceitful and false.
Only by following the human Way
Does one align with Heaven’s will.

**Oppressing the Lowly to Seize Merit**

**Commentary:**
A candle in darkness dispels shadows; a boat on water bears its load.
When conditions are ripe, merit arises naturally—
It need not be seized.
To insist on seizing it is to oppress those below.
Merit may never be achieved, but calamity will surely strike first.
This is what is meant by "oppressing to seize wealth" or "oppressing to exploit labor"—both are acts of oppression.

Consider the seizure of wealth. Wealth is what all people urgently need. Have you not heard of the cases of Wang Zi and Ma Jiang?

Wang Zi, known for his harshness in office, served as an assistant in the Sichuan Transport Bureau during the Shaoxing era. When ordered to inspect the salt wells of Tongchuan, he personally visited each well, compelling the well owners to acknowledge inflated quotas. Those who owed fifty catties were forced to pledge a hundred, ensuring their assets would be confiscated and the salt boiled by the state. Soon after, the well owners were ruined, and Wang died suddenly, reborn as an ox. On the night of his death, his friend Prefect Yang and his household saw Wang come seeking help—his official robe already split by a tail emerging from beneath. The next day, a neighbor’s cow gave birth.

Then there was Ma Jiang, another assistant official, even more ruthless. Whenever he went to collect taxes, he demanded exorbitant sums, heedless of actual quotas or market conditions. If his demands were not met, he would fly into a rage, earning the nickname *Ma the Scraper*—for his habit of scraping money clean. Someone warned him of Wang Zi’s fate, saying, *"Even if you grew two tails today, you wouldn’t care."* Soon after, boils erupted on his left back, then his right, the two sores facing each other like a beaten spine, over an inch deep, exposing his organs. His colleagues visited in shock. Only then did he confess: *"Let those who scrape money in the future take me as a warning."* His flesh wasted away, and only then did he draw his last breath.

Others, too, met grim fates: Li Zi died childless, Chen Shu’s soldiers perished, Lin Te’s descendants met unnatural ends—all for oppressing those below.

**Eulogy:**
The world has its "stalwart officials,"
Unafraid of imperial decrees.
They carve away at the people’s strength
To fill their bribe sacks.
Once their coffers overflow,
They offer "surplus" to their superiors.
High ranks are easily enjoyed,
Grand mansions easily occupied.
But what becomes of them and their descendants in the end?
Flattering the powerful to curry favor.

The *Tradition* states: The sovereign governs the statutes of life, death, sin, and fortune, and none oversee this more than Fengdu. Beneath the Northern Emperor of Fengdu, only the Heavenly Lord of Mystic Nourishment assists the Four Lords, the Six Heavenly Demons, and the minor officials of the Three Palaces of Heaven, Earth, and Water—none of whom reveal their names. Among their subordinates, as recorded in the *Declarations of the Perfected*, are all the loyal and upright of the world. Those above them include the Duke of Shaogong Shi, who became the Duke of Southern Brightness, and Wu Jizha, who became the Duke of Northern Brightness. Those of the next rank include Jia Yi, who served as the Forbidden Gentleman of the Western Brightness, and Zang Hong, who became the Northern Dipper Lord and Heavenly Gate Warden. Still others, like Ji Zhan, who became the Northern Heavenly Gate Keeper, and Wen Qiao, who became the Earl Overseer of the Sea, are also among them. Has there ever been one who curried favor with superiors that was counted among their ranks?

Generally, in serving a ruler, loyalty is paramount. A single word of flattery is enough to brand one as disloyal. The disloyal are inevitably condemned by Heaven—how can they hope for deliverance?

In the past, Emperor Gaozong of Tang sought to elevate Lady Wu as empress but hesitated, fearing the dissent of his ministers. One day, he summoned Li Ji, Changsun Wuji, Yu Zhining, and Chu Suiliang to deliberate. Suiliang and the others all opposed the idea, while Li Ji remained silent. Yu Zhining, too, glanced about and said nothing. Days later, the emperor secretly consulted Li Ji again. Had Ji spoken even a single word of remonstrance then, he would have remained loyal, and the Tang might have been spared the disaster that befell its imperial clan. But Ji did otherwise, replying instead that this was the emperor’s private affair, not to be questioned by outsiders. Thus, the emperor’s resolve was set, Lady Wu was enthroned, and the Tang’s imperial house was annihilated.

In Ji’s heart, he sought only to protect himself, fearing calamity. Yet he failed to see that the self cannot be truly shielded, nor can disaster be truly feared. Soon after his death, his entire clan was exterminated, his tomb desecrated—every misfortune befell him. Who can say that flattering superiors to curry favor brings no disaster?

There was also Guo Chongtao, who met the same fate. Having risen to high office and seeing his own merits as great, he grew restless and scheming. He petitioned the emperor to establish Empress Liu, intending to secure his own position. Little did he know that the hearts of others are hard to fathom and easily turned against one. In the end, it was Empress Liu who had him killed. Can such flattery of superiors be justified? Can it bring anything but ruin?

**Eulogy:**
Flattering superiors, chasing favor,
Opening the way to sin.
So-called "pleasing the ruler"—
No crime is greater.
When the ruler’s mind is yet undecided,
Change may still come.
A single word of compliance,
And their resolve hardens.
Ruined lives, fallen nations—
Blame not Heaven.
Ingrates receive kindness yet feel no gratitude.

The *Tradition* further states: According to the *Dharma Treasury Forest of Gems*, receiving kindness is like sowing grain. Though the seed depends on the earth, without rain, it will not grow. When the grain ripens, one cannot credit only the earth and ignore the grace of rain. As the *Great Perfection of Wisdom Treatise* says, ingratitude is worse than being a beast.

I once read a passage in Liu Zongyuan’s writings that further illustrates how ingratitude is a deed even beasts would not commit. To be human yet fall short of beasts—is that not worse than being a beast?

At Jianfu Temple, there was a hawk that perched atop the pagoda. One winter’s night, the temple’s bell-ringer caught a bird small enough to hold in his hand. Instead of killing it, he turned it gently in his palms to warm its claws. At dawn, he released it unharmed. After setting it free, he watched it intently as it flew—eastward when it vanished in the west, westward when it vanished in the east—seemingly unwilling to meet his gaze again, lest it be seized once more.

Alas! Hawks are creatures that seize and tear their prey to eat. Yet this one, having had its claws warmed for a single night, acted thus. How can those who receive kindness yet feel no gratitude claim to be anything but worse than beasts?

How lamentable that those who do not know gratitude are farthest from the Dao. For it is not ingratitude, but gratitude, that brings one closer to the Dao. And if one is grateful, their daily conduct is plain to see.

During the Xuanhe era, there was a scholar-official named Xu Wenzhong who once traveled to the Eastern Capital. One of his soldiers was accidentally run over by a cart and left lame, unable to walk. He encountered a Daoist who gave him a small dose of medicine, and soon the soldier walked as before.

The soldier was deeply moved and grateful. He traveled throughout the realm, seeking the Daoist to offer even a modest token of thanks. One day, he encountered him again on the road and wept as he bowed in gratitude. The Daoist said, "I have bestowed kindness upon many, yet none have been like you." Thereupon, he transmitted a secret formula, and the soldier attained the Dao.

When Xu Wenzhong heard of this, he visited the soldier to inquire about the Way. The soldier replied, "Stillness is the Dao; simplicity is supreme." Wenzhong was suddenly enlightened, and he too attained the Dao.

Who could claim that one who knows no gratitude may yet attain the Dao? How many in this world are like this soldier?

**Eulogy:**
In this world, there are those more cruel than wolves and tigers.
They take shelter under another’s wings,
Yet repay with axes and blades.
Such men sever all human bonds,
Neither lord nor father do they acknowledge.
Like the man who shielded the Duke of Wei beneath the mulberry,
Or the one who lacquered his body to feign madness—
To bear a debt and repay it is one thing,
But to cling to resentment without end is another.
The giver should not look back,
Yet the aggrieved nurse their grievances ceaselessly.

The *Record* states: Master Deshan addressed the assembly, saying, "Even a single thought tied to the dharma becomes the cause of the Three Wretched Realms. A fleeting emotion gives rise to eons of bondage." If mere attachment to the dharma can still be such a burden, how much worse is the mind that harbors resentment without cease?

Know this: the mind that clings to resentment binds itself most tightly, never to be freed, plunging swiftly into the Three Wretched Realms as if shot by an arrow, never hearing the teachings of repentance. Does this not confirm the saying, "Without enmity, there are no evil destinies"?

According to the sutras: "When hatred and resentment arise from mutual animosity, at the moment of death—before the body’s warmth has faded—stones will fly, cangues will lock, and cages, jars, and sacks will appear before the dying, ready to crush and confine." This is the fate of those who cling to resentment without end: they are bound for hell.

Further, it is said: "Greed and hatred are sins. When one’s punishment in hell is complete, they are reborn as venomous creatures, known as poisonous ghosts." This is the fate of those who cling to resentment: they fall into the realm of hungry ghosts.

Again, it is said: "When the poison of such ghosts is exhausted, they are reborn in the world as venomous beings." This is the fate of those who cling to resentment: they fall into the realm of beasts.

The Buddha’s words are never false. Who could deny this?

Yet there are those who, having already fallen into the Three Evil Realms, still do not cease their resentment. Like the bandit of Jizhou, who, after dying for his crimes and falling into hell, still harbored hatred for Prefect Zhang Yi, who had executed him beyond the law. His resentment unceasing, he sought to drag Zhang Yi into hell. Or like the ghost recorded in the *Vibhāṣā Śāstra*, who, having died with accumulated sins and fallen into the realm of hungry ghosts, still resented a woman who had killed him five hundred lifetimes prior. His resentment unceasing, he ultimately took her life in return. Or like the mule in Yang Bao’s stable, which, having died for its evil karma and fallen into the realm of beasts, still harbored resentment toward Wang He, who had once wronged it. Its resentment unceasing, it returned to kill Wang He.

Thus, is not the character for "resentment" the very thing that binds a person most tightly, ensuring they never attain liberation?

Have you not heard the words of the Thirty-First Ancestor, Great Master Can? The Thirty-Second Ancestor, Great Master Daoxin, once respectfully asked Can, "I beg the Master’s compassion—grant me liberation." Can replied, "Who binds you?" Daoxin said, "No one binds me." Can said, "If no one binds you, why seek liberation?" At these words, Daoxin awakened and was liberated.

If these words of Can were widely spread, then those who do not cease would cease, and those who are not liberated would be liberated.

**Eulogy:**
Boyi and Shuqi bore no grudges,
Their hearts set on seeking benevolence.
When others failed to return courtesy,
Mencius turned inward to reflect.
With empathy and principle, all things flourish in spring.
Yet Xi Ke remembered a slight,
And bones lay strewn between neighboring states.
A single thought of resentment
Is the root of ten thousand evils.

*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Eight—Concluded
*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Nine

**Biography of Li Changling, with a Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

To treat the people of Heaven with contempt—
Such are the harsh and rigid, who bring calamity upon themselves in an instant, and thus merit no further mention.

Let me speak of one or two benevolent officials who loved the people, that all may know and emulate their example.

Long ago, Lord Han Zhongxian, Qi, first served as Pacification Commissioner of the Yili Circuit. In relieving the starving, he saved over 1.9 million lives. Later, when he governed Hebei, the Yellow River burst its banks, and famine struck again. Lord Han once more devised many measures to provide relief and restore order, saving over 7 million lives.

Lord Fu Wenzhong, Gou, governed Qingzhou. When the river flooded, the people of eight prefectures fled entirely into the eastern capital region. Lord Fu urged the people to donate grain, amassing over 100,000 *hu*, which he stored in various locations to succor the starving. He also requisitioned over 100,000 vacant public and private dwellings, dispersing the refugees among them to ensure access to firewood and water. The following year, when the wheat ripened, he calculated the distance each household had traveled and provided them with grain for their return, saving over 500,000 lives.

Teng Zhangmin, Yuanfa, served as Prefect of Yunzhou. When famine struck Huainan and the eastern capital region, he repaired over 1,200 abandoned barracks outside the city to house refugees, saving 50,000 lives.

Li Yunze served as Prefect of Tanzhou. When famine struck, he requested to open the granaries for relief before submitting his memorial, but the Transport Commission refused. Li said, "If we wait for approval before acting, how can we address the crisis in time?" Thus, he pledged his family fortune as collateral to distribute grain, saving 50,000 lives.

Chen Dahui, Yaozuo, served as Prefect of Shouzhou. When famine struck, he declared, "Rather than issue orders, it is better to lead by example." He then donated his own money to aid the poor. The people of the prefecture vied to contribute, and together they saved tens of thousands of lives.

King Yimin of Su served as Prefect of Taiyuan. When the Fen and Yellow Rivers overflowed, flooding Pingjin and threatening the prefectural city, he swiftly prepared boats. The next day, when the waters arrived, the people were spared from panic. He also urged the great families to distribute grain for relief, saving one hundred thousand lives.

Hu Wengong of Su had once served as Magistrate of Yangzi. When a great flood struck and many people were swept away, he spent his own money to hire boats for rescue, reviving thousands who had already drowned.

Zeng Gong, the Grand Secretary, served as Prefect of Hongzhou during a great epidemic. From the prefectural seat to the counties, markets, towns, and post stations, he stored medicinal decoctions to await the sick. Soldiers and civilians without shelter were housed in official quarters, saving thousands of lives.

Su Shi, the Cultured and Earnest, served as Prefect of Hangzhou during a great epidemic. He used his own funds to prepare medicinal decoctions, dispatching officials with physicians to administer treatment across the wards. He also established infirmaries to house the sick, saving thousands of lives.

Could those who scorn such duties have acted thus? Yet if one does not know better while governing the people, one ought to act in this way.

In praise it is said:
Father Heaven, Mother Earth—
qi shared, bodies the same.
The eldest son, the steward of the house,
down to the hundred artisans.
All nurture one another,
aiding the work of transformation.
How could one indulge the high
while crushing the people into hardship?
Not the people’s ruin,
but the ruin of oneself—
disturbing and disordering the governance of the state.

The tradition says: Laozi said, "Governing a state is like cooking a small fish."
Lu Xiangxian said, "The world is fundamentally without trouble; mediocre men disturb it."
Generally, governance values acting without interference—
how much more so when governance is meant to rectify the people?
Officials have long followed this way,
and the people have grown accustomed to its convenience.
How could one rashly alter it
to follow one’s own ideas?
If one follows one’s own ideas,
it may not benefit the people,
but only bring about one’s own disturbance.
Impassioned, meddlesome words—
how could they all be trusted?

Long ago, when Duke Qi of Du governed and pacified Guanzhong,
a commoner named Zhang Dong—
bristling beard, dark face, clad in a black robe of coarse cloth—
presented a poem in lieu of a calling card, saying:
"Last night, an urgent dispatch came from the clouds;
who among the generals can sweep away the dust?
In Chang’an there is a guest with a face like iron—
report to the lord and king: build the terrace early."
Later, when he was granted an official post,
he was soon ruined by corruption.

When Han Weigong governed the region west of Shaanxi,
a commoner named Yao Sizong submitted a memorial on governance,
reciting a poem from Mount Kongtong:
"Trample the rocks of Helan,
sweep clean the dust of the western Wei.
If a commoner can accomplish this,
how pitiful to remain a stranded fish."
Later, when he was granted an official post,
he proved no better than a superfluous clerk.

When Lord Lü of Xu served at court, a commoner named Lei Jianfu was summoned for an audience upon recommendation. His qi could have swallowed enemies whole. After several memorials, he proved meticulous and quick-witted. The court wished to employ him in high office, but Lord Xu firmly opposed it. Eventually promoted to the post of Third Bureau Judge, he was no different in character from Sizong.

When Jiang Shaoyu governed Jizhou, a commoner named Li Jie submitted a memorial on governance, claiming to have studied the Dao of benevolence and righteousness from youth, second only to Yan Hui. He further declared that the Three Sovereigns were not sagely, that only Confucius was truly sagely, and that all thinkers from Mencius onward were unworthy of mention. When Han Zihua governed Chengdu, he too advanced this doctrine. Zihua was swayed by it, and when he later served alongside the Duke of Jing in the Third Bureau, he recommended Li Jie. Before the man could be appointed, he went mad and died.

Can such impassioned, attention-seeking words be trusted? Thus, when Zhao, Han, and Wang served as chief ministers, they paid no heed to any submissions from ambitious newcomers offering proposals on matters of advantage and harm. When Ji, Jian, and Mu held the chancellorship, they systematically excluded all memorials and suggestions from those eager for reform, given to grandstanding, fond of stirring up trouble, or of shallow and untested character. In their time, both ministers were praised for conducting themselves appropriately in office. Had they been quick to trust, would they not have easily invited chaos?

In praise it is said:

"Governance is rectitude; indulge in private interests, and it becomes biased.
The greedy perish for wealth, the vainglorious crave power.
Principle yields to force, law bends to favor.
Rewards and punishments are misapplied, discipline and order cast aside.
This harms the state—how then can you yourself remain whole?

To reward what is not righteous—
As the saying goes, Han Yu said: 'To act as is fitting is called *yi* (righteousness).'
Generally speaking, *yi* means 'what is fitting.'
To reward what is not *yi* is to bestow where it is not fitting.
Would a true gentleman ever do such a thing?

During the Jingde era, northern raiders harassed the frontier, and the commanderies and counties of the Hebei circuit repeatedly suffered their depredations. The generals in command, seeking only their own safety, dared not even slightly blunt the enemy’s edge. At the time, Li Juzheng, a minor official, was pressing for tax collection in a garrison town. Enraged by the raiders’ brutality, he rallied the townspeople, recruited able-bodied men, and struck back with all his might. Pursuing them deep into their lair, he recovered the women and children they had seized and returned them to their families. Though those in power admired his courage, not one would recommend him for reward—only Zhang Yongding secretly memorialized the throne. When the memorial reached the emperor, he was overjoyed and immediately promoted Juzheng several ranks, summoning him to serve as Gentleman of the Palace Gate. Juzheng, unaware of the source of his favor, was later told of Yongding’s role. He hastened to visit him, but try as he might, he could not secure an audience. He then bribed the gatekeeper to deliver a calling card, yet Yongding still refused to appear, merely writing at the bottom of the card: 'In wealth, you were generous; in battle, brave; in affairs, diligent; toward the people, kind. Add to this your prudence and reverence—these are the great proofs of service to the state. To decline a private meeting is but a small matter.' Juzheng received this with greater joy than if he had met him in person. He cherished and carried it with him always. Juzheng’s reward may indeed be called righteous.

Shi Ji served as military commissioner of Yanzhou. During the Kangding era, Tangut raiders attacked Yongping Fort in Yanzhou. The fort commander and the supervising officer both wished to withdraw their troops into the deep mountains and wait for the raiders to leave before returning. Ji led several hundred of his men to block the city gate, halting before their horses, and demanded: 'Where do you intend to go, Commander and Supervisor?' The two answered truthfully. Ji said: 'If you do this, the troops will be preserved, but what of the people in the city? Where will they take their grain and fodder? How can you conceal your tracks? One day, the authorities will impeach you. As military commissioner, I would not escape execution—so let me be beheaded before your horses first. Otherwise, I will not lead these troops to follow you.' Ashamed and fearful, the two turned their reins and returned. Suddenly, the raiders surrounded the city, and Ji again led his men in defense. After several days, the raiders withdrew, and the fort commander and supervisor were both promoted for preserving the city. Were their rewards not unrighteous?

Alas! Though Ji himself did not receive a reward, he later rose to the rank of brigade commander, all three of his sons were appointed to office, and one daughter married Guo Kui, becoming a commandery lady. This was far better than the two men’s unrighteous promotion by a single rank.

In praise it is said:
To gain high rank through unrighteousness—
this is a warning the Sage left us."

"To enfeoff the unrighteous as marquises—this was Emperor Guangwu’s failure of governance. To reward bandits is to encourage treachery; how then can the state enforce its decrees? When titles are bestowed upon the wicked and the virtueless, it is a profanation of Heaven’s Mandate. Thus did the Grand Steward instruct the king in the Eight Powers: punishment must reach the innocent.

As the tradition states: 'Punishment exists to chastise evil.' Yet when no evil exists and punishment is still meted out, this is what the Most High calls innocence. Whispers among men are heard in Heaven like thunder. If the innocent cry out to Heaven, will Heaven not hear?

In Caizhou there lived a man named Qian Ju, who falsely accused his younger brother of being of another surname and concealing over two hundred thousand in family wealth, refusing to divide it equally. The officials before and after all accepted Ju’s bribes and rendered unjust verdicts. Only the witness Zhu Xijie maintained a consistent account throughout, refusing to curry favor or submit. Later, when Xu Yi, the Vice Prefect of Xuzhou, was dispatched to Caizhou to establish a prison and investigate the case at its root, Yi again acted with recklessness. Manipulated by the interrogator Yang Zhonghe, he instead had Zhu Xijie sentenced to thirteen strokes of the heavy rod on the buttocks.

From that time on, Yi’s every action was obstructed in review and went unapproved. Hearing that at the Tianqing Abbey there was a Daoist named Pei Junsou skilled in memorializing the throne, he prepared incense and offerings and went to the abbey to perform three successive jiao rites, praying for auspicious favor. At first, Junsou prostrated himself to submit the petition and immediately fell into a snoring slumber. The next day, he rose like a dragon and said:

'I arrived at the Gate of Heaven and saw the Great Emperor of the North Pole leading the True Lord of the Hall of Blessing and Judgment of Good and Evil, the Perfected Warrior, to present a memorial. They stated: "The Vice Prefect in Caizhou was wrong to unjustly sentence the witness Zhu Xijie to thirteen strokes of the heavy rod on the buttocks." Then, glaring fiercely, they commanded: "Xu Yi’s petition is to be kept here. Should the Register of the Golden Script demand an accounting, your response must not err, or you will not be permitted to return." I then hastened back—such is the matter.'"

Xu Yi said: "So it was." With these words, his body drenched in sweat, he could not bear the grief and despair. Not long after, he indeed passed away.

There was also a scholar named Yang Zhiqi who went out at dawn to check the examination results. His wife, at home, had hired a mule to ride to the Water Gate to welcome her parents. Before long, on the road, she encountered a drunkard who, recognizing her, assaulted her. The muleteer fled in secret, and with no one to intervene, the matter was brought before the authorities. At that time, Wen Zhongshu was serving as the magistrate of Kaifeng. Seeing only the scratch marks on the drunkard’s face, he did not inquire into right or wrong but issued a uniform judgment. The woman, deeply ashamed, had gone several days without eating. Her husband, having failed the examination, returned from outside to find his wife beaten with the rod. Overcome with fury, he appealed to the prefectural court, but Zhongshu refused to listen. Thereupon, the couple drowned themselves together.

That very night, Zhongshu dreamed that the City God’s office reprimanded him: "You were far too reckless, rashly sentencing innocent people. I have already reported this to the Eastern Peak, and upon memorial to the Supreme Emperor, disaster will soon befall you." The next day, the emperor learned of the matter, and Zhongshu and his subordinates were all dismissed from office. Yet what of the retribution from the netherworld that awaits him?

In praise, it is said:

Should the offense be minor, pardon it; where guilt is doubtful, impose the lightest penalty. Such was the Sage’s intent: that punishment should aim to eliminate punishment itself. How much more then should the innocent be met with compassion? Yet when the prison gates swing open, the roots are torn up and the measuring lines drawn taut. With wrongful cages overflowing, can one not be sternly chastised?

To kill a man for his wealth—
As the saying goes: "Bandits force their victims into peril; physicians exploit their patients’ vulnerability. Both kill for gain, and in this their crimes are one and the same."

Let us speak of physicians, that the sick may not fall prey to poisonous hands.
To disregard urgency and demand exorbitant fees is to kill.
To insist on payment regardless of means is to kill.
To be lazy or drowsy, treating life with contempt, is to kill.
To diagnose carelessly and prescribe mistakenly is to kill.
To delay treatment until the illness worsens is to kill.
These five failings, when coupled with medical skill, make the physician’s killing more heinous than wielding a blade. Beneath the blade, if the strike misses vital points, life may yet be spared; but beneath the bowl, when the five organs are ruined, is there any hope of survival?

Long ago, Yan Ji sought treatment for illness and lost his life. Later, he revived, his breath faint and indistinct, neither dead nor alive, for thirteen years. Chen Jingren’s wife, Zhang-shi, suffered a minor ailment; the physician mistakenly administered a blood-separating drug, and she did not rise again. After death, the underworld refused her, her soul adrift and scattered. Wherever she wandered, she was driven away by the local earth gods, enduring unbearable suffering. One day, as Jingren traveled beyond the city, he chanced upon her. Their essences merged, and from then on, Jingren grew disoriented, singing and muttering to himself. At that time, his father was serving in the capital when the imperial envoy Qin Zhongli passed through his post. Zhongli, a devotee of Zhenwu, reported the matter to the Northern Dipper. Moved by the unjust death, Zhenwu, the Perfected Lord, immediately granted rebirth without passing through the underworld. Who can say physicians do not kill?

Alas, they know not that medicine is a craft where ascent and descent come swiftest. In a past life, the Venerable Bakkula offered a single harītakī fruit to a sick monk. By this merit, for ninety kalpas he was born in heaven and among men, never falling ill. In his final life, he met the Buddha, left the household, and attained arhatship, becoming the foremost among five hundred. Wang Sou made his living with acupuncture, never counting his fees, and his treatments were often effective. One day, he told the assembly: "Next summer, I cannot remain." When the time came, he departed, and the place where he lived was filled with fragrance for months. Liu Jin also practiced medicine, tireless in his labor, and his cures were many. On the day of his burial, only an empty coffin was interred. These physicians, by applying their hearts with goodness, swiftly attained liberation from the world.

In our dynasty, Ding Fengji was skilled in medicine; his son Du and a second son both passed the imperial examinations, rising to the rank of Grand Scholar of the Hall of Literary Glory. Zhang Zhonghe applied the methods of Zhang Zhongjing to treat cold damage disorders. His two sons, Qi and Zhao, both succeeded in the examinations. Zhang Xingfu also practiced medicine; his descendants have governed great provinces and held high office for generations. These too are physicians who, by applying their hearts with goodness, ensured the flourishing of their posterity.

The Bodhisattva Sincere Appearance once visited a hell where a sinner endured molten iron poured over his body and iron nails driven into his flesh. Bowing, he asked what crime had led to this. The Buddha replied: "In a past life, this man was an acupuncturist who recklessly applied needles, and thus he receives this retribution."

The Venerable Maudgalyāyana, leaving the city one morning, saw a hungry ghost weeping. It said: "This body of mine is like a lump of flesh, without hands, feet, eyes, ears, or nose. Birds and insects have long fed upon it. What crime brought this upon me?" Maudgalyāyana replied: "In a past life, you practiced medicine without mastering your craft, recklessly administering drugs, causing patients to die. This is your retribution."

The divine nun Hui Pu of Caizhou was once sitting with guests when two oxen were led past the hall. The nun said: "The ox leading the way was once an official who condemned an innocent man. The one following was a physician who killed in error." She called out their past-life names, and both oxen responded. These too are physicians who, by failing to apply their hearts with goodness, fell into the three evil destinies.

Alas! The deeds of good and ill are fully recorded above. Whether to follow or defy them—you must choose for yourself.

In praise, it is said:

The treasury of grievance, the root of profit’s strife—
hoarded yet never dispersed, still it brings ruin to men.
To slay for wealth, yet seek to preserve one’s life—
how many greedy clerks devour the rich and noble!
Confiscation, death, exile—does Heaven doubt their guilt?
To topple others for rank and title.

The *Scripture* says: The Primordial August Lord declared: Consorts, empresses, and ministers, though bound to the Purple Tenuity Enclosure, hold rank only by attachment to the Five Peaks and Four Waterways. In general, every office and post bears its own *yiming* (rightful destiny). Where *yiming* is ordained, one may rise today to the Bureau of Military Affairs, tomorrow to the Grand Councillorship—nothing difficult in this. But where *yiming* is absent, not even a single day’s tenure may be secured. Who would say otherwise?

Long ago, Su Yijian first served in the Hanlin Academy, while Li Hang entered later. Soon after, Li was appointed Vice Minister, and Su grew displeased. He presented ten *Steps of Emptiness* hymns to express his intent to the throne. That very day, he was made a Grand Councilor. Yet before many days had passed after his appointment, he died. Xiahou Jiazhen won favor for his literary skill and was named Right Remonstrator, Gentleman of the Historiography Institute, and concurrent member of the Secret Pavilion. Still unsatisfied, he said: “I delight in writing and love alchemy. If I could have but one silver coin from mercury-silver and one day as a Drafter of Edicts, it would suffice.” The emperor heard and granted his wish, but the edict had not yet been issued when he died. Li Jining, a man of talent and ability, served as Academician of the Bureau of Military Affairs. During an audience with Emperor Taizong, he was promised great advancement. Returning home, he told his brothers, overjoyed and drunk—then suddenly struck by vertigo, he died. Li Juyuan, acting Censor-in-Chief, was esteemed by Taizong, who once praised him to his face with intent to promote him. But by chance, while investigating a case, he was demoted to Prefect of Langzhou. One day, an urgent dispatch arrived at the post station, ordering him to return to the capital by post-horse. Juyuan opened the letter, overjoyed—then laughed and expired. Was this not a case of *yiming* being absent, where not even a single day’s tenure could be secured? How much less, then, for those who topple others to seize their positions?

Should one topple another to seize their position, they cannot escape a chain of karmic consequence—as seen in the cases of Mi Dechao and Lu Duoxun, whose downfall swiftly followed their rise. Formerly, Dechao observed that Minister of Military Affairs Cao Bin enjoyed the highest esteem and favor from the emperor. He slandered Bin with accusations of sedition, sparing no effort to ruin him. Thus, Bin was dismissed from his post, and Dechao was appointed in his stead. Yet within days, Zhao Pu returned to power and vigorously cleared Bin’s name. The emperor, realizing his error, banished Dechao that very day, while restoring Bin to his former standing.

Lu Duoxun, seeing that Chancellor Zhao Pu held unmatched authority and prestige, incited Wang You to aid him in undermining Pu. When You refused, Duoxun devised his own scheme, implicating Pu in secret misdeeds. Thus, Pu was stripped of his chancellorship, and Duoxun took his place. But when the truth came to light, Zhao Pu was reinstated, and Duoxun was exiled to Zhuya. If this is the fate of those who seize office by toppling others, should the impatient and ambitious not take heed?

**Eulogy:**
The work of governance is Heaven’s charge;
Position must match virtue’s weight.
Yet petty men, in shallow haste,
Force fate and strive with all their might.
They mar the noble Way in vain,
Gaining naught for their virtue’s blight.
Like mantises stalking cicadas,
Or yellow sparrows flapping in flight—
Heaven’s Way turns full circle,
And all shall see my warning’s light:
Punishment descends, bonds are severed.

**It is said:**
The highest principle declares:
Weapons are inauspicious tools,
Not instruments for noble men.
Only when no choice remains are they employed.
Those who delight in them delight in slaughter,
And cannot prevail beneath Heaven’s dome.
Thus, in auspicious rites, the left is honored;
In ill omens, the right takes precedence.
The Deputy General stands to the left,
The Supreme Commander to the right.
When the slain are many, mourn with tears of grief.
Even in victory, observe the rites of mourning.

Moreover, the weapons wielded are governed by stellar palaces:
The blade is called *Great Chamber*, ruled by the Void Star.
The sword is called *Loss and Wound*, ruled by the Horn Star.
The bow is called *Curved Tension*, ruled by the Root Star.
The arrow is called *Hesitant Wanderer*, ruled by the Sparkling Deluder.
The crossbow is called *Distant Gaze*, ruled by the Spread Star.
War is called *Great General*, ruled by the Triaster.

If these instruments may not be misused,
How can men delight in slaughter?
Has no one heard of Wang Xian’s fate?
Wang Xian, as *Envoy-Chancellor*, governed Dingzhou...

One day, a Daoist priest appeared—his hat torn, his robe threadbare, his beard bristling like a boar’s tusks, his grin stretching from ear to ear. He announced himself as the abbot of Fengdu Temple and presented a calling card, saying: "Yesterday, a decree arrived from the Celestial Court. Twenty thousand barbarian souls have been sent here, but this temple dares not yet enter them into the register of the dead. If you, sir, truly insist on slaughtering them, your name will resound through the ages, but you will forfeit ten years of your lifespan. Choose between the two." The general dismissed him as a madman and drove him away.

Shortly after, the Khitans led their troops to hunt at Liangmen. A sudden snowstorm rendered their bows useless. Recalling the thousands of bow and crossbow materials stored in his armory, Wang Xian ordered craftsmen to work through the night, shaping them into weapons. Seizing the enemy’s unpreparedness, he unleashed his troops, routing the Khitan forces. The next day, he erected a mound of the slain and counted exactly twenty thousand heads. Only then did he grasp the Daoist’s warning—too late to undo what was done.

When the victory was reported to the court, the emperor rejoiced and summoned him to the Bureau of Military Affairs. Yet Wang Xian left Dingzhou only thirteen days later—and died. Alas! These were souls fated for death, yet even for them, his insistence on slaughter cost him his years. How much graver, then, the sin of executing those who have already surrendered and submitted?

When Cai Juhou governed Yunzhou, five hundred bandits from Liangshan Marsh came to surrender. He had them all executed. The following year, while serving as Vice Minister of War on a ritual mission to Jinling, a carbuncle erupted on his back. He ordered Daoist priests to perform a grand offering to atone, instructing his confidant Wang Gong to compose the prayer on his behalf. The next day, Cai Juhou died; the day after, Wang Gong followed.

Later, Wang Gong’s spirit returned, saying: "When I arrived in the underworld, the magistrate rebuked me: ‘You, a scholar, dared to falsely compose a prayer, deceiving the Celestial Court!’ I replied, ‘It was Cai Juhou’s intent; I merely wrote the words.’ Soon, several ghosts dragged Cai out, his neck shackled in a chain of cangues, his body emaciated to the bone. Then two more ghosts appeared, holding a bucket of blood. They poured it over his head, and with each dousing, he shrieked in agony, thrashing as if on the verge of death. When he revived, they poured again; when he fainted, they revived him—repeating this four times in quick succession. From afar, he called to me: ‘Go back and tell my wife to save me at once! Here, I am only being made to account for the Yunzhou affair.’"

Can the execution of the surrendered and submissive ever be justified?

**Eulogy:**
The penal code debates punishments,
Yet even homicide in battle is deemed light.
To repel foes and take heads—this is called *just warfare*.
When no other course remains, the unseen realm may pardon the act.
But for those who have surrendered—why resort to arms or law?
To kill the bound and helpless—heaven’s way is ever watchful.

The *Annals* say: "Only with a Bo Le does the thousand-mile steed appear."
Yet thousand-mile steeds are ever present—Bo Le is not.
So too with men of talent: their fortunes depend on encountering the right patron.
If those in power were like Bo Le,
Then men of virtue and capacity could become prime ministers;
Those who grasp the greater good could guard the realm;
Men of strategy could command armies;
Those skilled in pacification could govern frontiers;
Men of resolve could oversee surveillance;
The incorruptible could manage finances;
And those who cherish the people could serve as prefects and magistrates.
Thus, every office, great or small, would be filled by the right man,
No talent left untapped, and all affairs would flourish.

Yet the truly upright and worthy must be given authority,
So that the court may be well-staffed, officials within and without may stand in awe,
And none dare entertain reckless ambitions.
How could such men be demoted or sidelined?
To push them aside is to remove obstacles—clearing the way for one’s desires.

Thus, those who delight in discerning and promoting talent
Are ever embraced by the Highest Dao, never overlooked.
But those who recklessly demote or exclude others
Are ever met with the Highest Dao’s stern retribution.

Long ago, Han Chong lifted Yuan An from a mere scribe to the rank of Minister over the Masses.
All praised Chong for his discernment, likening him to a radiant light in darkest night.
Yuan An’s upright worth was thus plain to see,
And Chong, too, was recognized by the Highest Dao for this.
He was commanded by the immortal official Wang Weixuan to transmit the *Mud-Pill and Purple Portal* method,
And so attained transcendence, ascending to Fengdu,
Where he now serves as Left Supervisor of the Registry of Determined Transcendents,
Overseeing those who first embark on the Daoist path.

In our dynasty, the Remonstrance Grand Master Xie Bi was most discerning,
Never offering praise lightly.
In his lifetime, he recommended only a few men—all of whom became prime ministers.

Each time he submitted a recommendation, he would burn incense, face the palace, and bow twice, saying: "This old minister has once more found a man for Your Majesty." Wen Zheng and Wang Dan were both among his nominees. On the day of his death, he bathed, burned incense, donned his feathered robe, sat upright, and passed away. His head did not tilt in the slightest—was this not a case of *shijie*? Such was the joy he took in discerning and elevating others, and thus was he recorded by the Highest Dao.

During the reign of Chen Houzhu, there was a favored courtier named Sima Shen who delighted in framing others. One day, while napping in the Ministry of State Affairs, a hideous bird suddenly alighted upon him, pecking at his mouth until blood soaked the mat. All pointed to this as retribution for his false accusations.

In the time of Emperor Shizong of Zhou, there was a man named Tao Gu who likewise took pleasure in slander. The execution of Li Song’s clan and the dismissal of Yu Chongliang were both the result of his single word. When he died, he was buried behind Zhaojue Temple. The tomb’s gate repeatedly shut only to reopen, so the temple monks sealed it with solid stone.

One night, a great thunderstorm arose, and the tomb’s gate yawned open once more—yet even the skull within had vanished. Was this not the profound punishment of the Highest Dao for wantonly disparaging and expelling others? Who could claim that slandering the upright and driving out the worthy bears no guilt?

**Eulogy:**
The worthy and the true,
embody the state and cherish the people.
If employed in the world,
they shelter all beneath heaven.
The harm of slander and expulsion
extends beyond the self.
The state’s woes go unheeded,
the people’s grievances unaddressed.
A calamity vast indeed—
its guilt is equal in measure.

*End of Scroll Nine of the Highest Dao’s Responses to Stimuli*
*Scroll Ten of the Highest Dao’s Responses to Stimuli*

**Biography of Li Changling, with Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

**Oppressing the orphaned, coercing the widowed.**
The *Scripture* states: "Pitying the orphaned and aiding the widowed" has already been established as the mark of a good person. Now, "oppressing the orphaned and coercing the widowed" is likewise defined as a transgression. Can the orphaned then be oppressed, and the widowed coerced?

Alas for those who, though they have attained deliverance from the world, still do not forget to care for the orphaned and widowed—how much more should those who have not yet attained such deliverance do so?

In former times, Master Shi Guangzu, an official of Zhen Ding in his early years, was passing through Tanzhou when he heard of a Daoist at Yuantong Monastery surnamed Guo, who excelled at growing vegetables. His produce was always fresh and abundant, supplying all who came. Guangzu went to visit him. The Daoist had long been mute, but upon seeing the master arrive, he suddenly spoke with clarity, imparting secret instructions. Thereupon, Guangzu resigned his post, bathed, lay down in peace, and passed away. The Daoist, too, vanished without a trace.

That very night, the Emperor in the Chongzheng Hall seemed to see Guangzu arrive riding upon the void. Guangzu presented himself and said: "Your servant was once Vice Staff Officer under the True Martial Lord of the Northern Culmen, in the Protector Saint’s Abbey. As for the Daoist Guo, he was the Inspector of Laws under the Northern Culmen. Both of us were banished to the human realm for minor transgressions, and now our allotted time has come—we are to return. Yet mindful of the grace of past emoluments and a small request to make, I have come especially to express my gratitude. My son, Tingzhi, has not yet sought audience on court affairs; I humbly beg Your Majesty to bestow instruction, that he may not hold office in vain and thereby tarnish his father’s reputation." With these words, he vanished.

A month later, a memorial arrived from Tanzhou, confirming that Guangzu had indeed departed this world.

It was further reported: There was once a scholar from Langzhou who, having failed the examinations, lingered long in the capital. While wandering Mount Bailiang, he was suddenly seized by two azure-clad attendants at the Cave of Celestial Longevity. They revealed to him the Jade Emperor’s golden register and led him to the Northern Culmen beneath the Central Heaven, where he was vested in robes of gold and purple and appointed as Vice Recorder of Life and Death, Merit and Demerit, Longevity and Brevity under the True Martial Lord of the Protector Saint’s Abbey. One day, as he accompanied the True Lord on his descent, he suddenly heard weeping. Looking down, he saw that it was his wife.

The Vice Recorder entreated the True Lord to allow him to send word to his wife, and the True Lord permitted him to write a letter. Taking an earthly courier’s horn-sealed packet, he dispatched a direct talisman to deliver it to the vice-prefectural office of his home prefecture. When the letter arrived, it could not be opened; only after his wife was summoned did it unfold. Within, he recounted in full his encounter with the True Lord and repeatedly urged her: “Above all, raise our orphaned children and safeguard our household.” Alas! Even those who have already attained liberation from the world remain so deeply attached—how much more so those who have not? Here, indeed, lies the root of ancestral grievances. If this were not the case, why would the Most High have established the Register of Petitions, urging people to submit memorials seeking absolution?

In praise it is said:
The orphaned and widowed, without refuge—
King Wen always placed them first.
Yet even with benevolent governance,
their plight may still not be fully redressed.
Alas for our single lineage,
brothers fallen into ruin.
How could one bear such cruelty,
bereaving those who depend on them?
For such a person,
retribution will surely follow.
Discarding the law to accept bribes—

The *Scripture* states: The Most High declared: “Of all matters of right and wrong, weight and lightness, why begin with discarding the law to accept bribes?” Because to discard the law is necessarily to accept bribes; once bribes enter, right is made wrong and wrong is made right—all else is disregarded. How pitiful that they do not know: for every law and statute, the spirits and gods are ever aware. To discard the law and accept bribes is to invite the stripping of rank and emolument.

Long ago, when Fan Zhiquality was yet obscure, he sat in a teahouse in Fengqiu, idly inscribing a fan with the lines:
*“In the scorching heat, the cruel official departs;
the cool breeze brings an old friend.”*
Suddenly, a grotesque stranger stepped forward and bowed, saying: “The suffering of those wronged by cruel officials is far worse than the scorching heat. One day, you must thoroughly investigate this abuse.” With that, he took the fan and left. Fan was left bewildered for a long while. Days later, passing a temple, he saw a clay-and-wood statue of a ghost at the gate—its appearance strikingly like the man he had met—and the fan was in its hand. Greatly astonished, when Fan later rose to high office, he first proposed revising the overly broad and baseless laws, where officials exploited loopholes for malfeasance. The Zhou Ancestor thus commanded him to compile and standardize them, creating the *Criminal Statutes*. Who can say the spirits and gods do not oversee all?

When Hou Jian served as magistrate of Jiangxia, he had long been acquainted with Abbot Juyue of Shengyuan Mountain. Whenever he found leisure, he would visit the abbot, and upon arrival, the monastery would always have prepared for his reception. One day, he came again, only to be received with unusual neglect. Startled, Hou questioned the abbot, who replied: “Whenever you visit, the local earth god always reports in advance, saying, ‘The Minister is coming.’ This time, he did not report, so we failed to prepare.” Hou was deeply alarmed and secretly instructed the abbot to ask the earth god why he had not reported. That night, the abbot dreamed that the earth god said: “Hou Jian was destined to become a grand councilor, and as such, he fell under my jurisdiction, which is why I always reported his coming. Recently, however, he accepted sixty taels of silver from the Hu family and wrongly decided a case. The Heavenly Bureau has already stricken his name from the register of grand councilors, leaving him only the post of provincial inspector. Since he no longer falls under my jurisdiction, I no longer report his visits.” If this is so, can it be said that his rank and emolument were not diminished? How pitiful that he did not know: had he not discarded the law, he would have attained liberation from the world.

In former times, Wang Changyu, originally a judicial officer in Tongchuan, never dared to abandon the law or accept bribes. In the end, the Supreme Emperor took him into his register, and he ascended to heaven in broad daylight, becoming the Perfected of Preserving Harmony. Wang Laozi, originally a judicial officer in Pu Prefecture, likewise never dared to abandon the law or accept bribes. He eventually encountered Master Zhongli, who transmitted to him the essentials of the Dao, enabling him to foreknow fortune and misfortune. During the Huizong Emperor’s reign, he was summoned to court and granted the title "Master of Penetrating the Subtle." Alas! With such a heart, how could one not unite with the Dao above? If even petty officials can do this, how can scholar-officials fail to do so?

**Eulogy:**
Refusing the horses of Lu, his avoidance of summons is still recalled.
Trembling before the "four who know," he escaped entanglement.
When material desires shift, emotions and conduct must change.
If bribes are accepted, even Tang the Completer would shun them.
Those who wish to uphold the law must shun even the appearance of melons and plums.
To twist the straight into the crooked...

It is recorded: When Lu Chong knew of the descent of the Perfected Lord of Assisting Sageliness in Fengxiang, he arranged a ritual and inquired about the essentials of conducting oneself and acting in the world. The Perfected Lord said: "Exert yourself in serving your lord, do not covet ill-gotten wealth, do not meddle in improper affairs, rectify and redress judicial cases, succor and heal the people. If your actions accord with the kingly Dao, you will naturally be a fortunate person. Accumulated transgressions become calamities, and one will surely sink into ruin. How then can those who hold judicial office fail to devote themselves wholeheartedly to rectifying and redressing wrongs? How dare they twist the straight into the crooked? Let me speak of one or two great elders, that all may know and emulate them.

In the past, when Lord Han of Wei governed Daming, litigation in Wei was most numerous. Matters great and small, he personally adjudicated all. Even when ill, he allowed reports to reach him and decided cases from his sickbed. Some, deeming his toil excessive, urged him to delegate to his subordinates. He said: 'When two parties are in litigation before the court, it is a matter of great consequence for the people. Life or death, bestowal or deprivation—all hinge on my single word. How can I treat this lightly?' After his death, he became a Perfected of the Purple Bureau.

Ouyang Wenzhong, the great Confucian of his time, whenever he conversed with guests, never spoke of literature but only of governance. Zhang Shunmin, finding this strange, asked him why. He replied: 'Literature merely adorns the self, but governance can reach others.' When I was demoted to Yiling in my prime, still unsatisfied with my learning, I wished to read the *Records of the Grand Historian* and the *Book of Han*. Neither public nor private collections had them, so I took the archived case files of bygone years and perused them one by one. Among them, right and wrong were often confused: what was not became what was, the crooked was made straight, laws were violated for personal favor, kinship was severed, and moral teachings were harmed—nothing was not present. I often sighed with joy, saying: 'Even in a small and remote place like Yiling, things are like this. The state of the realm can well be imagined.' Thereupon, I swore to Heaven: 'From now on, in all matters, I will be even more diligent and careful, never daring to be the least bit negligent.' To this day, over thirty years later, whether serving within the court or beyond, I have humbly held the three offices, conducting myself by this principle. Others may esteem me as a master of literature and governance, but in my own view, this is merely the reward for the oath I made in those days." After his death, he became the Transcendent Official of Divine Clarity.

If those who twist the straight into the crooked meet such ends after death, what fate awaits them?

**Eulogy:**
Walking the straight path, impartial and unaligned—
This is the good person, worthy of esteem and reward.
To slander them as crooked is to punish what should be praised.
What crime have the good committed to suffer such wrong?
After executing Wang Chang, where could Hong and Xian flee?

To twist the crooked into the straight...

It is recorded: Some cases are straight yet cannot prove themselves so; some principles are crooked yet their crookedness is not self-evident. This is why litigation is so contentious, awaiting officials to decide. Those who serve as officials must be impartial in heart. If they analyze even a single word, then the crooked and the straight will all become as clear as melting ice. Even if the case is tangled, it will surely be resolved as if splitting bamboo. How then could there be the error of twisting the crooked into the straight? Those who do so are simply unable to be impartial, and must be yielding to influence.

In former times, Lord Liu of Yizhou Prefecture, when first serving as the constable of Lianjiang, encountered a case where villagers had disputed over fields for ten years without resolution. The prefecture entrusted it to him, and he uncovered the deceit, immediately rendering a decision. Right and wrong were thus distinguished. People called him divine, but he was not divine—he was merely impartial in heart. When he left office, the party who had been in the right waited for him in Jian Prefecture. Drawing him aside, he said: 'I have some fine incense, a few catties, as a small gift to honor you.' When opened, it was gold. Lord Liu laughed and declined, saying: 'Your case was just; I did not favor you privately. How dare I accept a private bribe for public duty?' He firmly refused, and all at the time admired him. Later, his two sons, Yuanfu and Gongfu, both gained renown for their literary talents. Who can say that serving in office with an impartial heart brings no reward?

There was also Liu Anmin, originally a clerk in Danleng County. He maintained a fair and upright heart, long respected by both officials and people. When villagers had disputes, they did not immediately go to the county court but first sought him out, laying out the right and wrong, asking whether to proceed. Only then would they act. He also took pride in this, accepting their requests without refusal. If a case was just, he affirmed its justice, saying: 'The principle is straight; it can proceed.' If it was crooked, he forthrightly enumerated its faults, saying: 'This is crooked; it does not accord with the law. Proceeding will surely lead to error.' As a result, litigation in the county sharply decreased. Later, his two sons both passed the imperial examinations in succession. The elder, Ji, rose to the rank of Grand Master for Palace Attendance. The younger, Shi, became Gentleman for Court Discussion, Rectifier of the Imperial Archives, and Judicial Intendant of Zizhou Circuit. One daughter married Yang Jun and also held office. To this day, they have become a prominent family. Who can say that a clerk’s impartial heart brings no reward?

In general, whether crooked or straight, all receive blessings;
But those who twist the straight into the crooked or the crooked into the straight all incur misfortune.
Beware!

Praise says:
Crookedness defies principle, becoming flattery or depravity.
Such are called evil men—how can they be concealed?
To reward them as if upright is to gnaw at the state and ruin the family.
How fortunate are evil men to receive such favor and praise!
When Deyu was exiled, was it not [Li] Minzhong’s doing?
To elevate the light and make it heavy.

The *Records* states: The *Book [of Documents]* says: "In doubtful cases of guilt, incline toward leniency."
The *Analects* says: "If you grasp the truth of the matter, then pity them and do not rejoice."
These are the great intentions of the sages in their compassion for those subject to punishment.
Yet can the light be elevated and made heavy?
Thus the noble person is cautious of this.

Formerly, Wang Jin served as Judicial Intendant of the Two Zhe Circuits. Whenever he sentenced a prisoner to death, he would first burn incense and memorialize Heaven before issuing the decree. One evening as he sat in his chambers, he suddenly saw a divine being in a cloud-patterned cap and crimson robes, holding several scrolls of official documents. Beside him stood a jade maiden in long robes and wide sleeves, holding a corner of an official document. They stood beneath the eaves and called out to Jin from afar: "These are the cases you have memorialized in your life—each one accords with principle and law, without any injustice or excess. The August Emperor commends you and has already extended your father’s lifespan by twelve years, while doubling your own blessings for the same span. Your two grandsons will also one day serve as circuit intendants—strive even harder." With these words, they vanished.

Li Guixiang also held a judicial post and was long known for his integrity and prudence. One evening, as he passed over Sanjing Bridge, he suddenly saw a dozen or so people crying out in grievance and protest, approaching in twos and threes until they pressed close. Guixiang was greatly alarmed; he wheeled his horse around and galloped home, where he collapsed in exhaustion. Summoning his sons, he warned them: "If any of you enter officialdom in the future, never accept an appointment as a prison or judicial officer. Though I have lived my life with integrity and caution, who could have known such things would still befall me today? From this distress, I will never recover." And so he fell into deep melancholy and ultimately did not rise again.

Alas! Both gentlemen were men of renown in their time—yet while Lord Wang met such a fate, Lord Li’s was even more dire. Could it be that Lord Li still harbored some lapse in prudence? How much worse, then, for those who treat light matters as heavy?

Let me recount a few more cases, that all may take heed.

In former times, Ouyang Yu served as a judge in the Court of Judicial Review. There was a magistrate of Liyang County named Yu Shaoqing, imprisoned on charges that did not warrant death, yet Yu condemned him to execution. On the day the sentence was carried out, all deemed it a grievous wrong. From that moment, whether walking, standing, sitting, or lying down, Yu saw Shaoqing following him relentlessly, never leaving his thoughts, never granting him peace. In desperation, he journeyed to Mount Lu to seek the Nine Heavens Investigating Commissioner, where he arranged a three-day Yellow Altar ritual to atone for his deed. At the first night watch, he already saw Shaoqing at his side, filling him with dread. The next morning, he submitted another written confession, and just as he bowed in submission, he was suddenly seized by several ghosts, hurled before the altar steps, his body drenched in blood. After some time, he died.

When Li Ruoshui served as Judicial Commissioner of Huainan, five bandits were captured and imprisoned after a failed robbery. They claimed to have conspired with a monk named Zicheng. Though the five were soon executed, the monk was only then being pursued. The monk protested his innocence, insisting he had never been a thief. Ruoshui could have shown leniency in this matter, yet he stubbornly upheld the bandits’ testimony as truth. That night, he had the monk’s mouth and nose sealed with damp paper, then pressed a sack of earth upon him. In an instant, the monk’s belly split open, and he died.

A month later, the prison officer Li Neng suddenly cried out for no reason: “The monk had nothing to do with me—it was the Judicial Commissioner who forced me to do this!” With those words, he collapsed and died. The next day, the investigator Liu Yuan also died suddenly.

The following day, Ruoshui also suffered excruciating pain in his lower abdomen, shrieking in agony before he died. The day after, the military prefect Sun Yu and the judicial officer Zhao Yu likewise died suddenly. Before long, Ruoshui’s entire household perished, nearly extinguished without a single survivor left. Alas, Ruoshui’s calamity was truly cruel. From the moment of Zicheng’s death—his navel and belly torn asunder—was this not cruelty? This must have been the wrath of the August Heaven.

**Eulogy:**
The law weighs heavily upon wrongful convictions,
and fear of guilt extends even to doubt.
When two offenses are weighed together,
the measure of mercy may be known.
If even the statutes of the state are thus,
what more the legal officers?
The Tang ministers wove false charges,
and the extermination of their clans was just.
Long-nurtured disaster leaves a stench,
yet what benefit is there in dying thus?
To meet killing with rage—

**It is said:** According to the *Nirvana Sutra*, the dead in perilous straits
have no provisions;
their destination is distant, with no companions;
they wander day and night, knowing no bounds;
through deep, dark abysses, with no lamp to light the way;
entering places without doors, with no refuge;
though no pain afflicts them, it cannot be healed;
they journey without hindrance, yet cannot escape.
Alas, when a person comes to this,
it is truly pitiable.
When witnessing killing, how can one respond with anger?

In the days when the Venerable Zhigong was in the world, two Daoist monks traveled together. One went to a garden to buy melons, and the gardener grew angry: “The great households have not yet bought any—do you presume to do so?” He refused to sell. When the other monk went, the gardener happily sold to him, even consoling him: “Are you parched from the heat?” After selling one melon, he gave another. The two monks privately marveled and asked Zhigong about it. Zhigong said: “Five hundred lifetimes ago, the gardener was executed for a crime, and you two happened to be nearby. The one who succeeded in buying the melon had once pitied him, and so now rejoices at the sight of him. The one who failed to buy had once spoken words of ‘self-made, self-received,’ and so now meets him with anger. If a single careless word can leave such lasting resentment, how much more so when met with rage?”

Wang Tanlüe once slaughtered an ox. As the ox saw the knife and charged, Tanlüe grew furious and stabbed its eyes before killing it. Generally, there is no distinction between humans and beasts—when witnessing killing, one should find a way to save them. If saving them is impossible, one can only grieve and sigh in sorrow. How can one respond with anger?

**Eulogy:**
When a person bears guilt,
their circumstances may be unforgivable.
Yet if they submit to the law themselves,
what concern is it of mine?
To add fury to the matter
is called misplaced wrath.
When the judge casts down his brush at a death sentence,
those who witness it should awaken.
To pity and commiserate in all legal cases
is the duty of royal governance.

*Tai Shang Ganying Pian*, Scroll Ten—concluded
*Tai Shang Ganying Pian*, Scroll Eleven

**Biography of Li Changling**
*Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi:*
To recognize one’s faults and inevitably correct them.

The *Biography* states: Yanzi never repeated his mistakes, and Confucius praised him for it. "Never repeating" means that the moment a thought arises within, one corrects it at once, never allowing it to manifest in action. Generally, the value of a fault lies in its correction—failing to correct it makes it a true fault indeed. How can such faults be enumerated? Thus, the noble person, upon hearing of their faults, wastes no time in amending them.

In former times, Kou Laigong Zhun, from youth, enjoyed wealth and nobility, indulging in excessive luxury. When he was appointed Minister of Military Affairs, his rewards and gifts grew even more lavish, and his guests and visitors gathered to admire, none failing to praise him. Only an old serving woman wept silently. Startled, he asked the reason, and she replied: "When the late Lady passed away, we could not even obtain a single bolt of silk to make her shroud—how could we have known the Lord would reach such heights today?" Overcome with grief, he struck his chest in sorrow. From that day forth, he humbled himself and embraced frugality, never again daring to indulge in extravagance.

Cai Wenzhong Qi was fond of drinking throughout his life, always drinking to the point of intoxication. At the time, his venerable mother was already advanced in years, her heart heavy with worry. One day, Master Cundao visited his post and admonished him with a poem:

*The sagely lord’s grace runs deep, selecting you for high office;*
*Your kind mother’s years are long, her hair like the crane’s white plumes.*
*Neither the sovereign’s favor nor a mother’s kindness have you repaid—*
*If drink becomes a sickness, what regret could ever suffice?*

Upon receiving the poem, he was deeply ashamed. From then on, he abstained from wine, drinking only with close kin and old friends, and never again became drunk in his lifetime.

When Sima Wengong was but five years old, he tried to peel a green walnut but could not remove its skin. A maidservant poured hot water over it to loosen the husk. Later, his elder sister returned from outside and asked who had peeled it. Wengong replied: "I did it myself."

His father rebuked him from the side, saying: "How dare you, child, speak falsehoods!" From that moment, Wengong took heed and never again dared utter a lie throughout his life, treating all with utmost sincerity.

When Xu Jiexiao first met Master Anding, his head carriage was slightly tilted. Anding said in a stern voice: "The carriage of the head must be upright." Wengong immediately understood: "If the carriage of the head must be upright, how can the heart be allowed the slightest crookedness?" From then on, he never once allowed his heart to deviate, and whenever asked about the essentials of conducting oneself in the world, he would invariably write the two large characters "uprightness" and "integrity" to instruct them.

Were these gentlemen—when faced with their faults—men of understanding who refused to change?

In praise it is said:
The eclipse of sun and moon,
once passed, shines brighter still.
Who among men is without fault?
But to correct it with sincerity—
this is what matters.
To craft excuses, to cloak deceit,
to conceal one’s true feelings,
to pile fault upon fault—
how can the heart of goodness ever arise?
Not to begrudge a single word,
but to act with all one’s strength—
this is what is noble.
To know the good and not do it...

As the *True Person of Responding to the World* said: "To take the small is how one achieves the great; to accumulate the one is how one completes the myriad." The *Book of Changes* states: "Auspicious blessings must return to those who accumulate goodness." Generally speaking, goodness demands accumulation. What one knows must be done, and what one does must be done with all one’s strength. It is like planting a seed: though at first it is exceedingly small, with watering and nurturing, though its growth is not immediately seen, in time it will become great. One must understand that in the course of daily life, one must never regard small acts of goodness as without benefit and thus fail to perform them.

Have you not heard the words of the Primordial Lord? The Primordial Lord said: "When a person has one good deed, the heart is settled and the body at ease; with ten good deeds, the qi and strength are robust; with twenty, all illnesses vanish; with thirty, all that is sought is obtained; with forty, one flourishes in wealth and joy; with fifty, descendants multiply; with sixty, one escapes calamity; with seventy, heavenly spirits protect one; with eighty, one gains the advantages of the earth; with ninety, one is honored and renowned among the worthy; with one hundred, treasured omens descend; with two hundred, one’s name spreads through later generations; with three hundred, later generations become greatly prosperous; with four hundred, later generations attain purity and nobility; with five hundred, later generations enjoy long life; with six hundred, later generations are loyal and filial; with seven hundred, later generations are wise; with eight hundred, later generations embody virtue and power; with nine hundred, later generations become worthy sages; with one thousand, later generations become divine immortals; with two thousand, one becomes a teacher of the multitude, a sage, a perfected immortal, a general or an official; with three thousand, one becomes a national teacher, a sage, a perfected immortal, a general or a scribe; with four thousand, one becomes a teacher of the world, a sage, a perfected immortal who governs the host; with five thousand, one becomes a teacher of the spirits, a sage, a perfected immortal and marshal; with six thousand, one becomes a teacher of the divine luminaries, a sage, a perfected immortal and minister; with seven thousand, one becomes a teacher of the various heavens, a sage, a perfected immortal and high official; with eight thousand, one becomes a teacher of the Three Heavens, a sage, a perfected immortal emperor; with nine thousand, one becomes one of the Five Lords of the Primordial Beginning; with ten thousand, one becomes the Supreme Jade Emperor."

Thus, the ancients, in matters of goodness, knew without fail what must be done and did it without fail with all their strength. Formerly, Chancellor Li Fang once said to others: "Of the phrase in the *Analects*—'to be frugal and love the people, to employ the people at the proper time'—I have practiced it all my life and still have not fully attained it. This is what it means to strive with all one’s strength." Academician Yang Yingzhi, upon hearing a single good word, would invariably write it down and remember it—this is what it means to know without fail what must be done. Jia An, the Hanlin Academician, having passed the imperial examinations as *zhuangyuan*, said that what he gained from [Fan] Wenzheng was the two words "never deceive," which he used throughout his life without exhaustion—this is what it means never to deceive. Liu Anshi, the Grand Censor, renowned for his forthright speech, said that what he gained from Wengong was the two words "utmost sincerity," which he practiced throughout his life without the slightest deviation—this is what it means to be without fail sincere.

If every person were like these gentlemen, then to accumulate the one and complete the myriad—what difficulty would there be?

In praise it is said:

The Lord Guo excelled in approving the good—how then did the state perish?
Though he rejoiced at seeing virtue, his resolve was not firm.
Indolence harms the Dao, and in turn obscures right from wrong.
Thus the noble person sets his mind on self-strengthening,
