Ever feeling the day insufficient, hastening in earnest.
To blame oneself and cite others—
As the tradition says: According to the scriptures, in all the world,
Life and death follow one another in succession.
At the moment of death, before the warmth has left the body,
A lifetime’s good and evil all appear at once.
Generally, the visions that arise at death’s threshold
Are but the landscapes of one’s own heart.
The beads of sin held by Earth Treasury
Are the very pearls of one’s own mind.
The mirror of deeds before King Yama
Is the mirror of one’s own heart.
Moreover, when one commits a single wrong today,
Does it not linger always in the breast?
Was it not so when Zhao Pu, upon hearing the Daoist’s last word—“fire”—on the placard,
Knew at once it must refer to Qin Wang Tingmei?
Or when Wang Shao, upon hearing the words, “Has the eighteen-foot blade passed through your heart?”
Suddenly developed a carbuncle on his back and died?
Then is it truly beneficial to blame oneself and cite others?

Long ago, Zhao Ye passed the imperial examination in the *mingjing* degree
And was appointed magistrate of Qinghua in Bazhou.
Suddenly his spirit wandered, and he fell into a dreamlike stupor,
Only to be seized and taken to the underworld.
Upon arrival, he saw his brother-in-law Jia Yi there,
And his heart filled with dread.
For he and Yi had once slaughtered an ox together,
And now Yi had come to implicate him.
When he sought to share the blame, Yi’s words were sharp,
Leaving him no way to exonerate himself.
Then suddenly a mirror, over a *zhang* wide,
Hung suspended in the air.
In its brightness, he saw Yi’s crime of butchery at the gate,
And the reluctance in his face.
Only then did Yi admit his guilt, and Ye was allowed to return.

Moreover, there was a woman of the Chang clan named Meiniang, whose husband, Chu Chunqing, had a concubine surnamed Ma, named Chunnu. Chunqing doted on her. Unable to contain her rage, Chang seized the opportunity while Chunqing was away and bludgeoned Chunnu to death with a heavy staff. Later, when Chunqing died, Chang remarried a man named Cheng Xuan. As she was about to give birth, the labor lasted three days, and the child could not be delivered. In her exhaustion, she suddenly saw Chunnu standing before her. Terrified, Chang pleaded gently, saying, "I truly had no intention of killing you at first. The beating went too far, and it cost you your life. Could you not show me some leniency?" Chunnu replied, "My death was indeed at my mistress’s hands. How could you falsely implicate another?" She pressed harder, and Chang ultimately did not survive.

Thus, can one truly escape by blaming oneself and citing others? Once you know you are at fault, why not refrain from the deed altogether?

**Eulogy:**
Calamity is self-made, and its consequences are self-inflicted.
With deep remorse and repentance, perhaps it may yet be redeemed.
To ignore one’s own faults and instead implicate others—
Even if one evades the snares of guilt, Heaven cannot be deceived.
Sharing blame and accepting wrongdoing is the foundation of turning toward goodness.
**Obstructing the arts and techniques.**

As the saying goes: "So-called arts and techniques" refer to all who wield skills to seek gain. A single word of discouragement, preventing them from succeeding, is obstruction.

In Baisha Village at Guankou, there stood a Temple of Mount Tai of extraordinary efficacy, where vegetarian offerings and sacrifices were made nearly every day. One day, a group of physicians and diviners traveled together and encountered an extremely poor man on the road, whom they greatly disdained. Enraged, the man threw himself into the river. Moments later, he emerged holding a dragon and said to the group, "Each of you practices medicine or divination to relieve others’ suffering in times of urgency—all are close to the Dao. Henceforth, toward those of humble means, you must never harbor duplicity in your hearts."

Alas! Though medicine and divination are mere arts, the divine and the wise call them "close to the Dao"—can they be obstructed? Have you not heard the words of the Dragon Maiden of Yun’an? Long ago, when Zhai Qianyou was alive, he was renowned for his mastery of summoning and invocation. He noted that in the single district of Yun’an, the Yangtze’s currents were perilous in fifteen places. One day, he performed a ritual to summon the rapids’ spirits and command them to be pacified. That night, fourteen rapids answered his call. Only one failed to appear. The Heavenly Master grew furious and insisted on summoning it. When it finally arrived, it was a woman, wearing a towering headdress and voluminous sleeves, who approached and declared resolutely, "The Heavenly Master carries out Heaven’s transformations—how dare I, a mere water spirit, defy you? Yet there is something unsettled, and I cannot but report it. Your intent is merely to facilitate boats and vessels."

Yet how could you not know that those who ply the boats and vessels daily reap double the profit? Even if there are minor expenses, they are no true loss. Along this stretch of the river live three or four hundred humble folk, with no fields to till, no silk to weave—entirely dependent on hauling and porterage for their livelihood. If all the rapids were smoothed, it would indeed benefit the boats, but what would become of those small folk and their sustenance? The Most High’s intent would surely never permit such an outcome. I fear that in time, the blame would fall upon me, and I would be burdened with guilt—this matter must be reconsidered.”

The Heavenly Master sighed and said, “Your concern is beyond what I had considered.” Thereupon, he commanded the spirits of the fourteen rapids to restore their former dangers. That night, wind and rain roared, thunder crashed, and lightning struck. By morning, the fifteen rapids surged with waves, just as they had before.

From this, we see that not only must the arts of Dao not be obstructed, but all those who labor for their daily bread must likewise not be obstructed. When Master Fan Wenzheng was in western Zhejiang, the two Zhe provinces suffered famine, and the starving lay strewn like kindling. Observing that the people of Wu were fond of Buddhist rites, he summoned the abbots of the temples and instructed them that with grain expensive and labor cheap, construction should proceed everywhere. The government, too, launched major works, repairing granaries, government offices, and the like. Thus, artisans and laborers—those who toiled for public and private patrons—numbered in the thousands daily. The entire region relied on this, avoiding displacement. Was Master Fan’s intent not the same as that of the dragon spirit of Yun’an?

In praise it is said:
The minor arts may be observed,
Yet one must not cling to them.
The learning of the noble one
Must also wander through the arts.
What are called the esoteric arts
Are not abandoned by the sage.
Medicine, divination, astrology, calendrics—
All serve the world.
If I alone obstruct them,
How can I escape blame?
To slander the worthy and wise—
This is the true transgression.

The tradition states: The worthy and sagely are of three kinds. In the teachings of the Buddha, these are the three vehicles; in the teachings of the Dao, they are the five grades of immortals; and in our Confucian tradition, they are the masters Confucius and Mencius. Though their teachings differ, the gateway of the mind-ground is one and the same. Have you not heard what is said in the *Po Xie Lun*: that Confucius was the Bodhisattva Ru Tong, Yan Hui was the Bodhisattva Guang Jing, and Laozi was Mahākāśyapa? Alas, the worthy and sagely are distant indeed—yet to slander their writings is to slander them.

Now, among the scriptures of the Buddha, many are destroyed. Why? Simply because they speak of emptiness. It is a pity that their critics do not understand that the nature of the Tathāgata is like empty space, and the nature of empty space is the Tathāgata. Perfect in true purity and clarity, wondrously empty and spiritually penetrating, that which abides in true purity and clarity, wondrous emptiness and spiritual penetration—this is the Tathāgata. To dwell all day in true purity and clarity, wondrous emptiness and spiritual penetration, yet never truly be pure, clear, empty, or spiritually penetrating—this is the ordinary person.

If one can empty all that exists, leaving it utterly bare, exposed, and pure as empty space, then true purity and clarity, wondrous emptiness and spiritual penetration will manifest clearly, and one will become one body with the Tathāgata, without distinction. Who could say this is not so? Have you not heard the saying of Elder Pang: "Only desire to empty all that exists; do not desire to substantiate what does not exist"? Elder Pang in a former life was Vimalakīrti; Vimalakīrti in a former life was the Tathāgata Golden Grain. His words were certainly not deceitful.

Yet some awaken to this truth and some do not—this is simply because the capacity of each person differs.

Once, Duke Du Qi disbelieved in the Buddha-dharma, often mocking it in the presence of guests, for which Zhang Wending deemed him a flatterer of Buddhism. A physician surnamed Zhu frequented the households of both gentlemen and had long wished to find an opportune moment to exhort Duke Qi, yet without success.

One day, the Duke fell ill and summoned Zhu. Zhu, however, was in the midst of reading the *Śūraṅgama Sūtra* and did not go at once. When he finally arrived, the Duke flew into a rage, demanding, "What manner of book is this *Śūraṅgama*? I know only of Confucius and Mencius!" Zhu drew the first scroll from his sleeve and presented it to him. The Duke read it through, then demanded the remaining scrolls one after another, until—unawares—he had finished all ten fascicles. Sighing deeply, he exclaimed, "Truly, this is a wondrous text!" Thereupon, he dragged Zhu with him to call upon Wending, reproaching him for not having revealed it sooner. Wending merely smiled and said, "It is like losing something and then finding it—what need is there to question why it was found so late?"

From that day forth, the Duke seemed to have gained profound insight and placed great faith in the teachings. There was also Xu Xuan, a renowned scholar who had never before encountered Buddhist scriptures. The Middle Lord bestowed upon him a copy of the *Śūraṅgama Sūtra*, saying, "Read it thoroughly, and you will perceive its subtle principles." After more than ten days, Xu submitted a memorial, stating, "I have read it several times over, yet it is as though pouring from one vessel into another—utterly incomprehensible." The Middle Lord merely smiled at him.

Alas! Duke Qi, with but a single glance, devoured all ten fascicles, while Xu Xuan, after more than ten days, could not grasp even a single word—is this not a matter of differing capacities?

The eulogy says:
Men often speak of shielding their ailments and shunning physicians.
The sagely and worthy set forth teachings that cut to the heart of a hundred wrongs,
Yet those who cannot practice them proceed to slander and ridicule.
Chen Jia criticized Dan, and Shusun defamed Ni—
Words issuing from the mouths of mere youths—why do they not reflect?
Such is the way they assail and undermine the Dao and virtue.

The tradition states: According to the sutras, in ancient times, the youth Sudhana, in his cultivation of the bodhisattva path, diligently sought all bodhisattva teachings. He visited fifty-three great kalyāṇa-mittas, and only then did he attain enlightenment. These kalyāṇa-mittas are great guides—with but a single word, they can shatter the bottom of a lacquer barrel. The benefit they confer is by no means small. What is called the Dao and virtue resides in such persons, and this is not unique to Buddhism or Daoism. Since antiquity, renowned figures have never failed to rely on great kalyāṇa-mittas, who through grinding refinement and stirring encouragement enabled them to shine with brilliance and be celebrated through the ages. Examples include Zhang Liang with the Yellow Stone Elder, Zhuge Liang with Pang Degong, Li Jing with the Curly-Bearded Elder, Yan Zhenqing with Zhang Zhihe, Li Taibai with He Zhizhang, and Du Shaoling with Sima Ziwei. If this is so, can the Dao and virtue be of no benefit to people? Can they be assailed and undermined?

In the past, when Yin Gong Shilu was demoted to serve in Daliang, he associated with the monk Fayan Chan Master. One day, he said to Fayan, "Of late, I have come to value withdrawal and tranquility as the highest attainment." Fayan replied, "Why not forget both withdrawal and tranquility? Then you will gain insight, and at the moment of life and death, you will remain clear and undisturbed." The military commissioner’s judicial officer, Zhu Yan, had long studied Haiyan’s teachings but had yet to grasp their essence. One day, he asked the lecture monk Yijiang, "When this body dies, where does this mind go?" Yijiang replied, "Before this body dies, where is this mind?" Zhu immediately attained sudden clarity and was able to fully comprehend the meaning of the entire sutra. Minister Zeng Kai had long admired Chan but had found no resonance. One day, he visited Yuanwu and sought resolution. Yuanwu took out two cases from the *Gong’an* of Yunmen and Zhaozhou—"Mount Sumeru" and "Set It Down"—and showed them to him. Zeng immediately experienced a breakthrough, and all his lifelong doubts were dispelled. Ouyang Wenzhong did not believe in Buddhism or Daoism, but when he governed the district of Bo, he heard of the reclusive master Xu Changling of Later Tang and invited him to the prefectural residence. They spoke together daily, and with each exchange, he felt a sense of joy and attainment. If this is so, can the Dao and virtue be of no benefit to people?

The eulogy says:

The Dao is revered, virtue is noble—all things under heaven take them as their foundation. Yet envy and indolence breed discontent within. Outwardly, one may display arrogance and dominance, but this only reveals one’s mediocrity. To usurp is rebellion; to be haughty is misfortune. Though the cosmos is vast, one must cherish and restrain the self.

*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Eleven—end
*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Twelve

**Biography of Li Changling**
**Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

**On the Shooting of Flying Beings**

The *Tradition* says: The Supreme One declared: When the primordial chaos divided, Heaven and Earth took their places. The pure *qi* became Heaven, the turbid *qi* became Earth. The essence of yang became the sun, the essence of yin became the moon. The essences of the sun and moon became the stars. Harmonious *qi* became humans, peripheral *qi* became beasts, thin *qi* became birds, and dense *qi* became insects. Species arise from one another, converging to give birth, each following its karmic retribution and conditioned causes.

Yet how do humans differ from flying creatures? As the *Zhao Lun* states: "Heaven and Earth share the same root with me; the myriad things are one body with me"—this is no empty speech. The difference between birds and humans lies solely in their karmic fortune and the form of their bodily shells.

I have examined the records and found that some have fallen from humanity into such forms, while others—immortals—have played among them. Liu Anmin was once a wealthy man who pursued empty fame without true attainment of the Dao. After death, he became a phoenix, and after sixty years, he regained human form. In this life, he diligently cultivated the Dao and finally achieved liberation through *shijie*, now residing in the Qinghua Palace. Huang Anshi was once a scholar who longed for transcendence but lacked single-minded devotion. After death, he became a wild goose, and after ninety years, he regained human form. In this life, he advanced in cultivation and finally ascended, now dwelling in the Joyous Heaven of Huangzeng—are these not cases of humans falling into the realm of birds?

On the ornamental pillars of Liaodong, a crane once alighted. When someone tried to shoot it, the crane spoke: *"There is a bird, there is a bird—Ding Lingwei. / A thousand years away from home, now I return. / Though the city walls remain, the people are gone. / Why not learn the Dao? Graves lie heaped in rows."* Emperor Ming of Tang once shot a crane in Shayuan. Though struck, it spread its wings and flew straight toward the southwest. The following year, when the emperor fled to Shu and visited the Bright Moon Abbey, he saw the arrow lodged in the wall. When he asked where it came from, the Daoist assembly said: "On the Double Ninth last year, the Daoist Xu Zuoqing of Qingcheng brought it here and left it behind." Only then did he realize the crane was none other than Zuoqing—was this not an immortal playing among them?

The eulogy says:

In the age of the Great Dao, men harbored no intent to kill. The nests of crows and magpies could be peered into from above. Once the mind of cunning arose, even gulls took flight. Hawks possess loyalty, and crows are called compassionate. Why then are men so unkind, making sport of the hunt and chasing down prey?

The tradition states: The foregoing has briefly set forth the words of the Most High; now let the teachings of the Buddha serve as proof. The Buddha said: All sentient beings are fundamentally pure. Yet from deluded perceptions, deluded habits arise. From deluded habits, the cycle of birth and death continues. Birth follows habitual tendencies; death follows the flux of change. Those of pure thought ascend, surely reborn in heaven. When thought and emotion are balanced, they neither ascend nor fall, reborn again among men. Clarity of thought brings wisdom; obscurity of emotion brings dullness. When emotion outweighs thought, they flow into errant births—heavily as furred beasts, lightly as feathered flocks. Generally, the seven destinies of rebirth remain inescapable for those who have not entered true cultivation.

Those now born as other kinds were surely once human in past lives; those now human were not necessarily other kinds before. Thus the Buddhas, bodhisattvas, and perfected ones of the Great Dao constantly instruct men to recognize the perilous threshold of birth and death as most fearsome. Have you not heard how the arhat Mātṛceṭa in a past life was a macaque, or how Kālodāyin was once reborn as an ox? Have you not heard that Zhou Xun and Cai Junmo were both serpent spirits? According to the *Dongzhai Record*, when Zhou Xun governed Anzhou, one day while strolling in his garden, the gardener saw a great serpent resting its head upon the railing. Looking closer, it was Zhou Xun in a light slumber. Cai Junmo, governing Fuzhou, fell ill and ceased attending to affairs. Each night, he dreamed of wandering atop the drum tower, leaning upon the drum to sleep. The vice-prefect reproached the drum officer for failing to strike the third watch, who replied that a great serpent coiled upon the drum, making it impossible to approach. When Junmo recovered, he recounted his dreams to the vice-prefect, finding them identical to the drum officer’s account. If the seven destinies of rebirth remain inescapable for those who have not entered true cultivation, how can one avoid them?

Know this: those now chased may one day chase others. To escape the cycle of rebirth and avoid being pursued, when you see others hunted, you must strive to save them. Once, the Prince of Hejian suffered a long illness with no hope of recovery. Suddenly, a Daoist named Xuan Su arrived at his gate, offering to heal him. After administering a knife-tip of medicine, the prince vomited over a dozen small serpents, and his illness was cured. Astonished, the prince asked the reason. Xuan Su replied: "This is the lingering misfortune from six generations past. I am not human. In a former life, you saved a doe, who was in truth the mother of a qilin. Your kindness moved heaven and earth, so the Supreme Emperor sent me down to heal you." If one sees others hunted, can one fail to save them?

Li Ying and Li Yan once chased a deer, binding its four legs and hanging it from a tree branch. They first took its organs to roast, preparing to eat. Suddenly, a giant, nearly ten feet tall, appeared, carrying a massive sack. Striding forward, he nearly reached them. Then, gathering all the roasted meat, he placed it in his sack and departed into the mountains. Soon after, both Ying and Yan died suddenly. If one sees prey running, can one truly chase it?

The eulogy says:

The abandonment of Hou Ji was such that even oxen and sheep would not trample him. The orangutan speaks human words, and the gibbon’s nature is gentle and kind. All living beings possess awareness, and their principles are not distant. How can one bear to fill the cauldron with their flesh, savoring their fat and richness? Tigers and rhinoceroses may be escaped, but the snares set by humans are inescapable. The awakening of hibernating creatures—

The *Records* state: As the *Monthly Ordinances* says: "In the second month of autumn, thunder begins to withdraw its voice, and hibernating insects seal their doors. In the third month of autumn, hibernating insects crouch within, all plastering their doors. In the second month of spring, thunder resounds, and they open their doors to emerge." *Zhe* means "to hide." *Huai* means "to enlarge"—enlarging their doors slightly. *(Jin)* means "to plaster," covering their doors entirely. One hibernation, one emergence—all correspond to the qi and seasons, completing their transformations. If this is so, can one truly disturb their hibernation?

Thus, the Supreme Writ warns humanity, and the Buddhas especially emphasize protection. Long ago, the monk Huijue once crossed a stream to build a Mañjuśrī Hall, intending to lay planks by the water’s edge. He thought: "It is winter, and all creatures are hibernating. One must not lightly harm their lives." Thereupon, he held a three-day ritual by the stream, vowing that on the day of construction, whether creatures had one foot, many feet, or no feet, they might all be sheltered and kept far away. When the time came to dig to the spring and even after the bridge was completed, not a single ant was seen.

Again, in the Song era, an elder’s father fell ill, and he prayed before the Medicine Master Hall at Yongzuo Temple. After his father recovered, he prepared incense and offerings, intending to hold a ritual in the hall to repay the divine favor. The temple monk Yizhao said: "Beneath the Medicine Master’s seat in this hall, there is a swarm of bees that has dwelled for many years, with many kinds among them. Now is their hibernation season. If disturbed, many may be harmed or killed. Would it not be better to move to another hall?" The elder did not heed him. The monk then prayed in advance before the Buddha, saying: "On the day of the feast, may the divine power of compassion shield those within the hive, allowing them to temporarily feed on old honey without leaving their doors. May those outside the hive remain undisturbed, unstartled by any commotion." When the day arrived, lamps and candles blazed, and the clamor of cymbals and conches filled the air. For a day and two nights, not a single bee was seen.

Alas! Though this was the power of these two men’s vows, it was also the protective power of the sages. Who could say otherwise?

A Eulogy states:
A ruler of abundant virtue extends grace even to insects.
When building with rammed earth, one must choose the proper season;
in winter, all things lie in closure.
People seal their doors and windows—
to destroy a nest is no different.
Those that crawl, those that fly, those that breathe—
all share the impulse of life.
Whether tending the sick or stretching limbs,
all lie within the compass of our benevolence.

To startle the roosting—
As the tradition says: "The highest admonish men never to disturb the roosting."
This aligns with Confucius’ teaching: "In archery, do not shoot the roosting bird."
In essence, birds that have settled for the night are like men who have fallen asleep.
A sudden disturbance—would it not rouse the entire household?
Most see a difference between them and us,
yet those of universal heart see no distinction.

Long ago, the Immortal of Spiral Coils,
practicing the fourth dhyāna, entered and exited breath,
sat beneath a tree, utterly still.
A bird, mistaking him for wood, nested in his coiled topknot
and laid several eggs.
When the Immortal of Spiral Coils emerged from meditation,
he sensed the eggs upon his head and thought:
"If I rise now, I will surely startle the bird.
If the bird does not return, the eggs will perish."
Thus, he re-entered samādhi,
waiting until the fledglings hatched and flew away one by one
before finally rising.

There was also the Great Tree Immortal,
dwelling by the Gaṅgā,
who settled his spirit in samādhi for many years,
his form like withered wood.
Wandering birds nested upon him,
depositing seeds of the *nikkura* tree on his shoulder.
As seasons turned, the sapling grew, its branches shading him like clasped hands.
One evening, he suddenly arose from samādhi,
wishing to leave the tree.
Fearing he would startle the birds,
he gently and patiently moved the sapling aside,
and only then did he rise.
Heaven admired his virtue and bestowed upon him the name "Great Tree."
These beings had already attained the fruit of realization,
yet their hearts were thus toward a single creature, a single life.
How much more should those of shallow merit and power do the same?

Alas, people do not know of Li Xizi or Chen Anshi,
who, by daring not to disturb the roosting, attained realization.
Li Xizi was originally an old mountain woman.
Whenever heavy snow fell and birds had no perch,
they would flock to her home and remain.
She fed them grain and dared not startle them.
The Lord of Heaven, deeming her heart benevolent,
granted her transcendence of the world.
Now she resides in Fengdu, her rank and appearance most exalted.

Chen Anshi was originally a laborer in the household of Quan Shuben.
Throughout his life, he never trod upon living insects or took life.
Whenever he went out and saw birds in his path,
he would step aside to avoid them, unwilling to startle them.
For this reason, he too ascended to the heavens.
Know this: those who attain the truth
have never done so without a heart of compassion toward all beings.

A Eulogy states:
Let all that live abide in their place.
Night is for rest—man and creature are equal in this.
The sage’s concern is that not a single being lose its proper order.
"In archery, do not shoot the roosting bird"—
thus the *Analects* records.
Though disturbing the roosting may seem slight,
the harm to others is great.

To fill in burrows—
From a human perspective, it is but a hole.
From their perspective, is it not a palace?
Do they not see it as a golden chamber, a jade hall,
a deep mansion, a tower of jasper?
Have you not heard of the ant colony south of the *fen* residence,
which took its burrow for the great locust tree’s realm?
Or of the eastern ant colony beneath the great sandalwood tree
veiled in vines behind Wang Yi’s home,
which took its burrow for the realm of the great sandalwood and vines?

Ants taking their burrows for the realm of the locust or sandalwood
is like shrimps taking their holes for the domain of the long-bearded,
or swallows taking their nests for the realm of the black-robed.
Each honors its dwelling—
all creatures are thus, and it is no great wonder.
Yet can burrows be filled?
To fill a burrow is to block its gate,
to sever its path—
would this not bury the entire colony, leaving them to perish within?

As the scriptures relate, there was once a bhikṣu who had attained the six supernatural powers, dwelling in the wilds with a śrāmaṇera. In meditation, the bhikṣu saw that the śrāmaṇera would die in seven days. He therefore devised a skillful means, speaking kindly to him: "Your parents long for you. You should return home for a time and come back on the eighth day." The śrāmaṇera went home and, as foretold, returned on the eighth day. The bhikṣu, astonished, entered samādhi once more to examine the matter. He then understood that on the śrāmaṇera’s journey home, a stream of water was about to flood an ant colony. The śrāmaṇera had swiftly removed his kāṣāya robe, gathered earth, and dammed the water to keep it from entering. By this cause and condition, his lifespan was extended by twelve years.

The brothers Song Jiao and Song Qi were traveling together when they encountered an extraordinary monk. Gazing at them, he said, "The younger Song shall become the foremost scholar of the empire, while the elder Song will not fail to attain high rank." Ten years later, the elder Song again met the monk on the road. The monk exclaimed in great surprise, "Your spirit and bearing are most exceptional. Have you ever saved tens of thousands of lives?" The elder Song replied, "I have always been poor—how could I have done such a thing?" The monk said, "Ponder it carefully."

After a long pause, the elder Song said, "Recently, beneath the hall’s eaves, there was an ant colony suddenly inundated by a torrential flood. I hastily wove a bamboo bridge to ferry them across—could this be the deed you speak of?" The monk replied, "It must be so. This year, the younger Song shall indeed rank first among scholars, and you shall not fall beneath him."

When the examination results were announced, the younger Song did indeed achieve the highest rank. Yet Empress Dowager Zhangxian, declaring that a younger brother ought not precede his elder, decreed that the elder Song be placed first and the younger Song tenth.

But then—can an ant colony truly be filled in?

In praise it is said:
In the primordial vastness, men dwelt in caves,
Their burrows mere hollows, their companions deer and swine.
When the sages arose, they raised beams and laid roofs,
Yet though man and beast differ, both delight in their mounds of earth.
All creatures that move and breathe possess spirit—
How can they be left without refuge?
To overturn a nest—

As the tradition states: According to the *Preserving Descendants Chapter* of the *Highest Dao*, whenever a lineage is cut short by sorrow, it is because in past lives one overturned nests, shattered eggs, burned mountains, drained marshes, or caused miscarriages and stillbirths—violating one thousand six hundred and twenty precepts. Thus, can a nest truly be overturned?

Generally speaking, all beings, great or small, each have their place of dependence. There they nurture their young, give birth, and shelter them. If you overturn their nest, you seize their place of refuge, preventing their offspring from thriving—will their line not be severed?

Carefully consulting the *Materia Medica*: If one overturns a stork’s nest to seize its chicks, for sixty *li* around, rain will fail for months on end. For storks can flock together to stir the clouds, and when the clouds disperse, no rain falls.

Again, observe the osprey: as soon as its chicks hatch, they crawl out of the nest. Fearing they may fall or be scorched by the sun, the mother plucks leafy branches and arranges them around the nest, replacing them twice daily. If the leaves wither, her chicks have fled; if they remain fresh, her chicks are still within. Yet men, overturning the nest, take all the chicks at once.

Who would claim that nest-dwellers are without awareness?
Let me recount a few more examples—may all take heed.

In former times, when Wen Zhang served as Prefect of the Capital, he hung a bell-rope in the hall’s forecourt so that grievances might swiftly reach him. One day, sitting alone, he heard the bell ring repeatedly; tracing the sound, he found no one—only a crow perched upon it. Zhang said, “Surely someone has overturned its nest and taken its chicks.” Soon after, the culprit was indeed found and punished with the rod.

During the Yuanfeng era, Yuan Ruzhi governed Lushan. Suddenly a stork dropped a grass-woven cloak into the courtyard; he immediately ordered a search. It turned out that a meat-roaster had stripped off this cloak, climbed a tree, and was about to overturn a nest to seize the chicks for sale. Yuan likewise meted out punishment.

Can nests, then, be overturned? When nests are overturned, how can we know the birds do not cry out in sorrow, pleading for rescue? Yet no one understands their speech. Would that a book of the Nine Heavens’ avian tongue existed for all to read!

Bai Gui’nian, at the eastern crags of Mount Song, encountered Li Taibai, who said, “Your father, Letian, and I have both ascended as immortals.” He then produced a scroll of plain silk and handed it to him, saying, “Study this, and you will comprehend the speech of birds in the Nine Heavens and the cries of beasts in the Nine Earths. Cultivate further merit and conduct, and immortality may also be within your grasp.” Gui’nian followed the instructions and indeed achieved the promised effect.

One day, passing through Luzhou, the prefect, learning of his ability, invited him to sit. Just then, two sparrows chirped as they flew across the courtyard. The prefect asked, “What are they saying?” Gui’nian replied, “They say that a household west of the city has surplus grain in its granary; they invite others to join them in feeding there.” Investigation confirmed this.

After a while, a stable horse lifted its head and neighed. The prefect asked, “What is it saying?” Gui’nian replied, “The feed in the trough is too hot to eat.” Inquiry proved this correct as well.

In praise, it is said:

Once, it was heard that feathered kindred nest near humans,
Seeking distance from serpents and hawks, they draw close to man alone.
Who would know those in caps and shoes harbor hearts most unkind?
Having devoured their fledglings, they burn their very nests for firewood.
Phoenixes soar on high, while *niejing* beasts come tamed—
Yet who pities the unborn?

As the saying goes: creatures born of womb belong to one kind.
According to the Buddha’s teaching, all are bound by the wheel of defilement,
Driven by delusion to inversion.
Through union and growth, tangled thoughts—horizontal and vertical—arise,
Thus giving rise to wombs obstructed or blooming like *pūtán* flowers:
Humans, beasts, dragons, and immortals—each kind fills the world.

Alas! Humans, beasts, dragons, and immortals are clearly distinct,
Yet the Buddha groups them as one—why?
Because their shared defilement binds them to the same wheel of rebirth.
If humans cherish their unborn, do those others not cherish theirs?

According to the *Records of Immortals*, Bai Wei’nian entered an immortal’s grotto and obtained a scroll of plain silk. Thereupon, he could discern the speech of birds in the Nine Heavens and the cries of beasts in the Nine Earths. One day, passing through Luzhou, the prefect, learning of his ability, invited him to sit. Just then, an attendant drove thirty sheep across the courtyard. Among them, one sheep refused to move despite the whip and let out mournful bleats. The prefect asked, “Does the sheep have something to say?” Gui’nian replied, “The sheep says it carries a lamb in its belly and is about to give birth. Once the birth is done, it will willingly accept death.” The prefect then spared the sheep and did not slaughter it. Later, it indeed gave birth to two lambs.

Liu Daoyuan, having resigned as magistrate of Pengxi, lodged at the Qin family’s home. In a dream, a weeping woman appeared and said, “I was the wife of Master Qin. My husband took a concubine who refused to yield, so in my rage, I beat her to death. The Underworld Judge, swayed by the concubine’s accusation, sentenced me to die and, deeming me cruel, condemned me to be reborn as a sheep in the Qin family’s pen. Tomorrow, I am to be butchered for your meal. Though I accept my death as fate, I carry a lamb in my belly. If it perishes with me, my karmic burden will only grow heavier. I beg you to speak a word on my behalf—if I am spared, I would be most grateful.” At dawn, Liu recounted the dream, but the sheep had already been cooked. The household wept bitterly, retrieved the lamb from its belly, and buried it in a separate villa.

Can the unborn truly be harmed?

In praise, it is said:
A deer bound in the hall dies with its bowels torn.
An eel, though destined for the pot, coils to shield its young.
The love creatures bear for their offspring exceeds even love for themselves.
Humans, too, conceive to continue their line—
Why not reflect and extend such care to others?
To crush an egg...

The *Tradition* records: The bodhisattva Sincere Form addressed the Buddha, saying, “There are sentient beings who have no children, living alone and desolate. What karmic transgression has brought this upon them?” The Buddha replied, “In a former life, such a person was cruel and wicked, disbelieving in sin and fortune. They captured fledglings and devoured eggs, causing all flying birds to lose their young, their cries of grief so piercing that blood flowed from their eyes. This is their retribution.”

The Buddha spoke thus, and the Supreme Lord’s *Chapter on Protecting Offspring* says the same. Then can eggs truly be broken?

According to the *Vibhāṣā Śāstra*, as sentient beings cycle through rebirth, some become egg-born. Consciousness dwells within, bound and enveloped by the egg, until the hatchling pecks its way free. The karmic weight of this is self-evident.

It is also said: Cranes, geese, mandarin ducks, peacocks, parrots, mynas, *qianqiu* birds, and *Golden-Winged* birds—whether dragons or humans—are all egg-born. I once doubted this, thinking, “Birds are indeed egg-born, but how could humans also emerge from eggs?” Yet upon learning of the tales of Kāśyapa, Gavāṃpati, and King Xu Yan, I came to know that humans born from eggs do exist.

Long ago, in the land of Kuadi, a merchant ventured into the sea to gather treasures. He obtained two cormorants—one died, the other lived—and they transformed at will, their feathers resembling those of a crane. Soon, they laid two eggs. When the eggs hatched, two boys emerged, who later left the household life and attained arhatship. One was named the Venerable Kāśyapa, the other the Venerable Gavāṃpati.

According to the *Gazetteer of Xuzhou*, in ancient times, a palace woman of the Lord of Xu conceived and gave birth to an egg. When the egg broke, a child was born. He grew to be benevolent and wise, inheriting the throne of Xu. Because he was born in a reclining position, he was named King Yan.

Who can say there are no egg-born humans?

When Zhi Dun was young, he once debated with his teacher about the nature of living things. Dun argued, “Before an egg hatches, killing it does not count as taking life.” His teacher, unable to refute him, took an egg and cast it to the ground. The shell broke, and a chick emerged, only to perish soon after—its spirit and consciousness extinguished. Dun was deeply moved and thereafter ate only vegetables.

Li Daonian was fond of eating chicken eggs. In his later years, he fell gravely ill. Magistrate Chu Cheng fed him perilla juice, and he immediately vomited thirteen objects. When cut open, they were found to be fully formed chicken feathers, wings, claws, and spurs—all capable of flight. Daonian was terrified and never dared eat eggs again.

Then can eggs truly be broken?

I have often reflected that among egg-born creatures, none produce as many eggs as fish. The largest lay thousands, the smallest no fewer than hundreds. To kill a single fish is to destroy countless eggs—how can one even begin to describe the magnitude?

Li Chongyuan once intended to kill a catfish. The night before, he dreamed of a woman in black who pleaded, “In my belly are five thousand children. If I live, they live. If I die, they die. I beg for your mercy—spare my life.” Chongyuan released the fish.

During the Xuanhe era, the wealthy merchant Yang Xu dreamed that a deity told him, “You will die in ten days. If you can save a hundred million lives, you may yet be spared.” Xu replied, “The appointed time is near, and the lives of creatures are limited. How can I possibly reach such a number?” The deity said, “The Buddhist scriptures state: Fish eggs, if not dried and salted for three years, can still revive. Why not act on this?” Xu then inscribed the deity’s words on the walls of the main thoroughfare. From then on, people took heed—whenever they saw someone killing fish, they would retrieve the eggs and cast them into the river. After a month, Xu dreamed again of the deity, who said, “The hundred million lives have been saved, and your lifespan may now be extended.” And so it came to pass.

In general, if we can save the lives of creatures in their moment of peril, our own lives will not fail to be prolonged when death draws near.

Eulogy states:
All living forms possess inherent nature.
Why did Hundun perish? Because of the piercing.
Those who recklessly harm the unknowing—
can this be called impartial kindness?
The phoenix gave birth to Shang,
abode of sagely numen.
Its mysteries hidden and revealed—
how could one not be vigilant and reverent?

*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Twelve concludes
*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Thirteen

*Biography of Li Changqi*
*Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi*

"May others err."

The *Biography* states: The *Nirvana Sutra* says: All who cultivate the mind must employ the six equalizing powers of wisdom to govern and restrain their own minds, ensuring that in all actions, everything remains equal.

What are the six?
First, the equalizing power of wisdom like an echo;
second, the equalizing power of wisdom like a mirror’s reflection;
third, the equalizing power of wisdom like an illusion;
fourth, the equalizing power of pure wisdom;
fifth, the equalizing power of worldly dharmas;
sixth, the equalizing power of conditioned arising.

In general, one who possesses such wisdom will naturally gain such power. Are not the ten thousand dharmas of the world like an echo, like a reflection, like an illusion? To understand thus—is this not wisdom? Are they not pure in their arising, and equal in their conditions? Know then that "may others err" applies only to those who have not yet reached this state.

At Bozhou’s Drum and Horn Tower, every night at the third watch, a ghost would cover the drum so it could not be struck. The watchman on duty was repeatedly flogged for this, yet could do nothing to stop it. Hearing of Chan Master Huo’s virtuous conduct, he went to ask for his advice.

The Master said, “Why not seize it?”

The soldier replied, “How can a ghost be seized?”

The Master said, “Simply restrain your qi and remain silent—then you may seize it.”

The soldier followed this instruction and indeed succeeded in capturing the ghost. The ghost said, “In this region, the only ones I fear are Chan Master Huo and Huang the Second—just these two. From the prefect down, I fear no one else. How could I fear you?”

Later, they sought out Huang the Second and found him to be an old gardener who had sold vegetables for a living for thirty years. He had no other skills, only that in judging the tenderness of his greens, the size of his bundles, and the fairness of his prices, his mind remained unwavering.

Alas! That an unwavering mind should command the respect and fear of ghosts and spirits—know then that one whose mind is divided will surely be mocked and toyed with by them.

Have you not heard of Vice Envoy Yu’s affair? When Li Shigeng was sent as an envoy to Goryeo, the military officer Yu Ying served as his deputy. All the gifts and tributes received from Goryeo, as well as other offerings, Li paid no heed to, entrusting everything to his deputy. Fearing the ship might leak on the voyage home, the deputy placed all of Li’s goods at the bottom of the hold, keeping only his own on top. Before long, they encountered a great storm, and the ship nearly capsized. The sailors, in their haste, began tossing cargo overboard without sorting, grabbing whatever came to hand and casting it into the sea. When the wind subsided and they took stock, they found that all that had been discarded belonged to the deputy. Li’s goods remained entirely untouched, not a single item lost.

Such a reversal—how could it not be the work of sea spirits mocking him?

In praise, it is said:

The people fall short of Yao and Shun—Yi Yin blamed himself for it.
To stand alone as a gentleman—Bo Yu rejected such folly.
Would you share life with others? Then bear no debt to loftiness or depth.
Yet those vile men drag the multitude into disgrace.
What heart is this? Not even birds and beasts would feel no shame.
To ruin another’s success—

The *Sutra* states: The Buddha told Elder Śuka:
"Good deeds are ten in number, enabling beings to attain great majesty and virtue.
First, toward all beings, harbor no jealousy;
Second, upon seeing others gain, rejoice in their good fortune;
Third, upon seeing others suffer loss, arouse compassion;
Fourth, toward another’s fame or profit, feel joy;
Fifth, when another’s reputation is lost, share their sorrow;
Sixth, arouse the mind of enlightenment, craft Buddha images, and offer jeweled canopies;
Seventh, toward one’s parents and the noble sages, show reverence and serve them;
Eighth, urge others to abandon deeds of little majesty;
Ninth, urge others to cultivate deeds of great majesty;
Tenth, upon seeing those without majesty, do not despise them."

Vimalakīrti Bodhisattva said:
"One who forgets resentment toward the multitude, follows goodness as a flowing stream, and counters the mind of jealousy—such a one, as karmic fruit, gains a retinue of great worthies.
In general, toward others, harbor no jealousy or slander; in oneself, majesty and virtue must increase, while in them, no lack of gratitude or joy arises.
When conditions meet in days to come, they will either become great spiritual friends or great retinues of worthies.
Who would say this is not so?"

During the Campaign of Chanyuan, the Khitans accepted the treaty and withdrew. Lord Lai often bore an air of self-satisfaction, and the Emperor, too, regarded it as a great achievement. Lord Ji, envious, approached the Emperor and said:
"At Chanyuan, Kou Zhun treated Your Majesty as a gaming piece cast against the barbarians. Had we not defeated them, they would have defeated us—this was no long-term strategy for Your Majesty. Such is the *Spring and Autumn*’s ‘treaty beneath the city walls,’ which even the feudal lords deemed shameful. Does Your Majesty consider this a merit?"

The Emperor was deeply disheartened. Later, when Kou Zhun memorialized the Guangzhōu campaign, Transport Commissioner Wang Han, inspecting his circuit, reached Haizhōu, where the prefect, Chong Jian, urgently reported an attack by wax-sealed dispatches. Wang advanced to Huìzhōu, where the people of Guǎngzhōu galloped to seek aid. He issued an order: from each village, three great households were to recruit two hundred stalwart men. He also instructed each county under his jurisdiction to enlist twenty archers, and personally recruited twenty valiant men for his own guard. Soon after, he captured over ten raiders, all of whom he waist-slaughtered as a warning. Upon reaching Guǎngzhōu, though his son died at the hands of the rebels, he did not weep. From then on, the southern gate was left unbarred, grain transports flowed unimpeded, and the rebels withdrew.

At the time, Judicial Commissioner Bao Ke stationed troops in Cháozhōu and daily submitted memorials accusing Wang of cowardice and refusal to fight. Officer Li Dui echoed these charges. Wang was thus demoted by one rank to supervise the salt and tea taxes of Xīnzhōu, while Bao alone received rewards.

Alas! Lord Lai’s merit was indeed great, and Wang Han’s merit was no small matter. Yet a single word from Ji Gong Li Dui brought it to this. Were these two lords practicing good deeds? Did they know how to counter such minds?

Know then that in days to come, the karmic retribution will yield neither great virtue nor powerful deeds, nor will it produce a lineage of great worthies.

In praise it is said:
Three sovereigns in concord brought governance to the heights of Cheng and Kang.
Xiao’s rules followed by Cao—thus flourished the Han foundation.
Li Xun’s envy and scheming brought the Ganlu disaster upon Tang.
When matters do not originate from oneself, one recklessly expands and exaggerates.
Many are the states ruined thereby—can one’s own peace not be harmed?
To endanger others for one’s own safety.

The *Sutra* records:
Subhūti addressed the Buddha, saying: “World-Honored One, if good men and good women arouse the mind of *anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi*, how should they abide, and how should they subdue their minds?”
The Buddha replied: “Excellent, excellent! Good men and good women who arouse the mind of *anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi* should abide thus, and subdue their minds thus.”
Upon hearing these words, Subhūti silently smiled and thought: “This ‘thus’—what meaning does it truly hold? If I wish to subdue, I may simply subdue—it is only thus. What more is there?”

How could those who endanger others for their own safety ever comprehend this?
Alas that they know not the great treasury of teachings, for what is spoken in this passage concerns only the matter of the mind.
Now, to place others in peril in pursuit of one’s own safety—is this subduing?
If others are truly imperiled and oneself truly secure—
more often than not, one incurs offense against Heaven, and calamity will soon befall.

Formerly, Wang Wenzhenggong Dan and Wang Jigong Qinyi served together in the government. One day, the emperor presented a poem on the joyous rain to the two ministries. Wenzheng tucked it into his sleeve and returned to discuss it with his colleagues: “There is a single miswritten character in the emperor’s poem—should we enter the palace to report it?” Jigong said: “This is of no consequence.” Later, he secretly memorialized the matter. The emperor flew into a rage and the next day rebuked Wenzheng in a stern voice: “Yesterday, there was a miswritten character in my poem—why did you not report it?” All below, including Wenzheng, bowed twice in apology, save for Ma Zhijie, who did not bow and instead presented the facts in full, extolling Wang with the words: “He is a true prime minister.”

When Su Wenzhong was in Hangzhou, the emperor wished to employ him. Wang Yuyu said: “In his poems, Shi once wrote, ‘Only the hibernating dragon knows it.’ Now that Your Majesty has ascended to the heavens as a dragon, do you not show reverence, but instead seek the knowledge of a hibernating dragon?” The emperor replied: “Since ancient times, many have been called dragons—such as the eight dragons of the Xun family, or Kongming the Reclining Dragon. How could such terms befit the Son of Heaven alone?” After the audience, Zhang Zizhen reproached Yuyu: “Does the prime minister intend to destroy the Su clan?” Yuyu replied: “Those were the words of Shu Dan.”

Zhang Zizhen said: “Could one consume the spittle of Shu Dan?” Alas, these are all those who endanger others to excess. Had the sagely emperor not seen through their schemes, would the calamity that befell the two lords have been a light one? Truly, they remain ignorant of such discourse.

In praise it is said:
Through *Kan* one practices teaching,
Through *Gu* one rouses the people.
Through *Jian* one rectifies the state,
Through *Zhun* one weaves great plans.
The noble person dwells in peril,
Yet his will serves others.
Heaven aids those who follow the Dao—
Their Way expands greatly.
Wang Yan’s three burrows
Only brought about his ruin.
To diminish others is to harm oneself.

The tradition states: At ten years of age, a person still retains the spirit of childhood. At twenty, having come of age, one may yet hope for achievement. If there is a future ahead, one ought to strive forward. But if one lacks such fortune, one cannot escape drifting through time. Then, with wife and children weighing on the heart and household affairs on the mind—whether holding office, where official duties swarm, or pursuing profit, where market ways are petty—unaware and unknowing, the great term suddenly arrives. Empty-handed, with nothing to accompany one, even if one’s qi and flame once scorched the heavens, none can escape. Have you not heard the words of the Pure Origin Chan Master?

Vice Minister Yang Jinqing asked the Pure Origin Master: “When this body is born, from where does it come? After death, to where does it go?”
The Master replied: “Like a person dreaming—from where does he come? When he awakens, to where does he go?”
Yang asked: “What is the ultimate truth?”
The Master said: “In dreams, one cannot say there is nothing; upon waking, one cannot say there is something. Though there is being and non-being, there is no place of coming or going. This poor monk’s body is likewise.”
He then spoke a gatha:
*See life as a dream,*
*In dreams, all is clamor.*
*Suddenly awakened, all things cease—*
*Just like the realization in dreams.*
*The wise comprehend the dream,*
*The deluded believe in its clamor.*
*To grasp the dream is twofold:*
*One awakening, no other awakening remains.*
*Wealth and honor, poverty and lowliness—*
*There is no separate path.*
The Master’s words may be called exhaustive. All the myriad phenomena of the world—none are not dreams.

Guo Congyi was garrisoning Heyang and built a grand estate in Luoyang, comprising over a thousand rooms. All were constructed of fragrant cypress, even the beams made of figured catalpa. The floors were paved with patterned stone, ponds dug and waters channeled in, hills raised and fruit trees planted. Mills and grindstones were installed throughout, and stables, storehouses, pavilions, and towers—nothing was lacking. The estate’s completion required five thousand *ding* of silver.

The following year, Guo was summoned back to the capital. Arriving at dusk, he took up a candle to survey his estate but had not yet seen it all when the time came for court assembly, allowing no delay. After dining and resting briefly, he departed before dawn. Having attended court and returned, he died upon reaching the Eastern Capital, never again setting foot in his estate. His family could not bear to live there and offered it to the court, which bestowed it upon the Prince of Fu. When the Prince of Fu was exiled to Fuzhou, the estate at Fangling became an abandoned hall.

Chen Gonggong, in Runzhou, built a grand estate of equal magnificence. Pavilions, terraces, ponds, and gardens stretched for several hundred paces. Upon its completion, Gonggong had already fallen ill and could only be carried in a sedan chair to ascend the western tower before he, too, soon died. People named his estate the "Three Impossibles": *unable to reside, unable to maintain, unable to sell*.

Looking back on the days of planning and building—was it not all a dream? From this, one sees ever more clearly that what enriches others diminishes oneself, and what enriches oneself only diminishes. Have you not heard the saying of the Most High: *"Some are diminished yet enriched; some are enriched yet diminished"*?

**Eulogy:**
*Enriching the lowly, diminishing the high,*
*The people rejoice without end.*
*Impoverishing others to enrich oneself—*
*Such a principle cannot endure.*
*The hexagrams bear no blame,*
*For humility is noble and radiant.*
*Waxing and waning, ebb and flow,*
*Each in turn restrains and uplifts.*
*Those who seek to enrich themselves*
*Rarely avoid ruin.*
*Exchanging good for evil—*

**Record:**
*"This body of mine—ears hear, eyes see, body senses, mind knows—clearly exists."*
Yet according to the Buddha’s teaching, earth, water, fire, and wind—four conditions combined in illusion—falsely give rise to the six senses. If the four great elements disperse, where would this illusory body of mine reside?

*"This mind of mine—doing good, doing evil, manifest in daily life—clearly exists."*
Yet according to the Buddha’s teaching, the four great elements and six senses, inner and outer, combine to form the illusion of conditioned *qi*, accumulating within. A semblance of conditioned appearance arises, falsely named the mind. Knowing this truth, one seeks awakening beyond illusion.

Yet according to the Buddha’s teaching, to speak of awakening in dependence on illusion is still to speak of illusion. To assert the existence of awakening is still not to depart from illusion. To assert its non-existence is likewise. Thus, this body, this mind, and even the seeker of awakening—all are named illusion. How much more so external things—what among them is not illusion? Why, then, falsely distinguish good and evil within them?

Vihevahu said:
*Borrowing the four great elements to form a body,*
*The mind, originally unborn, arises with conditions.*
*If prior conditions vanish, the mind too vanishes—*
*Karma and merit, like illusions, arise and perish.*

The Fourth Patriarch said:
*Conditions bear no beauty or ugliness;*
*Beauty and ugliness arise from the mind.*
*If the mind does not cling to names,*
*From where do deluded thoughts arise?*

The Brahmin Hei once employed spiritual power, holding in each palm a blossoming parasol tree to offer to the Buddha. The Buddha said, *"Put it down."* The Brahmin put down the flower in his left hand. The Buddha said again, *"Put it down."* The Brahmin put down the flower in his right hand. The Buddha said once more, *"Put it down."* The Brahmin replied, *"World-Honored One, I held only two flowers and have already put them both down. Now I stand empty-handed—what more would you have me put down?"* The Buddha said, *"I do not ask you to relinquish the flowers. I ask you to put down the six external dusts, the six internal roots, and the six consciousnesses in between. If you can relinquish all three, with nothing left to relinquish, that is where you escape birth and death."* The Brahmin suddenly attained great liberation.

During the reign of Emperor Dezong of Tang, Wu Yuanqing served as the Six Palace Attendant. When the flowers of Luoyang were in full bloom, Yuanqing grew deeply attached to them. Suddenly, a voice from the void said, *"Illusory forms, blooming and fading without cease—how can they corrupt good roots? Why cling to them?"* Yuanqing suddenly attained liberation.

Thus, Wang Wenzheng disdained thousand-*jin* jade belts, and Lü Wenmu rejected mirrors that could illuminate two hundred *li*—they understood this truth. How could one who exchanges good for evil be told such things? Even if they remain unenlightened by Buddhist scriptures, why not reflect on the Confucian teaching *"What you do not desire, do not impose upon others"* and awaken thereby?

When Wang Dan was chief minister, someone presented a jade belt through his younger brother, saying, *"This belt is exquisite."* Wang replied, *"If I wear it myself, bearing its weight, and others say it is fine—is that not wearisome?"* Thus, throughout his life, he wore only the belt bestowed upon him.

When Lü Wenjing was chief minister, his son-in-law sought to sell an ancient mirror, saying, *"This mirror can illuminate two hundred *li* beyond."* Lü replied, *"My face is no larger than a plate—why would I need to see two hundred *li*?"*

**Eulogy:**
*Zilu’s coarse hemp, disdaining fox-fur.*
*Alchemizing copper into gold—Lü Dongbin scorned the craft.*
*Simplicity in poverty and lowliness—*
*The noble man delights in such things.*
*Purple usurps vermilion,*
*Zheng music disrupts proper harmony.*
*The cunning of thieves and burglars*
*Never stoop in shame.*
*To abandon the public for the private—*

**Record:**
*"Forget the state for the sake of the family, forget the self for the sake of the lord, forget the private for the sake of the public—such is the duty of a minister."* How can one abandon the public for the private? If one does so, right may be twisted into wrong, the worthy into the unworthy, and the affairs of state will swiftly fall into chaos. Thus, the noble man dares not act thus.

Minister Zhao Kangjing, in his early years, served in the Hanlin Academy alongside Ouyang Wenzhong. Kangjing was grave and reticent, and Wenzhong somewhat slighted him. Later, when Wenzhong was implicated in a scandal involving his nephew’s misconduct, the emperor’s wrath was severe, and the case pressed urgently. The two chief ministers sought to fabricate charges against him, and none among the officials dared speak.

Kangjing alone submitted a memorial with solemn resolve, saying: *"Ouyang Xiu has long been esteemed for his literary attainments; I cannot, on account of vague and sordid matters within his private chambers, lightly cast aspersions upon him. Though my relations with Xiu have always been distant, and his treatment of me likewise indifferent, what I cherish is the greater principle of the court."*

Some later remarked to Kangjing: *"Did you not bear a grudge against Lord Ouyang?"* He replied: *"To abandon the public for the private is a course I cannot abide—how could I dare act thus?"*

During the Zhihe era, Zhao Bian served as censor, and Fan Gongzhen as a remembrancer. When they clashed over the case of Chen Gonggong, resentment arose between them. In the Xining era, Wang Jiefu held power and, resenting Jingren for repeatedly denouncing him before the emperor, sought to exploit an opportunity to frame him. One day, the emperor questioned Jiefu about Jingren. Jiefu replied: *"Ask Zhao Bian, and you will know his character."* When the emperor inquired of Zhao Bian, he answered: *"A loyal minister."* The emperor asked: *"How do you know him to be loyal?"* Bian replied: *"During the Jiayou era, when Emperor Renzong fell gravely ill, Gongzhen was the first to petition for the establishment of an imperial heir, securing the altars of the state—was this not the act of a loyal minister?"*

Afterward, Jiefu reproached him sharply: *"Did you not bear a grudge against him?"* Bian replied: *"How could I dare abandon the public for the private? My willingness to speak plainly without concealment arises from placing the state before personal enmity. These two gentlemen may truly be called those who forget the private for the sake of the public."*

**Eulogy:**
When the public prevails, principle triumphs; when the private speaks, profit is invoked. Once the desire for gain stirs, the heavenly principle fades into oblivion. At the extreme of dissipation, thieves and bandits arise in rebellion. It begins with the self, then twists and turns toward bias and partiality. To cloak private ends in the guise of the public—this is yet more egregious.

*Here ends Scroll Thirteen of the* **Taishang Ganying Pian** *(*The Supreme Scripture on Retribution and Response*).*
*Scroll Fourteen of the* **Taishang Ganying Pian** *begins.*

**The Biography of Li Changling, with Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

To appropriate another’s abilities—this is theft.

The *Tradition* states: When the Venerable Kāśyapa crossed the river, he suddenly heard the Lotus Fragrance River Spirit reproach him, saying, “Noble One, you are an Arhat—why do you steal my fragrance?” Kāśyapa replied, “The scent came to my nose of its own accord—how can this be called theft?”

I have pondered these words thrice and found them deeply significant. Generally speaking, the scent-dust exists as it is; it is only through my nasal faculty that the perception of smell arises, distinguishing it as fragrance. Yet according to the Buddha’s teaching, this is already called theft.

Know then that to seize another’s abilities and claim them as one’s own is true theft.

Consider the case of Gao Ling: in his later years, though he held the post of Libationer, his Dao and qi failed to commune, and his spirit lacked a master. Thus, a host of malevolent forces assailed him, hastening his misfortune. The Perfected Lord of Life Protection said to Director Xu: “Gao Ling’s lack of virtue has endured for long; the entanglements of ghostly litigation have accumulated. Moreover, he once defaced the steles and inscriptions of ancients, passing them off as his own work. The Water Official condemned him for obscuring goodness to aggrandize himself, secretly injuring spirits and deities. Thus, his case was placed at the forefront of the docket to be reported—his guilt was inescapable.” Soon after, he indeed perished.

Can the abilities of others truly be stolen?

Have you not heard of the case of Shi Manqing? Years after his death, he appeared in a dream to his friend Guan Yong, saying: “In my lifetime, I composed many poems, but of all of them, I took greatest pride in *Substituting Yangping for Intent*—its words and meaning were profound and beautiful. Yet though the world praises it, only you can ensure this poem spreads widely.” Guan awoke with the lines of *Hidden Measures of the Daoist Adept* and *The Immortal’s Invitation* expanding in his mind, and he set them to song. Later, he dreamed again, and Manqing came to express his gratitude.

Alas! Even for a poem left uncelebrated, one must plead for its recognition—how much worse, then, to steal another’s ability and claim it as one’s own?

**Eulogy:**
Seeing the worthy, one strives to emulate;
Seeing the sagely, one follows their path.
When myriad virtues are complete,
It lies in man to seek them within.
To think oneself inferior to Shun
Is cause for self-reproach.
How can one of sheep-like nature
Bear the guise of a mighty beast?
To deceive others is to deceive oneself—
A shame most profound.
To obscure another’s goodness…

As the saying goes: Since ancient times, those whose skills won them fame in later ages never did so without relying on others. Consider Yuan Ying of the Prince of Teng’s mastery of butterfly painting, Madam Gongsun’s sword dance, Cao Gang’s pipa, or Mi Jiarong’s singing—these were but incidental mentions in palace lyrics or verses by Tang poets. Yet even the slightest talent is a form of goodness. To leave it unrecognized is to suppress it.

Have you not heard of the affair of Lord Xu, the Perfected Duke? In his mortal days, Xu the Perfected once renovated a Daoist abbey and wished to record the event. Chancing upon an ancient stele whose inscription had worn away, he scraped off the old text and engraved his own account. Thereafter, he grew restless and unsettled. One day, as he strolled through the courtyard, he suddenly heard a voice from the void: “Lord Xu, Lord Xu, hasten to the Water Official to seek deliverance! Else, unforeseen calamity may befall you.” Startled, Xu lit incense and prayed devoutly, wishing to learn the reason for his peril. After a long while, the voice spoke again: “Though the old stele’s text was effaced, its original author has lodged a complaint with the Water Official, accusing you of usurping his name to exalt your own. Thus, the Water Official is preparing to summon you for judgment—you must act swiftly.” Xu then sought out the original inscription and had it carved anew on a separate stone. That night, he dreamed of a spirit who thanked him, saying: “Since you have restored my name, I can offer no repayment but to request a grand Water and Land Ritual, proclaiming it to the myriad spirits, that the Three Officials may record your merit and aid your attainment of the Dao.” Xu followed the instructions, and later indeed achieved the Way.

When Caizhou was pacified, Emperor Xianzong ordered Daoists to perform a ritual at the Purple Ultimate Abbey. The abbey had once been a shrine to Wu Shaocheng, but Pei the Duke of Jin had repurposed it as a Daoist sanctuary. On the day of the ritual, a great thunderclap resounded. The stele bearing Han Hong’s *Inscription on the Pacification of Huaixi* had originally been Wu Shaocheng’s *Stele of Virtuous Governance*. That day, it wept beads of sweat that turned to mud. Within ten days, an imperial envoy arrived to grind away Han’s inscription on the pacification of Huai and restore Shaocheng’s text on virtuous governance. Such transformations, though glimpsed in dreams, are not without consequence.

Alas! If even the written word is thus, how much more so for goodness greater than mere text?

**Eulogy:**
The sin of suppressing the worthy
Is called inauspicious.
Trace it to its origin—
Envy of another’s excellence.
Jade glistens within wood,
Pearls gleam in the night.
All things possess inherent worth,
Yet how can they be concealed?
To stifle ability and bury goodness
Is to seek obscurity yet achieve renown.
To expose another’s shame…

As the saying goes: “Shame” refers to the shame of ugliness; “expose” means to lay bare. Consider Nie Chongyi’s love of mockery or Zhang Qiyi’s talent for slander. Chongyi, though skilled in poetry, delighted in ridicule, sparing not even colleagues. Ultimately, he was exiled to Dengzhou for slandering governance, his corpse left by the roadside, unclaimed by any who passed. Zhang Qiyi, also a poet fond of mockery, would expose others’ misdeeds in verse, ensuring their infamy spread unchecked. Some of his accusations were exaggerated, even to the point of breaking marriages and severing bonds. In the end, he contracted a loathsome illness and died chewing his own tongue.

Can one’s shame truly be exposed?

One would do well to heed the tale of Huangkeng, for had they heard it, they would surely take warning. A military officer named Duan Gou once passed through Huangkeng. His attendant, walking ahead of the horse, picked up several pounds of skull fragments. Among them was a piece bearing faint traces of the words “runaway slave”—the ink had seeped into the bone. That night, the attendant dreamed of a man covering his face with his hands, pleading for the bones: “I am deeply ashamed. I beg you, bury them deeply where no one may see. I will repay your kindness.” The attendant awoke in alarm and immediately interred the remains. When he later passed through Huangkeng again, he dreamed of the same man, who said: “In gratitude for your mercy, a few steps from here lies buried treasure—let it be my thanks.” Following the instructions, he dug and uncovered wealth worth over ten thousand coins.

Thus, to expose another’s shame—who can say what retribution may come in days to come?

**Eulogy:**

A beauty like Wang Zhaojun was misrepresented by a painter’s error—how much more so when it comes to profit! To surpass even the crimson emaciation of a consumptive: such is the art of depiction, refined through meticulous imitation. Once the crowd has clamored and spread the tale, it can never be washed clean. With but a few words of mine, I plunge another into grave wrong. To expose another’s secrets—Confucius said: “To expose is not merely to depict; it is to attack outright,” as did men like Lü Jifu.

When Zeng Zixuan served as Minister of Revenue, Lü Jifu succeeded him and immediately petitioned the throne, claiming the ministry’s affairs were incomplete or improperly managed. When Zhang Cuiming held the same post, Shu Dan replaced him and, having gathered all the clerks, declared the ministry’s business unresolved. Zhou Shihou, as Transport Commissioner of Hubei, entrusted several dozen bottles of tribute wine to the Salt Supervisor Zhang Shangying for sale. Shangying reported this to Inspector Pu Zongmeng, who impeached Zhou, leading to his demotion. Later, when Zhang Shangying held a court appointment, he asked Examination Supervisor Shu Dan to recommend a candidate. Dan submitted a memorial denouncing Shangying’s negligence, and Shangying was likewise stripped of office. Did they not know Confucius’ disciple Zigong’s warning that those who expose others’ faults under the guise of honesty are detestable? Would a man of profound virtue ever act thus?

When Han Weigong Qi served as Chancellor, whenever he encountered documents attacking another’s hidden faults, he would personally seal them to prevent others from seeing. Chen Zhongsuxuan Guan, while serving as a censor, spoke out on every matter, yet his impeachments never touched on personal affairs. Prime Minister Su Fan, during his tenure in Hangzhou, was approached by an influential figure with a private request, which he refused. Later, when that man became a censor, he unleashed a torrent of slander against Su. Advisors urged Su to present the original letters of solicitation as evidence, but he only smiled and said: “To expose another’s secrets—would I ever do such a thing?”

Magistrate Hu Suxiao governed Runzhou. His predecessor, Governor Teng, had vigorously promoted education, spending millions of cash. Before the project was completed, Teng was dismissed, and petty officials began spreading slander, accusing him of misusing funds. From the vice-magistrate downward, none would sign the accounts. Hu addressed them in assembly: “Gentlemen, how long have you served under Governor Teng? If there were any impropriety, why did you not speak up sooner, instead of silently observing and only now recording it after his departure? Is this honorable?” The entire assembly hung their heads in shame and signed the ledgers. To act with such magnanimity is as distant from pettiness as heaven is from earth.

**Eulogy:**
To expose differs from forthrightness—even the sages despised it.
It is treacherous and cunning, striking at flaws when opportunity arises.
Swearing oaths to heaven, laughing and clasping hands in feigned friendship—
Yet when slight harm touches them, they turn and bare their fangs.
Neither rhinoceros nor tiger, what manner of man is this?
To deplete another’s wealth—Confucius said: “To drain another’s wealth, leaving their household ruined and their descendants destitute—is this not a grievous sin?”

Consider the case of Wen Qi. He wandered among the nobles of Shu, peddling his alchemical arts. All who took an interest in the furnace and flame were deceived by him. A wealthy merchant named Li the Fifteenth had amassed a fortune of ten thousand strings of cash. Enamored of Qi’s sorcery, within three years his wealth was utterly exhausted. The knowing laughed at him, and in despair, he hanged himself. Qi then fled, renting a house in Jianzhou where he forged elixirs. Soon, a fire broke out, engulfing an entire ward. Qi fled in panic, losing himself in the forest, where he was pursued by wild beasts. Deeper into the ravine he stumbled, only to be followed again. As dawn broke, he was captured by hunters. The case was tried, and he was beheaded at the site of his alchemical work, to appease the residents. If another’s wealth may not be depleted, may one’s own wealth be squandered?

Let me recount another tale, that young men may take heed. Wang Zude died in Qinzhou in the yichou year of the Shaoxing reign. One evening, his wife and son were sitting in the hall when they dimly perceived Zude returning from outside. He reproached them: “I hear the family plans to sell the estate—how can you part with our ancestral home?” He continued: “The treasures of my lifetime—Huang Quan’s and Guo Xi’s landscapes, Li Cheng’s wintry forests—ten scrolls in all. I hear they too have been taken out for appraisal. I have been dead but a short time, and the family is not yet impoverished. Why such haste?” He sighed: “I long for home, yet have no way to return. By chance, it is the Ghost Festival, and I was able to come briefly. If you do not change your ways, I will bring disaster upon you.” His wife, terrified, abandoned the idea of selling. From this, we see: whether another’s wealth or one’s own, neither may be wasted.

**Eulogy:**
The drought demon starves the poor; the poverty ghost resents the rich.
As omens and calamities, their logic defies reason.
Human nature is most noble—kin, clan, and friends.
What heart could envy what others possess?
Lord Liu loved wealth, yet enriched his people.
To sunder another’s flesh and blood—Confucius said: “Flesh and blood are bound by kinship, the great human relationships. How can they be severed?”

Have you not heard the tale of Zhang Songshan? Long ago, Zhang Xiaoji was the son-in-law of a wealthy man from the same commandery. The rich man had only one son, who committed a minor offense. The household, united in resentment, drove him out. Soon after, the rich man fell ill. On his deathbed, he entrusted all his wealth to Xiaoji. Years later, Xiaoji encountered the rich man’s son begging by the road. He called him over and asked: “Can you tend a garden?” The son replied: “To be fed and sheltered would be my greatest fortune.” Later, when the son had proven himself, Xiaoji asked: “Can you manage the accounts?” The son answered: “To tend a garden was already my good fortune—if I could manage the accounts, it would be even more so.” Thus, Xiaoji entrusted him with the ledgers. Over time, the son grew ever more diligent and respectful, his former faults erased. Xiaoji reflected: “He is truly his father’s son. How can I profit from his wealth alone, letting this noble deed pass unfulfilled?”

Thus, Xiaoji returned all the family wealth to the son. At the time, none who heard of it failed to praise and marvel, deeming it unheard of since ancient days. After Xiaoji’s death, several of his friends traveled to Mount Song. Along the way, they encountered a nobleman, his banners filling the fields, his retinue of attendants and outriders streaming in procession—like a regional governor newly assuming office. Glancing at the occupant of the grand carriage, they saw it was Xiaoji. They stepped forward to bow and asked how he had come to attain such a state. Xiaoji replied: “It was because of the matter of returning the wealth.” Before he could finish speaking, he suddenly vanished.

Alas! How many sons-in-law in the world are like Xiaoji? How many others in his place would have done the same? Long ago in Hangzhou, there was a scholar who also married into a wealthy family. When the rich man fell ill and neared death, mindful that his son was only three years old, he entrusted his son-in-law with his assets and left a will stating: “When the time comes to divide the property, give three-tenths to my son and seven-tenths to my son-in-law.” When the son grew up, he took the matter to court. At the time, Zhang Zhongding was the prefect. After reading the will, he poured a libation of wine upon the ground and said: “Your father-in-law was truly a wise man. Because his son was young, he devised this plan to ensure you would diligently raise and protect him. Had he not done so, your hands would not have spared the child’s life.” He then ordered that seven-tenths go to the son and three-tenths to the son-in-law. Both departed in tears, expressing their gratitude.

Ah! If one were to compare this son-in-law to Xiaoji of Mount Song, the very ground beneath Mount Song would already lie far beyond the heavens—how could he not become an immortal? If one were to seek such a man among others, even this son-in-law, though humbled, still understood righteousness. How much better is he than those who are cruel, unfeeling, and unworthy of trust? Yet his aspiration to Mount Song remains as distant as the sky.

In praise it is said:

Of old, there was the Great Ancestor who ordered the ranks of ancestral temples. The teachings of the Zhou classics also united the clan. Later generations gathered in righteousness, valuing kinship and harmony. Even a sage like the Duke of Zhou was slandered by the two uncles. The venomous tongues of flatterers wrought bitter poison.

*End of Scroll Fourteen of the Supreme Scripture on Retribution and Response*
*Scroll Fifteen of the Supreme Scripture on Retribution and Response*

**Biography of Li Changling, with a Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

To encroach upon what others cherish.
