It is said: When the Perfected Lord Xu was in the world, he once exterminated a serpent spirit in Nanchang. His Daoist companions who wished to follow him numbered no fewer than a thousand. The Perfected Lord selected only three hundred whom he knew well and set out with them. Later, they lodged for the night at the foot of a mountain. Fearing that their cultivation lacked strength, he took three hundred lumps of charcoal from the fire and transformed them into three hundred beauties to test them. At dawn, when he examined them, only Wu Meng and Shi Daoyi had no charcoal stains on their robes. The remaining two hundred and ninety-eight had all embraced the charcoal. At the moment of embrace, did they not each see a beautiful woman? How could they have known it was merely a lump of charcoal? For the most part, their desires had not yet been severed, and so they were easily deceived—how much more so for those who do not cultivate at all?

In former times, Tao Gu was sent as an envoy to Jiangnan. Though his bearing was lofty and his demeanor stern, even in casual conversation, he never relaxed his guard. Han Xizai said to his confidant: “I observe that this man is no true gentleman. His resolve can be broken. I shall make the court laugh at him.” He then selected a famous courtesan, Qin Ruolan, dressed her in ragged clothes, and had her pretend to be the daughter of a courier station attendant. Morning and night, she swept the courtyard with a broom. Ruolan was of unmatched beauty, and Tao was moved by her. He abandoned his vigilance in solitude and composed several *Youting* poems, which he had Ruolan sing. A few days later, the King of Li held a grand feast and poured wine for the envoy from a massive goblet. Tao remained as stern and unyielding as before. Then Ruolan was brought out to sing the poems he had gifted her as entertainment. Tao was deeply ashamed and ended up drunk, vomiting upon the mats. From then on, the King of Li treated him coldly. Upon his return, the court already knew of the affair and never employed him again.

When Wen Lugong was in Shu, he often held feasts and gatherings, and rumors spread. Coincidentally, the imperial censor He Shengcong, whose given name was Tan, returned to Shu on leave. Wen tasked him with secretly investigating the matter. As He was about to cross the border, Wen arranged a grand reception for him. Zhang Shaoyu, whose given name was Yu, said to Wen: “He Shengcong’s arrival is nothing to fear. When he previously served as prefect of Hanzhou, there was a gathering of fellow townsmen. A courtesan skilled in dance caught his eye. He asked her surname, and she replied, ‘Your servant is surnamed Yang.’ He Shengcong said, ‘So you are the famed Willow of Yang Terrace.’” Shaoyu then took her scarf and inscribed a poem upon it:

*A beauty of Shu is called Slender Waist,*
*The Eastern Terrace censor cherishes her fiery grace.*
*From now on, call her Willow of Yang Terrace,*
*Dancing away ten thousand strands of spring wind.*

He had the courtesan sing it as a *zhuzhi* poem. He Shengcong became thoroughly drunk, which revealed his true nature. Wen said, “Keep this secret for now.” When He Shengcong arrived, he indeed conducted himself with strict dignity. Wen held a banquet for him and had the courtesan mingle among the official entertainers, singing Shaoyu’s poem as entertainment. He Shengcong became drunk once more. The affair soon spread, reaching the emperor’s ears. Upon his return, he was never again granted an audience. Thus, the slander against Wen Lugong was silenced.

Alas! Tao Gu had no self-restraint, and He Shengcong, though known for his integrity, how could he have acted thus? For the most part, when words take shape, the heart has already been moved. Is this not because desires remain unsevered, illusory forms are mistaken for reality, and love and attachment arise from within?

In praise it is said:

People cherish themselves more than jade,
And love their children as they love themselves.
Extending this love to others,
None should be treated otherwise.
Why then do they cruelly plunder,
Indulging in predation?
A beauty and a gem nearly overturned Han’s chariot.

**To aid others in wrongdoing.**

It is said: One version reads: *To teach others in wrongdoing.* For the most part, this is about encouraging others’ vices and failing to guide them toward goodness. Have you not heard the Buddha’s words? The Buddha said: To expound the Dharma and teach others is called the gift of Dharma, enabling beings to awaken through hearing the Way. For this cause, in future lives, seven rewards are obtained:

1. When beings hear the Dharma and instantly dispel anger, in my future lives, I shall attain a sublime form.
2. When beings hear the Dharma and cultivate compassion, refraining from killing, in my future lives, I shall enjoy long life.
3. When beings hear the Dharma and do not steal others’ wealth, in my future lives, I shall abound in treasures.
4. When beings hear the Dharma and open their hearts to joyful giving, in my future lives, I shall gain great strength.
5. When beings hear the Dharma and instantly cast off delusion, in my future lives, I shall attain unobstructed eloquence.
6. When beings hear the Dharma and hold firm in faith, free from doubt, in my future lives, my faith shall be clear and bright.
7. When beings hear the Dharma and skillfully follow its guidance, in my future lives, I shall attain the bodhi path.

Thus, guiding others toward goodness—how is this not far better than aiding them in wrongdoing? Let me present examples for each, that all may take heed.

During the Yuanfeng era, Li Zhichun served three terms as transport commissioner of Chengdu. He was benevolent toward the people and cherished all things, and the people of Shu lived in peace. Beyond his official duties, he paid particular attention to burying the exposed bones of the dead. A minor official, Xu Xi, originally from Mei, was well-versed in doing good. Li entrusted him with this task, and Xu took it upon himself with dedication. One day, a man named Wang Bin from Jinhua Street in the western market fell ill and entered the netherworld. He saw several officials in crimson and purple robes gathered in a hall. They summoned Xu Xi before them and said: “A celestial decree has just arrived. Li Zhichun, for his merit in burying dry bones, shall be granted another term as prefect of Chengdu.”

The officials declared, “As you have diligently managed the documents, we grant you a son who shall pass the imperial examination.” They continued, “Since you are sincere and trustworthy, you may return and proclaim this, that all may be encouraged.” In the third year of the Yuanyou era, Li Zhichun was indeed promoted to Academician of the Baowen Pavilion and appointed prefect of Chengdu. Xu Xi had a son named Xu Shi, who also passed the imperial examination that year under the Li Changning roster. This is an example of one who aided others in virtue and likewise received its reward.

During the Zhenghe era, an edict was issued to extensively renovate the Western Palace. At the time, Wang Ju served as transport commissioner, but was dismissed for imposing excessive levies. The court then summoned Song Sheng of Huixu to serve as chief transport commissioner and oversee the project. Sheng’s intentions were overly exacting: he demanded vast quantities of ox-bone ash to plaster the palace walls, insisting they be made lustrous and smooth. However, his orders were too harsh, driving the people to slaughter oxen and horses, or even exhume graves to obtain bones—yet still, it was not enough.

At the time, Sun Kuang and Han Rong were both members of the staff. Kuang disapproved of the project and resigned on grounds of illness. Rong alone proposed a solution, suggesting they excavate over a dozen cluster graves from the Five Dynasties period outside the city to meet the demand.

During the Xuanhe era, Kuang suddenly died and found himself at Mount Tai. There, he saw a gate bearing a placard that read “Pure and Peaceful.” An underworld official brandished an iron fork, compelling him to sign a confession of clan extermination. Kuang replied, “When I first opposed this matter, I did so precisely to avoid such an error.”

Suddenly, several ghosts dragged forth two prisoners, both weighed down by massive wooden cangues and iron shackles. Beside each walked another ghost, wielding iron fans studded with nails to strike them, so that blood streamed down their bodies. Looking closely, Kuang saw they were Zhu and Sheng. One of them finally confessed.

As Kuang was about to leave, Rong was also brought in. The underworld official again brandished his iron fork, compelling him to sign a confession of clan extermination. Rong said, “I am indeed guilty, but what crime have my father and ancestors committed? If my entire clan is to be wiped out, is this not excessive?” Soon, a voice from the hall above declared, “Let him confess only to the extinction of his immediate family.” Kuang relayed this. Before long, every member of Rong’s household indeed perished.

This was a case of aiding another’s wrongdoing and immediately reaping the consequences. Yet if aiding another’s wrongdoing brings such retribution, is it ever worth doing?

**Eulogy:**
He who aids in brewing wine tastes the dregs;
He who aids a state in turmoil suffers the wound.
Good and evil follow their kind—
Such is the constant way of human affairs.
To abet the mighty in tyranny
Is to share in their ruin.
Holding firewood to feed the flames,
Pouring boiling water to raise the steam—
The scorching disaster that follows
Is as certain as a debt repaid.
Indulging one’s will to wield power—

**It is said:** In *The Six Regrets of Minister Kou* it is written:
To bend office for private ends brings regret when opportunity is lost;
To spend wealth without restraint brings regret when poverty comes;
To neglect learning in one’s youth brings regret when the chance has passed;
To ignore affairs when they arise brings regret when the need arises;
To speak wildly in drunkenness brings regret when sober;
To fail to rest when at ease brings regret when illness strikes.
These words of Minister Kou are truly the great principle for avoiding regret.
I cherish his wisdom, and so I quote it here as a warning to those who indulge their will to wield power.
Will they not regret it afterward?

Long ago, He Jia, serving as *Lu Shi Can Jun* and overseeing the Ever-Normal Granary of Zizhou Circuit, was tyrannical wherever he went, beating and flogging officials and commoners to establish his authority. The local officials and people, cowering in fear, hid with nowhere to turn, even to the point of insulting his superiors, placing himself above them. Whenever he issued orders to prefectures and counties, he would insist, “Without official authorization, no directive from the transport commissioner’s office may be carried out.” When Vice-Commissioner Li Song and Judge Chen Gang discussed matters with him and disagreed, he would berate and curse them. When the prefect came to report, He Jia would force him to dismount outside the gate and proceed along the corridor, often not rising from his seat even when the prefect drew near.

He also sought to abolish Guang’an Prefecture. The consensus held that since it lay far from other commanderies, it could not be abolished. A local official named Zhang Pifang obtained a *Record of the Drum and Horn Tower* written by He Jia’s father, Academician He She, which explicitly stated the reasons it should not be abolished, and presented it to him. He Jia replied, “All matters should follow public consensus—this is hardly sufficient evidence.” Li Song and others then memorialized his conduct, and an imperial edict removed him from office.

After his dismissal, someone along the way memorialized Li Song and the others, accusing them of every conceivable offense. When He Jia reached the capital and was handed over to the Kaifeng Prefecture for interrogation, he demanded ten thousand sheets of paper to submit his defense. The prefectural authorities provided the exact number. Yet when he submitted his response, each sheet bore only a single large character, filling the page. The court found four instances of falsehood, all of which were baseless and absurd. The case was concluded, and he was stripped of his rank.

At that moment, did He Jia not regret his actions?
How lamentable that he never understood:
To indulge one’s will and wield power
Is something Heaven does not permit.

In days past, when Du Xunhe had yet to rise to prominence, he dreamed of a monk who warned him: "You shall surely gain the ruler’s favor, but if you indulge your will and wield power wantonly, you will surely die." Later, when the Liang Ancestor founded his dynasty, Xunhe, relying on imperial favor, grew violent and overbearing, even plotting to murder those he had long disliked. Before he could carry out his scheme, he suddenly dreamed of a spirit rebuking him: "Have you forgotten the divine monk’s words?" Soon after, the emperor was about to punish him—yet before the sentence was carried out, he died.

**Eulogy:**
The auspiciousness of wielding power
Lies in turning inward.
To possess awe-inspiring authority,
One must follow proper conduct.
Solemn in bearing, one naturally commands respect.
Severity should be directed at oneself,
Not used to oppress the people.
"Act not with tyranny"—
Thus the Book admonishes your ministers.
To humiliate others in pursuit of victory—

As the scriptures record: In the Buddha’s time, there was a man who made his living by hauling goods with carts, keeping two oxen. One ox bore a calf with long, broad horns, which he named Longhorn. The other bore a calf with a bald head and no horns, which he named Baldhead. One day, while hauling goods, he encountered another carter. Each boasted of his ox’s strength, and they agreed to wager on which could ascend a steep slope. Baldhead, however, lagged behind. Enraged, his master cursed him: "Baldheaded beast, why do you not hurry?" Baldhead’s eyes reddened with fury, and he slowed even more, ensuring his master’s defeat. For he resented the public humiliation.

Alas! Even beasts behave thus—how much more so men? Know then that to humiliate others in pursuit of victory is truly unacceptable.

Long ago, when Prince Shao of Shaoling, Lun, governed Ying, Wu ( ) served as his retainer. When Zhang Zuan was appointed to govern Xiangdong, Lun held a farewell banquet for him in the suburbs, and ( ) was also in attendance. At the time, Zuan was newly exalted and looked down upon ( ). Suddenly, he raised his cup and toasted ( ), saying: "Wu ( ), rejoice! Today you are honored to attend this feast." ( ) flew into a rage and immediately left. His son, Lunru, heard of this and was so overcome with indignation that he died. ( ) mourned his son and resented Zuan, soon falling ill and passing away himself. His wife, unable to bear the fury, perished shortly after. The people of the time remarked: "Zhang Zuan’s single cup of wine killed three of the Wu family."

Can men truly be humiliated?

In Wuyuan County, Huizhou, there lived a man named Yu Yanfu. All his life, he was hot-tempered and domineering, bullying his neighbors. Whenever he coveted another’s land or house, he would use schemes to seize it. Even in old age, he showed no remorse. In the *renxu* year of the Shaoxing era, he suddenly fell gravely ill. As he lay stricken, he repeatedly neighed like a horse.

After a long while, his hands and feet transformed into hooves, and within days, he died.

Who would claim that humiliating others to seek victory brings no harm?

Alas for those with mistaken views—knowing only that overpowering others is strength, yet unaware that true strength lies in overcoming oneself. Have they never heard Laozi’s words: *"To overcome others is to have force; to overcome oneself is to be strong"*?

**Eulogy:**
When some suffer humiliation,
they roll up their sleeves, glaring in fury.
If not cowed by might,
then crushed by power.
Though rage chokes their words,
they are held in check—
but once might fades and power wanes,
none fail to bite back.
Even one who can best me
strikes fear into Yu’s heart.

To ruin another’s crops—
as the saying goes, such acts are the work of boorish rustics,
hardly worth mention.

Let me instead speak of the fine deeds of those who toiled in agriculture, revered grain,
and studied waterworks—
that later governors may take them as models.

Gao, the Grand Master for Consultation, governed Tang Prefecture. The land was vast, the people few. From the Five Dynasties to our present dynasty, it had four counties and six thousand one hundred fifty-five households. Upon arrival, he surveyed the fields and plains, recognizing that the uncultivated land could be tilled—if only for want of labor. Thus, he recruited great numbers of migrants from the Two Rivers, allotting fields by household. The population grew by eleven thousand three hundred eighty-one, and the land granted totaled thirty-one thousand three hundred twenty-eight *qing*. Even the wilds of mountains and forests were transformed into fertile fields.

Scholar Zhang Lun, Vice Commissioner of the Jiang-Huai Transport Bureau, dredged five canals to channel Lake Tai, irrigating the people’s fields. The annual tax exemption in grain amounted to six hundred thousand *hu*.

Xu, Minister of the Court of Imperial Sacrifices, governed Xingyuan and restored the great mountain rivers and weirs, irrigating over forty thousand *qing* of farmland.

Lu Kaichu, Prefect of Queshan in Jizhou, revived abandoned reservoirs to water thousands of *qing* of fields.

Cheng Shimeng, Compiler of the Academy, governed Shizhou. Wherever the valleys of Fen and Jin held water to irrigate farmland, he surveyed the terrain, channeling streams into canals. The reclaimed fields totaled over one thousand *qing*.

Minister Xu Gui governed Danyang. During a severe drought, he defied the prohibition and breached Lian Lake to save the people’s fields, yielding a bumper harvest of over ten thousand *qing*. Breaching Lian Lake was a capital offense.

Counselor Chen Shenghua governed Liyang County. The Zheng-Bai Canal, which could draw water to irrigate farmland, had long been monopolized by neighboring jurisdictions. Upon arrival, he presented the full history to the authorities, and at once, the long-standing obstruction was removed. The restored waterworks irrigated over one thousand *qing* of fields.

Chief Justice Wang Ji governed Longxi County. A reservoir stretched for dozens of *li*, its taxes paid by great clans who alone reaped its benefits. Upon arrival, he seized it entirely to irrigate the people’s fields, sparing the county from drought and famine.

Marshal Miao Shizhong governed Ningling County. An ancient river, long silted and abandoned, lay there. Upon arrival, he petitioned for laborers to dredge it, irrigating the people’s fields. Thus, the entire county’s land became fertile.

**Eulogy:**

To seize grain without sowing—*The Book of Songs* satirizes such rulers. The earth yields no sustenance, and so the ancients buried their dead within it. Who robs the farmer of his season leaves the people without store. Who flays the people’s strength denies them their nurture. Harm those who nurture, and Heaven will surely send down ruin.

To sunder another’s marriage—*The Tradition* says, *The Record of Rites* declares: Only with husband and wife does father and son arise. Thus the Way of marriage may be called great; how can it be sundered? Whether severed before union or after, it is still a severing. Love’s murder cuts deeper than the blade; who would call it harmless?

In his youth, Zheng Hezhong was betrothed to a woman of the Wang clan. Soon after, his father died, and his family’s fortunes waned. The Wangs then sought to break the engagement. Her brother, Wang Gu, serving as judicial commissioner of Wanzhou, controlled the matter. Yet pressed by public opinion, he reluctantly allowed the marriage to proceed. A son was born but did not survive, and so Hezhong was kept apart, never permitted to return. The Wangs sent him clothing, but Gu punished the bearers. Hezhong grew ever more bitter, fell ill in heart, and died.

One night, the Wang woman dreamed Hezhong appeared to her, saying: “I have lodged my complaint in the courts of the unseen. I have been sent to the Eastern Peak of Quzhou, where my case is being investigated.” By then, she had remarried to Lu Song, then serving as instructor in Jizhou. Within a month, Gu died, and all who had taken part in the decision to break the engagement perished. Only the Wang woman, who had never intended to betray her husband, survived alone.

Later, Wang Gu’s son married a woman of the Xu clan. After barely two years, her family reclaimed her. Was this not retribution?

Assistant Magistrate Yang Hong, enraged that his son-in-law Yao Gong was unlearned and inept, sent him away. Gong begged to bid his wife farewell, but was refused. The daughter, consumed by grief, fell ill and died. Her coffin was interred in a monastic hall. When Gong arrived, the hall’s door locks sprang open of their own accord, and the coffin’s lid lifted. Her mother, hearing of this, was overcome with sorrow and died within days. Over a decade later, Yang Hong himself dropped dead at the coffin’s side.

Can the sundering of marriage ever be justified?

**Eulogy:**
The Qi lord spurned the maiden’s hand and so lost his state. Zizhe’s betrothal nearly drowned him in lust. The great bond of husband and wife is matched by virtue. Who disrupts its fulfillment sows the seeds of treachery. A lineage of a hundred generations falls to a single malicious word.

*Here ends Scroll Fifteen of the* Supreme Scripture of Stimulus and Response.
*Supreme Scripture of Stimulus and Response, Scroll Sixteen.*

**The Biography of Li Changling, with a Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

Those who attain wealth and grow arrogant—
The tradition warns: gazing at the sky from the bottom of a well, they belittle all things; dwelling in idleness without discipline, they indulge their own complacency.
Before the horse’s hooves, they flaunt the skill of Yan Ying; from their lofty perch, they brandish the arms of a mantis.
Atop the towering pavilion, they fuss over the trifling fame of grinding ink; by the Cai River’s edge, they vie for the softest morsels at the feast.
Such are the ways of those who, having gained wealth by chance, become haughty.
Yet if you yourself choose arrogance, who will admire it?
Alas that they do not awaken—if today you are wealthy and proud, then tomorrow you shall bear the guilt of wealth and pride. The Supreme Lord has made this warning clear; how could it be otherwise?
Have you not heard Laozi’s words: “Wealth and honor breed arrogance, and bring misfortune upon oneself”?
Confucius said: “Duke Jing of Qi owned a thousand teams of horses, yet on the day he died, the people found nothing to praise.” But Boyi and Shuqi starved beneath Mount Shouyang, and the people still extol them to this day.
Such is the fate of those who gain wealth by chance—those whose Way is full cannot be so easily measured.
Long ago, when Master Fan Wenzheng had yet to rise to office, he studied with Master Liu on Changbai Mountain. Each day, they boiled two *sheng* of millet, waited for it to congeal, then cut it into four pieces, eating two at dawn and two at dusk. Later, both became ministers.
Han Yi and Li Ruogu once traveled together to take the examinations, sharing a single quilt and a single felt mat. Whenever they went out, they took turns serving as each other’s attendants. Later, both became ministers.
Zhu Ang gathered paulownia seeds to read by; Kong Yanzhi split pine torches to read by. Later, both became imperial attendants.
Li Xingjian, too poor to afford paper, gathered leaves to practice writing; Li Xuzhou, too poor to own books, constantly borrowed copies from others. Later, both became academicians.
Du Yan, in his youth, was desperately poor. He traveled between Meng and Luo, copying texts to support himself. Later, he rose to the rank of chief of the Bureau of Military Affairs.
Wang Sui, in his youth, was so destitute that he once fled from a creditor demanding repayment for a meal. He was seized and taken before the authorities—yet later, he too became a minister.
How vast is the gulf between those whose Way is full and those who gain wealth by chance!
Consider Shi Culai in his student days, studying in the southern capital. When Minister Wang Ji heard of his poverty, he sent a tray of food during a banquet. Shi refused it, saying: “Today is fine, but what of tomorrow?”
When the young Hengpu was at school, he endured bitter cold with neither proper clothes nor bedding. A wealthy villager offered him a fur robe, but he declined, saying: “To be resolved in poverty is the very time to cultivate oneself. If one does not restrain oneself with discipline, desire will arise, shame will be lost—where then is the work of self-cultivation?”
Such was the resolve of these gentlemen.
How could those who gain wealth by chance ever be justified in their arrogance?

A Eulogy states:
Wealth gained by chance forsakes righteousness and harms benevolence.
Scheming day and night, they flaunt their pride in broad daylight.
Burglars strut with swagger, riding grand carriages and silken cushions.
Boasting of their ill-gotten riches, they mock and ridicule the poor and upright.
Yet success and failure shift like the wind—west winds scatter dust on the granary.
To escape by any means, shameless—what manner of men are these?

The *Scripture* states: The Buddha said, “I possess two white laws that can save all sentient beings. What are these two white laws? The first is *shame (惭)*, the second is *remorse (愧)*. *Shame* is to refrain from committing sins oneself. *Remorse* is to prevent others from doing so. *Shame* is inner self-reproach. *Remorse* is to confess before others. *Shame* is to feel humiliated before others. *Remorse* is to feel humiliated before Heaven. Those without shame or remorse are called beasts.” This is the teaching of the *Nirvana Sutra*. It further states: “The robe of shame is the foremost adornment among all virtues. Shame is like an iron hook, restraining unlawful deeds. Without shame, one loses all virtue. If the heart holds shame and remorse, enmity and guilt both vanish.” This is the teaching of the *Sutra of Bequeathed Instructions*. Our Master Confucius also said: “Conduct oneself with shame.” The *Book of Rites* likewise states: “In the face of adversity, do not seek escape by any means.” Yet today, men seek escape by any means and remain utterly shameless—what kind of people are they?

Generally, shame must never leave a person, not even for a moment. Without shame, the shame of having no shame leads to every conceivable transgression—what more need be said?

Once, the monk Yuancheng was skilled in talismans and incantations. When Wang Cong’s infant son cried incessantly at night, Yuancheng uttered a single incantation, and the child fell silent. Cong deeply revered him and recommended him to Zhang Sheng, Duke Shen. At the time, Duke Shen was inspecting the northern and southern circuits of Jinghu, intending to pacify the river valleys and caves. Someone suggested that the barbarians practiced southern sorcery and feared talismans. When Duke Shen arrived in Chenzhou, he ordered Yuancheng, along with Zhang Yu, Li Ziming, and others, to enter Jiangnan to accept surrender. Upon reaching the caves, Yuancheng and his companions indulged their desires without restraint, even defiling the barbarian women. The chieftain Yuan Meng, enraged beyond endurance, impaled Zhang Yu and the others on pillars, tearing them apart. When Yuancheng’s turn came, he beat his cheeks and begged for mercy. Meng, a devout Buddhist, spared him. Yuancheng thus escaped unscathed. Upon his return, he was rewarded for his “labor in the caves” with the purple robe and the title of Master, styling himself the Grand Master of Investigation. He rode a grand horse, surrounded by attendants bearing maces and swords, utterly without shame. Did he possess shame? Where, then, were the “two white iron hooks” of his daily scripture recitations?

Fan Libo and Wang Kui, a retired official of the Bureau of Fields, were old friends from the same village. One day, Wang Kui composed a poem on the six *huo* (活) to critique current affairs. The provincial surveillance commissioner arrested Libo to coerce Kui. Libo, lacking principle, betrayed all of Kui’s past associations to save himself. As a result, Kui was convicted of slander, stripped of his rank, and exiled. Libo alone escaped punishment by informing and was soon promoted. His letter of appointment contained the phrase: “Is it not difficult to be your friend?” The judgment of contemporary moral discourse was thus evident. Yet Libo accepted his promotion with arrogance, utterly devoid of the shame of the threefold disgrace. Did he possess remorse? Where, then, were the teachings on shame from his days of study and self-conduct?

A Eulogy states:
Seeking survival by any means, shamelessly currying favor—
As a son, disloyal; as a minister, unfaithful.
In Han, Zhang Yu fell; in Jin, Jia Chong collapsed.
First fearing loss, they ended in treachery and villainy.
How to punish such men? With the wind of Boyi.
Recognizing favors, shifting blame.

The *Scripture* states: The Supreme One said: “To teach others to cultivate the heart is to cultivate the Dao. To teach others to cultivate the Dao is to cultivate the heart. Generally, the heart *is* the Dao. Before speaking of cultivating the Dao, first govern the heart. Thus, the ancients governed their hearts: when restless, they calmed it; when deviant, they corrected it; when grasping, they relinquished and restrained it; when turbid, they purified and clarified it. In action and stillness, in haste and leisure, over time, external things grew dim, while inner spirit shone clearly. Naturally, the heart’s ground became level, and the Dao was even and straight. The thought of recognizing favors and shifting blame would never arise.”

Once, Wang Wenzheng served as Chancellor. When Duke Lai sent someone to privately petition for the title of Chancellor-Envoy, Wenzheng was astonished: “The duties of general and minister—how can they be sought? Moreover, I do not accept private requests.” The Duke resented his words.

Later, an edict was issued appointing Du [Zhong] as Military Commissioner of Wusheng Army and concurrent Grand Councilor. Du entered court, wept, and expressed his gratitude: “Had Your Majesty not known me, how could I have attained this?” The Emperor said: “This was Wang Dan’s recommendation.” Only then did Du feel shame and admiration, considering himself inferior.

When Li Wenzheng served as Chancellor, if any scholar-official sought a post through private request, he would sternly refuse if he deemed the man unworthy. Yet once appointed, he would never speak of it again. His disciples asked: “Why not let them know?” He replied: “Employing the worthy is the ruler’s prerogative. If I were to inform them, it would be me currying favor and trading personal gratitude. Should the favor be claimed by me, who would bear the resentment? Would either of these two gentlemen [Wang Dan and Du] have claimed credit?”

When Wei Renpu served as Chancellor under the Zhou, Emperor Shizong was narrow-minded and quick-tempered, harsh in punishment and execution. If any inner court official or close minister offended him, Renpu would invariably take the blame upon himself. Through his intercessions, seven or eight out of ten were spared. When Cao Wuhui first campaigned against Shu alongside Wang Quanbin, Quanbin slaughtered over three thousand surrendered soldiers. Wuhui remonstrated, but was ignored. Upon their return, the Emperor was furious and determined to investigate. Wuhui then falsely confessed, and Quanbin was thus spared. When Wuhui was later reappointed to campaign against Jiangnan, the Emperor admonished him: “This time, do not repeat what you did in Xichuan—recklessly slaughtering people.” Wuhui then truthfully replied, adding: “The reason I firmly took the blame before was to prevent Quanbin from bearing sole guilt.” Thereupon, the Emperor held him in even higher esteem. Would either of these two gentlemen [Wei Renpu and Cao Wuhui] have shifted blame?

Know that those who claim credit for favors and shift blame are merely unable to govern their minds, and thus cannot escape calamity that swiftly follows. How could one instruct them with the supreme teaching that cultivating the Dao is cultivating the mind?

In praise it is said:
To please others, one bestows favors—not for oneself.
To avoid slander, one bears blame—not for oneself.
Claiming credit and shifting blame become a thicket of resentment and wrath.
One seizes the reputation for oneself—who then bears the fault?
Ice and fire lodge in the gut; even Bian Que would flee in alarm.
Selling misfortune and peddling evil—

As the saying goes: The supreme teaching states, "That which gives me life is spirit; that which destroys me is the heart." Now, to shift misfortune onto others only to have it return to oneself, to sell evil to others only to have it rebound—is this not self-destruction?

In the Qingli era, Yao Zi was serving as co-clerk of Tongcheng County in Shuzhou alongside Wang Hu. They were dispatched to Dayun Granary to measure and inspect tribute grain. Yao Zi accepted bribes of gold, silver, and valuables from the supervising officials, then falsely reported a deficit of over eight thousand *shi* of wheat. Wang Hu merely signed his name without knowledge of the matter. When the affair was exposed, they were sent to Shouzhou for investigation. Unwilling to bear the disgrace of corruption alone, Yao Zi took a gold ingot and five silver ingots he had originally received, coated them in lacquer, and bundled them with other items. He sent a trusted servant to deliver them to Wang Hu, anticipating refusal. The servant was instructed to feign a river mishap en route, claiming the letter had been water-damaged and requesting to leave the package at a roadside shrine until he could retrieve a new letter upon return. Wang Hu, unsuspecting, kept it. Soon after, the superior office suddenly issued documents to the Shouzhou investigative court, summoning Wang Hu to confront Yao Zi’s corruption charges. Only then did Wang Hu realize he had been betrayed, but his regret came too late. When brought to prison, Yao Zi falsely accused Wang Hu of colluding in the bribery. Officials retrieved the original ingots from Wang Hu’s home, and upon inspection, they proved to be pure gold. Wang Hu, speechless and unable to defend himself, soon choked with rage and died. Yao Zi then bribed the investigators heavily, shifting all blame onto Wang Hu and thus escaping punishment.

Years later, Yao Zi became Vice Prefect of Luzhou. One day, while traveling along the Huai River, Wang Hu’s wandering spirit appeared across the water, seized him, and refused to release him. From then on, Yao Zi grew deranged, as if constantly battered by unseen hands, his mouth and nose often bleeding. Though he would revive, this torment recurred five to seven times a year for three years. Neither physicians, shamans, nor Daoist talismans could cure him.

At the time, Qian Yannian served as Prefect of Luzhou, and Chen Mian as Transport Commissioner. They reported the matter to the imperial court, petitioning for the temporary dispatch of Daoist Registrar Song Zhicai to the prefecture to perform deliverance rites. Once Zhicai arrived, he memorialized the True Martial Sovereign. As the first memorial was being submitted, Yao Zi suddenly scrambled from his sickbed and prostrated himself before the True Martial’s altar, confessing in detail: “It was I who first conceived the scheme to accept the bribe—Wang Hu had no part in it. When the affair was exposed, I wrongly shifted the blame onto him, causing his unjust death. I dare implore your compassion and beg for absolution.” Qian Yannian ordered an official to record his words from the side, and upon waking, Yao Zi was shown the transcript. Overcome with shame, he resigned from office that very day and died soon after.

Guo Huangzhong, Prefect of Yun’an Commandery, once visited the Western Rosy Cloud Abbey ten *li* outside the city to burn incense. In a dream, the abbey’s tutelary deity appeared to him, saying: “Your Excellency’s beneficence has blessed this land, and its people have received much grace. Yet there is a concealed matter I dare not withhold: tomorrow, a butcher will arrive with an ox to be slaughtered. Among the nine men presented, you must examine them closely, lest an innocent man be wronged.” The next morning, the patrol bureau indeed brought nine bound men before him, along with a soldier claiming to have captured them and seeking reward. In truth, the soldier had killed the ox himself, framed the nine men, and then arrested them to claim the bounty. Guo questioned him once, and the soldier confessed.

Alas! In one case, the grievance reached the sacred hearing of the True Sovereign; in the other, the crime was foreknown by the abbey’s deity. Can one then shift blame and sell wickedness? Do heavenly punishment and karmic retribution ever cease?

**Eulogy:**
Fortune and misfortune arise from oneself;
Good and evil stem from the heart.
Guard what lies within,
And external calamities cannot invade.
Woe to those treacherous and deceitful,
Whose hearts are deep as fortress moats.
They set snares and pitfalls,
Engaging in endless strife.
Though they may hide in secret,
The Lord on High watches over you.
Seeking hollow fame through fraud—
How can such deeds endure?

The Transmission states: The Lady of the Southern Marchmount said, "People follow customs and seek after florid reputations. It is like burning incense—though all may smell its fragrance, they do not realize it scorches the one who burns it. When the incense is consumed, its qi dissipates; when a name is established, the person perishes. Thus do the lofty scorn and shun such pursuits."

The Perceiver of the Unseen and the Inaudible True Person warned the gods, saying, "A name is the fairest vessel of past and present, yet the Creator deeply abhors it. Between heaven and earth, no name remains unblemished. When a man’s name is about to rise, something must inevitably ruin it. Is a name, then, not weighty indeed? Even for one who possesses true substance, it is still forbidden—how much worse for those who merely trade in hollow reputations?"

In former times, Sheng Junmin, son of Minister Canzheng Du, was proud of his exceptional talent from youth. He passed the imperial examination with distinction, his fame resounding so that none dared surpass him. When the palace examination was held, Wang Gongchen ranked first, and Junmin was deeply aggrieved. One day, he dreamed of wandering in the netherworld and paying homage to its ruler, to whom he pleaded for the reason behind his misfortune. The ruler replied, "You are the reincarnation of Yang Su, Duke of Yue during the Sui dynasty, having passed through several lifetimes. Though you have endured poverty without rising above it, your present suffering is retribution for deeds committed in the Sui era. Even in this life, your fate remains ill-starred. Though your karmic fortune differs, your talent remains unchanged." Upon hearing this, Junmin was utterly disheartened. Later, he indeed achieved nothing and died.

Alas! That Yang Su of old should become Junmin of today—he had truly fallen from heaven to earth. Yet he did not lose his male form, which is still preferable to being reborn as a woman.

Li Shu served as Minister and was renowned for his purity and eloquence. When Xu Ling met him, he was deeply impressed, declaring himself inferior. Shu, too, was rather proud of his own abilities. Before he had a son, he suddenly died. His wife, née Yuan, later remarried Zhao Qi. One night, she dreamed of Shu weeping and saying, "Tomorrow, I will be reborn as a girl in the Liu household, east of the dead-end alley south of the Seven Emperors’ Ward crossroads. That family is extremely poor and already has many daughters—they will surely not raise me. If I die again, who knows where I might end up? I beg you, remember our old bond as husband and wife and come quickly to redeem me."

Yuan氏 made no reply. Shu said, "It seems you hesitate out of deference to Master Zhao. I shall tell him myself." Soon after, Zhao too dreamed the same dream as Yuan. At dawn, he took gold and sought out the household, where indeed a girl had just been born—already cast into a basin of water. Zhao hastily rescued her and took her home.

Alas! The once-renowned Li Shu, now reduced to an unwanted infant girl—how far he has fallen! Is this not even worse than Yang Su’s downfall? Could this be retribution for his arrogance and frivolity in the days when his fame and reputation were at their height?

**Eulogy:**
Within determines without; fame is but a guest to substance.
Supreme virtue and perfect goodness—brilliance shines forth daily.
Yet praise, like sweet dew from Ganling, and reputation, like the fleeting breath of Shaoxi,
Are but croton sores and gardenia whips—overvalued, inflamed.
The *Spring and Autumn Annals* wields its brush to condemn the heart.
To harbor a treacherous mind—
**The Scripture says:**
The Bodhisattva Earth-Holder addressed the Buddha: "I recall that in a past age, when the Tathāgata Universal Light appeared in the world, I was a bhikṣu. Wherever there were fords, crossroads, or treacherous fields hindering carts and horses, I would level the ground, build bridges, or carry sand and soil—laboring tirelessly thus. Through countless Buddhas’ appearances, until the time of the Tathāgata Vipaśyin, when the king invited the Buddha to a feast, I leveled the earth to await the Buddha. Vipaśyin placed his hand upon my head and said, ‘If you level the earth of your mind, the earth of the world will all be level.’ At once, my mind opened: I saw my body as dust, no different from the dust of the world. The nature of dust does not clash or grind—thus I attained perfect enlightenment and was named Earth-Holder.
If the earth of the mind may not be leveled, how can treachery be harbored?"

Generally, petty men take risks for fleeting gain—such schemes cannot be forestalled by wisdom or foresight. Long ago, the Attendant-in-Ordinary Cao Liyong carried himself with the pride of an old meritorious official; even the Grand Empress Dowager held him in awe. Whenever imperial favors were bestowed, Liyong often blocked their execution. As his refusals multiplied, even when three were issued in succession, he could not reject them all. Over time, this was noticed by petty men: whenever a request was denied thrice, they would petition again. The Grand Empress Dowager said, "The Attendant-in-Ordinary has already refused." The petitioner replied, "I have already informed the Attendant-in-Ordinary’s wet nurse—or his kinswoman, Lady Chun—who said he had consented. Liyong truly knew nothing of this." Later, when the favor was granted, it was only because three refusals had already been made. Enraged, the Grand Empress Dowager’s wrath gave rise to the disaster of Cao Rui.

Chen Guan, serving as Vice Minister of the Three Departments, had an underling of extreme cunning who secretly curried favor with the powerful. Matters in the ministry were routinely referred to him, and when he voiced his assent, the ministers and vice ministers would feign yawns, as if unworthy of his courtesy. Guan, indignant, resolved to dismiss him. The underling, sensing this, served him all the more diligently. Over a year passed without any delays in affairs, and Chen began to treat him well. Once, while entertaining guests, he entrusted the underling with preparing the feast. Unaware of his true intentions—his treacherous heart—the next day, the underling brought a ten-year-old girl to the street outside Donghua Gate, wailing, "Vice Minister Chen invited guests on such-and-such a day and ordered me to prepare the feast. Not even a tenth of what was needed is ready! What am I to do? I must sell this girl to raise the money!" He then bribed the secret agents of the Imperial City Bureau to report this. The court believed him and nearly demoted Chen on the spot. Only through the intervention of the chief minister was the matter resolved—yet a year later, Chen was still dismissed because of it.

Alas! Were the schemes of these two men not treacherous? Such treachery—how can it be guarded against?

**Eulogy:**
A treacherous heart towers ten thousand *ren*,
Within it, five weapons arise.
Yi Fu’s faint smile, Lin Pu’s gentle demeanor—
Their faces give nothing away; fortune and misfortune strike like lightning.
They boast of their cunning, surpassing even the gods.
Yet how could they know the Creator sees all, unseen?
To crush another’s strengths—
**The Scripture says:**
Luo Zhongxin asked Mount Tortoise, "To fully realize the mind is to know one’s nature. What is the principle of fully realizing the mind?"
Mount Tortoise replied, "Before discussing the full realization of the mind, one must first understand what the mind is. The mind is clarity, penetration, vastness, stillness, and unity. If you grasp this clearly, then we may speak of fully realizing the mind. Without such understanding, what mind is there to realize?"
The Great Silence Chan Master said, "The mind and nature of man are like the cold moon. When it is cold, water freezes into ice. When it warms, ice melts back into water. When beings are deluded, their nature congeals into mind. When they awaken, their mind dissolves back into nature—it is ever thus."
Alas! These teachings urge us to recognize the mind and illuminate the nature.
Can those who crush another’s strengths understand this?

Long ago, Mu Xiu was renowned for his poetry and often traveled between the capital and Luoyang. Someone inscribed his verses on the walls of the palace, and when Emperor Zhenzong saw them, he marveled, asking whose poetry it was. His attendants replied, "Mu Xiu’s." The emperor said, "With such literary talent, why have the ministers not recommended him?" Duke Jin interjected, "This man’s conduct does not match his writing." With this single remark, the emperor never inquired about him again. Was Duke Jin’s heart clarity, penetration, vastness, stillness, and unity? Or was it the dissolution of mind into nature? This is why he died without a place to rest.

When Zhang Yaoya governed Shu, a clerk and adjutant, old and infirm, neglected his duties. The governor reprimanded him, saying, "Why do you not retire?" The next day, the adjutant requested leave and bid farewell with a poem, the gist of which was:
*The autumn light is as thin as an official’s favor,
The mountain hues pale beside the longing to return home.*
Zhang was startled and apologized, saying, "This is my fault. Among my colleagues is such a poet, and I did not know—how could I not be at fault?"

Thus, he firmly urged him to stay and consoled him with recommendations. Had he not devoted himself to the learning of exhausting the mind and knowing one’s nature, or to the doctrine of dissolving the mind into nature, could he have attained such understanding? This is why he comprehended life and death before his death, already able to foreknow the month and day, secretly recording it upon his posthumous portrait.

An ancient saying goes: "A single deviant thought creates a gulf as vast as heaven and earth."

**Eulogy:**
A person’s slightest goodness,
Is like beholding a propitious omen.
Nurture and perfect it,
Lest it be thwarted or disturbed.
Whoever harbors envy,
Will sink into obscurity.
Concealing another’s innate worth,
Only deepens one’s own negligence.
Harming oneself and others,
Invites calamity that pierces to the bone.
Yet shielding one’s own flaws—

As the *Tradition* records: Someone asked Master Sun the True Man, "On the doctrine of nurturing one’s nature, I wish to hear it." The True Man replied: "To nurture one’s nature is to cultivate one’s inherent nature. The essential lies in perfecting all conduct; even without medicinal elixirs, one may preserve longevity. If one’s virtue and conduct are deficient, even daily ingesting golden cinnabar and jade nectar will be of no avail. Thus, in daily life, can one shield one’s flaws? If one shields them, then in daily life, nothing will not be flawed—how can this be spoken of? If only one realized the folly of shielding flaws, and over time nurtured one’s inherent nature until no flaws remained, one would be called a person of perfect goodness. How distant then would one be from transcendence?"

Formerly, Huang Wanyou cultivated the Dao in the uninhabited wilds of southern Qian. Wang Jian invited him to Chengdu, honoring him with utmost reverence as a teacher. One day, he asked about the method of ingesting elixirs. Wanyou replied: "I am no immortal, nor one who ingests elixirs. I merely empty my mind to nurture qi, cultivate benevolence in my conduct, and minimize my faults." Later, he earnestly sought to return and could no longer be summoned.

Liu Yi hailed from an illustrious family and passed the imperial examinations in his youth. Within a decade, he rose to an official post of two thousand *shi*. During the Chongning era, he governed Hanyang. Whenever he had leisure, he would disguise himself in plain clothes and wander among the mountains of Dabie, conversing daily with farmers and rustics. He inquired into the efficacy of prefectural governance and the hardships of the common folk. If he found any matter amiss and deemed it reformable, he would return and rectify it. Eventually, he encountered an extraordinary person and thus attained transcendence.

As for these two gentlemen, would they have shielded their flaws? Could they have had flaws at all? This is how they nurtured their inherent nature and ultimately ascended to the rank of immortals.

Let me further expound on lesser examples, that those who cling to shielding their flaws may each take heed and strictly guard against it.

Li Ji rose through the ranks to high office. On the day of his death, he summoned his son and said: "I have no other regrets in life, save for one thing: during my time in Hangzhou, I once purchased a set of *White Anthology*—that is my sole remorse." Cai Junmo once wrote in large characters on fine Wu paper to embellish the matter. Huang Zongdan, while governing Suzhou, had Wang Zhi as his assistant magistrate.

One day, over a hundred counterfeit coiners were apprehended and sent to prison for interrogation. Huang Zongdan joyfully said to Wang Zhi: "I caught them all using my secret methods." Wang Zhi’s expression darkened as he replied: "When crimes leave no trace, you employ secret methods to ensnare them and then consign them to death—do you still rejoice in this?" Zongdan suddenly rose from his seat, stepped down, and bowed in apology: "Without your words, I would have nearly erred."

Generally, those of earlier generations were often like this. In this present age, are there truly no such men? To become such a person, one must first embody their example.

**Eulogy:**
The malady of concealment admits no remedy.
Clinging to wrongs, lost in delusion—
calamity’s end is assured.
A thousand virtues struggle to take root,
while ten thousand evils find their foundation.
The sages of old had faults,
yet welcomed others’ awareness.
Those who shield their flaws
deem themselves wiser than Confucius.

*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Sixteen (conclusion)
*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Seventeen

**Biography of Li Changling, with Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

**Coercion Through Power and Threats**

The *Biography* states: When Wang Wenzheng governed the realm, in the early Tianxi era, Xue Jiansu served as Transport Commissioner for Jiang-Huai. Upon taking leave, the minister offered no other counsel but this: "The people of the Southeast are utterly exhausted." When Zhang Shixun became Jiangxi Transport Commissioner and took leave, the minister again offered no other counsel but this: "The court has wrung every last profit from the land."

Generally, to guide the ruler and nourish the people, one ought to act thus. Now, those in high office show no concern for prefectures and counties, while those governing prefectures and counties show no concern for the common folk—this is coercion through power and threats.

Formerly, when Song Xuan’gong served as Judge of the Three Bureaus, the court issued an edict to remit all outstanding debts nationwide. Yet sixty-eight prefectures delayed their reports past the deadline. He memorialized the throne, proposing that surveillance commissioners be charged with verifying the accounts by a set date. As a result, several million in debts were truly remitted, and three thousand two hundred and six prisoners were released from their shackles.

When Wang Wenmu (Qin Ruo) oversaw debt settlements, the Bureau of Finance submitted a petition to cancel all overdue taxes nationwide to extend imperial grace. From the Qiande to the Xianping eras, over ten million were remitted, and more than three thousand prisoners were freed from confinement.

When Minister of Personnel Chen Buliang oversaw the Bureau of Revenue in the Three Bureaus, eighteen brine wells in Rongzhou had long since become exhausted. Yet the authorities continued to enforce the original quotas, confiscating the property of three hundred fifteen households. He repeatedly argued the precedents, restoring all confiscated assets and annually remitting over three hundred thousand in nominal quotas. These were men who held office but lacked coercive power—yet how could those who rely on threats and intimidation achieve the same? Their purpose was simply to avoid further burdening the people’s strength.

There was another official who likewise dared not unjustly seize anything. Let me recount it fully, that later generations may know of leniency and compassion.

At the founding of the dynasty, when Wang Zhi was sent as an emissary to equalize miscellaneous taxes in the Two Zhe circuits, the former law of the Qian family levied three *per mu*. Upon his arrival, he ordered it reduced to one *per mu*. The court reprimanded him for acting without authorization. He replied: “The Two Zhe are now the sovereign’s people—how can we again follow the laws of a spurious state?” The tax of one *per mu* thus began with Zhi.

When Wang Gongchen proposed reimposing the salt monopoly in Hebei, Zhang Fangping vehemently opposed it: “Under the old law of Hebei, violators were put to death. The people petitioned to equalize the quota and pay it along with their taxes, and only then was the prohibition relaxed. If we now revisit this matter, it would amount to a second imposition.” Thereupon the emperor understood and abolished it that very day.

When the people arrived, they held Buddhist rites for him for seven days.

Li Yunze, governing Tanzhou, found the Ma clan had imposed brutal exactions on the prefecture’s people: a tax of silk called "land-tax silk"; for every room in a house, a levy of one *zhang* and three *chi* of silk, called "house-output silk"; and for every plow-ox, an annual tax of four *sheng* of rice—even if the ox died, the tax was still due, called the "dry-bones tax." Upon his arrival, he abolished them all.

Chen Shiqing, governing Guangzhou, found the region suffering from the harm of a per-capita salt purchase. Upon his arrival, he memorialized to have it abolished. Thus the people of Five Rams first had whole clothes and full bellies. The people of Guangzhou sang:
*"Father Shao, Mother Chen—
freed us from two pains."*
For the former governor Shao Ye had dug an inner moat to berth boats, sparing the people the harm of the wind-tossed waves.

Alas! Better to show leniency and mercy, winning the praise of ten thousand mouths, than to ride roughshod with threats and coercion, incurring the people’s resentment and the gods’ wrath. Has one not heard the saying that where joy gathers, fortune arises, and where grievances accumulate, disaster strikes?

**Eulogy:**
*"Mighty Qi’s might—
preserved Wei, repelled the Di."*

At Jiagu’s might, the three armies recoiled in awe.
At Xiangru’s single rage, the King of Qin was rebuked.
To untangle strife and rescue from distress—this is called true power.
Yet to ravage creatures and annihilate the people—even jackals and tigers would refuse such food.

To indulge in violence and slaughter:
As the *Sutra of Holding the Earth* states, the sin of taking life casts beings into the Three Evil Realms, and even if reborn among humans, they suffer five retributions.
First, for tormenting sentient beings, they fall into hell at the slightest touch of scorching heat, enduring every agony.
Second, for lacking compassion and acting contrary to humanity, upon exhausting their hellish punishment, they plunge into the realm of beasts, to be butchered by men.
Third, for craving rich flavors, after their beastly suffering ends, they descend into the hungry ghost realm, daily tormented by hunger and thirst.
Fourth, for cutting short lives, once their ghostly punishment is complete, though they regain human form, their lives are short.
Fifth, for the moment of killing, when myriad sufferings converge upon those beings, they not only live briefly but also endure frequent illness.
Thus speaks the Buddha—how could it not be so?

Pity those who fail to see that humans and beasts, though their forms differ, share the same nature of life.
But reflect through poetry:
If I were ill now, would I willingly die?
No—I would seek physicians and diviners, hastening to recover.
If my allotted span were at its end, would I still accept death?
No—I would pray to heaven and earth, begging for extended years.
Since I fear death, do they not fear it too?
Since I cling to life, do they not cling to it as well?

Once, Zhang Daoji was fond of hunting. Beside his ancestral tombs stretched a vast forest, teeming with deer and fowl. Daily, Daoji and a band of ruffians roamed there, setting nets on all sides—what they called "the sky-spanning net." A single sweep would trap no fewer than hundreds. When the haul grew too great to gather, they simply trampled the creatures from above, crushing them to death, then divided the spoils to carry away.

Once, beside the Mingyang Abbey, he shot and killed a hare—tiny, with a notch in its white ear. From that moment, he grew distraught, as though maddened. He gathered all his hunting gear from days past and burned it, then built a hut beside the abbey, dwelling there day after day.

One day, two hares approached him directly and spoke in human words: "I have been a hare for three hundred years, living by the eastern tombs of the Zhang family. Now you have killed me."

The other said: "I have been a hare for one hundred and eighty years, hiding beside Mingyang Abbey. Once, an eagle seized me, tearing off one of my ears, but I struggled free and escaped. Twenty years have passed since then. Against eagles, hounds, nets, and traps, I could always evade them—never did I expect you to use a crossbow. I have already lodged my grievance with Heaven. You must repay my life."

Not long after, he indeed died.

Liu Xiang was also fond of hunting. Whenever the urge struck, he would go out, undeterred by cold or heat. The creatures he wounded and killed over time were beyond counting. In the seventh year of Shaoxing, he suddenly fell gravely ill: three growths appeared on his back, resembling turtles but black, lurking beneath his skin and flesh. After several days, their heads and limbs took shape, becoming three distinct turtles. Soon they could move, and when fish was offered as bait, they would thrust their heads forward, as if eager to feed. Before long, they began gnawing at his flesh on either side, causing unbearable pain. After more than a month of this, he died.

Five days after his death, he possessed his daughter-in-law and spoke: "For the killing and wounding of lives in my lifetime, I have received this retribution. Even now, the underworld court is swiftly punishing me. Quickly gather my hunting gear and burn it—perhaps they will know I have repented."

Thus, can wanton slaughter and harm be justified? Even dogs, when provoked to kill, are counted among the ten foremost transgressions—how can such violence be indulged?

In praise it is said:
Punishment is to halt crime,
Arms are to quell disorder.

Grieve with compassion when passing judgment, mourn with propriety in funerals, and wage war only when unavoidable—thus may the necessity of killing be discerned. To cherish the people and love all creatures is to make no distinction between noble and base. Those cruel and destructive men, beast-hearted though human-faced, wantonly cut and waste. As the *Daoist Canon* records: the three stars of Weaving Maidens govern silk and precious treasures, while the four stars of Woman’s Lodge oversee the weaving and tailoring of cloth. How then can wanton cutting and waste be justified? Have you not heard how the silkworm originated from the transformation of Horse Neigh?

In a former life, the Great Master Horse Neigh was once king of Vaiśālī. In his realm lived a people shaped like horses yet clothed in nothing. The king, exercising his divine power, divided his own body to become silkworms, that they might have garments. Later, reborn in Central India, the king’s former subjects—those horse-shaped folk—yearned for him and neighed in sorrow without cease, whence he was called Horse Neigh, the Twelfth Patriarch.

In general, a single garment costs a thousand silkworm lives. According to the Buddha’s teaching, even wearing silk incurs a debt to be repaid—how much worse, then, to cut and waste it without cause? Thus did Emperor Wen of Han ensure his favored ladies’ robes did not trail upon the ground. Emperor Wenzong of Tang once bared his sleeve before his ministers, saying, “This garment has been washed thrice.” Duke Wen of Jin wore no double-layered silk; Zixia’s robes were patched like a quail’s plumage; Ji Wenzi kept no concubine clad in silk; Yan Ying’s concubines wore no silk; Dong Wei pieced together scraps into a robe called “Hundred Knots”; and Ji Tong wore no duplicate garments—all in frugal reverence for fortune.

Have you not heard that wanton cutting and waste bring retribution after death? In the early years of Shaoxing, Guo Yinzhi’s daughter, named Yin Feng, was seized by the underworld court and saw countless sinners. Among them was a woman surnamed Zhao, named Twelve, bound in the corridor. Before her lay several heaps of cloth—shredded bolts, remnants, and countless garments both new and old. Yin Feng asked, “What crime is this?” The underworld officer replied, “This woman delighted in fine silks and wasted bolts by cutting them. She is ordered to mend every piece back into whole cloth before she may be released.”

Zhao Shizhou’s wife, née Quan, after dying for several days, possessed her maid Lai Xi and said, “In life, I squandered silk for shoes and stockings, and used excess water for washing my hair and feet. The Water Bureau of the underworld now punishes me, flogging me daily with unbearable torment. Pray convey this to Shizhou.” Her family, hearing this, was overcome with grief. How then can wanton cutting and waste be deemed without sin?

Moreover, adorning oneself to seduce others incurs even graver sin—how can one not take heed?

**In Praise:**
Spring fills the cloud-woven curtains,
The weaving maid labors through the night.
Thread by thread, the measure grows—
Only after endless toil is a bolt complete.
Embroidery drapes the walls and halls,
Silk robes clothe the lowliest servant.
Who pities the frostbitten fingers,
The ceaseless clatter of the freezing loom?
The mother scolds Gongsun for flaunting his coarse cloth,
Yet even this is spun from forbidden excess.
To slaughter without ritual propriety—

As the saying goes: Some flay fish for their rosy flesh, others cage crabs in troughs to keep them alive. Such utter failure to transform one’s nature plunges swiftly into the Three Evil Realms, swifter than an arrow—hardly worth debate. How can one not realize all creatures possess awareness, no different from ourselves in essence?

Humans know to love their kin—
When Deng Zhi shot the mother ape, she fell from the tree, and her young swiftly plucked the arrow to seal her wound, then bore her away on its back.
Do they not love their kin?

Humans know to love their children—
When He Quan shot the mother stork, the arrow pierced her belly, yet she clung to life, wrenching the shaft with her beak until her fledgling tumbled free.
Do they not love their children?

Humans know to revere their mates—
In the kingdom of Huan, when a wild bull elephant died, the herd of cows encircled him, refusing to leave until they had buried him with all their strength.
Do they not revere their mates?

Humans know to cherish their spouses—
When Zhang Ziming shot the mandarin duck, killing the female and boiling her in a pot, the male, seeing this from afar, plunged into the seething cauldron without hesitation, crying once before perishing.
Do they not cherish their spouses?

Humans know to repay kindness—
The mountain spirit honored the loyalty of a flock of parrots. Once, when the parrots dwelled in a distant mountain, the beasts there loved them and would not harm them. Later, when the parrots returned home and saw the distant mountain ablaze, they flew into the water, soaked their wings, and scattered droplets into the sky to quench the flames. The mountain spirit, moved by their righteousness, instantly extinguished the fire.
Do they not repay kindness?

Humans know to nurse grudges—
Liu Yongxi kept a dog. When he served as magistrate of Qiancheng County, a guest arrived, and he offered steamed buns. Yongxi gave one to the dog, but the guest said, “Feeding dogs and swine to humans was scorned by the ancients—how much more so with delicacies?” The dog refused to eat, glared, and left. That night, it waited until the guest slept, then forced its way in. The guest, sensing its intent, climbed onto a beam to escape. The dog leapt onto the bed, found no one, and in a fury, hurled itself to its death.
Do they not nurse grudges?

Humans know to remain loyal to their masters—
On the day Wei Dajian’s horse was to die, he prayed, “I owe you much. Now you are ill, and I have done all I can to heal you. If you must perish, go outside—do not die before me, for I cannot bear it.”

The horse then knelt upon its forelegs, as if in farewell, rose with a mournful sigh, and only after stepping outside did it breathe its last.
Do they not remain loyal to their masters?

Humans know to care for the orphaned—
as with the cat recorded by Han Yu of Changli. Two cats had both given birth, but one suddenly died, and the other nursed its orphan.
Do they not care for the orphaned?

Humans know to heed admonition—
as with the bees invoked by the monk Renxi. The monk Renxi once wished to build a Buddhist shrine, and a man offered a great sandalwood tree as an offering. When the monk went to fell it, tens of thousands of giant bees swarmed and gathered upon it, so that the workmen dared not approach. The monk admonished them, saying: “You have come to this through evil karma. I am performing Buddhist rites, and if you do not yield to me, I will burn you after three days if you do not depart.” When the appointed time came, not a single bee remained.
Do they not heed admonition?

Humans know to cultivate realization—
as with the five hundred bats of the South Seas, which dwelt in a withered tree. One day, a group of merchants gathered beneath it and lit a fire. Among them was a man reciting the *Abhidharma Piṭaka*.

Before long, the fire flared up, and the tree was consumed in flames. The five hundred bats, so rapt in listening to the sound of the Dharma that they could not bear to leave, all perished in the fire. Later, they were reborn as humans—bright, keen, and quick-witted—renounced their homes to cultivate the Dao, and together attained the fruit of sagehood. Later, King Kaniṣka and the Venerable Pārśva gathered five hundred sages in Kashmir to compose the *Vibhāṣā Śāstra*. Those five hundred sages were none other than the five hundred bats of former days.

Do they not cultivate realization?

In general, humans and creatures are not inherently different; what sets them apart is the power of fortune and karma, and the shell of the body. Have you not heard of the power of not killing? Long ago, Aṅgulimāla was once a king who delighted most in slaughter. Repenting his sins, he left the household life and went begging. Arriving at a household where a woman was in difficult labor, she clutched him and begged for help. Aṅgulimāla said, “I have only just entered the Way—how can I save you? Wait until I return and ask the Buddha.” When he returned, the Buddha said, “Hurry back to the bedside of the woman in labor and loudly proclaim: ‘I have come from the Dharma of the sages and have never taken a life.’ Upon hearing these words, no child fails to be born.” Aṅgulimāla followed the instruction, and the child was indeed delivered.

Alas! This was but a single declaration of not killing, yet its power to deliver from distress was so great. If such is the case, then how much more power must there be in wanton butchery to sink one into suffering?

**Eulogy:**
A scholar, if not for necessity,
should not kill dogs or swine.
To eat meat for seventy days—
its weight is thus.
For offerings to guests or ancestors,
it may be unavoidable.
How dare one indulge the palate,
wielding knife and spoon at will?
Beholding the suffering of living beings,
feel their pain as your own.
To scatter and waste the five grains—

The *Tradition* says: The Celestial Granary of the five grains resides among the twenty-eight constellations, governed by the star *Wēi*. Generally, humans regard food as heaven; to go a single day without eating is to suffer the pangs of hunger. Thus, the sages devoted themselves to agriculture and valued grain. The Son of Heaven plowed the fields himself to encourage farming, and in spring and summer, prayed to the Supreme Emperor for abundant harvests. How then can the five grains be scattered and wasted?

Once, during a famine in the Three Rivers region, the price of grain soared, and people resorted to gathering mountain tubers and wild vines to survive. The dead numbered no fewer than fifteen in ten. At that time, Wang Fajin, having completed his merit and attained immortality, ascended to the heavens. Yet he returned to plead with the Emperor on behalf of the people. The Emperor said: “Humans occupy the great position among the Three Powers, embodying the harmony of heaven and earth. To attain human form and be born in the Central Land is not easily achieved. Heaven moves the origin of the four seasons; earth endows the essence of the five phases, producing the five grains and a hundred fruits to nourish the people. Yet humans fail to appreciate the nurturing grace of heaven and earth, carelessly wasting grain, disdaining silk and hemp, so that farmers and weaving women toil without enough to eat, exhaust themselves without warmth, and are further ungrateful for their labor. This is why the divine spirits reproach them, and heaven and earth do not protect them.”

Recently, the local administrators of mountains and rivers all submitted memorials, reporting that people despise and discard the five grains, failing to value the foundation of clothing and food. The Supreme Emperor had already commanded the Bureau of Mount Taihua to summon the spirits of the five grains, causing their cultivation to fail and leading gradually to famine as a form of punishment, hoping to chasten their hearts. Yet soon after, the Most High issued a merciful decree: “The Great Dao cherishes life and must first save beings. Though the recent famine was brought upon themselves by the people, they know not the cause and have no path to repentance or redemption. You shall now serve as an attendant in the Celestial Palace. Temporarily return to the mortal world and universally proclaim to the people that they must repent their sins, treasure and cherish agriculture and sericulture, and not dare to waste or squander. This, too, shall be your hidden merit.”

Fajin received the decree and returned, speaking his message before departing again. Liu Yuanzhen was also one who attained the Dao. In his youth, he dreamed of a Daoist who fed him a peach, after which he could abstain from grain. One day, on the road in Zhongnan, he encountered a nobleman with a grand retinue. Yuanzhen said to him, “Have some regard for my people.” The nobleman nodded in assent. A companion asked, “Who is that?” Yuanzhen replied, “That is the mountain spirit. The Supreme Emperor, seeing that the wheat harvest would be abundant but that people would not cherish it, ordered that two-tenths be reaped. I pleaded on behalf of the people.” Soon after, a great wind and rain arose, ruining two-tenths of the wheat. From this, we know all the more that the five grains truly must not be carelessly discarded.

Moreover, each person’s allotment of sustenance is fixed by destiny. For example, if one has a thousand strings of cash and spends one hundred a day, it will last ten thousand days. If one spends a string a day, it will last a thousand days. Between a thousand days and ten thousand days—would you not prefer the latter?

Formerly, Minister Feng Ji often said: “In my youth, I personally witnessed Xuedou teaching people to cherish their fortune, saying: ‘Humans do not die by fate but by the exhaustion of their allotted blessings.’” Yuan Houzhi died and was revived, and recounted: “When I first arrived in the netherworld, the master said to me: ‘Your blessings are not yet exhausted; in time, you will rise to the rank of minister in two bureaus. However, you must cherish your fortune to do so.’” From then on, Houzhi reduced his expenses in every matter. Even with a single cup of rice, he would first set some aside before eating. In all his provisions, he dared not exceed his due. Later, he indeed attained the position of chief minister and lived past seventy. If even those with meager fortune must cherish it, how much more should others?

The *Gazetteer of Wuyun Mountain* records that the Great Master Zhi Feng was once seated in meditation in the Hall of Samantabhadra when suddenly a spirit knelt before him. The master asked, “Who are you?” The spirit replied, “I am the guardian of the precepts.” The master said, “I have long been troubled by past transgressions—do you know of them?” The spirit said, “What sins have you committed? However, there is one small fault. Since you have asked, I dare not conceal it. For example, the water used to rinse your alms bowl is also the property of the donor. To discard it as you do is not proper. From now on, you will suffer a minor illness.”

The spirit vanished upon finishing its words. Thereafter, Master Zhi Feng indeed suffered from a stomach ailment for thirteen years.

Alas! Even the leftover rinsing water, when discarded, brings such consequences—how much more so for other things?

**Eulogy:**
When the palace guard discarded food, Emperor Yuan rebuked him with severity.
When Jia Shirong jested by planting rice in a thief’s footprint, his father, Pei Shixing, meted out stern punishment.
Food is life itself—its significance is not trifling.
Revered as "Heaven," it bears the highest mark of respect.
A single grain of rice, a single kernel of millet—no pearl or jade can surpass their worth.
Yet how often do we thoughtlessly burden the people?

It is recorded that when Shao Bowen first entered office, he sought counsel from Master Cheng Yi. The master said: *"Whenever you handle official documents, if you can ease even a fraction of urgency in their demands, the people will receive that same measure of relief."*

Ah! The master’s words may truly be called benevolent.
If others wish for peace, how can I not wish the same?
If others desire joy, how can I not desire it?
To claim comfort and ease for oneself while imposing toil and hardship upon others—this is true heartlessness.

Let me recount how the elders, when governing the people, dared not inflict the slightest burden, that all may know and emulate their example.

When Lü Wenjing oversaw the criminal courts of the Two Zhe provinces, the capital was in the midst of constructing grand palaces and temples, requiring timber from the south. The officials enforced deadlines with excessive harshness, and some laborers died in service. Those who fled were branded as fugitives, and their wives and children were arrested and imprisoned.

Lord Lü submitted a memorial outlining his concerns, arguing that in the depths of winter, when rivers ran low, the people suffered greatly from hauling timber, and that the deadlines should be relaxed until the waterways flowed freely again, when soldiers could be dispatched to transport the logs with ease. The emperor, pleased, heeded his advice. From this, his compassion for the people and concern for the realm became known, and he was subsequently elevated to high office.

When Sima Chi served as magistrate of Guangshan County, the imperial palace caught fire, and all counties were ordered to supply bamboo and timber. The prefectural decree demanded delivery within three days. Sima, knowing the region produced no large bamboo, had to purchase it from Qi and Huang, which could not arrive in three days. He therefore set a separate deadline for the people, with penalties for late delivery. Sheng Du, the prefect, flew into a rage upon hearing this. Yet when Guangshan delivered its bamboo, it was the first among all counties to do so.

When Lord Fan Zhennan governed Qingshe, the Hebei region suffered a severe grain shortage, and the tribute grain from Qingshe was to be transported to Bozhou for collection. The people were greatly burdened by the transport, yet grain prices in Hebei were not particularly high. Lord Fan instructed the people to pay three *huan* per *hu* at their local prefecture, then dispatched his staff to carry the funds to Bozhou, where they offered double the price to purchase grain. The high price attracted vast quantities, and within five days, the quota was met, with surplus funds remaining for several years. Lord Fan distributed the remainder to the people according to their contributions.

When Wang Ji served as registrar of Longxi, the prefectures of Fujian were ordered to procure crane feathers for arrows. As crane feathers were not a common resource, and the officials pressed the people with excessive urgency, the people could not endure the hardship. Wang instructed them to substitute goose feathers instead and promptly dispatched a memorial to the court via courier. The court approved his proposal, and the practice extended to other prefectures.

Ah! If all those who governed the people were like these gentlemen, would not those who impose toil and hardship feel ashamed?

**Eulogy:**
Rest and peace—these are the desires of all.
A year’s labor for the people should not exceed three days.
Axes should fell trees in season; nets should not be cast too often.
When mountains and rivers are at peace, birds and beasts multiply.
Yet when petty men disturb them, the cries of wild geese and cranes fade in and out.

*This concludes Chapter Seventeen of the* Taishang Ganying Pian. *Chapter Eighteen of the* Taishang Ganying Pian *follows.*
