**The Biography of Li Changling, with a Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

To ruin a family and seize its wealth and treasures—this is recorded in the *Biographies*: if such destruction arises unintentionally, the wrong is already no small matter. But if one acts for the sake of wealth and treasures, thereby deliberately ruining a family, it is truly a great evil. Let us recount those who, having once destroyed a family, later restored its property—one or two virtuous deeds—so that those who govern the people in later times, should they encounter such matters, may all act likewise. In this way, those whose families have been ruined may yet find benefit.

When Liu Chang governed Yangzhou, the prefecture contained a reservoir called Leibei, originally belonging to the people but later seized by the authorities to store water for the convenience of canal transport. Over the years, the reservoir fell into disrepair, and the canal transport no longer relied upon it. Eventually, it was occupied by the Hao clan, and though the original landowners repeatedly petitioned, they received no redress. Upon taking office, Liu immediately ruled that the land be returned. Thus, thirty-six households—over three hundred men and women—regained their fields and were spared from hunger and want.

When Hou Shuxian served as magistrate of Sizhou County, the county contained many confiscated fields. Among them was the estate of Li Cheng, encompassing ten *li* in circumference, with a river flowing through its center—some of the most fertile land in the region. A hundred tenant families, all prosperous clans, had farmed it for generations. The original assessed price of fifteen thousand strings of cash had never been met, and when Jia Weigong held power, he sought to raise the price by another five thousand strings. Chen Daogu, dispatched on official business, arrived to discuss increasing the price with Hou. Hou sighed and said: "Does the gentleman know the full story of this matter? Li Cheng, during the reign of Emperor Taizu, served as a wine officer. When the goods under his care were damaged by flooding, he was held liable for the loss, assessed at five thousand strings of cash. At the time, the court had allocated funds from the Ministry of Revenue, instructing local officials to purchase materials for arrows, bows, and crossbows in preparation for military needs. When Li Chongjin rebelled and the court prepared to raise troops, the pre-purchased materials failed to arrive. Enraged, Emperor Taizu ordered that all those indebted to the government have their property confiscated. Though Li Cheng had never personally received the funds, the officials, fearing the emperor’s wrath, dared not distinguish his case, and his estate was seized along with the rest. His descendants were thus reduced to ruin. It is regrettable that the prime minister did not correct this injustice and return their land. Would it not be better to reduce the price by five thousand strings, allowing Li’s descendants to repurchase it? The government would still profit handsomely, and Li’s descendants would avoid destitution. What does the gentleman think?" Chen replied: "I was truly unaware of these details. If it benefits the people, how could I object?" Thus, the price was reduced by five thousand strings.

Hou then summoned Li Cheng’s descendants and explained the matter. They wept and bowed, saying: "We are deeply grateful for your kindness, but our family is too poor to afford it." Hou next called the tenants together and addressed them: "You were all once humble households who, by farming Li’s land, have grown prosperous—your granaries full, your families elevated to wealth. Now the government sells this land, and the Li family, impoverished, cannot afford it. If another buys it, they will surely tear down your granaries, dismantle your homes, and drive you from your livelihood. Will you still enjoy such prosperity? Would it not be better for all one hundred households to pool your money, allowing the Li family to repurchase the land? I will then extend your payment deadlines. In this way, the land returns to the Li family, and you retain your tenancy for generations to come—both sides benefit. Does this not seem right?" The tenants all bowed and said: "We will do as you say." Thus, Li Cheng’s descendants regained their land.

Those who ruin families and seize their wealth and treasures—could they ever act thus? It is a pity they do not comprehend the heavens and hells, for their fate is sealed by such deeds. What delusion drives them to act so?

**Eulogy:**
A family is built through toil and hardship,
Passed down through generations, gathering and preserving.
Who are these bandits and plunderers,
Seizing what others have amassed?
The wolves in power, the tigers of harsh governance,
Lurking in your halls, specters watching at your door.
They breach the dikes, set fires,
To harm the people’s homes.
As the *Biographies* say: floods and flames,
The cruelest disasters in the world.
Yet they breach the dikes, set fires,
To harm the people’s homes—can their crimes be light?
This is why the Perfected Ones above cannot tolerate them,
And the luminous spirits will surely punish them.

In the second year of Tiansheng, the Yellow River’s embankment at Daming Prefecture suddenly collapsed, and the water surged forth with great force. When Han Weigong governed Beijing, an official rushed to report: "At the breach, a great turtle has long been the source of trouble. When it lurks beneath the water, the river flows calmly. When it surfaces, the waters flood. Now it has surfaced—what is to be done?" At that moment, the emissary Zheng Gui, dispatched on a mission, happened to arrive at the office. He shot the turtle dead with a single arrow. Later, the turtle lodged a complaint in the underworld, and King Yama summoned Zheng Gui to face it in judgment. That very day, the Perfected Warrior, the Spiritually Responsive True Lord, descended to Mount Luofeng to review the ledgers of life and death. By his grace, the demonic turtle was exonerated, shattered into dust in midair, so that it could never again harm the people. Zheng Gui was then ordered to be escorted back to the mortal realm by a divine messenger.

If this is how the gods deal with those who breach dikes, how much more will they punish those who harm the people?

Li Yuan was most filial to his mother. One evening, a traveler arrived seeking lodging. Li was by the stream, boiling a chicken, and the guest assumed it was for his meal. When the rice was served, however, it was coarse and unpolished, and the guest grew furious, refusing to eat before storming out. Li explained: "My mother is ill and craves meat. Living in the mountains, we have none, so I boiled this chicken for her. I had nothing to offer you." The guest’s anger only grew. That night, he crept behind the house, set a fire, and fanned the flames toward Li’s home. Suddenly, the heavens sent rain and reversed the wind, extinguishing the fire.

The villagers gathered to watch and saw a man lying in the ashes, his torch still clutched in hand. Upon closer inspection, it was the guest, who muttered: "I am a knight-errant, fleeing here in exile. Never did I expect the master to treat me so. When I set the fire, I clearly saw a great spirit, towering like a divine lord, standing in midair and commanding the flames to reverse. Thus the fire turned back upon me, and now I am dead." With these words, he expired.

Is arson, then, permissible?

**Eulogy:**
Officials who govern the people, if they act carelessly and shrink from duty—
Failing to guard against floods is no different from breaching dikes.
Failing to extinguish fires with vigor is akin to setting them.
They ascribe disasters to heaven while shirking human responsibility.
When judging crimes by intent, deeds and thoughts are one.
To disrupt another’s plans is to ruin their achievements.

As the saying goes: When a man establishes merit, though the glory is his own, it is the ancestral temples, the altars of state, and all living beings who truly depend on it. How can the incompetent be allowed to sabotage his designs and rejoice in his failure? Though they may claim to have merely ruined his work, in truth they have undermined the state’s affairs. The consequences are no small matter—can they be without guilt?

During the campaign in Huan and Shu, Yang Ye was ordered to assist Pan Mei in the expedition. After reaching the enemy territory and capturing Huan Prefecture, Yang said to Pan: "The enemy’s forces are at their peak, and our troops are not yet ready for battle. We should lead our army out via the Dashi Road and first dispatch messengers to notify the commanders of Yun and Shu Prefectures. Once our main force departs Daizhou, order them to advance with their troops. When our army reaches Yingzhou, the enemy will surely muster all their strength to resist. At that moment, the officials and people of Shu Prefecture can be gathered into the Shijie Valley, while a thousand strong crossbowmen are stationed to guard the valley’s entrance. All cavalry should be deployed to reinforce the central route. Thus, the forces of the three prefectures will be entirely secure."

The supervising military commissioner Wang Shen obstructed the plan, preventing its execution, and reproached Yang, saying: "Are your words not born of fear of death?" Yang replied: "I do not fear death—proceeding now will mean death. Moreover, I exhort Pan: When I depart, you and the others may claim merit at the valley’s entrance. Divide the troops, with the strong crossbowmen forming the left and right wings to serve as my reinforcement. When I fight my way back here, unleash the soldiers to strike, and we shall surely achieve great victory." Pan followed his words and arrayed his forces at the valley’s mouth.

Later, Wang Shen, believing the enemy had already fled and eager to seize the credit, led his troops away. When Yang arrived, he found not a single soldier. Beating his chest, he wept bitterly, then roused himself for a desperate battle. He slew hundreds with his own hand and endured over ten spear wounds. Exhausted, he was captured by the enemy. Refusing food, he died three days later.

Had Wang Shen not obstructed the plan, would success not have been assured? Thus, those who understand this principle know that when entrusted with responsibility, one must first make preparations.

During the campaign at Beizhou, Ming Gao served as commander. Many of his military reports and requests were obstructed by the Bureau of Military Affairs chief Xia Song. At the time, Wen Yanbo, serving as assistant minister, was deeply indignant. He personally requested to oversee the battle and petitioned the throne, saying: "While I am in the field, allow me to act at my discretion without awaiting further approval." The emperor granted his request.

Upon arrival, two garrison soldiers, Dong Xiu and Liu Bing, proposed tunneling into the city. Day and night, they dug, hiding in the tunnel by day and disposing of the soil in the water by night. When the tunnel was complete, they emerged in the training grounds. Once out, they scaled the walls, killed the defending officers, and enabled the following troops to ascend by rope. The rebel leader Wang Ze and his followers Zhang Luan and Bu Ji attempted to break out but were routed. Zhang and Bu died in the chaos, while Wang Ze was captured by government forces.

Xia Song still insisted the rebels might not be genuine. Only when they were caged and sent to the capital did he fall silent.

Had the assistant minister not made preparations in advance, would success not have been thwarted?

The eulogy states:
Du Yu pacified Wu, yet the merit was Yang Hu’s.
Fang Xuanling excelled in strategy, and Du Ruhui always followed.
Once their enterprises were established, their fame and achievements soared.
Yet narrow-mindedness, like Qi’s downfall, consumes all.
They failed, and what use was it to me?
To damage another’s tools to exhaust their use—
The *Classic* says: "To damage another’s tools to exhaust their use."
The *Taishang Shu* warns against this, for it is also a failure of the heart.
A failure of the heart is indeed a sin, but some thereby incur great calamity and sow endless enmity—such acts merit the gravest retribution.
Thus, to damage another’s tools to exhaust their use—how can one not beware?

Long ago, the Huayan Master was skilled in the *Huayan Sutra* and delighted in expounding it, earning his name and drawing many followers. He favored fine alms bowls and begging bowls, and his three hundred disciples all emulated him. Among them was a monk whose conduct was unmatched, yet he had fallen ill and remained secluded for months. He possessed a precious glass bowl, which he treasured.

One day, a visiting monk arrived from afar, lacking a bowl. Someone suggested borrowing the sick monk’s temporarily. At first, the monk refused, but after persistent pleas, he relented, warning earnestly: "Return it promptly after the meal." When the bowl was returned, the visitor soon stumbled, shattering it on the ground. The sick monk flew into a rage, cursing violently, and died shortly after.

Years later, when the Huayan Master moved to Mount Song, both the sick monk’s disciple and the monk who had borrowed the bowl were present. One day, as he was expounding the teachings, a rushing wind and water suddenly arose outside the temple. The master knew what was coming and hastily had the visiting monk sit behind him. Moments later, a great python, nearly ten *zhang* long, glared with bulging eyes and bared fangs as it slithered in. The crowd nearly scattered in panic, but the master restrained them. Brandishing his staff, he restrained the python, saying: "Among three hundred disciples, your cultivation was the highest. Yet for excessive anger and refusing my teachings, you have come to this. Even now, over a single bowl, do you seek to harm this monk?"

He then commanded the assembly to administer the Three Refuges, the Three Reverences, and recite scriptures and mantras to repent and liberate the python. The serpent coiled and departed. The Huayan Master summoned the disciple and said: "The great python just now was your master. Through kalpas of cultivation, he was on the verge of attaining fruition. But in his final moments, a single outburst of anger brought him to this. Had he not encountered me, he would have devoured that monk—and if he had, he would surely have fallen into hell. From there, how long would his transmigration have lasted?"

He then addressed the assembly: "Tomorrow, after the cremation of the deceased monk behind the mountain, you will find that the great python has already died, its skull shattered." After the cremation, the Huayan Master summoned the disciple again and said: "Next year, your master will be reborn as a girl in the Pei family. When the birth is difficult, he will grow enraged and harm his mother, deepening his karmic burden. When the time comes, hasten to save her. Call his name and relay my words—then no harm will come." And so it came to pass.

Alas! Even those who cultivate the Way—if a single moment of inevitability brings them to such a state—how much worse for those who do not cultivate at all? When anger arises, it is because the heart harbors poisonous intent.

In praise it is said:
*To fashion vessels, one models after images—*
*Thus the Changes are consulted.*
*But if one follows private wisdom,*
*Standards are thrown into chaos.*
*Weights and measures, scales and rules—*
*The craftsman alters them at will.*
*Such deceit and deception*
*Are worse than outright destruction.*
*When the people have no standard,*
*Is this not a vicious power?*

To see others prosperous and noble and wish for their downfall—

The *Records* state: The common people comprise four classes—scholars, farmers, artisans, and merchants. Yet those who attain prosperity and nobility stand above them all. Blessings, too, are of five kinds—wealth, longevity, health and peace, love of virtue, and a timely end. And those who attain prosperity and nobility stand above them as well. How, then, could such a station be reached by mere chance? It is only because they have long planted the roots of virtue that they come to this. Those who have not reached such heights—how could they rashly harbor resentment and jealousy, wishing for their ruin?

In the early years of the dynasty, when Lu Duoxun was exiled to Zhuhai, Li Fu said to the Grand Councilor Zhao Pu: "Though Zhuhai lies beyond the sea, its climate and terrain are not so harsh. Chunzhou, though within the realm, is a place from which none return alive. Why not exile him to Chunzhou instead? Outwardly, this would appear a lenient sentence from the court, yet in truth, it would consign him to certain death." Zhao Pu did not reply. Soon after, Li Fu himself was implicated in a crime and exiled to Xuanzhou. The emperor’s wrath had not yet subsided when Zhao Pu presented Li Fu’s words to the throne. That very day, Li Fu’s sentence was changed to exile in Chunzhou. He arrived at his post and died within ten days.

When Minister Kou was demoted, Ding Wei bore much of the responsibility. When the matter of his place of exile was first discussed, Ding Wei and Feng Zheng were both in the Grand Council. As Ding Wei took up the brush to draft the edict, he turned to Feng Zheng and said: "I had intended to exile him to Yazhou, but feared the perilous journey across the whale-infested waves. So I settled on Leizhou instead." Soon after, Ding Wei himself was implicated in a crime and faced exile. Feng Zheng was still in the Grand Council and happened to be drafting the edict. Smiling, he said to Zeng Shen: "The Crane Chancellor once wished to exile Minister Kou to Yazhou, lamenting the dangers of the whale-infested seas. Now, he may trouble the Chancellor to make that journey himself." And so, Ding Wei was exiled to Yazhou.

Thus, to see others prosperous and noble and wish for their downfall—can this be right?

A Eulogy says:
Rank and title come by chance, not true worth to see.
Like flowers in their beds, watered from jars they be.
Pure in wind and dew, scattered in mist and rain,
Flourishing or withering in a fleeting span—how worth holding dear?
With such thoughts in mind, envy melts like ice clear.
To see others wealthy and wish their ruin near—

It is told: The Twentieth Patriarch, Śayata, asked the Nineteenth Patriarch, Kumāralāta, “My family has long upheld the Three Jewels, yet we are constantly afflicted by illness. All our endeavors come to naught. Meanwhile, our neighbors, who have long practiced *yogācāra*, enjoy robust health and success in all they do. Why are they so fortunate, and what wrong have we done?”

Kumāralāta replied, “The retribution of good and evil unfolds in three times. People see only the virtuous die young and the wicked live long, fortune favor the perverse and misfortune the righteous, and so they dismiss cause and effect as empty, karma and its fruits as false. Yet they do not know that echo follows sound, shadow follows form—even the slightest deviation remains unerring. Though a hundred thousand kalpas may pass, it is never erased.”

Śayata’s doubts were instantly dispelled.

Kumāralāta continued, “Though you have faith, you have not yet attained clarity. *Karma* arises from delusion, perception from conditioning, perception from unawareness, and unawareness from the mind. The mind is fundamentally pure—without birth or extinction, without creation or cessation, without retribution or victory or defeat. It is still, it is luminous. If you enter this gate of the Dharma, you may become one with all Buddhas.”

Alas! Can those who see others wealthy and wish their ruin comprehend this?

Even if the wealthy are unvirtuous, their downfall will come in due time—yet one must not harbor the thought of wishing their ruin.

Zhou Yifu of Hong County was immensely wealthy, dominating his region and acting with great arrogance. A man from a neighboring prefecture, Sun Shizhi, once gently admonished him. Yifu flew into a rage, cursing, “What does a little ghost like you know? How dare you meddle in my affairs!”

Later, when Shizhi passed the imperial examinations and became the transport commissioner of his circuit, Yifu still raged, “Even if he’s a surveillance commissioner, what can he do to me?”

One day, Shizhi conducted an inspection in Hong County. Just then, Yifu was beating a man in the marketplace, and the victim lodged a complaint. Shizhi subtly encouraged the man to report Yifu’s lawless acts. That very day, he sent the case to the authorities for investigation. Before long, the ringleader died in prison. After deliberation, the officials memorialized the throne, transferring the case to another prefecture. Yifu was ultimately sentenced to death for conspiracy to murder, and his descendants were registered as government bondsmen. Within a few years, Shizhi was transferred to serve as transport commissioner in Hebei. At the time, war had broken out, and his entire family perished at the hands of the invaders. Their ruin was no different from Yifu’s—was this not retribution?

Qian Ruoshui served as an assistant magistrate in Tong Prefecture. A wealthy family lost a young servant girl, and her mother and grandfather filed a complaint. The prefecture ordered the registrar to investigate. The registrar had once sought a favor from the wealthy family but was refused, so he falsely accused them of father and son jointly murdering the servant girl and throwing her body into the water. The case was submitted to the prefecture, and the officials who reviewed it found no discrepancies. Only Ruoshui doubted the verdict and steadfastly refused to sign. He even spent his own money to secretly send men to search for the servant girl. Eventually, they found her, and the family was exonerated. Had they not encountered Ruoshui, the wealthy family might have met the same fate as Yifu.

In my humble view, not only did the wealthy family owe him gratitude, but the registrar did as well. Had the wealthy family died, could the registrar have lived in peace?

A Eulogy says:
A dragon-prowed ship may carry ten thousand *hu*—
Yet even such might, in time, meets its due.

A single leaf may stir the waves,
while piled feathers still capsize a boat.
The roc and the wren roam at ease,
each content in its own desire.
Their capacities differ, yet each finds sufficiency in its allotted portion.
Inner fire burns one from within,
as if drinking poisoned wine.
Seeing another’s beauty—be it form or dwelling—
one stirs the mind to covet it.

The scripture says: In the cycle of birth and death,
there is no escape—
all arises from the Five Aggregates,
all arises from the Five Skandhas.
The principle is the same;
only the translators’ renderings differ slightly in wording.

What are the Five Aggregates?
Clearly distinguished, the first is *form*;
receiving and accepting in the mind, the second is *sensation*;
the mind contemplating this principle, the third is *perception*;
acting upon this principle, the fourth is *mental formations*;
defiling the true nature, the fifth is *consciousness*.

What are the Five Skandhas?
Composed of the four great elements, the first is *form*;
receiving pleasure, pain, or neither, the second is *sensation*;
grasping various objects, the third is *perception*;
all mental states, impoverished yet ceaseless, the fourth is *mental formations*;
discerning the nature of objects of perception—also called mind or intention—
gathered by accumulation, the fifth is *consciousness*.

All worldly people, deluded by sounds and forms,
generate confusion and attachment—
none are free from this.
Had they awakened to the emptiness of form,
would they have come to this?

Formerly, when Zhang Bao governed Chengdu,
there was a Hua Yang constable named Li,
whose wife was of unmatched beauty,
renowned throughout Shu.
Bao desired her in secret.
During the Lantern Festival, when men and women went out to roam,
he sent men to watch her comings and goings,
then disguised himself to steal a glance.
At first sight, he confirmed her peerless beauty.
Thereupon, he enlisted nuns and female attendants to convey his intentions.
At first, the wife firmly refused,
but over time, she seemed to waver.
Before long, Constable Li was charged with corruption.
Bao impeached him before the court and had him sent to prison for interrogation.
Once convicted, Li was exiled beyond the southern mountains
and died on the road.
Bao then lavished gifts upon the constable’s mother
and forced the wife into marriage.
For the wife had long harbored resentment toward her husband,
and now she willingly complied.
Bao, overjoyed at gaining her,
found her fully compliant in return.
They delighted in each other, inseparable day and night.
Before long, the wife fell ill,
and in her delirium, she constantly saw Constable Li by her side.
Soon after, she died.
Bao also fell ill.
In a dream, his wife told him:
*“Though I am grateful for your kindness and dare not repay it with ingratitude,
Li has already appealed to Heaven.
Your time is near.
If you remain secluded, you may not be easily seized,
but should you venture out lightly, you will surely be taken.”*

One evening as he sat, he saw from afar a crimson sleeve beckoning from among the bamboo beneath the hall—he took it to be the constable’s wife. Forgetting her warning, he rushed toward it in haste. Upon arriving, it was the constable who struck and cursed him: *“You thief! Had I not beckoned you with the red sleeve, would you have come?”* After a long while, blood flowed from his mouth and nose, and he collapsed dead upon the ground.

Thus we know that to harbor secret desire is the way of men, yet to silently consent is the nature of women. Had she not lightly yielded and so brought this disaster upon him, would it not have been better to hold firm in resolve and, in this life, pass through this one retribution-body?

In his youth, Yan Wu lived next door to a military commander. The commander had a daughter of great beauty, whom Wu secretly admired. By every means he lured her to his home, and together they fled. Later, the commander learned of this and submitted a petition to the throne. An edict was issued, and an imperial envoy dispatched to arrest them. Fearing punishment, Wu got the girl drunk one night, removed the strings from her pipa, and strangled her with them before sinking her body in the water. The next day, when the envoy arrived, nothing could be found. Thus he escaped punishment.

When Yan Wu fell ill in Shu, he clearly saw the girl appear before him and reproach him, saying: "Though my following you was indeed improper conduct, I bore you no true ill will. Even if you feared punishment, why not abandon me and flee, rather than kill me? You are truly a cruel man. I have already lodged my complaint with Heaven, and the appointed time is tomorrow." Overcome with shame and dread, Wu begged for his life, yet at dawn the following day, he indeed died. From this we may further understand: women who possess even slight beauty often meet such ends. Had she remained steadfast in resolve, her will as unyielding as ice and snow, could he have seduced her, even if he wished to? Thus I speak with absolute certainty: though the impulse to seduce arises from the man, the tacit consent that permits it comes from the woman.

In places of leisure and pleasure, between walls and partitions, one must be ever vigilant.

**Eulogy:**
Zixia said, "Virtue is easily displaced by desire for beauty."
The teachings of the ancient sages always urged, "Cherish virtue."
A wise woman’s beauty may topple a city—yet she is the thief of true virtue.
When the heart loses its proper course, it descends into madness and delusion.
To debase one’s will and disgrace one’s body—all from a single misguided thought.

*Here ends Scroll Eighteen of the* **Taishang Ganying Pian**
*Scroll Nineteen of the* **Taishang Ganying Pian** *begins*

**The Tale of Li Changling, with Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

To covet another’s goods and wealth, to wish another’s death—such is the way of the wicked.

**Account:**
Li Yue served as Vice Minister of War. Once, while traveling by boat, his vessel drew alongside that of a foreign merchant. The merchant suddenly fell gravely ill and summoned Yue to his side. Upon arriving, Yue was bequeathed a luminous pearl, along with the care of the merchant’s two daughters—both of surpassing beauty. The next day, the merchant died, leaving behind tens of thousands in treasure. Every soul aboard the vessel cast covetous eyes upon it. Yet Yue meticulously cataloged the wealth and entrusted it to the authorities. He arranged for one daughter to be wed, then prepared the merchant’s body for burial. As he did so, he secretly placed the luminous pearl in the merchant’s mouth, unseen by all. Later, when the merchant’s kin arrived to claim the fortune, Yue requested the officials to open the coffin. There lay the pearl, and all who witnessed it marveled at his integrity.

Wei Zidu was traveling at dusk when he encountered a scholar collapsed by the roadside, clutching his chest in agony. Zidu dismounted and tended to him, but the scholar soon died. Searching his belongings, Zidu found a scroll of plain silk and ten ingots of gold. He sold two ingots to cover the funeral costs, then placed the remaining eight beneath the scholar’s head and laid the silk scroll beside him. Years later, a man inquired of Zidu about the scholar’s burial site. Zidu led him to the grave, and upon exhumation, both the gold and the scroll were found untouched. From that day forth, Zidu’s reputation resounded far and wide.

These are the noble deeds of our forebears, recorded in the annals of history, their brilliance dazzling the eyes and ears of all who hear them. Such conduct is far beyond the reach of those who covet another’s wealth and wish another’s death.

Yet even so, let us recount these few examples, that all may take heed.

In Yongjia, there lived a merchant named Xu Hui. Once, in Danyang, he borrowed over a thousand strings of cash from a wealthy broker. Before he could repay the debt, the broker died, and Xu kept silent. The broker’s family, unaware of the loan, never sought repayment.

Later, Hui fathered a son, remarkably bright and clever, whom he cherished deeply. At the age of eight, the boy fell ill, and Hui summoned physicians and purchased medicines, spending beyond measure. Though his wealth was exhausted, the child’s illness showed no sign of abating. One day, as the boy lay sick, he suddenly spoke to Hui’s close acquaintance, the nun Shi: “I wish to return home.” The nun replied, “This is your home. Your parents love you so—where else would you go?” The boy said, “I am from Danyang. Long ago, Master Xu borrowed a thousand strings of cash from me, and when I died, he never repaid the debt. Now I have come to be reborn in his household to reclaim what is mine.” With these words, he passed away.

There was also Bai Yuantong, who once owed five strings and four hundred coins to Yang Jun of the Eastern Market. Before he could repay the debt, Jun died suddenly, and Yuantong concealed the matter. After his death, he was reborn in Jun’s family as a donkey. One day, Jun’s son rode the donkey to the market, when it suddenly spoke in a human voice: “I am Bai Yuantong. I owed your father five strings and four hundred coins, and this is my retribution. Now, the bran-seller in the Southern Market owes me the same amount. Sell me to him at once, and with the money you receive, settle your father’s debt.” Jun’s son did as instructed, and two days later, the donkey died.

Can those who covet another’s wealth and wish another’s death ever escape their fate?

**Eulogy:**
Wu Zixu repaid kindness, casting gold into the river’s rapids.
Ji Zha honored his vow, hanging his sword upon a tomb’s tree.
Though their fates differed, their hearts never wavered.
Entrusting life and orphans, their names endure through integrity.
But those who deceive the dead are as filth to flies and gnats—
their schemes unfulfilled, they curse and resent.
As the saying goes: “Those who know no shame pursue only gain, save for men of principle.
If, when their demands go unmet, they immediately curse and resent, they are the basest of men.”
How could they grasp Han Changli’s teaching on self-accountability and forbearance toward others,
or Fan Zhongxuan’s principle of holding others to the same standard one holds oneself?

Changli said: “The noble man examines himself strictly and thoroughly;
in dealing with others, he is lenient and forgiving.
Strict and thorough, he never falters;
lenient and forgiving, others delight in doing good.”

Zhongxuan said: “All my life, I have studied but two words—loyalty and forbearance—
and they have never failed me, whether serving at court, attending to my lord,
interacting with colleagues, or fostering kinship.
I once warned my descendants: ‘Even the dullest man is sharp when judging others;
even the wisest grows dim when excusing himself.
If you can judge yourself as you judge others, and forgive others as you forgive yourself,
you will not fail to reach the realm of sages and worthies.’”
Do those who clamor for gain understand this?

In this world, few indeed refrain from grasping hands outstretched for favors.
Let me recount a few more examples, that all may feel shame.

Director Hu Bao of the Ministry of Justice once told others: “When I served as magistrate of Wu County in Suzhou, many scholar-officials resided there, and none failed to make requests of me. Only the retired Grand Supervisor Fu Yan, though living in Suzhou for three years, never once imposed upon me.”

Senior Academician Qiu Shunqing also said: “When I governed Xinjian County in Hongzhou, the powerful clans of the region often interfered with official affairs. Only the retired Director Wang Shu, though living in poverty and seclusion, his clothing and food insufficient, never once sought help from others.”

These two gentlemen’s integrity and virtue surpass others—how worthy they are!

**Eulogy:**

The heart free of desire is like still water.
Once a single plea arises, waves and surges erupt.
Gaze upon their compliance or refusal—
it stirs my love or resentment.
Slightly thwart their wishes,
and grievance and anger mount.
Why not reflect instead,
desire restrained, wrath quelled?

See another’s misstep,
and at once proclaim their fault.
As the saying goes: “If a man by chance suffers misfortune,
it is his calamity—how can one then speak of his fault?”
To speak of his fault is to delight in misfortune,
to rejoice in disaster.
Yet the Most High has clearly warned against such conduct—
how can this be permissible?

Long ago, when He Shenxi was about to receive his appointment,
he suddenly fell ill and died.
His wife wept in grief,
until her sister-in-law, a woman surnamed Zhao, interrupted:
“Your husband, in life, frequented the pleasure quarters,
treating you as if you did not exist.
Why grieve so?”
From that moment, the wife’s sorrow ceased, and she no longer mourned.

One night, Zhao dreamed of Shenxi, who said:
“Life and death are fated—how can you mock another?
You have but four years left,
and your death will be worse than mine.”
Zhao awoke in terror.
When the time came, it proved true:
at her death, her husband was far away, her two young sons foolish and helpless—
truly, worse than his fate.

Li Xiong was most learned in letters,
devoted in filial piety, and swift in action,
praised by all in his village.
Then, struck by illness,
a single misprescribed dose proved fatal.
Though his wife was with child, the child had not yet been born.
His family, stricken with grief, knew not what to do.
His brother-in-law, He Shi, then proclaimed abroad:
“This gentleman always claimed to manage affairs well—
now let him manage this for me.”
Those who heard this thought little of his character.
The following year, He Shi himself died.

Thus, is it right to speak of another’s misfortune?
Are we ourselves truly without fault?

**Eulogy:**

A single misstep by Mengming ultimately led Qin to hegemony. Forty-nine faults did Yu rectify, yet each day he renewed himself. A stumble in half a step is no grounds to judge a man. To cast stones upon him—how could this not be cruelty? This is called delighting in misfortune, and calamity will befall one’s own person.

To laugh at another for an incomplete or flawed physique—
As the tradition records: Duke Jin sent Xi Chou to summon an assembly in Qi, and Duke Qing of Qi had women concealed behind curtains to observe. Xi ascended the hall, and the women laughed. Xi grew enraged. In the second year of Duke Cheng, this led to Qi’s invasion.

When Guo Ziyi fell gravely ill, officials came to inquire after him in an unbroken stream. His concubines filled the chamber, yet he never sent them away. But when Lu Si arrived, he dismissed them all. Asked the reason, Ziyi replied: “That man is ugly in appearance but treacherous within. If my attendants see him, they will surely mock and humiliate him. One day, should he gain power, not a soul of my clan will remain.” Ziyi’s discernment was profound—how far removed from Duke Qing’s!

Again, like the beauty of Lord Pingyuan who laughed at the hunchbacked servant hobbling to fetch water and had him executed; or the man of Zhao County who mocked Mengchang Jun as a puny husband and was put to death. These are overturned carts before one’s path—warnings to heed, yet few awaken to them.

A person’s standing rests upon their capacity and insight, not upon their physique. Though their form may be flawed, if their capacity and insight are abundant, how can they be slighted?

Of old, Lu Xie’s appearance was unremarkable, his speech unclear—his name *Xie* was mocked as *Hui* (“broom”), for his tongue was short. Once, he presented a composition to Minister Wei Zhou, whose sons and nephews openly ridiculed him. Wei Zhou said: “Though Lu’s person is unremarkable, his writing has beginning and end. One day, he will rise high.” Later, Lu indeed passed the examinations, swiftly ascending to the halls of governance, promoting officials in the capital, and becoming the Fujian observer.

Wang Yigong Qinruo was gaunt and ungainly, his manner rustic, with a growth upon his neck. Once, he presented a composition to Qian Gong Xibai, who looked upon him with disdain. By chance, a diviner was present, who exclaimed in astonishment: “This is a man of the highest nobility—how can he be slighted?” Qian Gong asked: “In my hall, could such a one become chief minister?” The diviner replied: “It may not be long.” Within a few years, he indeed rose to the position of grand councilor.

Sun Wenyi Gong was likewise unprepossessing in appearance. Once, traveling to the capital for the examinations, he sought a certificate from the county to sell his fields. The magistrate’s aide, Li Zhaoyan, jested: “How many men like you are there?” That year, Wenyi ranked third in the examinations. Within a few years, he became a judge of the Bureau of Appointments. By then, Zhaoyan was himself arriving for his official posting. At first sight, he was overcome with shame and returned home.

Lord Hou Yuan’gong was likewise homely and ungainly in appearance. When first recommended among fellow provincials, his advanced age and plain looks drew little respect. Some frivolous youths made a paper kite, painted his likeness upon it, and flew it aloft by its string. Yuan’gong happened to see it and laughed heartily. He composed a *Linjiangxian* lyric and inscribed it on the kite:

*Who would believe my hidden worth before success?*
*Now my name remains unproclaimed, my talents unmarked.*
*Yet some skilled hand has limned my form—*
*Catching the wind, lightly borrowing its force,*
*I soar at once into the highest heavens.*

*Once the breath of praise begins to steady me,*
*I seem to fly straight to the moon’s palace.*
*After rain, the evening sun glows red;*
*How many stand on level ground*
*While I gaze down from the azure vault?*

That same year he passed the examinations and rose to the rank of chief minister.

Was it not laughable, then, to judge by mere physical form?

**Eulogy:**
The sage fulfills his form, not by outward appearance.
He who venerates the feet fears only blindness of heart.
To scorn what one does not know may cost one a Ziyu.
Fair of face and tall of stature, the wise clan aids them.
If a finger falls short of others, why must one despise it?
To see another’s talent as worthy yet suppress it—

As the tradition says: “To see another’s talent as worthy yet suppress it—this is the petty man’s envy of those ahead, not what a noble man would dare do.”

When Minister Wang Wengong Dan was long ill and unable to recover, the emperor ordered a palanquin to bring him into the palace to offer encouragement, repeatedly asking: “If, heaven forbid, you should pass, to whom should I entrust the affairs of the realm?” The minister replied: “In my humble opinion, none would be better than Kou Zhun.” The emperor said: “But what of his harshness and narrowness?” The minister replied: “If it were another, I would not presume to know.” Zhun was then elevated to high office.

When Minister Lü Shen’gong sought retirement, the emperor said: “Since you cannot be kept, whom may I appoint in your stead?” The minister replied: “If Your Majesty must employ a brilliant and far-sighted talent, I cannot presume to know. But if you seek one to entrust with the weight of age, to stabilize all measures, to understand the hardships of the realm—none surpasses Yao Zuo.” Chen was then elevated to high office.

Minister of War Qian Ruoshui delighted in drawing scholars to him, tirelessly and without weariness. Many of the younger generation emerged from his gate.

Minister Ouyang Wenzhong Xiu was ever eager to advance worthy men, never harboring envy or a competitive spirit.

When Attendant Censor Miao Shizhong served as Vice Minister of Revenue, he investigated the equal-fields and corvée systems in the Zizhou Circuit. Upon completing his report, he submitted it and recommended over ten capable officials, all of whom were later promoted.

Minister Du Qigong Yan, having governed prefectures and overseen circuits as pacification commissioner, whenever he encountered men of literary talent, administrative skill, exceptional conduct, or peerless virtue—even if he had never met them—never failed to recommend them according to their abilities.

Censor Sun Bian and Tang Jie were both reinstated to office, though they had never met. Hearing only of Jie’s firm integrity, Bian forcefully recommended him. When Jie became a censor, both displayed resolute strength.

Minister Zhang Shugong and Minister Wen Lugong had never met, but hearing of Lugong’s magnanimity, Shugong, upon their first meeting, vigorously recommended him. Later, Lugong served as both general and chief minister, proving himself truly exceptional.

Prefect Zhang Zhongding Yong and Zhang Xiyan had never been heard of, but when Palace Attendant Fan Yangui reported that under Xiyan’s administration, bridges, roads, post stations, and courier relays were all well-maintained; fields were reclaimed; no farmers neglected their work; gambling dens were shuttered; markets were free of disputes; and the night watches were clearly marked—Yong laughed and said: “Xiyan is certainly a capable official, and Heaven too favors good officials.” That very day he recommended both, and later each won acclaim.

Could any of these gentlemen have suppressed talent?

In praise it is said:
Heaven brings forth talent to serve the world.
What is prized in those who have already achieved is their exaltation and encouragement of those who follow.
Qi Xi recommended his enemy; Youfu promoted the overlooked.
Through this, governance was established, and the nation’s affairs were brought to fruition.
Those who envy the worthy and resent ability seldom avoid inviting calamity.
To bury the virtuous and suppress others—

As the tradition states: According to the *Laws of Xuandu*, exceeding two thousand seven hundred transgressions constitutes a single harm. Those who cause harm will see their households produce male and female shamans.
Thus, to be born as a shaman is already the consequence of one’s past life.
If one, having already incurred sin in this life, is born again as a human and once more buries the virtuous or suppresses others, this only compounds their transgressions. When they fall in the future, how could it compare to the suffering of this present life?
One must know that to sink into the Three Evil Realms is as swift as an arrow—truly, this is what is meant by "alas, woe!"

Formerly, Ji Nanshou, prefect of Jianzhou, was summoned to the netherworld to adjudicate a yin case.
The case involved Wang Zicheng, a shaman from Xizhou, who had dispatched ghostly soldiers to slaughter two young members of Gao Zhen’s household and to kill several cattle belonging to Zhang Yuan’s family.
Nanshou, judging solely by the statutes, ruled:
Wang Zicheng wantonly practiced wicked arts, harming living people and livestock alike. He is to be punished with an iron rod across the spine and sent to the Avīci Hell to endure all manner of suffering.
Once his sentence in Avīci is complete, he shall be reborn as a lonely spirit among the outer paths, and his entire family—children and descendants—shall all be cast into ruin.
All his lineage shall be utterly obliterated.

Yet can one truly bury curses to harm others? Those who conceive such malice and compel others to enact it bear a guilt even heavier than the sorcerer’s. According to the *Record of Strange Phenomena*, when the soldier Lü Yongzhi was executed, his central hall was searched, and a stone casket was found. Inside lay a copper figurine, nearly three feet tall, its body bound in shackles, its mouth pierced by a long nail, its back inscribed with Gao Pian’s native village, official rank, surname, and given name—all part of a *yasheng* curse. Thus was Gao Pian ever constrained by Yongzhi’s machinations.

Alas! If Gao Pian was already under Yongzhi’s control, why then did Yongzhi meet his own execution? Know this: burying curses to harm others may not truly harm them, but only hastens one’s own calamity. Beware!

**Eulogy:**
Sorcery and curses arose,
Nearly extinguishing Han’s light.
Trusting in heretical ways,
Their ruin burned fierce and bright.
Chuan Yi tested spells—
The foreign monk fell in defeat.
Ning clung to ghostly soldiers—
He died in Yue’s retreat.
Arrows loosed recoil,
Self-inflicted is the debt.
To poison trees with potions—
The *Highest Writ* warns against it,
Lest living things be hurt.

Thus did Xin Ling nurture herbs and trees to attain immortality, and Gao Chai, who never broke a blade of grass, earned Confucius’ special praise for his conduct. Yet if one poisons trees with potions, does one not incur guilt? Have we not heard of the Iba Dragon King, who damaged leaves and fell into the dragon realm, or the Taiyi Cave Dragon, who in fury harmed crops and was reborn as a horse?

The Great Dao cherishes life; even a single being must not be wantonly harmed, let alone trees, for within them may dwell sages. As the *Water Classic* records, Yi Yin was born from a hollow mulberry. Some, having erred in their cultivation, find their spirits lodged within trees, as the *Sutra of Karmic Retribution* tells of Han Yuanshou transformed into a tree spirit. Others harbor consciousness no different from humans, like the pine root encountered by Qian Shiyu. Still others, who steal trees and invite disaster, are first known to the tree spirits, as with Qian Renkang’s theft of the peony.

Of these many accounts, let us set them aside for now. Consider only the cases of Qian Shiyu and Qian Renkang—may all take heed and be warned.

In former times, at the Flying Hall of Eastern Gorge Mountain, there stood an ancient pine. During the Daguan era, a retainer of the Imperial City Commissioner Qian Shiyu struck its roots with an axe, and sap flowed forth in abundance. The following year, Palace Attendant Qian Ji passed by the temple and dreamed at night of an old man who said to him: "Though I am not one of those round-headed and square-footed beings, I am a plant imbued with spiritual essence. Yesterday, Qian Shiyu’s man struck my knee with an axe, taking my sap to use as candle wax, and even now my blood still flows. I beg you to inform the abbot, that he may swiftly mend my wound, so I may not be shaken by blind winds and thus fulfill my allotted years—I would be deeply indebted." At dawn, Ji set out in haste and failed to notify the abbot, but upon meeting Magistrate Qian Lu, he recounted the dream. Lu went to the temple to inspect the tree and found it just as Ji had described. He promptly had the wound sealed with lacquer.

The residence of Military Inspector and Minister of Works Qian Renkang adjoined that of the Central Army Captain Marshal Jin Zhao. In Zhao’s garden grew a peony, which each year bore no fewer than three hundred blossoms, their petals crimson as blood, their edges gilded like gold, and atop each stem, the calyx bore silken veins, every one resembling a natural butterfly—truly a marvel of its kind. Whenever the flowers bloomed, Zhao would host a feast, and Renkang was always invited to attend. One night, Renkang drank heavily, brandished his sword, stripped to his undergarments, slung a large basket on his back, and dagger at his waist, leapt over the wall, and stole the peony away. Zhao remained unaware. As he was stealing the plant, a voice sounded from the air, faint as a bee’s hum: *"Three hundred blossoms on a single stem, / Smiling in the spring breeze. / Next year in the third month, / Each petal shall be a crimson of heartbreak."* The following year, when the flowers bloomed, Zhao once more held a feast, and Renkang again attended. At the sight of the peony, he flew into a rage. Upon returning home, he slit his own belly and died, fulfilling the verse spoken at the time of the theft.

Alas! Renkang perished for the sake of a flower, for the spirit had foreseen his fate even as he stole it. If such is the case, can one then recklessly use potions to kill trees?

**Eulogy:**
*Virtue extended to grasses and trees—*
*Thus the royal enterprise of Zhou arose.*

At dawn’s misty ascent, poets conceal their hidden sorrows.
Heaven and earth bring forth life—where harmonious qi roams.
To harm without cause is no different from severing a ditch’s flow.
The sacred oak appeared in dreams, its parable no idle tale.

*The Nineteenth Scroll of the Supreme Scripture on Retribution and Response* concludes here.

---

**The Twenty-First Scroll of the Supreme Scripture on Retribution and Response**

**Biography of Li Changling, with Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

Rewards and punishments are not impartial.
The tradition states: According to the *Great Treasury*, when heaven and earth first parted, there were already men and women.
To sustain life, each coveted accumulation.
Through accumulation, they began to encroach and steal.
To prevent theft, they collectively chose one.
The wise established him as king, to administer rewards and punishments, calling it equity.
Thus, the use of rewards and punishments is not a matter of today.

Of old, the elder Wang was most courageous. He often lamented that rewards and punishments were unjust, leaving him to grow old in the ranks. Whenever he heard of strange or monstrous affairs, he would clench his fists and gnash his teeth, longing to eradicate them. Having long heard of the numinous wonders of the Guardian Sage Perfected Lord, he sought to leave the mortal register, daily offering incense and fire. Morning and evening, he would bow twice before the hall, crying out in a loud voice: *"I cannot abide injustice. Now I am old, and when my body dies, I wish to serve at your side, ready to be commanded."* The Perfected Lord granted his request.

Years after his death, when a ritual feast was held in the hall, a voice suddenly sounded from the air: *"Why not summon my Daoist?"* Zhang Shouzhen said: *"Beneath the Perfected Lord, only four attendants have ever been venerated. What deity are you? I beg you to reveal your name."* After a moment, the voice spoke again: *"I am the elder Wang of former days. The Perfected Lord has taken me in, appointing me to wield the iron wheel, my rank below the Four Generals. In future rites, do not forget me."*

Alas! Wang made his vow thus, and the Perfected Lord’s acceptance was thus—who can say that unjust rewards and punishments escape the knowledge of the Perfected on High? Generally, rewards encourage merit, while punishments chastise evil. Without rewards and punishments, how can one encourage or chastise? This is why the Supreme One inscribed them in the scripture, and why the Perfected Lord took in Wang.

Let me recount one or two more examples, that those who wield military authority hereafter may know what to emulate.

During the campaign against Liu Xu, soldiers vied to carry severed heads, seeking rewards. Chancellor Bao said: *"In the heat of battle, who has time to claim heads? These must have been cut from the dead afterward. Who knows whose they are?"* Duan Lun replied: *"Your Excellency’s words are truly divine."* At the time, Duan served as vanguard, and those who charged into the enemy’s ranks were all wounded. The commander-in-chief had already ordered them to report to camp for commendation. The chancellor said: *"Bring them all here."* When they arrived, he first recorded the merits of those who had fought valiantly, then those who bore heads. Thereupon, the army’s morale soared, deeming the rewards and punishments perfectly just, and they rejoiced as one.

During the Ganling campaign, only the Ding troops clamored for rewards, their resentment nearly erupting in protest beneath the city walls. At the time, Han Wei Gong commanded the army. Applying military law, he identified those who had caused disorder and could not be disciplined, dragging them to the camp gate to be beheaded. For soldiers who died in battle, he generously compensated their families, providing for their orphans and ensuring their sustenance. With trust in both grace and authority, the troops’ morale redoubled.

Can rewards and punishments, then, be anything but impartial?

**Eulogy:**
Rewards and punishments lie with Heaven—blessing the good, calamity the wicked.
Rewards and punishments lie with man—steadfast as stone and metal.
They bring it upon themselves; why should I bear the burden?
If chastisement and encouragement are misapplied, chaos and ruin follow.
Clear proof and great verification are recorded in ancient and modern times.

---

**Excessive Pleasure**

The tradition states: The Venerable Monk Zhigong and Emperor Wu of Liang discussed matters of pleasure. The monk requested the emperor to bring forth several condemned prisoners to test his theory. Thereupon, he ordered the prisoners each to hold a full cup of water and walk around the courtyard. He warned: *"If not a drop spills, I shall spare your lives."* Then he commanded music to be played, to stir their hearts. After a long while, he observed them—none had spilled a drop.

The emperor then sighed and asked, *"Did you hear the music?"*
They replied, *"We did not."*
The master said, *"They were only afraid of death, trembling lest the water spill—how could they hear the music? If Your Majesty were likewise, ever mindful of awe and dread, then the heart of excessive pleasure would naturally not arise."*

Master Huìyuǎn said to Magistrate Zhang Mi, *"Adversity is easy to endure; prosperity is hard to endure. When things run counter to my wishes, I need only bear with them for a moment, and the trial passes. But when all goes smoothly, every matter aligns with my desires, leaving no escape. It is like a lodestone meeting a needle—unaware, unknowing, they inevitably draw together. If even insensate things behave so, how much more we, who are sentient, whose whole lives are bound up in the snares of emotion?"*

The words of these two masters may be called perfect. Generally, when a person dwells in prosperity, the heart knows no fear, and in excessive pleasure, one cannot avoid fault.

Long ago, Jiǎng Xīlǔ governed Chengdu. Among the courtesans in the register was one named Chén Fèngyí, whose beauty and skill surpassed all others. Xīlǔ cherished her deeply. At every feast, she never left his side, and for this he drew censure, which reached the court’s ears. When Lord Lù succeeded him, he sternly corrected such lapses. Though courtesans filled the hall before him, he never once glanced their way.

One day, while hosting a banquet, the front of his robe came undone, and he could not fasten it himself. Chén Fèngyí stepped forward to secure it, and the Lord turned slightly to smile at her. Fèngyí said, "So Your Lordship does glance at others after all." From then on, his heart was at ease, and he spoke with her often. Rumors spread, and the court dispatched the imperial censor Hé Tán to investigate in secret. When Tán arrived in Hànzhōu, he saw among the courtesans one surnamed Yáng, whose dancing was exceptionally graceful. Someone remarked in passing, "This must be the famed *Yáng tái liǔ*." The words spread until they reached the emperor’s ears.

If even in moments of leisure and pleasure one must be so cautious, how much more so for others? If these two lords—men of such standing—could not avoid fault, what of those beneath them?

Dù Yǎn, the Duke of Integrity, when dining at home, would take but a single dish and a single bowl of rice. If someone remarked on his frugality, he would say, "I was once a poor scholar. My rank, longevity, and fortune—all my robes and emblems—belong to the state. Should I depart this life, I would return to being a poor scholar. How then could I indulge myself?"

Fàn Zhòngyān, the Duke of Culture, once said of himself: "Each night before sleep, I tally the day’s expenses for food and comfort, then review what I have accomplished. If the two balance, I sleep soundly, free of shame. If not, I toss and turn, unable to rest."

Lǚ Gōngzhù, the Duke of Application, often declared: "Fine robes ill befit a man of restraint; a full granary seems to fear the books in one’s belly."

Fù Zhègōng, the Duke of Wealth, likewise said: "Guard your words as you would a sealed jar; guard your thoughts as you would a fortified city."

Such vigilance leaves no room for indulgence, let alone excess. One must recognize that the conduct of the ancients was ever thus—not merely the words of these two masters.

**Eulogy:**
Dwelling without seeking ease,
Even benevolence demands effort.
Great Yǔ rode a sled,
Táo Kǎn moved his bricks.
Chǔ’s calamity brought daily warnings,
The *Book of Changes* urged evening vigilance.
Who am I to lounge in comfort,
Careless in sleep and food?
The sages of old left their teachings—
An inch of time is worth a foot of jade.

As for cruelty to subordinates: The *Traditions* say, "To serve in office yet harshly oppress officials and commoners, or to manage a household yet excessively beat servants and maids—this is cruelty." Have you not heard of Wáng Jùnkuàng’s father, who in his entire official career never struck anyone unjustly? He had eight sons, all of whom entered the civil service, and Jùnkuàng himself became the empire’s finest scholar.

Have you not also heard of Yuán Láng’s mother, who in life excessively beat her servants and maids, and after death fell into hell, suffering every torment? These are clear examples of karmic retribution, warnings not to be ignored.

There are those who beat their pregnant concubines—before the child in her womb is even born, it has already suffered harm. To cut off a man’s descendants is no small sin. They lament their own lack of heirs, yet others have them—this is the work of cause and effect. If you insist on having an heir, can you truly force one to come? If you insist on preventing one, can you truly stop it? The matter of descendants is grave, and bestowal or denial rests with Heaven. Creation has its own mysteries.

Long ago, Fèi Xiàoxiān was renowned for his divination by shadows. Once, the ceremonial officer Yáng Shàoyán visited his shop to consult the oracle. Examining the shadow, Xiàoxiān saw two women giving birth to a single child. Shàoyán was astonished and confessed, "I have long struggled to have a son, so I took a concubine. Now she is with child, but my wife is also pregnant—could this be the meaning?" Xiàoxiān said, "Let us wait and see." When the concubine went into labor at midnight, no one was available, so the wife herself held her as she gave birth—yet afterward, the wife’s own pregnancy had vanished. Was it that the concubine was fated to bear a son, but the wife, unable to remain silent, also conceived, merely to appease her own heart? The workings of creation are wondrous indeed.

The Buddha taught: "Those who, in wealth and high position, delight in whipping and beating others, after death fall into hell for tens of thousands of years, suffering every agony. When their hellish punishment ends, they are reborn as water buffaloes, their noses pierced with ropes, made to pull boats and carts, beaten with great cudgels to repay their past deeds." He also said: "Those who develop festering sores on their bodies do so because they once whipped and beat living beings."

If even servants and maids must not be treated cruelly, how much less so a pregnant concubine? The Buddha’s words are thus, and the Most High’s words are the same—how can one not take heed?

**Eulogy:**
From dukes and ministers to scholars and commoners,
Each rank follows in succession.

Servants, grooms, herdsmen, and stable-hands,
All rush to serve and drive the carriage.
Though their stations differ,
Their feelings are to be pitied.

Barbarians, southern tribes, Min peoples, and slaves—
All are listed in the ritual classics.
They too are children of men;
How profound was Yuanming’s compassion!

To terrify others—
The *Sutra* states: "Guanyin Bodhisattva, in the midst of fear and urgent peril, bestows fearlessness upon beings, thereby attaining perfect enlightenment—this is the foremost practice." Thus, all beings in Jambudvīpa call him "the Bestower of Fearlessness." If this is so, then how should one regard those who terrify others?

Therefore, the noble person does not act thus; whenever encountering someone in fear, they spare no effort to offer comfort. Alas that such people do not know the harm in startling others—after death, they are reborn as river deer or stags. These creatures, by nature, flee in terror from all beasts by day, startling at every movement. When they sleep, they hang their antlers on branches, curling their bodies to rest. Yet the moment they doze, their limbs scatter in alarm; startled awake, they sleep again, only to be startled once more. From dusk till dawn, they never know a moment’s peace—such is their retribution.

Those who lord over others and terrify them—even bailiffs, constables, and ruffians who freely intimidate for profit—do they understand this? Have they not heard of Li Shunqing, who, pressing a tenant farmer, Zhang San, for unpaid rent, drove him to hang himself? Or Sun Jiming, who, investigating a maid, Lai Xi, and a young servant for some fault, hounded them until they drowned themselves? Before even speaking of karmic consequences in the next life, such torment already brings no peace in this one. Though they took their own lives, in truth, it was *we* who killed them.

One need only consult the *Taishang Sutra of Karmic Retribution and Causes* to know these words are not false.

**Eulogy:**

In ancient times, no governance was more excellent than ensuring the people’s peace. When wild geese return to roost and serpents coil in hibernation, all is settled. Yet when cruel officials raise their voices in clamor, even the stoutest tremble in terror, their gall bladders frozen with fear. The virtuous find their qi stifled, and a hundred dreads assail them. Who bears this responsibility? The officials charged with nurturing the people.

To blame Heaven and resent others—
As the saying goes: The Jambudvīpa world is ever flawed and wanting. How can those who dwell within it expect every matter to conform to their wishes? That which does not conform is simply their lot. For in general, those who accumulate little receive little in return. Yet can Heaven truly be blamed? Can others truly be resented? Such grievances bring no benefit; they only hasten one’s own misfortune.

Long ago, Hu Ang, fallen into destitution and idleness, often drowned his sorrows in drink, cursing Heaven for his repeated failures in the imperial examinations. In the early years of the Taiping Xingguo era, while traveling through the Hebei region, a sheet of black paper suddenly descended from the sky, landing before his horse. Hu Ang retrieved it and saw that it bore golden characters in celestial script—a rebuke from the August Emperor. Well-versed in seal script, he read it through, then scoffed, "What nonsense!" He tore it to shreds, cast it aside, and spurred his horse onward. In an instant, dark clouds gathered, rain poured down, and with a single thunderclap, both man and steed collapsed. Hu Ang was struck dead. His servant gathered their belongings and rushed to the nearest inn, only to find the sky clear and the sun shining, not a trace of cloud or shadow.

Jiao Junming, a Gentleman for Literary Composition, passed the examinations in his youth but languished in office for years. Bitter over his misfortune, he railed against Heaven and later submitted a memorial in prayer. That very night, a scroll of white silk fluttered down before his brazier. Upon close inspection, he saw sixteen characters in celestial script. Unable to decipher them, he heard that the immortal He Xiangu possessed the Dao and took the scroll to her. She said, "This is celestial script; there is no need to ask." But Junming pleaded, so she relented: "It reads: *You accepted five taels of silver, which cost you ten years. Wrongfully killing one person—your retribution awaits after death.*" Had this truly occurred? Junming was left speechless, unable to respond.

One must reflect upon oneself and not rashly blame or resent others.

**Eulogy:**
To seek alliances among the feudal lords, yet cast aside the tortoise oracle and curse—
Yuan Shao’s defeat at Guandu, for which Tian Feng was not spared—
Zhuge Liang’s failure at Mount Qi, where he bitterly reproached himself—
Zixia, counting his faults, received the lesson with reverence.
Observe these men: who was wise, and who was not?

**Cursing wind and rain—**
As the saying goes: Confucius said, "When violent thunder and fierce winds arise, one must change one’s demeanor." He also said, "When there is a great wind, violent thunder, or torrential rain, one must change." Even in the dead of night, he would rise, dress properly, and sit upright—such was his awe of Heaven. How can the common folk, in summer’s scorching rain or winter’s biting cold, do nothing but complain?

Few understand that wind is the messenger of Heaven and Earth. Heaven has eight winds, corresponding to the eight solar terms, through which yin and yang transform and the myriad things are nurtured. Thus, the sage kings aligned with the eight winds, enacted the eight policies, and accorded with the eight trigrams. On the day of *Lichun*, the *Tiaofeng* arrives—pardon minor offenses and release those detained. On the day of *Chunfen*, the *Mingshu Feng* arrives—rectify borders and cultivate fields. On the day of *Lixia*, the *Qingming Feng* arrives—present gifts of silk and honor the feudal lords. On the day of *Xiazhi*, the *Jingfeng* arrives—appoint great generals and reward the meritorious. On the day of *Liqiu*, the *Liangfeng* arrives—repay all debts and sacrifice to the four directions. On the day of *Qiufen*, the *Changhe Feng* arrives—release prisoners and refrain from playing zithers. On the day of *Lidong*, the *Buzhou Feng* arrives—repair city walls and defend frontier towns. On the day of *Dongzhi*, the *Guangmo Feng* arrives—punish the guilty and impose major penalties.

Rain is the messenger of the River Earl. His spirit wears white robes and a black cap, riding a white horse with a red mane. His host is divided into two battalions: one called the *Left Falling Thousand*, the other the *Right Falling Thousand*. Each battalion commands fifty thousand troops, massed at the edge of the clouds. They scatter the seeds of rain, with the rain troops leading and the wind chariots following. The quantity of rain is precisely measured.

There are two kinds of rain seeds: one resembles horse-tooth saltpeter, called *dry rain*; the other is stored in two small jars—this is the common rain that falls when the world lacks water.

The Most High said: Since I brought forth Heaven and Earth, I have been called the Primordial Beginning, the Void Sovereign. In rain, I am the Rain Master; in wind, I am the Wind Earl. Can one then curse the passing of wind and rain?

Long ago, when the Grand Duke served as magistrate of Guantan, for a full year not a single branch stirred in the wind. One night, King Wen dreamed of a beautiful woman weeping in the road, saying, "I am the Most High’s divine maiden of the Eastern Sea, wed to the Western Sea. Guantan lies upon my path home, but its magistrate is so virtuous that I dare not pass with violent wind and torrential rain. When may I return?" The next day, King Wen summoned the Grand Duke. No sooner had he left the prefecture than the wind and rain descended in full force.

Do those who curse wind and rain possess virtue like the Grand Duke?

**Eulogy:**
In the age of Yao and Shun, ten rains and five winds came in due season.
The *Hongfan* records omens: "mad" and "deluded."
When human affairs are not tended, calamities arise.
Revere Heaven’s wrath, approach with solemn awe.
To do nothing but complain—such is the sorrow of the common folk.

**Quarrels and lawsuits—**
As the saying goes: Master Zeng said, "To be wronged and not retaliate."
Laozi said, "The superior person does not contend."
The Buddha said, "I have attained the samadhi of non-contention, foremost among men." He also said, "Of the six perfections and ten thousand practices, forbearance is foremost."

Long ago, Zhang Gongyi’s family lived in harmony for nine generations. Emperor Gaozong visited his home and asked the secret of their unity. Gongyi wrote the character "forbearance" in response. The emperor was moved to tears and bestowed upon him bolts of silk before departing.

Thus, when faced with injustice, forbearance is the way. Without forbearance, conflict arises; unchecked, it escalates into strife. Taking disputes to the authorities may lead to imprisonment, flogging, or torture—even broken skin and severed limbs.

The shame and humiliation, the pain and suffering—none can be spoken of. Parents share in their sorrow, wives and children weep with them, brothers are alarmed, and friends and relatives sigh in despair. They empty their coffers to seek powerful allies, prepare feasts to bribe clerks and runners, and if fortunate, the matter may be resolved in ten days—perhaps. Yet if misfortune strikes, years drag on, the case twists and turns, ascending from the local court to the prefecture, from the prefecture to the provincial authorities, and from there to the imperial censorate. What they contend over is trifling, but the harm they suffer is vast. The dispute may involve no more than a few strings of cash, yet the cost mounts a hundredfold. Soon, they exhaust their family wealth, sell their fields and gardens to sustain the fight, neglecting their parents, losing their wives’ affection, and failing to educate their children. Day after day, they rush between courtrooms, at the mercy of officials and clerks. Even if they possess grand halls and spacious homes, how can they dwell in peace? Warm clothes and ample food—how can they enjoy them? Alas, as lawsuits escalate, grudges deepen, like wildfire spreading across the plains, impossible to extinguish. Had they endured the initial grievance, they would never have reached such an extreme.

Yet some disputes arise from necessity, and none can entirely avoid them. But those who incite conflict, teaching others to litigate for profit, seeking only to satisfy their own desires—I have never seen such men escape misfortune, nor spare their descendants from its consequences.

Let me now speak of one or two who resolved disputes and quelled strife, that those who wish to mediate may find no obstacle, and those who mediate may succeed.

The *Sutra of Encounters* states: "There are two kinds of giving—giving of wealth, which exists among men, and giving of the Dharma, which exists within great compassion." If the merit of Dharma-giving is not slight, then when two parties rage in conflict, a single word of mediation that brings them both peace is also a form of Dharma-giving.

Long ago, Li Wujiang was transferred to an official post in the Eastern Capital. Passing through Zhu Xian Town, he saw two beggars fighting in the road. One cried, "I begged all year to save a few hundred coins, and you borrowed them, refusing to repay!" Wujiang, seeing their violent struggle, paid the debt with his own money. One beggar departed, while the other said, "Sir, you have resolved our fight—you are truly a good man. I live in Longhe Alley; if you visit me someday, I will surely repay you." Wujiang had already traveled several miles when he thought, "He is but a beggar—how could he reward me?" Yet upon reaching the Eastern Capital, he found time to seek him out. Indeed, he found the man and was invited into his home. The beggar offered him wine, but Wujiang declined. He then presented peaches, and though Wujiang politely refused, he took two and placed them in his sleeve. On the road, he examined them and found they were made of gold.

Cui Wei, at Kaiyuan Temple, saw an old beggar woman stumble and knock over a wine vendor’s jar. The vendor beat her mercilessly. Wei hurried to intervene, asking, "How much was the wine worth?" The vendor replied, "One string of cash." Wei immediately removed his robe and paid the debt. The old woman left without a word of thanks. Days later, he encountered her again on the road, and she said, "Yesterday, you resolved my trouble—I cannot forget your kindness. I am skilled in treating growths and warts. Here is a small bundle of Yuejinggang mugwort as a token of gratitude. If you encounter such ailments, use it to cauterize them—one treatment, and they will heal." Later, Wei met a monk with a growth dangling from his ear. One cauterization, and it vanished. The monk led him to a wealthy household where the master suffered from a similar growth. One treatment, and it was cured. From then on, Wei’s reputation spread, and many sought his help. Soon, he became a man of wealth, yet he never forgot the old woman, thinking of her day and night. One day, he met someone who told him, "That old woman was Bao Gu, daughter of Bao Jing and wife of Ge Hong. She has practiced this moxibustion in Nanhai for many years."

Thus, when witnessing disputes, should one fan the flames or seek to extinguish them?

In praise I say:

Words that strike at the heart of reason resolve disputes and dispel difficulties. Yet slander and deceit, though whispered, suffice to sow discord. When doubt and resentment gather among friends, regret and fault spread like creeping vines. The two noble scions’ quarrel ultimately brought calamity to the state. Alas! Glib tongues trade in fortune like merchants—recklessly chasing factions.

The *Scripture* states: The Supreme Lord, through the Twenty-Four Gates of Essential Methods for Governing Thought, instructed the Perfected One of Universal Salvation:

- For those who delight in killing and harm, govern with compassion.
- For thieves and robbers, govern with justice and sharing.
- For the licentious and dissolute, govern with chastity.
- For the miserly and grasping, govern with generosity.
- For the wrathful and contentious, govern with joy.
- For the deluded and confused, govern with expedient means.
- For frivolous and ornate speech, govern with simplicity and rectitude.
- For false and deceitful words, govern with trust and compliance.
- For harsh curses and maledictions, govern with recitation and invocation.
- For divisive slander and abuse, govern with praise and admiration.
- For those who drink wine and eat meat, govern with fasting and abstinence.
- For defamation and disparagement, govern with discourse and exposition.
- For arrogance and disdain, govern with reverence and prostration.
- For rigidity and aggression, govern with softness and humility.
- For disbelief in karma and past lives, govern with dependent origination.
- For clinging to existence or nonexistence, govern with the Middle Way.
- For partiality and self-interest, govern with equanimity.
- For greed and filth, govern with purity.
- For obstinacy and immobility, govern with cyclical change.
- For ceaseless fabrication, govern with non-action.
- For narrow adherence to convention, govern with all-pervading adaptability.
- For restlessness and competition, govern with stillness and concentration.
- For sorrow and discontent, govern with carefree ease.
- For unrelieved affliction, govern with liberation.

By and large, the vices of the world are not easily eradicated. Thus, the Supreme Lord revealed these methods to teach restraint. Yet now, people recklessly pursue factions, indulging in frivolous speech, deceitful words, divisive slander, and partisan strife. Measured against these precepts, such conduct incurs the censure of the Supreme Lord.

Have they not heard the *Discourse on Factions*? It states: The noble form factions through shared principles; the petty, through shared gain. A ruler need only dismiss the false factions of petty men and employ the true factions of the noble. The words of Ouyang Xiu may be called perfect.

Long ago, the Khitans and the Xia clashed beyond the Yellow River. At the time, Lord Fan Wenzheng was ordered to pacify Hedong and requested troops for support. Duke Du Zhengxian argued that the Khitans were unlikely to come and that troops should not be rashly deployed. Wenzheng grew furious, even insulting Zhengxian, who bore no grudge. Later, the Khitans indeed did not come. If these two lords were labeled as factions by the vulgar, how could such discord in state affairs justify banishing factions?

Sima Junshi and Fan Jingren enjoyed profound harmony, each deeming the other unmatched. They once said: "We share the same aspirations in life and the same legacy in death. In matters of employment or dismissal, we act in unison without prior consultation. On the three great affairs, our words seem as if spoken by one man, our actions as coordinated as left and right hands." They also declared: "Jingren and I are brothers—our only difference is our surname." Yet when debating the tuning of bells, they endlessly disputed, never reaching accord. Thus, the noble know that though these two agreed, it was not blind agreement. How, then, could factions be banished?

Generally, whether at home or in the community, in matters great or small, one should defer to public judgment. How can factions be recklessly expelled on mere suspicion?

In praise I say:
Words may be right or wrong—resolve them with reason.
People may differ—judge them by their virtue.
To stand alone may seem upright, yet not all who do so are true gentlemen.
When the Way aligns and aspirations converge, it must be the noble.
