Distinguish the upright from the crooked, and the question of factions is settled.

*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Twenty-One, concludes.
*Tai Shang Gan Ying Pian*, Scroll Twenty-Three.

**The Transmission of Li Changling, with a Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

To abandon the proper and emulate the perverse.

The *Transmission* states: Shi Que said: "The lowly encroaching upon the noble, the young overbearing the elder, the distant estranging the near, the new supplanting the old, the petty overriding the great, and lewdness subverting righteousness—these are the Six Perversions. The lord acting with justice, the minister with loyalty; the father with kindness, the son with filial piety; the elder brother with affection, the younger with respect—these are the Six Proprieties. To abandon the proper and emulate the perverse is to hasten calamity."

Generally, the discourse on propriety and perversion cannot surpass this. Formerly, Fan Wei, a great clansman of Chang’an, amassed vast wealth and falsely claimed the county magistrate of Wugong, Fan You, as his grandfather. When You held office as magistrate, he exempted Fan Wei’s household from corvée for over fifty years. Later, when western affairs arose, all lesser households were conscripted and levied, falling into dire straits—only Fan Wei remained untouched. When Liu Chang became prefect of Chang’an, he uncovered the matter, and Fan Wei was punished, his family’s property confiscated by the authorities.

Zhou Zong, having failed the examinations repeatedly, traveled to Qiao Commandery and bowed to the clerk Zhou Ji as his father, daily ranking among his sons. He adopted all three generations’ names and taboos as his own. The following year, he unexpectedly passed one examination, but his father reproached him in verse. Zhou Zong, deeply disheartened, died of shame and regret. Was this not a case of abandoning the proper and emulating the perverse? How lamentable that he never heard the words of the True Lord of the Southern Culmen, Longevity Star.

The Perfected of the Great Ultimate, Xu Laile, once encountered the True Lord of the Southern Culmen, Longevity Star, and inquired about matters of longevity, early death, fortune, and misfortune. The Star Lord said: "The Way of Heaven blesses the good and calamitizes the lewd; the divine spirits reward the proper and punish the perverse. If you can devote yourself to goodness, revere yourself and align with Heaven, remain tranquil when the Dao is fitting, and act in accord with fortune, then your life rests with you—not controlled by the Lord of Death, nor seized by ghosts and spirits. You will not seek longevity yet attain it naturally; you will not pursue life yet preserve it effortlessly.

But if you undermine the moral order, defy Heaven and Earth, indulge in folly and rebellion, insult the divine spirits, oppose benevolence and kindness, and diminish loyalty and filial piety—then in the manifest, the net of punishment will bind you; in the hidden, ghosts and spirits will execute you. When death approaches and you speak of goodness, or when transgressions overflow and you seek repentance, it will be too late."

Judging by the Star Lord’s words, can one truly abandon the proper and emulate the perverse?

**Eulogy:**
In public, nothing is not proper; in private, nothing is not perverse.
Public conduct stems from principle and righteousness; private conduct arises from the heart’s whims.
Subduing emotion to follow principle, all endeavors remain upright.
A single errant thought, and ten thousand affairs are overturned.
Fortune favors the proper; misfortune befalls the perverse—this is the Emperor’s standard.

To turn from kin toward the distant.

The *Transmission* states: Confucius said: "One who does not love their kin but loves others is called perverse in virtue. One who does not respect their kin but respects others is called perverse in ritual." Generally, the distinction between kin and the distant must be made clear. One must not fail to guard against perversity in ritual and virtue.

Formerly, Shen Jizhong, the bedchamber son of Xuan Yilang Qi, had a grandmother who was the paternal aunt of Hanlin Academician Yang Yuansu. At the time, Yuansu had a son afflicted with illness who could not manage affairs. When Jizhong was born, Yuansu, due to their kinship, took him as his own son. Later, Yuansu had two more sons in succession, and Jizhong, though aware of his origins, remained silent and dared not speak. He redoubled his filial devotion, ensuring no slander or estrangement entered between them, and Yuansu grew to love him. On two occasions when Yuansu recommended him for office, Jizhong firmly declined, insisting the positions go to his two younger brothers. He vowed to advance through the examination system by his own efforts. In the fifth year of Yuanfeng, he indeed passed the imperial examinations.

Several years after placing, Yuansu died in Yuhang. Jizhong escorted his coffin home, observed the full mourning rites, then arranged marriages for his younger brothers and sisters, selecting reputable families. Once all was settled, he handed over the entire family estate to his two brothers and departed with nothing. He also composed a treatise, *On Returning to One’s Lineage*, several thousand words long, to show his relatives. In essence, he argued that the kindness of one’s birth parents and adoptive parents is equal. Having repaid his adoptive parents, he could not neglect his birth parents.

After returning, he paid respects to his father and mother and sought out his birth mother, née Du. After a long search, he found her in a humble alley, her face unwashed, her feet bound, barely surviving. Mother and son embraced and wept bitterly, moving all who witnessed it. Twenty years later, his parents died. After observing the full mourning rites, he returned all his ancestral lands to his elder brothers and left empty-handed, renting a house to live in. When urged to accept even a small portion, Jizhong firmly refused, saying: "The Yang family’s wealth was ten times this, yet I cast it aside like a worn-out sandal. How could I bear to take anything from my brothers’ livelihood to enrich myself?" The scholarly community universally admired him.

At the time, Xu Guangyi, serving as Transport Commissioner, recommended him to the court. An imperial edict praised him and granted him an official post, appointing him as Supervisor of Schools in the Yongxing and other circuits. Shortly after, he died. Xu Guangyi, along with Xuanhe Hall Academician Xue Sichang and Drafter of the Central Secretariat Yuwen Huangzhong, jointly memorialized the throne, and a second edict was issued, granting an official post to one of his sons.

Alas! How many in the world are like Jizhong? Truly, he was one who knew how to conduct himself rightly.

**Eulogy:**

Of all human bonds, none are closer than those between parent and child. Brothers share the same qi, like feet and hands. Extending this principle, the clan expands to nine degrees of kinship. Though their ranks may differ, the affection and duty between them ought to be profound. To abandon them and turn elsewhere is to act contrary to virtue—what good could come of it?

One may invoke Heaven and Earth to attest to one’s sincerity. As the tradition states: Heaven has thirty-two modes of covering, Earth has thirty-two modes of bearing; Heaven covers, Earth bears, each governing its own qi. Humanity dwells between them—fortune follows compliance, misfortune defiance, the response as swift as an echo. Even with utmost caution and reverence, one still fears incurring fault; how much more so if one rashly invokes Heaven and Earth to attest to one’s own base sentiments—is this not blasphemy?

In former times, the father of Wang Yigong, though unlearned himself, was devoted to scholars of the classics. Whenever he encountered discarded paper, he would gather and cleanse it, then store it in scented water. Once, he made a vow: “May my descendants achieve prominence through literary accomplishment.” One night, he dreamed that the Sage Confucius patted his back and said, “How diligently you honor my teachings! Yet you are already aged, with no hope of personal achievement—thus I shall send Zeng Shen to be reborn in your family.” In his later years, he indeed had a son, who was none other than Yigong. He named the child *Zeng* after the disciple, and the boy eventually passed the imperial examinations as *zhuangyuan*, rising to the rank of Vice Director of the Secretariat and Grand Councilor.

Li Jingxun’s mother, née Zheng, was strict in educating her sons. One day, a wall collapsed, revealing a jar of coins. Lady Zheng burned incense and prayed: “Could it be that Heaven and Earth pity us, mother and sons, in our orphaned and widowed state, and have bestowed this upon us? Yet my sole wish is that my sons complete their studies, enter officialdom, and receive their salaries—this money is not what I desire.” She then buried it again. Later, Jingxun indeed passed the examinations and rose to the position of Junior Guardian of the Heir Apparent.

Alas! With a single utterance from Wang’s father and a single utterance from Lady Zheng, Heaven and Earth responded so decisively—who can say that invoking Heaven and Earth to attest to one’s sincerity will not incur their reproach?

**Eulogy:**

The mother of Lu Zhuang’s oath was a grave breach of filial piety and kindness. Vowing not to serve and swearing at the grave drew ridicule even from Xi Zhi. Yet when Dai asked twice about the shoes, ten eyes could not deceive—heart and deed stood clear, leaving no room for doubt. How could the August Heaven and Earth heed wanton words? To invoke the divine to witness base matters!

As the tradition says: Each body has its own spirits, each dwelling its own deities. The spirit of the brain, *Jinggen*, is called *Nijiu*; the spirit of the eyes, *Mingshang*, is called *Yingxuan*; the spirit of the ears, *Kongxian*, is called *Youtian*; the spirit of the nose, *Yulong*, is called *Lingjian*; the spirit of the teeth, *Efeng*, is called *Luoqian*; the spirit of the tongue, *Tongming*, is called *Zhenglun*; the spirit of the hair, *Cangying*, is called *Taiyuan*; the twelve-storied *Chonglou* is called *Taiyi*; the spirit of the lungs, *Haohua*, is called *Xushen*; the spirit of the liver, *Longyan*, is called *Sheming*; the spirit of the gallbladder, *Longyao*, is called *Weiming*; the spirit of the heart, *Danyuan*, is called *Shouling*; the spirit of the spleen, *Changzai*, is called *Hunting*; the spirit of the kidneys, *Xuanming*, is called *Yuying*; the spirit of the palms, *Cunxiang*, is called *Jingning*. Then there are the Jade Youth Lord, Jade Maiden Lord, Dao Father Lord, Dao Mother Lord, Master Father Lord, Master Mother Lord, Drinking Sea Lord, Life-Uniting Lord, Thirty-Six Lion Lords, Cinnabar Field Lord, Upper Harmony, Middle Harmony, and Lower Harmony Venerable Lords, and the Three Primordial Perfected Lords—all spirits of the body.

The ghost of the gate is called *Chengbo*; the ghost of opening and closing is called *Shibo*; the ghost-official guarding the main gate is called *Zhengong*; the ghost-official of the small gate is called *Xiaozhen*; the ghost of the dwelling is called *Xizidu*; the ghost-official of the chamber door is called *Wanlun*; the guardian of the stove is called *Yanjing*; the guardian of the privy is called *Nu Zhi*; the ghost of the road is called *Wu Ziding*; the guardian of the road is called *Shigong*—these are the spirits of a dwelling. Know that in every place, wherever one is, there are always spirits presiding. How could one rashly invoke them to witness lewd or vulgar acts?

Long ago, Wang Shaoyi was imprisoned for a long time. Tao Hongjing said: “This man offended the spirits by recklessly invoking them in his grievance, thus bringing this misfortune upon himself.” This further shows that the divine must never be rashly invoked.

**Eulogy:**

The tradition holds that coercive oaths are not heard by the spirits. When the people of Qin cursed Chu, their virtue was not strong enough. The invocators falsely raised their pleas, yet through fear they amended their governance. When Zheng did not perform rites to avert fire, Kong Ning prayed for illness. The spirits are keen of hearing and upright—how could flattery with the mouth suffice? To give and then regret it.

It is recorded: During the *Mingdao* era, the Taiping Prefecture suffered consecutive droughts and locust plagues, leaving the people severely lacking in food. Suddenly, a kind of dark grass grew in the mountains and marshes, which the people gathered to eat, relying on it to stave off hunger. In the *Zhidao* era, the Huanqing Circuit faced famine, and the five grains failed to sprout. Suddenly, artemisia spread across the fields, bearing seeds like millet that could fill the stomach. At the beginning of the *Xining* era, the Huaizhe Circuit suffered famine. Suddenly, mushrooms grew across the fields, sweet and soft, quite delicious, and could be made into soup. During the *Yuanfeng* era, Qingzhou and Zizhou experienced great famine. Suddenly, a kind of stone flour appeared among the mountain rocks; mixed with a little flour, it could be made into cakes. In the *Shengli* era, Zizhou faced a severe famine. The Perfected Lord of True Martiality transformed into an old man, entering the city daily to sell steamed cakes, aiding many. At the end of the *Jianlong* era, the Hebei Circuit suffered famine. He again transformed into a merchant, transporting 150,000 *hu* of millet and wheat into the city. When the goods arrived, the merchant vanished; the officials took them to provide relief, extending aid to several neighboring prefectures.

Generally, famine and scarcity are disasters and the workings of fate, but the growth of such things is the original intent of Heaven and Earth. The Perfected Lord’s actions were likewise the intent of Heaven and Earth. Now, those who receive the harmony of Heaven and Earth, embody the image of Heaven and Earth, yet lack the heart of Heaven and Earth—how can they be called human? It is lamentable that they do not know how superior persons conduct themselves in the world: with great compassion, impartiality, and the virtue of universal salvation. To extinguish one life and give rise to another is to ascend to the supreme fruit. Have they not heard of the deeds of Zhou Huihua and others?

Zhou Huihua was fond of aiding the poor and distressed, enduring hardship without weariness. One day, the Perfected Lord of Wise Observation transformed into a beggar, begging for food in the streets to test his resolve. Zhou indeed delighted in giving, so that very day he was delivered and is now the Perfected Lord of Western Florescence. Liu Kuan was fond of helping the poor and never neglected this duty; as a result, he was recorded by the Most High and now serves as the Marshal of the Youthful Beginning Prefecture, overseeing those who first embark on the Dao. He warned against the dangers of hoarding wealth, urging that one should not惜千金, but rather give freely until nothing remains. The Lord on High praised him and granted him deliverance from the world. Liu Baiyun was also wealthy but initially knew nothing of cultivating the Dao. However, he delighted in giving and did not惜钱帛. Eventually, he encountered the Perfected Lord of Joy, who transmitted the essentials of the Dao to him, allowing him to ascend as an immortal.

How worthy are these men, who through their joy in giving swiftly attained the supreme fruit! Has there ever been one who gave and then regretted it, yet still achieved such rank? Know that the act of giving is the swiftest way to establish merit. Even if one does not immediately attain the fruit, they will surely receive their reward. Have they not heard of the deeds of Lord Yang and others?

Yang Boyong was fond of offering righteous libations. One day, he encountered an extraordinary person who gave him a pint of jade seeds; his descendants all became greatly wealthy and noble. The three brothers Pei Yannian all delighted in generous giving. One day, an old man came to their door begging for a drink, and the brothers treated him with utmost respect. The old man said: “I see that you brothers are all virtuous men. With such accumulated virtue, how could you lack good fortune?” During the An Lushan rebellion, they were led by the old man into a cave, thus escaping the calamity of war. Later, the brothers all attained high office, and their entire families lived past a hundred years.

Xi Baisan was originally a poor man. One day, he saw a Daoist approach a shopkeeper, begging for a single copper coin, but the shopkeeper refused with a scowl. Baisan, indignant on the Daoist’s behalf, reached into his own belt and gave him a coin. That night, he dreamed the Daoist gave him a jade pendant. When he awoke, the pendant had indeed appeared on his chin.

Tang Shumi had been dead for several days when he appeared in his grandson’s dream, saying: “Though I was harsh and narrow-minded in life, I committed no great transgressions. This year, the harvest is poor. If you could open the granary to relieve the people, it would be far better than performing Buddhist rites for me, and I would also benefit.” His grandson that very day gave five hundred *shi* of rice to the county magistrate Sun Kuang for distribution. That night, he dreamed again of Tang Shumi, who smiled and said: “I have now ascended to Heaven.”

All these are examples of those who delighted in giving and each received their due reward. Can those who give and then regret it hope for the same?

The Buddha said: In the single act of giving food, five kinds of blessed rewards may be obtained. The first is the gift of life. The reward for this gift is longevity and boundless wealth in every lifetime. When a person lacks food, they will surely die within seven days; if I provide it, I restore them to life—this is called the gift of life. The second is the gift of beauty. The reward for this gift is a flawless appearance in every lifetime, bringing joy to all who see it. When a person lacks food, their complexion must wither; if I provide it, I restore their radiance—this is called the gift of beauty. The third is the gift of strength. The reward for this gift is great vigor in every lifetime, never diminishing. When a person lacks food, they must grow weak; if I provide it, I restore their strength—this is called the gift of strength. The fourth is the gift of peace. The reward for this gift is safety and stability in every lifetime, free from disaster and misfortune. When a person lacks food, they must suffer distress and peril; if I provide it, I restore their peace—this is called the gift of peace. The fifth is the gift of eloquence. The reward for this gift is wisdom and fluency in every lifetime, with words that others heed. When a person lacks food, they are too weary to speak; if I provide it, I restore their voice—this is called the gift of eloquence.

How great indeed! If the single act of giving food already yields five such rewards, how much greater must be the blessings for all acts of giving, in every circumstance where giving is due?

In praise it is said:
To walk the Way in search of fortune,
to move grain in hope of the people’s favor—
giving only to seek reward,
the hegemon feigns benevolence.
Seeking fame abroad,
their pretense is not sincere.
Should their schemes falter,
they curse Heaven and blame others.
When the five grains fail to ripen,
even chaff and millet become precious.
To borrow and not repay—
as the saying goes: in this world, many act thus.
How could they know that though they may not repay in life,
they must repay after death?

There was an official named Chen Yi who borrowed two hundred thousand coins from Wu Zongsi but refused to return them. Wu reproached him, saying: “If I owed you money in a past life, I repay it now with this. If I did not owe you, but you owe me, then you must become a donkey to repay me.” A year later, Wu sat alone in his hall when he suddenly saw Chen Yi arrive, carrying the money to repay him. Not realizing Chen was dead, Wu called out to question him, but Chen had already slipped into the stable—his tracks vanished. Soon after, a mare gave birth to a foal. Upon inquiry, Wu learned that Chen Yi had indeed died that very day.

Zhang and Chen had a daughter named Fo’er, fifteen years old, who suddenly died. After half a day, she revived and said: “At first, two ghosts seized me and took me past Bi Cliff. Then I saw two other ghosts wrapping two people in a black blanket and carrying them to the Chen household. Next, they wrapped a servant in a patterned blanket, saying: ‘You owe this family fifteen hundred coins—now you must repay them.’ Suddenly, a figure in green appeared and said: ‘This person has also awakened to prajñā; let us forgive her misstep.’ At once, she fell into a pond and awoke with a start.” The next day, her father went to Bi Cliff to investigate and found a Chen family who that night had given birth to three puppies—one brindle and two black. The brindle one had accidentally fallen into a pond and drowned. Hurrying home, he took the money and repaid the Chen household, but they refused to accept it.

Thus, they took the money together and offered it to Huangyan Temple to sponsor Buddhist rites, seeking to atone for their transgressions.

Yet if even borrowed things may not be repaid, how much worse to leave debts unpaid in life?

**Eulogy:**
The Jin ate Qin’s grain—three gifts went unrewarded.
War’s chains and woes brought ruin, a nation’s lament.
The deer repaid its debt; who calls the tiger cruel?
A single meal not forgotten marks the worthy’s resolve.
With no burden on the heart, gods and men alike approve.
Yet to seek beyond one’s lot—

**Tradition says:** In this world, men may be noble or rich, humble or poor, yet each has his destined portion. What the unseen records, the visible receives—all is the karma of past lives. To seek beyond one’s portion—can such things be obtained?

Long ago, Liu Gang was a man of letters who believed himself certain to pass the imperial examinations with high honors. At the time, the Perfected Lord of Auspicious Aid descended to speak on Mount Zhongnan, and Gang, bearing incense, came to inquire. The Perfected Lord said: “Your writing is fine, yet your fate is meager. If you accept your lot and withdraw, you may preserve your remaining years. Should you seek beyond measure, your life will be cut short.” Gang did not believe him and ultimately died without achievement.

Thus, rank and degree are indeed fixed by fate. Even a single degree cannot be sought and gained.

Lord Wu, an official who rose to the rank of Grand Master, was over seventy when he prepared to retire. One night, he dreamed a spirit told him: “You still have seven hundred thousand in salary unclaimed from your post.” At dawn, he examined his records and found the sum exact. He warned his sons and nephews not to claim it, and they obeyed, making no mention of it. Soon after, he died. His descendants sighed: “Were dreams not to be trusted?” That very night, he dreamed again of the spirit, who said: “After resigning your cap, did you not claim half your salary? Was that not seven hundred thousand?” His descendants were stunned, their regret too late.

Thus, lifespan and emolument are indeed fixed by fate. Even what one is due cannot be fully possessed.

When Xiahou Jiazhen had not yet passed the examinations, a boy named Liu who was skilled in physiognomy once told him: “You will attain high office through reputation, but all else is weak. Beyond your salary, you will receive a hundred taels of ill-gotten gain—unless you fall ill, you will die.” Later, Jiazhen rose to become Remonstrator and Straight Historian. Sent as an envoy to celebrate the Prince of Yi’s birthday, he received several hundred thousand in gifts. As he returned home to store them, intending to renovate his house, a single string of cash suddenly rose from the ground and stood upright for a long while before toppling. Startled, he fell ill and died.

Thus, every coin and ingot is indeed fixed by fate. Even ill-gotten gains cannot be possessed.

Have you not heard of the Underworld’s *Lüesheng* Enforcers? After Pei Pu’s death, he became the *Lüesheng* Enforcer of the Three Rivers in Longyou. In a dream, he told his friend Wei Yuanfang: “Every sip and bite, every treasure in the world, is recorded. There is a limit to what one may obtain; if one exceeds that number, I am empowered to seize the excess.”

After Zhang Yu’s death, he was punished by being made a *Lüesheng* Ghost in Yangzhou. In a dream, he told the monk Minchu: “In the world, those like us number in the thousands and tens of thousands, beyond counting. Every trade and profit has its fixed measure. If one exceeds that measure, we are empowered to seize the excess.”

Yet if this is so, are the misfortunes of the world indeed caused by such spirits seizing what exceeds one’s allotment?

In praise it is said:
What nature desires is governed by fate.
Those rich without seeking,
content even to hold the whip,
abide in ease awaiting their turn,
while those who rush through peril stumble and fall.
He who does not overreach his station
preserves both body and name.
O you hundredfold gentlemen,
why strive in vain upon the fields of others?
To force labor beyond measure—
this is what the Most High calls "imposing toil above."

The tradition states: "He who spares not the strength of others but drives them with excessive harshness enacts precisely what the Most High condemns as 'imposing toil above.'" How could they know that greed in accomplishment becomes sin, and that such sin leads to death and rebirth in evil destinies?

"Greed in accomplishment" refers to exploiting the labor of others to achieve one’s ends. As recorded by Li Xuzhou, this is plainly evident. During the Jiayou era, Shen Yu governed Hangzhou. At every weir he passed, oxen were assembled to haul boats. It was the height of summer, and from the supervising officials down to the lowest clerks, all slept exposed upon the weirs, awaiting the ebb and flow of water. Suddenly, they heard voices calling one another by birth order: "What work awaits us tomorrow?" One replied, "Tomorrow Shen Ji’s son will pass through—he who governs Hangzhou. We shall have another round of toil." Another asked, "Has Shen Ji so soon a son governing Hangzhou?" At this, they broke into sobs and lamentations. The officials, struck by the uncanny tone of their speech, sent men to investigate and found only several weir-oxen. Seeing one shed tears, they sighed deeply: "Who knows but that these oxen were once kin or acquaintances of Shen Ji?" Xuzhou heard of this and recorded it in large script as a warning against "imposing toil above," yet many remain heedless.

Formerly, Lu Bing served as Salt Transport Commissioner and dug a new canal solely for salt barges. Autumn rains fell without cease, and the harvest remained unfinished. Yet Bing daily pressed thousands of laborers to dig through mud and rain, driving them relentlessly. The people suffered greatly. Dongpo composed a poem on the matter, including the lines: "Like swine and ducks, they are flung into the mire, splashing and startling one another"—from which the cruelty of their treatment is clear. Alas! Had they seen Xuzhou’s account, would they not have trembled in fear?

In praise it is said:

On a thousand-fathom cliff’s edge, even Peng and Yu would be gripped with fear—should one fall, they would plunge into the abyss. Why then impose reckless toil, indulging in wanton cruelty? Resources meant to aid the people instead become their poison. Yet we rely on the sagely sovereign, who bestows the Five Blessings.

*The Twenty-Third Scroll of the Supreme Scripture on Stimulus and Response* concludes.
*The Twenty-Fourth Scroll of the Supreme Scripture on Stimulus and Response*

**Biography of Li Changling, with a Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi**

Excess in desire and indulgence.

It is recorded: According to the *Daoist Canon*, humans are the spirits of all beings, granted a lifespan of 43,200 days. Their spirit possesses 36,000 primordial yang qi, originally weighing 384 *zhu*, corresponding internally to *Qian*. If one fails to preserve it, allowing it to dissipate, they will meet an untimely end midway.

*Qian* embodies the six yang forces before any movement or expenditure. Those who understand this and cultivate accordingly become immortals.

From age fifteen to twenty-five, unrestrained expenditure depletes 48 *zhu* of qi, leaving a state corresponding to *Gou*. If desires grow more intense over the next decade, another 48 *zhu* is lost, leaving a state corresponding to *Dun*. Without proper nurturing, another decade brings another loss of 48 *zhu*, leaving a state corresponding to *Pi*—here lies the central qi of Heaven and Earth. Without nurturing, another five years deplete 72 *zhu*, leaving a state corresponding to *Guan*. Another five years without nurturing deplete 96 *zhu*, leaving a state corresponding to *Bo*. Beyond this, the sixty-four hexagrams exhaust their qi, and primordial qi is spent entirely, corresponding to *Kun*.

*Kun* is pure yin. Those who subsist on grains alone may yet linger, hence the term "provisional longevity." At this stage, death is near.

Moreover, the kidney chamber is the Mysterious Pass. Heart and kidney unite as one meridian, white as a thread, linked like a ring, spanning one *cun* and two *fen* in breadth. It encases the essence of the entire body, the wondrous qi of the Nine Heavens’ True Unity and Void Harmony, the deep root of life’s most refined vitality. The five viscera, six bowels, hundreds of joints and meridians, golden fluids and jade liquids, the radiance of sun and moon—all reside here. By preserving and consolidating it, the seas of the two regions respond to the rhythm of a single breath, their tides naturally arising to reach the Gate of Life.

In essence, *ren* is the foundation of life, and the kidneys are the lower palace of the Metal Chamber, hence called the Sea of Longevity. If the Tailbone Gate is left unguarded, the vast ocean will run dry. Have you not heard the words of Qibo?

Emperor Huang asked Qibo: "I have heard that people of high antiquity lived past a hundred years in spring and autumn, yet their movements did not decline. Today’s people, however, reach but half a century, and their movements already weaken. Is this because the world has changed? Or have people lost something?"

Qibo replied: "The people of high antiquity, those who understood the Dao, modeled themselves upon yin and yang, harmonized with the arts of calculation, regulated their eating and drinking, maintained regularity in rising and resting, and did not recklessly exhaust themselves. Thus, their form and spirit remained united, and they fulfilled their natural lifespan, living past a hundred years before departing.

Today’s people, however, take wine as their broth, treat recklessness as normal, enter the bedchamber while drunk, exhaust their essence with desire, dissipate their true qi, fail to preserve fullness, fail to govern their spirit, seek only to gratify their hearts, and act contrary to the joys of life. Their rising and resting lack regulation, and so they decline by fifty.

The *Huangting Jing* also states: 'For long life, strictly guard the bedchamber.' The urgency of this lies precisely in the weight of beings’ karmic burdens. How lamentable that they do not know—women’s allure, as the Buddha taught, is the root of all suffering, the source of obstacles, the origin of killing and harm, the wellspring of sorrow. Thus, the enlightened keep far from it.

Ren Huigong grew stronger in his later years. When asked about his art of nurturing life, he said: 'I gained insight from reading the *Wen Xuan*.' Someone pressed: 'May I ask where the insight lay?' He replied: 'A stone that holds jade makes the mountain gleam; water that cradles a pearl makes the river radiant.'

Pu Chuanzheng, while serving as prefect of Hangzhou, was visited by an elder from his hometown named Li Jue, who was already a hundred years old yet had the glowing complexion of an infant. Pu said: 'I wish to hear your method of self-cultivation.' The elder replied: 'My method is most simple and easy—there is nothing to it but abstaining from desire early on.'"

Shall indulgence in lust then know no bounds? Should the teaching of restraining desire remain unpracticed? As for the transgression of illicit desire—such as gazing upon another’s fair wife and harboring secret designs, or engaging in clandestine acts in secluded places—

The eulogy states:
To teach what harms oneself is forbidden;
What benefits, one should bear for life.
Guard against lust above all,
For nurturing life is paramount.
Heed the sagely words with awe,
And to the end of your days, you shall have no regrets.
Yielding to desire injures life—
To whom can one complain at death?
A venomous heart beneath a gentle guise—

As the saying goes: *"A venomous heart beneath a gentle guise"*—such were the likes of Li Yifu. Li Yifu’s countenance was most courteous and tender; whenever he spoke, he wore a cheerful, gentle smile. Yet in secret, he was treacherous and quick to anger, his malice rooted deep in his heart. The slightest offense would provoke him to strike. People called him *"the dagger in the smile"* or *"the human cat"*—soft in appearance, yet truly harmful. Later, he was exiled and died in Jun.

Can such venom in the heart be justified? Most in the world harbor such malice, yet how many realize that after death, they shall fall into wretched destinies?

Long ago, Lu Zhen had a maidservant named Xiaojin who was suddenly tormented by a female ghost. Though many shamans and exorcists were summoned, none could cure her. They then prayed before the Buddha. One night, Lu dreamed of an old man riding a lion, his form like that of a bodhisattva, who said to him: *"The one harassing Xiaojin was once the daughter-in-law of the Wu family in the eastern neighbor’s house, named Zhu the Twelfth. For her lifelong malice, she fell into the serpent realm after death. She dwells beneath the great paper mulberry tree by the She Tianzhu Temple, where a passage leads to an underground cavern. Over the years, she has grown into a spirit and gained the power to transform. If you wish to escape this calamity, hasten to a household of good people and hide in secret—then she will not dare approach."*

Lu followed the warning, and indeed, the ghost no longer came.

Yet consider this: when it comes to the heart’s intent, should one wield it with malice—or with goodness? One path leads to death and rebirth in the serpent realm; the other ensures even ghosts dare not draw near. Between these two, you must choose for yourself.

**Eulogy:**
When the raptor strikes, it first conceals its form.
The pitfall for men is veiled as level ground.
Soft words and gentle countenance—
even foes remain unalarmed.
Yet the venom of aconite and the toxin of the *zhen* bird
may still be met with retribution.
So too the "human cat"—
a name not earned in vain.

**To feed another with defiled food:**
As the saying goes, *"To feed another with defiled food"*—even our Confucian scholars speak of this. The *Book of Rites* states: *"Do not return fish or meat"*—meaning what has passed one’s own lips. Yet if we speak in terms of karmic cause and effect, such acts not only forfeit blessings but surely invite disaster.

Have you not heard of the Venerable Shi-yan and Qu Yongshou?

Long ago, the Venerable Shi-yan abandoned his rank to leave the household life. As his eyes bore a double pupil, his younger brother grew suspicious. To allay doubts, he feigned an eye ailment, constantly wiping his eyes with a cloth—hence his name, "Wiping-Eyes." Yet wherever he went, a radiant glow emanated from him, deepening his unease. One day, as the temple kitchen prepared congee, he removed his undergarments and shook them over the cauldron. That very day, his glow vanished. If feeding others with defiled food does not forfeit blessings, then what does?

Qu Yongshou made his living as a rice merchant. In the *yimao* year of the Shaoxing era, he suddenly heard that rice prices had soared. Seizing the moment, he drew water from a paddy field to moisten his grain—unaware that the field had been fertilized with manure. Soon, dark clouds gathered, and thunder roared. Yongshou knew his transgression and feared the worst. Reaching into his sash, he pulled out a string of cash and handed it to a traveling companion, entreating him to return it to his mother. No sooner had he spoken than the skies cleared, and he escaped unharmed. If feeding others with defiled food does not invite disaster, then what does?

Yet according to the Buddha’s teachings, the consequences extend far beyond this. After death, one is destined to be reborn as a pig, a dung beetle, or a hungry ghost. How can one fail to guard against this?

**Eulogy:**
The grain of the unrighteous—
even Master Zhongzi would retch at the taste.

Those who steal to feed others may spit out the food without soiling their teeth, yet to kick it toward them is to invite the beggar’s scorn. If even proper offerings are made without reverence, what merit do the three sacrificial beasts confer? To treat men as beasts—how can this not be deemed callous?

**Left Daoism deludes the masses.**
As the saying goes: *Left Dao* refers to sorcery. To make sorcery one’s art—how can it not end in ruin?

Hou Mochen Liyue gained favor through leftist arts and rose to become Defense Commissioner of Zhengzhou. Profligate and lawless, he wantonly killed many. Zhao Pu sent men to investigate and uncovered his crimes; he was stripped of his rank, tattooed, and exiled to Shangzhou. Later, Zhao Pu forcefully petitioned for his execution. An imperial edict was issued, dispatching an envoy to cut out his heart as a warning to the masses. Soon after, the court regretted the decision and sent another envoy to spare him. Before long, the messenger’s horse stumbled on the road to Bian, throwing him off and exposing the heart. After switching mounts and hurrying onward, the envoy arrived only to find Liyue’s abdomen already slit open, his corpse stoned and hung from a post at the relay station.

His son, Li Tan, practiced sorcery and was implicated in the scandal. Father and son were both imprisoned in the Censorate’s dungeon. Once the charges were confirmed, Tan was demoted to Vice Prefect of Nan’enzhou, while his sons were all exiled beyond the Five Ridges. They were escorted out of the city under guard that very day. Later, as his attendants took their leave, Tan took his own life.

Yang Qian claimed to have mastered the arts of Mozi, able to command ghosts and spirits, summoning food even when the objects were clenched in another’s fist. He could also refine cinnabar into dry mercury, and none could best him at dice. He could pick locks, alter appearances—nothing was beyond him. At the time, the powerful and eminent all held him in high regard, bestowing upon him offices and robes, which he wore with great pride. Before long, his schemes unraveled, and he was ultimately executed.

Ming Chongyan was skilled in sorcery, able to instantly summon objects from a thousand *li* away. One day, while sleeping alone in his chamber, he was suddenly stabbed to death by a ghost.

Upon his death, the dagger remained lodged in his heart, and all said it was because he had overworked the spirits, so they killed him in return.

Yet if heterodox ways delude the masses, can this be tolerated?

**Eulogy:**
The people of Qi and Ai were long ago restrained by prohibitions.
Confucius did not speak of the uncanny, fearing it would disrupt the constant order.
Humaneness, rightness, ritual, and music—these were the guiding principles of the hundred kings.
Daily sustenance—nothing more than farming and weaving.
Even Wen Cheng and Wu Li were executed by Emperor Wu.
Yet now, short measures and narrow gauges prevail.

**Commentary:**
The *Canon of Shun* states: "Standardize the pitch-pipes, measures, weights, and balances."
The *Great Commentary* says: "When the sage faces south to govern the world, he must begin with the human Dao."
The human Dao encompasses nine matters, and establishing standards of weight and measure comes first.
The *Royal Regulations* states: "Cloth and silk that do not meet the proper fineness or width shall not be sold in the market."
The *Monthly Ordinances* says: "In the mid-autumn days, unify the measures, balance the scales, correct the steelyards, and calibrate the peck and bushel."
Mencius said: "Only by measuring can one know what is long and what is short."
In ancient times, the Duke of Zhou assembled the feudal lords in the Bright Hall, instituted rites and music, and promulgated standards of measure—whereupon all under heaven submitted.
"Measure" refers to lengths like *zhang* and *chi*, heights and depths, breadths and narrows.
"Capacity" refers to the volumes held by *dou*, *ou*, *dou*, *hu*, baskets, and boxes.
In essence, when the human heart is unbalanced, these standards are set to maintain its equilibrium.
Yet now, instead of adhering to this balance, people shorten and narrow their measures—this is to defy the prohibitions of the Most High, to lose the equilibrium of one’s own heart. It is not merely deceiving others but deceiving oneself.

Do you not heed the teachings on the spirit of the heart?
The southern recluse Zhu Zhenbai said: "The world speaks of not deceiving the gods and spirits. I say it is not the gods of heaven and earth one must not deceive, but the spirit of one’s own heart—then one does not deceive the gods and spirits."
Someone asked the old pedant: "Should one serve the spirits?"
He replied: "Serve the spirit of your own heart. No millet, no sacrificial animals—only the practice of not deceiving is of use."

Alas, how many truly understand this?
Because they do not, short measures and narrow gauges are but the least of their transgressions.

**Eulogy:**
To bend a foot for a yard—this is what the noble man finds shameful.
Goods that do not meet the measure shall not be sold in the market.
Not a hair’s breadth of partiality, not a grain’s weight of discrepancy.
How dare one adorn the heart of a thief and cast aside public principle?
What harm does it do to the measure? The harm to oneself is far greater.

**Light scales and small measures.**
**Commentary:**
Heaven has its Hall of the Dipper and Scale, and man has his use of the dipper and scale. Among the twenty-eight constellations, it is the Star of Zhang that governs them. In this text, the Most High has decreed it a sin.

Generally, the *zhu* and *li*, the *sheng* and *ge*—these are the daily necessities of the common folk. Every coin, every cash is hard-won. Thus, not a *zhu*, not a *li*, not a *sheng*, not a *ge* may be made light or small.

According to the *Cloud Script of the Five Thunders*, during the reign of Chen Houzhu, a man in the market was struck dead by lightning, and on his back were written the words: "..." The Daoist adept Li Juzheng recognized them as the four characters for "light and heavy in scales and measures."

Furthermore, in the *Lotus Sutra*, when the scripture was being expounded, ten rakshasa women, along with their children and retinues, made a solemn vow before the Buddha to protect this sutra, saying:
"Like the sin of killing one’s parents,
Like the misfortune of pressing oil,
Like the crime of cheating with scales and measures,
Like Devadatta’s sin of splitting the Sangha—
Whoever harms this Dharma-teacher
Shall incur such retribution."

Thus, are light scales and small measures truly a minor sin?

For this reason, Cheng Sichang did not use uneven scales and measures, and was recorded in the ledger of good deeds as having been inspected by the Perfected Warrior and True Lord.
Li Yongzheng crafted scales that twisted in midair and bushels with false bottoms—these were condemned by the Supreme Emperor, who commanded local spirits to warn him openly and punish him in secret.
Ying Xianwu the Martial and Illustrious repeatedly ignored the warnings until both his eyes were blinded, his wife exiled, his son dead, and he himself reduced to begging in the streets.

All these are recorded in the annals—how can one not stand in awe?

**Eulogy:**

"Scales arise from the standard weight,
Weighing things with impartiality.
Measures turn and bushels gauge,
Entrusted with all giving and receiving.
Yet some seek profit,
Deceiving others’ ears and eyes.
Indulging private desire,
They rely on public instruments.
Scales and measures must not deceive—
To deceive the heart is most fearful.
To mingle falsehood with truth—
As the saying goes: to mingle falsehood with truth,
Like Lu Chang beneath Mount Lu,
Who mixed fish fat into oil;
Or Huang Yu before Jianlong Abbey,
Who made candles from ox tallow.
After days accumulated, both were struck by thunder.
Generally, to deceive and mislead others
Brings the gravest of sins.
Not only these two cases are so—
In all matters of food and drink,
Medicinal substances,
Or even the exchange of gold and silk,
Any deceit or error is impermissible.
Have you not heard the tales of the two immortals?

Long ago, when Lü Dongbin first studied the Dao,
He met a stranger who taught him the method of nurturing the Dao.
Dongbin said, 'I wish to hear of it.'
The stranger replied, 'Boil copper into silver.'
Dongbin asked, 'Will it change?'
'It will,' said the stranger, 'but only after five hundred years.'
Dongbin said, 'To mislead people five hundred years later—who would wish to learn?'
With this single remark, he was named among those hidden in the Celestial Treasury.
Later, he met Zhongli Quan and thus attained transcendence.

Li Zhen was gentle and frugal,
Never having wronged another.
One day, he met a Daoist who called himself Zhang Qiwu,
Who taught him the art of transmuting base metals.
Zhen said, 'My fate is meager—I do not wish to learn.'
Qiwu was greatly astonished and said,
'Your insight surpasses mine.'
Thereupon, he drew his hairpin, wielded it as a sword,
And slashed the earth—it opened.
He cast himself into the fissure, and the earth closed behind him.
Thus was he revealed as an immortal,
And later, Zhen too attained the Dao.

Yet those who mingle falsehood with truth—
How can they avoid falling?
After the deaths of Lu Chang and Huang Yu,
What fate awaited them?

**Eulogy:**
To counterfeit truth with falsehood,
The harm to things is but slight.
Yet when men disrupt the constant norms,
Households and states are imperiled.
Acting like market swindlers,
Deceitful, greedy, treacherous, and foul,
They falsely claim to follow the great current,
Forming cliques and currying favor.
Once deceived,
One may unknowingly consume the beak of a bird.

To seize illicit gain—
As the *Highest* says: to mingle falsehood with truth.
It also says: those who seize illicit gain
Do not merely mingle falsehood with truth.

Long ago, Yang Bing lived beside the great river.
Each year, he amassed hay and straw,
Hoping the river would burst its banks to double his profit.
At the time, Zhao Changyan governed Tianxiong Prefecture
And learned of his deeds.
When autumn floods came,
Bing again enticed corrupt villagers to breach the dikes.
The dike officials sent urgent reports,
And Changyan ordered his men to go directly to Bing’s home,
Seize all his stored hay and straw,
And use it to reinforce the dikes.
He then sent Bing to prison for interrogation,
And the case was concluded with his punishment.
From then on, such harm ceased,
And those dwelling by the river dared not seek illicit gain.

Sun Nanjin was adopted as the heir to a wealthy family.
Relying on his power,
He befriended officials and clerks,
Aiding lawsuits and disrupting governance,
Stopping at nothing.
In one or two major cases,
The officials gained little,
While he alone secretly amassed great wealth,
Acquiring fields and estates,
Ever more arrogant and overbearing.
He even melted coins to forge vessels,
Drained ponds to catch all the fish,
Exchanged wine for hemp,
Built boats and installed pestles,
Encroached on waterways,
And seized commoners’ fields.
Whatever others dared not do,
He did without hesitation;
Whatever others dared not take,
He took without restraint."

For several years thus, he suddenly contracted a malignant illness, unable to take food or drink, his spine withered like a jackal’s. When he died, people all said he had already been reborn as a donkey. If this is how one fares by seizing illicit gain, can such conduct be justified?

**Eulogy:**
Profit through deceitful words,
Acquisition through cunning schemes.
Trace his intent—
A thousand twists, ten thousand turns.
True venom finds no cure,
Like fire set beneath tinder.
His methods sold through treachery,
His harm wrought by fierce obsession.
Stinging the blind, gnawing the hidden,
Like arrowroot, wall-scorpions, and burrowing lizards.
Crushing the good to serve as the base—

As the saying goes: *"Crushing the good to serve as the base"*—this is precisely what those who deny cause and effect dare to do. How could they know that those now reduced to bondservants and maidservants are all souls who, in past lives, committed sins and accumulated evil until their transgressions numbered one thousand eight hundred? If one who does not truly belong among them is forced into their ranks, this is what is called *"crushing the good to serve as the base."* Would any person of discernment ever consent to such a fate?

Long ago, Ma Juan’s father, having no son in middle age, took a concubine of surpassing beauty.

Each time he trimmed his hair, he would invariably withdraw upon seeing the master, his demeanor as though disheartened. The master, finding this strange, questioned him, to which he replied: "My father once held such-and-such an office, but died unluckily far from home, leaving no means to return. Thus I was sold into servitude, and even now the period of mourning rites has not yet ended. When I bind my hair, I wrap plain white silk beneath, covering it temporarily with red cloth—I did not wish for you to see it, and there was no other reason." The master was moved with pity and, that very day, sought out his mother to return him, moreover providing generous aid.

That very night, he dreamed of a feather-robed figure who said: "Heaven bestows upon you a son; joy flows like a stream." The following year, a son was indeed born, and he named him *Juan*, meaning "streamlet"—this was Juji. When the boy grew and sat for the examinations, the feather-robed figure again entered his dream, saying: "If you wish to pass, you must be first thirteen times." Later, he ranked first in the Imperial Academy, first in the provincial recommendations, and when the roll was called, he stood first in the realm—counting on his fingers, it was indeed thirteen times first.

When Cheng Yanbin, Prefect of Luocheng, advanced to attack Suining, the day the city fell, his subordinates presented three maidens, all strikingly beautiful. The master, then in his cups, addressed the girls: "You are as my own daughters—how dare I offend you?" He personally sealed the door and confined them to a chamber. At dawn, he sought out their parents and returned them.

They wept in gratitude, saying: "We pray the Grand Marshal will soon be granted the banner and staff of command." Yanbin replied: "I dare not hope for such honors—only that when my time comes, I may die without illness, and that will be blessing enough." Later, he rose to the rank of Surveillance Commissioner and lived to ninety-seven, passing without sickness. All his sons held office.

Who would say that such deeds go unrewarded? From this, we must know that those who oppress the good and reduce them to servitude will surely incur retribution.

**Eulogy:**
Lou Jing halted the imperial carriage;
Wei Qing was once a bondsman.
The lowly must not be despised—
how much less the good, who may be oppressed?
Duke Wen of Jin washed his hands,
provoking the anger of Qin’s lady.
Women must not be scorned—
how much less men?
As parents to the people,
what then should be their way?
Beware deceiving the foolish.

**Tradition says:** All deception is forbidden—
but to deceive the foolish is especially forbidden.
Long ago, Mao Lie built his fortune through injustice. If a man had good fields, Mao would covet them relentlessly until he seized them. There was a man named Chen Qi, whose three younger brothers were still young. He sold all his family’s land at a low price to Mao’s household. When the brothers came of age and divided their inheritance, Chen secretly gathered funds to redeem the land. Mao, however, detained him when he came to reclaim it, refusing to return the deeds. Over time, he grew increasingly deceitful. Chen pleaded his case repeatedly but found no justice, so he went to the shrine of Mount Yue and submitted a petition. Soon after, Mao died; the next day, Chen died as well. Later, Chen revived and said: "In the courts of Mount Yue, Mao pleaded with me, saying: ‘In my life, I committed such acts thirteen times—all the contracts are in such-and-such a chest in such-and-such a place. When you return, urge my family to search for them and return each to its rightful owner. Only then may my punishment be lessened. Otherwise, my sins will only grow heavier.’"

Zhang Gai owned a grand and stately residence. In need of funds, he mortgaged it to Zhang Jun for a thousand strings of cash. Jun coveted the house and bribed the broker to draft a deed of absolute sale. Once the contract was made, he swapped the first page to turn it into a deed of sale. Years later, when Gai lacked funds and sought to reclaim his property, Jun produced the contract—now a deed of sale. Gai was speechless, left in tears, and looking to the heavens, he cursed: "May your descendants one day suffer as I do, unable even to speak their grievance." Later, Jun’s descendants all lost their voices and died.

Can deceiving others be justified? Even if they are not foolish, it must never be done.

**Eulogy:**
Meet the blind with respect;
enter an empty room as if others were present.
In darkness, guard your solitude;
step outside as if greeting a guest.
Let your heart be without deceit,
and regard all things with impartiality.
The people may be simple,
but even the foolish are watched by the divine.
Who is more foolish than the common folk?
Those who deceive themselves.
Greed knows no bounds.

**Tradition says:** Laozi said: "He who knows contentment is rich." And again: "No sin is greater than desire; no disaster greater than discontent." And: "Which is dearer, name or self? Which is greater, self or wealth? Which is more harmful, gain or loss? Excessive love leads to great waste; hoarding invites heavy loss. Know contentment and avoid disgrace; know restraint and avoid danger—this is the way to longevity."

Mozi said: "It is not that there is no secure dwelling, but no secure heart; not that there is no sufficient wealth, but no contented heart."

Zhuangzi said: "The roc and the wren differ in size; the cedar and the morning mushroom differ in lifespan. Yet each finds its own peace, its own fulfillment." These are the words of sages and worthies—models for conduct in the face of wealth.

Alas! People wear clothes only to cover their bodies, yet those draped in thousand-tael furs still feel lacking, unaware that those in patched robes are at ease. They eat only to fill their stomachs, yet those who feast on thousand-coin banquets still feel wanting, unaware that those content with simple fare are joyful. Houses exist only to shelter from wind and rain, yet those in towering mansions with carved walls still feel unsatisfied, unaware that those in huts with earthen windows are at peace. Utensils serve only their purpose, yet those who dine with jade cups and ivory chopsticks still feel deprived, unaware that those who drink from chipped vessels are content.

Because of this, the heart is never satisfied. How can one awaken to the truth that all things in this world vanish in an instant? This body of ours will ultimately perish. What does not perish is the single luminous essence. Rather than toiling in vain over what is transient, why not devote effort to what is eternal?

Long ago, King Prasenajit asked Śākyamuni Buddha: "Now that I am weighed down by age, I know I must perish." The Buddha said: "Have you ever seen the waters of the Ganges?" The king replied: "When I was three, I saw them." The Buddha asked: "Now that you are sixty, have you seen them again?" The king said: "I saw them just yesterday." The Buddha asked: "How do they compare to what you saw at three?" The king said: "They are unchanged." The Buddha said: "Though your face is wrinkled, your faculty of sight has never wrinkled. What wrinkles is subject to change; what does not wrinkle is beyond change. What changes must perish; what is beyond change was never born and never dies." The king was enlightened in that moment, realizing that after death, life does not cease but continues—there is no true annihilation.

The Third Patriarch, Śāṇavāsa, asked Upagupta: "How old are you?" Upagupta replied: "I am seventeen." The Patriarch asked: "Is your body seventeen, or is your nature seventeen?" Upagupta said: "Master, your hair is white—is it your hair that is white, or your mind?" The Patriarch said: "Only my hair is white, not my mind." Upagupta said: "My body is seventeen, but my nature is not seventeen—it is the same."

In general, the body of birth and death cycles through existence, a wheel without beginning or end. Moreover, this flowing mind is unbroken—what appears as bubbles rising and vanishing is but delusion. From the first moment of consciousness to the last breath, it is all the same.

If all in the world could awaken to this truth, then that single point of luminous clarity would remain ever evident. Once it emerges, what fear of lacking fortune? What wealth or possessions could ever be wanting?

In praise it is said:
How brief is man’s sojourn in the world—
morning dew clinging to the stalk,
autumn leaves parting from the branch.
Life has its bounds; why must one seek excess?
The ruins of Deer Terrace,
the slopes of Black Dragon Palace—
wealth exhausted, rank at its height,
all crumble into mounds and hollows.

As the *Tradition* states: "So-called curses need not be formally inscribed in memorials. Whenever, in the heat of anger and dispute, one recklessly invokes spirits, that is a curse." According to the *Chapter on Oaths and Curses*, if one utters curses, then beneath the ninefold earth, in the four cardinal directions and four intermediate quarters, within mountains and marshes, upon the five kinds of soil, malevolent ghosts—those who exact punishment through curses and oaths, those who transmit lingering corpse qi, the ghosts of the Five Hills and Five Graves, spirits that bring calamity and harm, the ghosts of deceased officials and earthly essences, the ghosts of plague and pestilence that deplete and destroy, specters and demons, inauspicious and ruinous spirits, the ghosts of the five directions and five soils that rebound with retribution, those that lurk and linger without retreat, the ghosts that bind and strangle, that execute and kill, that spread epidemic and flood—all manner of vicious ghosts seize the opportunity to inflict harm. Unless one submits a memorial to seek absolution, invoking the Heavenly Master’s Generals—*Breaker of Ghosts*, *Destroyer of Temples*, and *Terminator of Curses*—to descend and disperse them, such curses cannot easily be eradicated.

Yet is it truly worth seeking vindication through curses?

In former times, the Zhang family, father and son, would quarrel in anger and point to heaven and revile the sun. Later, when the son and his nephew disputed their inheritance, they too sacrificed to ghosts and invoked spirits, cursing one another. Within a few years, nearly all perished; only one branch, uninvolved in the strife, remained—yet even they declined, never matching their former prosperity.

There was also Elder Yang, who frequented the home of a widow, appearing quite intimate. Some spoke of it. The widow, fierce by nature, could not contain her rage and called upon heaven and sacrificed to ghosts to curse him. Within a year, the widow and Elder Yang died on the same day. Was it that, at the time, there was no truth to the rumors, and she sought only to clear her name? Or was there truth, and she acted merely to silence the slander? Otherwise, how could two die on the same day? Whether the affair was real or not, none can say—yet it serves as a warning.

In praise it is said:

"In the *Rites of Zhou*, the five modes of hearing reveal nothing hidden in speech or countenance. How could mere words suffice? For therein lies the liver and kidneys. When rival states forge alliances, the king’s grace is thus extinguished. Excessive speech leads to repeated failure—better to endure with silence. If one heeds only the divine, how can trust be shown? Thus concludes the twenty-fourth scroll of the *Taishang Ganying Pian*.

---

The *Taishang Ganying Pian*, Scroll Twenty-Eight
*Biography of Li Changling*
*Eulogy by Zheng Qingzhi*

It is said: Spitting or urinating toward the north—

The *Tradition* records: Yang Ziyun wrote an essay arguing that during the Spring and Autumn period, day and night were of equal length, for the sun rose due east at *mao* and set due west at *you*. Huan Tan, however, composed his *New Treatise* to refute him, saying: "Ziyun’s argument refers to the *mao* and *you* of the human realm, not the celestial *mao* and *you*. The celestial *mao* and *you* align with the Northern Dipper’s pole. The pole is the Celestial Pivot; the pivot is the axis of heaven. Just as a canopy has its *baodou* (central pin), though the canopy turns, the *baodou* remains fixed—so too does the Northern Pole, though heaven revolves. From man’s perspective, the north is indeed north, yet not the true center of heaven. Thus, during the Spring and Autumn period, the sun’s rising and setting always occurred near the Southern Dipper. One must recognize that heaven turns like a canopy, its northern path ever distant, its southern path ever near. How could day and night have been equal? Ziyun, at a loss, could not rebut him. From this, we know all the more that the Dipper is the center of heaven—no doubt remains.

Therefore, the Primordial Supreme Lord calls the Northern Pole the *Central Pole*, and the Precious Upper Perfected One names the Northern Dipper’s seven stars the *Seven Stars of the Central Heaven*. Even our Confucians say: 'The Dipper is the Emperor’s Chariot, revolving at the center, governing the four directions, dividing yin and yang, establishing the four seasons, balancing the five phases, shifting the nodal measures, and fixing all celestial markers.' Who could deny this?

In ancient times, the Heavenly Sovereign’s Northern Pole and the Northern Dipper’s seven stars transformed and took form from within the Seven-Treasure Lotus, coalescing into radiant light. They flew to the Central Pole, nine million nine hundred thousand *li* above the earth, and manifested as the Nine Great Treasure Palaces—the Purple Tenuity Enclosure, the Great Void Palace, and the Curved Array’s domain—where they grasp the talismans and charts, govern the primordial transformations, and preside as lords over all stars.

- **Greedy Wolf** aligns with Heaven as the *Celestial Origin*, governing the body.
- **Giant Gate** aligns with Earth as the *Terrestrial Origin*, governing fate.
- **Salary Store** aligns with Fire as the *Solar Origin*, governing fortune.
- **Literary Curved** aligns with Wood as the *Lunar Origin*, governing emolument.
- **Integrity Perfected** aligns with Earth as the *River Origin*, governing official rank.
- **Martial Curved** aligns with Water as the *River Origin*, governing longevity.
- **Broken Army** aligns with Metal as the *Oceanic Origin*, governing wives and children.

Each star commands thirty-six palaces, and each palace oversees thirty-six bureaus. The Three Realms and the Ten Directions, the hundred spirits and host of perfected beings, all submit to their authority. Thus, is not the Central Heaven’s Northern Pole most revered? How could the dwelling of such majesty be defiled?

According to the *Scripture of Longevity*, the term *north* need not mean true north. In spring, it is east; in summer, south; in autumn, west; in winter, north—wherever the lunar station points, one violates the Dipper’s handle, swiftly diminishing lifespan. Beware!

In praise it is said:
The aim of self-mastery: move not without propriety."

Spitting without regard—brave yet devoid of righteousness.
Urinating into a basket—even plucking every hair would not suffice to atone.
Standing firm without shifting one’s ground—this the noble person holds weighty.
How much more so for dwelling in the north, where all stars converge in reverence?

Facing the hearth to chant or weep:
As the *Yellow Emperor’s Scripture of the Hearth* states, one must not sing, recite poetry, weep, curse, or shout at the hearth’s entrance. All such admonitions align with the foregoing.
Yet people see the hearth as mere piled bricks and clay—how could they know its mighty power, its inviolability, to be thus?

Long ago on Mount Song stood a temple of extraordinary numinosity. Within its hall stood only a hearth, utterly untouchable; to approach was to court disaster. Thus, from near and far, offerings and prayers came without cease. One day, a Chan master arrived at the temple, strode directly into the hall, and struck the hearth thrice with his staff, saying: “You are but clay molded together—where does your sacredness arise? From whence comes your spirit? How dare you thus boil and butcher living beings?” Having spoken, he struck thrice more, and the hearth shattered at his touch.

Presently, a green-robed attendant emerged, his tall cap swaying as he leapt forth. Bowing to the master, he said: “I am the hearth spirit of this temple. Long have I craved requital, but now, through the Chan master’s teaching of the *Endurance of the Unborn Dharma*, I have attained liberation from this place and shall be reborn in heaven. How could I not offer my deepest gratitude?” The master replied: “This is your inherent nature—no coercion from my words.” The spirit bowed again and vanished.

Thereafter, people called the master “Breaker of the Hearth.” Later, a monk who had long served at his side asked: “Though I have been near you for years, you never once guided me to enlighten this hearth. By what principle did it swiftly attain rebirth in heaven?” The master said: “I merely pointed out that you are but clay molded together—there is no other truth.” The monk stood in contemplation, and the master asked: “Do you understand?” “I do not,” the monk replied. The master said: “Your inherent nature—what is there not to understand?” The monk bowed in reverence. The master exclaimed: “Fallen! Fallen! Shattered! Shattered!” The monk then experienced great awakening.

Alas! This hearth, upon hearing the Chan master’s words, swiftly attained the *Endurance of the Unborn Dharma*. If such is the case, how could chanting or weeping before it not invite the calamity of blasphemy?

In praise it is said:

Silence at meals, no words to speak;
Feasts are not the place for sighs.
Sorrow and joy each have their kind,
Each with its proper time and place.
How can chanting or weeping be recklessly performed?
Not only does it profane the divine,
But it disrupts the proper order.
Attend to your duties with reverence,
And be mindful of silence.

Moreover, burning incense in the hearth fire:
As the tradition states: According to the statutes of the Heavenly Master’s school,
The ashes and embers beneath the hearth are called *"Hidden Dragon’s Dung"*—
Thus, incense must not be burned there.

I have perused the scriptures and teachings,
And the prohibitions regarding incense and fire extend beyond this single case.
For instance, oil-stained paper wicks must not be used to ignite paper money,
For this is called *"Wasted Accumulation of Wax Coins."*
Piled high as mountains in the Eastern Peak,
Yet the celestial and terrestrial offices of yin and yang all refuse them.

Again, when making offerings to the Perfected Warrior,
In summer, plums must not be used;
In winter, pomegranates must not be used.
When inviting the descent of the Highest Perfected,
One must not burn *"Nipple Incense"* or sandalwood,
For these are called *"Bathing Incense."*
The *"Monthly Rose"* is called the *"Untimely Flower,"*
The *"Golden Paulownia"* is called the *"Ghost Flower."*
All these must be avoided.
For what is better—to heed these prohibitions with reverence,
Or to disregard them and invite offense?

In praise it is said:

Fire, as recorded in the Zhou canon, is dispensed and received in due time. When the burning-mirror draws from the sun, its purity and sincerity are manifest. Where reverent intent resides, there may sacrifice and prayer be offered. Yet to gather smoldering embers—this is not fitting. If even one thing is not done with care, the gods will take heed.

Impure firewood makes impure food. As the saying goes: though the firewood may not burn, its qi still rises upward—this is why impure firewood must not be used for cooking. Have you not heard of the matters of *"labored firewood"* and *"refined charcoal"*? Once, Xun Xu was seated beside the emperor when the royal chef presented the meal. Xu said: "This must have been cooked with labored firewood." The emperor did not believe him, but upon summoning the chef and questioning him, it was confirmed that an old carriage axle had indeed been used for fuel. Yang Shou, though he ate plain fare, always cooked with charcoal. One day, Li Zhang hosted a banquet for his two sons, also using charcoal for the fire. The two sons would not touch the food, barely nibbling on some fruit instead. Their retainer, the monk Shenggang Lian, asked: "Even if other dishes are inedible, what fault lies in rice cooked with charcoal?" The two sons replied: "When cooking with charcoal, one must first burn the charcoal until it is hot, hence the name *refined charcoal*. Otherwise, it still carries smoke and qi—this is why we do not eat it." Alas! A carriage axle was used for fuel, yet Xun Xu recognized it as labored firewood; the charcoal was not properly refined, yet the two sons still avoided its smoky odor. How much more so, then, should one avoid using impure firewood when preparing offerings for the gods?

In praise it is said:
Millet is called *"bright grain,"*
The first taste precedes the sacrifice.
Vegetarian meals are prepared with care,
Transforming simple fare into a grand feast.
The bounty of Heaven must be honored,
With no hint of negligence.
The fire’s measure must be precise,
The firewood pure and bright.
For offerings to guests and gods alike,
Purity is paramount—what barrier lies between man and spirit?

To rise naked at night: As the *Daoist Canon* states, the sun and moon each span eight hundred and forty *li*. The sun travels through *qian*, the moon through *kun*; the sun gains clarity from the moon’s essence, the moon gains brightness from the sun’s spirit. Clarity is their qi, radiance their substance. Yin and yang are their Way, the crow and hare their symbols. *Mao* and *you* are their paths, day and night their cycles. Their union is their function. Generally, day belongs to yang, night to yin—night is the time when the hundred spirits converge, and to be naked then risks offense. Have you not heard of Huang Sangou and Yuan Qing? Once, Zhu Shoucheng was robbed three nights in a row, yet the thieves could not enter.

The bandit leader Huang Sangou, enraged, was determined to fulfill his intent. He returned once more, but as he entered the second gate, he saw flames blazing fiercely, and several armored warriors shouting to seize him. Terrified, Huang Sangou scrambled to escape, unwittingly wounding his own men in the chaos. When the case was tried, the armored warriors he had seen were none other than the attendant deities from the portrait of Zhenwu worshipped in Zhu Shoucheng’s home.

The courtesan Yang Suzhen of Shanzhou lived alone and had amassed great wealth. The military officer Yuan Qing, through a hundred schemes, seduced her, and they became husband and wife. He took all her possessions and set off with her for the capital. Stopping at Mengyang, he got Yang Suzhen drunk that very night and drowned her in the deep abyss. By chance, Zhenwu was passing through the heavens at that moment and saved her, restoring her to life. Thus, even in the dead of night in a secluded place, who can say there are no divine spirits? How then could one rise naked at night?

In praise it is said:
Dwell with body ever covered,
Gaze not below the sash.
In all form and appearance,
Move as though in reverent caution.
Carelessness invites theft,
Diligence knows no rest.
How can one claim the dark is hidden,
And thus indulge in ruin?
By this, one may examine fortune—
No need for tortoise or milfoil.

The Eight Nodal Days for Enforcing Punishment

It is transmitted: The Shangqing Perfected says: On the eight nodal days, one should devote oneself to good deeds and avoid anger, resentment, or strife. These are great taboos for heaven and humanity, and the offenses incurred are no small matter—how much less should one dare to carry out punishments? Generally, on these days, the Primordial Celestial Worthy dispatches the Heavenly Worthies, Heavenly Lords, Heavenly Emperors, divine immortals, and countless hosts of celestial soldiers to transform and enlighten the world, opening the path to salvation for all beings. The Northern Dipper and Southern Asterisms, the Four Controllers and Five Emperors, also descend to record merits and transgressions.

For example, the sun’s usual course is along the Red Way, the moon’s along the Yellow Way. But at the Beginning of Spring, the sun travels the Green Way, emerging east of the Yellow Way. The Three Primordial Sovereign Ladies ascend to the Celestial August Emperor, roaming the Primordial Radiance, performing the Dao, receiving immortality, and the Heavenly Worthy of Ascending Saints, together with the Lord of the Beginning Azure Heavens, descend.

At the Beginning of Summer, the sun travels the Red Way, emerging south of the Yellow Way. The Supreme Perfected of the Great Ultimate and the Three Primordial Perfected ascend to the Purple Tenuity Palace, roaming the Mystic Radiance, performing the Dao, receiving immortality, and the Heavenly Worthy of Cherishing Life, together with the Lord of the Beginning Vermilion Heavens, descend.

At the Beginning of Autumn, the moon travels the White Way, emerging west of the Yellow Way. The Supreme Perfected White Emperor of the Great Simplicity ascends to the High Perfected of the Jade Heaven, roaming the Primordial Radiance, performing the Dao, receiving immortality, and the Heavenly Worthy of the Great Numinous Void, together with the Lord of the Beginning Pale Heavens, descend.

At the Beginning of Winter, the moon travels the Black Way, emerging north of the Yellow Way. The Perfected of the Highest Clarity, the Imperial Lord, and the August Ancestor ascend to the Supreme Jade Emperor of the Nine Heavens, roaming the Grotto Radiance, performing the Dao, receiving immortality, and the Heavenly Worthy of the Infinite Great Flower, together with the Lord of the Beginning Mystic Heavens, descend.

At the Spring Equinox, the sun again travels the Green Way, emerging east of the Yellow Way. The Heavenly Emperor of the Supreme Tenuity ascends to the Supreme Jade Emperor, roaming the Beginning Radiance, performing the Dao, receiving immortality, and the Heavenly Worthy of the Jade Treasure, together with the Azure Spirit Beginning Elder Emperor, descend.

At the Autumn Equinox, the moon travels the White Way, emerging west of the Yellow Way. The Supreme Perfected Red Emperor of the Southern Culmen ascends to the Nine Numinous Ladies of the Upper Terrace of Langfeng, roaming the Bright Radiance, performing the Dao, receiving immortality, and the Heavenly Worthy of the Supreme Mystery, together with the Radiant Spirit August Elder Emperor, descend.

At the Summer Solstice, the sun travels the Red Way, emerging south of the Yellow Way. The Great Emperor of Fusang ascends to the Supreme Tenuity Palace, roaming and receiving the Radiance, performing the Dao, receiving immortality, and the Heavenly Worthy of the Myriad Blessings of the Mystic Perfected, together with the Vermilion Spirit Perfected Elder Emperor, descend.

At the Winter Solstice, the moon travels the Black Way, emerging north of the Yellow Way. The Supreme Perfected of the Great Empyrean and the Jade Consort of the Great Void ascend to the Supreme Emperor of the Supreme Tenuity Palace, roaming the Pure Radiance, performing the Dao, receiving immortality, and the Heavenly Worthy of the Jade Dawn of the Mystic Empyrean, together with the Five Spirit Mystic Elder Emperor, descend.

All beings that contain *qi*, on these days, undergo transformations—soaring, creeping, plants and trees rising and sinking—responding to conditions, shedding the old and embracing the new. The light may become heavy, the heavy may become light; good and evil reverse, and the movements of *qi* and images proceed naturally.

Scholars of the higher studies, on these days, should meditate and offer prayers, adapting to change, cultivating good and eliminating evil, advancing brightness and abandoning darkness, entering rectitude and governing perversity, refining the false into the true, striving to ascend to sagehood, opening the path to salvation for all beings, and bringing forth the myriad things—thus aligning with the intentions of the Most High and the host of Perfected. Those who carry out punishments—what petty creatures dare act thus?

In praise it is said:
The primordial *qi* flows and circulates,
Each with its allotted measure.

The Dipper turns and stars shift, the cauldron renews and the old is transformed. Rewards and punishments serve Heaven, following the patterns of images and numbers. The seasons unfold their beginnings, and the impulse of life resides within. Revering the times and receiving blessings, one should restrain might and anger. Yet some spit at shooting stars.

It is said in tradition: The divine descends from the Dipper and the Celestial Master is born; the brilliance divides at *Xu* and *Wei* and True Warrior is born; the Maiden’s Node senses the star and the White Emperor is born; a shooting star enters the mouth and Master Ye is born. How then do stars divide their numinous essence and nurture brilliance?

A shooting star is but one kind of star. The *Śūraṅgama Sūtra* states: Some see two suns or two moons, or even halos, jade pendants, comets, flying and streaming stars, inverted rainbows, and double rainbows—all these are inauspicious signs. The *Erya* says: A fleeing star is called *yue*, which is a shooting star; a broom star is called *chan*, and a rushing star is called *bei*.

People know only that fleeing and streaming stars are ominous, yet they do not realize that such stars are not inherently ominous—rather, ominousness is self-invited. One should be fearful, swiftly cultivate and reflect. How then can the virtue of expiation be met with contemptuous spitting? Have you not heard of Duke Jing’s three wise words, by which *Yinghuo* retreated three lodgings?

In praise it is said:
