The celestial messenger of shooting stars soars through the vast heavens.
Their portents of fortune or misfortune gather where disaster or blessing alights.
When a meteorite fell in Song, it was taken as an omen;
when one struck Zhuge Liang’s camp, his fate was sealed.
When movements stir above,
how dare one fail in reverence and awe?

The doctrine of suppression and triumph arose from the eastern lands of Qi.
(They point to rainbows and halos.)
As the *Yan Kong Tu* states: “The *ni* is the disorder of the Dipper.”
When the essence of the Dipper strays from its proper measure, it casts forth *ni* in response.
The *He Tu Ji Yao Gou* says: “When the Star of Stability scatters, it becomes the rainbow and *ni*.”
The *Chun Qiu Yun Dou Shu* says: “When stars scatter, they form rainbows.”
One must recognize that rainbows and *ni* are indeed the residual *qi* of the Dipper manifesting in form and color.

In former times, Confucius composed the *Spring and Autumn Annals* and compiled the *Classic of Filial Piety*.
Upon completion, he purified himself and, facing the Northern Dipper, declared the purpose of his writings.
Suddenly, a crimson rainbow descended from the heavens, transforming into a yellow jade inscribed with characters.
Confucius knelt and received it.
Who can deny that rainbows and *ni* are the residual *qi* of the Dipper?
Should one rashly point at them, how could one escape blame?

In praise it is said:

When the rainbow arches in the east, no poet dares point to it.
The white rainbow pierces the sun—Gao Jianli crosses the Yi River.
The *Li Sao* alludes to petty men, its intent shared with the whirlwind.
When celestial signs transform, calamities arise.
Those who witness them stand in awe—how could they regard them lightly?
To rashly point at the Three Luminaries—

The tradition states: The Most High says: If you see the sun, the moon, the Northern Dipper, or the Southern Dipper, you must reverently bow your head, beseeching protection. Speak aloud, invoking the Most Sacred, the Most Honored, the True Sovereign, the True Lord, entreating mercy for mortal folly and pardon for transgressions. Then bow twice—do not treat them with disdain, lest you invite misfortune.

Can the Three Luminaries be pointed at?
The Three Luminaries are the sun, the moon, and the stars.
Since the sun and moon are already too distant below, let us speak of the Northern Dipper, the Southern Dipper, the Five Elders, the Five Stars, and the Twenty-Eight Constellations.

Generally, those who cultivate blessings in the world are few, while those who commit evil are many. Thus, for years they falter, year after year they suffer hardship, and few find peace. If one receives the protection of the Lord of the Nine Yi of the East, the Venerable Lord of the Hu, the Lord of the Year Star Chonghua, and the lords of the seven lodges—Jiao, Kang, Di, Fang, Xin, Wei, and Ji—one may resolve the disasters of the three destinies, punishments, and harms in the years, months, days, and hours of yin, mao, chen, jia, and yi. Within the eighty-one myriad paces of the eastern quadrant, the *qi* of the five disasters, nine calamities, and earthly harms may be repelled. In the first, second, and third months, one may be safeguarded from misfortune.

If one receives the protection of the Lord of the Eight Man of the South, the Venerable Lord of the Yue, the Lord of the Fire Virtue and the Sparkling Deluder, the Lord of the Great Yang and the Southern Dipper, and the lords of the seven lodges—Jing, Gui, Liu, Xing, Zhang, Yi, and Zhen—one may resolve the disasters of the three destinies, punishments, and harms in the years, months, days, and hours of si, wu, wei, bing, and ding. Within the sixty-four myriad paces of the southern quadrant, the *qi* of the five disasters, nine calamities, and metallic harms may be repelled. In the fourth, fifth, and sixth months, one may be safeguarded from misfortune.

If one receives the protection of the Lord of the Six Rong of the West, the Venerable Lord of the Shi, the Lord of the Metal Virtue and Venus, and the lords of the seven lodges—Kui, Lou, Wei, Mao, Bi, Zi, and Shen—one may resolve the disasters of the three destinies, punishments, and harms in the years, months, days, and hours of shen, you, xu, geng, and xin. Within the thirty-six myriad paces of the western quadrant, the *qi* of the five disasters, nine calamities, and wooden harms may be repelled. In the seventh, eighth, and ninth months, one may be safeguarded from misfortune.

If one receives the protection of the Lord of the Five Di of the North, the Venerable Lord of the Qiang, the Lord of the Water Virtue and Mercury, the Lord of the Great Yin and the Northern Dipper, and the lords of the seven lodges—Dou, Niu, Nü, Xu, Wei, Shi, and Bi—one may resolve the disasters of the three destinies, punishments, and harms in the years, months, days, and hours of hai, zi, chou, ren, and gui. Within the twenty-five myriad paces of the northern quadrant, the *qi* of the five disasters, nine calamities, and fiery harms may be repelled. In the tenth, eleventh, and twelfth months, one may be safeguarded from misfortune.

If one receives the protection of the Lord of the Three Qin of the Center, the Venerable Lord of the Cang, the Lord of the Earth and the Star of Stability, and the lords of the seven lodges—[...], Gouchen—one may resolve the disasters of the three destinies, punishments, and harms in the years, months, days, and hours of chen, xu, chou, wei, wu, and ji. The *qi* of earthly harms in the center may be repelled, accumulated malevolent spirits may be dispelled, and within the four seasons, one may be safeguarded from misfortune.

Can the Three Luminaries be rashly pointed at?

In praise it is said:

Emperor Yao faced south, gazing upon the central stars.
Shun aligned the Seven Governances, with jade and pivoting instruments of observation.
With reverence and diligence, he observed and contemplated, examining all affairs.
Shining and overseeing from above, nothing hidden was left unnoticed.
Those who rashly point—common folk without understanding—dare to gaze long upon the sun and moon.

It is transmitted: The Sun Sovereign, Lord of the Sun, presides as the supreme overseer of the Yang Palace, with the twenty-eight lodges as his subordinate officials. The Moon Sovereign, Lady of the Moon, presides as the supreme overseer of the Yin Palace, governing the five sacred peaks and the twenty-four *qi*, administering the mountains as Earthly Controllers, and the Three Rivers, Four Seas, Nine Jiang, and Twelve Streams as the Water Bureau.

Each year comprises twenty-four *qi*, and at the beginning of each seasonal *qi*, the Sun Sovereign, Lord of the Sun, arrives first, patrolling the corresponding lodge for that *qi*. On that day, the Moon Sovereign, Lady of the Moon, also arrives. On such days, the twenty-four *qi* govern the mountains. The Lords of the Twenty-Four *Qi* each compile the good and evil, disasters and blessings, longevity and fortune, life and death of the world’s people, submitting them to their respective sacred peaks. The Controller of Destiny of each peak then transcribes and presents them to the Yin and Yang Sovereigns patrolling the lodge of that *qi*.

At the *Li Chun* (Beginning of Spring) *qi*, the report is made at the Xu Lodge, with the Yongquan Yin Official, Left Supervisor of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Yu Shui* (Rain Water) *qi*, at the Wei Lodge, with the Beiping Yin Official, Right Supervisor of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Jing Zhe* (Awakening of Insects) *qi*, at the Shi and Bi Lodges, with the Chougeng Yin Official, Left Inspector of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Chun Fen* (Spring Equinox) *qi*, at the Kui Lodge, with the Scribe Yin Official, Right Minister of Merit of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Qing Ming* (Pure Brightness) *qi*, at the Lou Lodge, with the Mengqin Yin Official, Left Director of Merit of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Gu Yu* (Grain Rain) *qi*, at the Wei Lodge, with the Pinggai Yin Official, Right Director of Merit of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Li Xia* (Beginning of Summer) *qi*, at the Mao Lodge, with the Yuntai Yin Official, Left Supervisor of Merit of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Xiao Man* (Lesser Fullness) *qi*, at the Bi Lodge, with the Jinkou Yin Official, Right Supervisor of Merit of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Mang Zhong* (Grain in Ear) *qi*, at the Zi and Shen Lodges, with the Gongmu Yin Official, Right Capital *Qi* Lord.
At the *Xia Zhi* (Summer Solstice) *qi*, at the Jing Lodge, with the Yuju Yin Official, Left Inspector of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Xiao Shu* (Lesser Heat) *qi*, at the Gui Lodge, with the Pinggang Yin Official, Left Minister of Merit of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Da Shu* (Greater Heat) *qi*, at the Liu Lodge, with the Beimang Yin Official, Right Inspector of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Li Qiu* (Beginning of Autumn) *qi*, at the Xing Lodge, with the Gegui Yin Official, Right Director of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Chu Shu* (End of Heat) *qi*, at the Zhang Lodge, with the Houcheng Yin Official, Left Capital *Qi* Lord.
At the *Bai Lu* (White Dew) *qi*, at the Yi Lodge, with the Benzhu Yin Official, Right Inspector of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Qiu Fen* (Autumn Equinox) *qi*, at the Zhen Lodge, with the Qinzhong Yin Official, Left Director of the Spirit *Qi* Lords.
At the *Han Lu* (Cold Dew) *qi*, at the Jiao and Kang Lodges, with the Yangping Yin Official, Left Pacifier of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Shuang Jiang* (Frost Descent) *qi*, at the Di Lodge, with the Heming Yin Official, Left Elder of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Li Dong* (Beginning of Winter) *qi*, at the Fang Lodge, with the Liyuan Yin Official, Right Elder of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Xiao Xue* (Lesser Snow) *qi*, at the Xin and Wei Lodges, with the Gengchu Yin Official, Right Capital Director of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Da Xue* (Greater Snow) *qi*, at the Ji Lodge, with the Shangzhi Yin Official, Left Capital Supervisor of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Dong Zhi* (Winter Solstice) *qi*, at the Dou Lodge, with the Zhenduo Yin Official, Right Director of the Spirit *Qi* Lords.
At the *Xiao Han* (Lesser Cold) *qi*, at the Niu Lodge, with the Changli Yin Official, Left Capital Supervisor of the *Qi* Lords.
At the *Da Han* (Greater Cold) *qi*, at the Nü Lodge, with the Lutang Yin Official, Right Pacifier of the *Qi* Lords.

Then, may the sun and moon be gazed upon for long?

In praise it is said:

At ancient times, the lord of men would face the sun at dawn and the moon at dusk. They oversaw the receipt and disbursement of offerings, presiding over the regulation of *qi*. In the Bamboo Palace, they performed rites of obeisance—such rites endured even into the Han. Yet as canonical rituals gradually fell into disuse, reverence and diligence waned. What purpose remains in vacant gazing, if the torch’s flame extinguishes itself?

*The Twenty-Eighth Scroll of the* Supreme Scripture on Stimulus and Response *concludes here.*

*The Twenty-Ninth Scroll of the* Supreme Scripture on Stimulus and Response

*The Biography of Li Changling*
*In Praise of Zheng Qingzhi*

In spring, they would hunt with fire.
It is recorded: Cheng Sichang, throughout his life, observed the precept against killing and never engaged in butchery. While in Mizhou, he witnessed several hunters trapping birds—some with heads crushed or chests pierced beneath the talons and beaks of hawks and owls, others with legs broken or wings torn in snares and nets. That very night, he burned incense beneath the open sky and declared: “I intend to take all living beings my family consumes, along with the karmic ties of my seven generations of ancestors, and compile them into an *Illustrated Precept Against Killing*, then print and distribute it. Perhaps this land may yet awaken to reform.”

After printing and circulating the work, a man named Peng Jing requested a copy. His wife, née Hua, upon seeing it, flew into a rage, seized the book, tore it to shreds, and cast it into filth. The next day, she bought a fish and prepared to slice it for minced fish. Suddenly, the fish leapt, striking her eye and splattering blood across the floor. In an instant, the blood transformed into worms, swarming and coiling around her body. Amid the uproar, the regional inspector, Xiang Shuhuang, beheld the Perfected Warrior Lord, who declared: “I have observed this land and found one of supreme goodness—Cheng Sichang, a sojourner from Xinghua Commandery, who printed and distributed the *Illustrated Precept Against Killing*. Yet this woman, Hua, dared to tear it apart. Her sins are grave, and she cannot escape retribution. As for the rest of the unrepentant, they too shall incur punishment.”

When I descend again on the twenty-seventh day, I shall observe what transpires further. Yet is hunting with fire permissible? It is lamentable that people do not recognize that all sentient beings possess the Buddha-nature. If guided by righteousness, none would fail to comprehend.

In former times, when Wei Nan’gang governed Shu, there was a parrot of remarkable intelligence. Its keeper, instructing it in the Dao of the Buddha, said: “If you wish to recite the Buddha’s name, begin with thought, and from thought proceed to thoughtlessness.” The parrot lifted its head and flapped its wings, as though listening and assenting. When bidden to recite the Buddha’s name, it remained silent. Questioned why it did not recite, it suddenly cried out, “Amitābha Buddha!”—its intent seemingly awakened: it took thought as conditioned arising, and thoughtlessness as the true limit.

One day, without trembling or falling, it folded its wings, drew in its feet, and passed away in stillness. When cremated, ten śarīra relics appeared. The governor had a stupa built to inter them, naming it the Parrot Stupa. Who would claim it lacked the Buddha-nature?

Lady Li, wife of Wei Jingyu, was widowed at twenty. Though her parents urged her to remarry, she vowed never to consent. A pair of swallows nested in her home; one day, one flew off alone. Moved by this, Li said to it: “Can you be like me?” She tied a thread to its foot. The next year, it returned alone as before. Li composed a poem:

*Last year you left without a mate,*
*This spring you still return alone.*
*Our bond runs deep—how could I bear*
*To see you fly again as one?*

The following year, it returned once more, only to find Li already dead. Told of her death and burial place, it flew straight to her grave, cried out in grief, refused food, and perished. The people buried it there, calling it the Swallow’s Mound. Who would claim it lacked devotion to righteousness?

The Most High warns against hunting with fire, particularly in spring, for spring is the season when all things come to life. If one indulges in such hunting, the harm from the chase may yet be counted; but the passage of a single fire—can its toll be numbered? The *Monthly Ordinances* likewise admonishes against it. How can one fail to heed this warning?

In praise it is said:
The Zhou rites and Xia hunts selected only barren game,
Yet even spring’s culling spared the breeding fowl.
The intent was to train troops, not to slaughter wantonly.
Birds and beasts mate and rear their young when life’s course runs smooth.
How could a humane heart bear to burn the forest for sport?

To revile while facing north—how much worse! As the tradition states: The Venerable Bhaguka, before attaining arhatship, once cursed a man for eating slowly, calling him an “ox chewing grass.” Even after becoming an arhat, he could not escape the karmic retribution of being fed like an ox. The deva prince Sudarśana, in his former life, once reviled a visiting monk as a “blind fool.” When his heavenly blessings were exhausted, he too could not avoid the retribution of blindness. Who would claim that malicious speech is harmless? How much more so when directed toward the north?

The north is where the Supreme One dwells, home to the Northern Asterism. Among its five stars, the first is the Heavenly August Emperor, and the fifth is the Northern Culmen Emperor. Of the Dipper’s nine stars, the seventh is the Star of Ruinous War, known as the Celestial Supreme Thearch, the Mysterious Yang Gate, the Radiant Brilliance, the Most High Jade Sovereign Dao Lord—his majesty is beyond all others.

According to the *Sutra of Past Lives*, the Primordial Supreme Thearch declared: “If faithful men and women, on the first spring day, wholeheartedly observe purification rites, reverently invoke the divine, and perform the Ninefold Radiance Offering to welcome the Purple Radiance Holy Mother and the Seven Primal Lords, then even the gravest of the ten evils, the bitterest of karmic retributions, shall melt like ice beneath the nine suns. Their blessings and longevity shall increase, immeasurable heavenly perfected beings shall gather to protect them, their present life shall be fulfilled, their descendants shall flourish, and their merit and virtue shall flow like a sweet spring, ever replenishing, boundless in use. After death, they shall ascend to the Great Brahmā Heaven of True Reality.”

How, then, can one revile while facing north? Have you not heard how Envoy Liu, through a life-mandate offering, was indeed pardoned of his sins, and how Yanlou, through prayers for his father, did indeed secure his extended years?

Have you further not heard how Li Da, through his devout service, begot the future Duke Wei, a meritorious minister of Tang; and how the Marquis of Wu’s daughter, emulating the filial Peng maiden in her devotions, likewise ascended to the heavens? These accounts are all recorded in the sacred canons, and such instances are too numerous to recount—here but a few are cited to instruct the world.

In praise it is said:
When mortals face north, they turn in reverence toward their sovereign.
When heaven’s north pole is named, it is called the Celestial Apex and the Dipper.
In the span between bowing and raising one’s head,
there should be no careless word or deed.
Once, while combing his hair, Guan Ning recalled his past transgressions.
If even in quiet seclusion one must not offend,
how much less dare one loose one’s tongue in idle speech?

Without cause, to kill a tortoise or strike a snake—
the scriptures say: The Perfected One of the Manifest World declared:
“None among the winged or creeping beings may be slain.
Of all creatures, the tortoise and the snake in particular must not be killed.
Should one rashly take their lives, or even slay those possessed of spirit,
their hidden essence shall surely bring harm to the slayer.”
Thus do the wise ever strive to protect them.

Long ago, Liu Yanxiang’s father served as an aide to the prefect of Huzhou.
On his return from the silver mines, someone presented him with a great tortoise, saying:
“Whoever obtains this tortoise shall live a thousand years.”
That very day, his father personally returned it to the place where it was found.
After his father’s death, Yanxiang became a registrar in Fangzhou.

Suddenly, a violent flood surged forth from the mountains, rising several feet above the flat ground. The entire household was seized with terror, not knowing where to flee. Just then, a great tortoise appeared, seemingly intent on leading the way. Following the tortoise, they passed through more than ten locations, each time finding shallower ground, and thus escaped the calamity of the flood.

That very night, Yanxiang dreamed of the great tortoise, which said: “Long ago in the silver mines, I once received the kindness of your late father, the Prefect’s aide, who saved and released me. Today, I have come to repay that debt.”

When the Perfected Master Sun was in the world, he once traveled through the mountains on the Dao. He saw villagers striking a green snake and forcefully intervened to save it. A month later, as he passed by the same place again, he saw a young man galloping toward him on horseback, who invited him to return home. Upon arrival, it was a royal residence, and a figure in crimson robes emerged to express gratitude, saying: “Yesterday, my foolish son nearly came to harm, but thanks to your intervention, he was saved. Here, our blood kin are many, and all are deeply grateful. Thus, I have sent my eldest son to welcome you and convey our humble thanks.”

Then, he was led into a deep palace, where a consort emerged, holding a young boy dressed in green, to bow and express gratitude. They repeatedly urged him to stay. For three days, he was offered exquisite delicacies, fine meals, silks, pearls, and jade—nothing was lacking. Yet the Master declined all gifts, accepting only the *Thirty Immortal Prescriptions of the Dragon Palace*, bestowed by the Celestial Emperor, which he brought back with him. The *Thousand Gold Remedies* is said to have drawn from these thirty prescriptions.

If this is so, can the killing of tortoises and the striking of snakes truly be justified?

**Eulogy:**
The tortoise ranks among the Four Spirits,
The snake leads the hundred hibernating creatures.
Yet even Yuju’s net could not spare them from capture,
And the essence of the White Emperor wept upon the path.
Who would expect the ignorant to bring such misfortune upon themselves?
If one encounters a wise soul, they should hasten to relieve such distress.

Such sins as these are recorded by the Deities of Fate, who diminish one’s allotted lifespan in proportion to their severity. When the tally is exhausted, death follows; if the transgression exceeds the debt, the misfortune extends to one’s descendants.

As the saying goes: the offenses described above have each been expounded in detail in the preceding text, and so shall not be reiterated here. Instead, let us speak of the karmic causes and effects governing rebirth into the Seven Realms, that all may come to know them.

Within the cycle of the Seven Realms, none attain true purity, for all is but illusion. Even the habits of killing, stealing, and lust persist, following one’s inclinations. The sutra states: “In all the worlds, the succession of death and rebirth follows habitual tendencies; at death, it flows with transformation.”

At the moment of passing, before the warmth has fully departed, the entirety of one’s lifetime of good and evil deeds appears in an instant. Death reverses life’s course, and the two habits intersect. If pure thought prevails, one ascends and is surely born in the heavens. If the mind takes flight while still possessing blessings, wisdom, and pure vows, the heart naturally opens. One beholds the Buddhas of the ten directions and all the pure lands, and is reborn according to one’s wishes. With more thought than emotion, one rises lightly but not far—such are the flying immortals.

The mighty ghost kings, flying yakṣas, earth-walking rākṣasas, and those who roam the four heavens move without obstruction. Among them, those with virtuous vows and good hearts protect my Dharma, uphold the precepts, follow the precept-observers, guard the divine mantras, accompany the mantra-holders, or safeguard meditation and preserve the endurance of Dharma. These beings proceed directly to the seat of the Tathāgata. With thought and emotion in balance, they neither ascend nor fall, but are reborn among humans. Those with clear thoughts become wise; those with deep emotions grow dull. With more emotion than thought, one drifts into the realm of beasts—heavy as furred creatures, light as feathered flocks. With seven parts emotion and three parts thought, one descends even lower into the watery wheel.

Born in the realm of fire, one receives the fierce breath of fire, becoming a hungry ghost, constantly burned. Water harms them, and they have no food or drink, enduring for hundreds of thousands of kalpas with nine parts emotion and one part thought. Descending into the fiery wheel of the abyss, the body enters wind and fire, where the two elements clash. Those lightly burdened are born in the intermediate state; the heavily burdened fall into the realm without respite. These two hells are purely emotional—one sinks into the Avīci Hell. If the heart harbors slander of the Mahāyāna, violates the Buddha’s precepts, deceitfully expounds the Dharma, falsely covets offerings, or usurps reverence, committing the five heinous crimes or the ten evils, one is reborn in the Avīci Hells of the ten directions.

Though the evil karma is self-inflicted, it also affects those sharing the same conditions, for the original ground is thus. The hells, hungry ghosts, animals, humans, gods, immortals, and asuras—all who delve into the Seven Realms—are benighted, bound to the conditioned phenomena of saṃsāra. Rebirth arises from delusion, and delusion follows karma. In the wondrous, radiant, and uncreated original mind, all is like flowers in the sky, fundamentally nonexistent—mere illusion without root or thread.

These beings, unaware of their original mind, endure this cycle of rebirth for countless kalpas without attaining true purity, all due to following the habits of killing, stealing, and lust.

What are the Seven Realms?

**First, the Heavenly Realm.**
The sutra states: “Worldly beings who do not seek permanence, unable to forsake the love of wives and concubines, yet do not stray in licentiousness, their minds clear and bright—at death, they dwell near the sun and moon. Such are called the Heaven of the Four Heavenly Kings. Those who have slight attachment to their wives, unable to fully savor the purity of their abodes, at death transcend the brightness of the sun and moon, residing atop the human realm. Such are called the Heaven of the Thirty-Three. Those who engage in desire briefly and depart without longing, active little and still much in the human world—at death, they dwell serenely in the void, beyond the reach of the sun and moon’s light. These beings possess their own radiance. Such are called the Suyāma Heaven. Those who are always still, yet when touched cannot refuse—at death, they ascend to the subtle, no longer touching the lower realms or the abodes of humans and gods. Such are called the Tuṣita Heaven. Those who, without desire, comply with others’ actions, finding the act as tasteless as chewing wax—at death, they are born in the realm of transformation. Such are called the Heaven of Joyous Transformation. Those without worldly minds, yet conforming to worldly deeds, clearly transcending in their conduct—at death, they fully surpass the realm of transformation. Such are the two kinds called the Heaven of Freely Enjoying Transformations by Others. These six heavens, though their forms transcend movement, their hearts and traces remain entangled. From here downward is called the Desire Realm. Even the eighteen heavens of the Form Realm and the four heavens of the Formless Realm—all these heavenly beings, as the Buddha expounded, fail to comprehend the wondrous, luminous mind of enlightenment. Delusion accumulates and arises, falsely creating the three realms, falsely following the Seven Realms in sinking and drowning. Each *pudgala* follows its kind.”

**Second, the Immortal Realm.**
The sutra states: “There are also those who do not rely on true enlightenment, cultivating *samādhi* but separately nurturing deluded thoughts, fixing their forms through visualization, dwelling in mountains and forests where humans do not see. There are ten kinds of immortals: Those who persistently consume elixirs without rest, perfecting the Way of ingestion, are called Earth-Walking Immortals. Those who persistently consume herbs and plants without rest, perfecting the Way of medicine, are called Flying Immortals. Those who persistently refine metals and stones without rest, perfecting the Way of transformation, are called Wandering Immortals. Those who persistently regulate movement and stillness without rest, perfecting the essence of *qi*, are called Sky-Walking Immortals. Those who persistently refine bodily fluids without rest, perfecting the virtue of moisture, are called Heaven-Walking Immortals. Those who persistently refine essence and form without rest, perfecting the absorption of purity, are called Permeating Immortals. Those who persistently uphold mantras and prohibitions without rest, perfecting the arts of magic, are called Way-Walking Immortals. Those who persistently cultivate thought without rest, perfecting recollection, are called Radiant Immortals. Those who persistently advance in interaction without rest, perfecting resonance, are called Essence Immortals.”

Those who persistently refine transformation without rest, perfecting awakening, are called Utmost-Walking Immortals. All these cultivate the heart among humanity, yet do not cultivate true awakening, and thus attain a separate principle of life, living for thousands or tens of thousands of years. They dwell in deep mountains or on great sea islands, cut off from the human realm. Yet this too is but the turning of the wheel—deluded thoughts flowing on, without the cultivation of samadhi. When their retribution is exhausted, they return again, scattering into the various destinies.

The third is the asura destiny. The sutras say: Within these three realms, there are four kinds of asuras. If, within the ghost destiny, they protect the Dharma with their powers and attain penetration into emptiness, these asuras are born from the *mao* direction and belong to the ghost destiny. If, within the heavens, their virtue declines and they fall, their dwelling is near the sun and moon—these asuras emerge from the womb and belong to the human destiny. There are asura kings who hold the world in their grasp, their power pervading without fear, able to contend with Brahma, Indra, and the Four Heavenly Kings for dominion—these asuras arise from transformation and belong to the heavenly destiny. There is also a lesser class of asuras who are born in the heart of the great sea, submerged in water with gaping mouths; by day they roam the empty sky, and by night they return to the water to rest—these asuras arise from damp qi and belong to the animal destiny.

The fourth is the human destiny. The sutras say: Those who are noble and exalted as humans, or become kings or elders, come from revering the Three Jewels. Those who are upright in appearance, with pure and radiant complexions, foremost in bearing, come from the practice of forbearance. Those who are diligent and untiring, delighting in merit and virtue, come from the practice of diligence. Those who are serene, with careful speech and conduct, come from the practice of meditation. Those who are wise and bright, comprehending profound Dharma, expounding sublime meanings, awakening the ignorant, and whose words are treasured as jewels by those who hear them, come from the cultivation of wisdom. Those with clear and resonant voices come from singing the praises of the Three Jewels. Those who are pure and free from illness come from the practice of compassion. Those who are fearful, with hoarse voices, come from hell. Those who are dull and lazy, gluttonous, inarticulate, timid, delighting in foolish company, and fond of darkness come from the animal destiny. Those who are unclean come from pigs. Those who are fierce and cruel come from vipers. Those lacking in aspiration come from dogs. Those who are stubborn and self-willed come from sheep. Those who delight in fine food and drink, recklessly take lives, and lack compassion come from jackals, wolves, foxes, and hawks. Those who are restless and intolerant come from macaques.

Furthermore: Those who are tall and respectful come from respecting others. Those who are short and small come from disdaining others. Those who are ugly come from anger. Those who are ignorant at birth come from lack of study. Those who are dull-witted come from not teaching others. Those who are short-lived and sickly come from killing. Those who lack livelihood and suffer constant poverty come from theft. Those who are childless come from adultery. Those whose children are licentious come from defiling virgins. Those who are widowed, orphaned, or solitary come from mistreating children. Those who suffer hunger come from hoarding food. Those who are slaves or servants, burdened by debt and unable to repay, come from disrespecting the Three Treasures. Those who are ugly and repulsive come from obstructing the Buddha’s light. Those born as water buffaloes come from official cruelty and oppressing the common people. Those born as deer come from startling others. Those born among dragons come from delighting in mischief. Those whose bodies bear incurable sores come from flogging others. Those who bring joy to others come from delighting in others. Those who are disliked come from disliking others. Those who are imprisoned by officials, bound in cangues and fetters, come from confining living beings. Those with protruding lips come from enjoying fishing. Those born in the Land of Naked People come from disrespectfully entering Buddhist monasteries without clothing. Those born in the Land of Horse-Hoof People come from trampling Buddha-land with sandals. Those born in the Land of Pierced-Chests come from regretting acts of generosity and merit-making.

It is also said: Those of the owl kind repay their debts and regain human form, mingling with the dull-witted. Those of the fault kind mingle with the foolish, those of the fox kind with the mediocre, those of the venomous kind with the wolf-like, those of the parasitic kind with the insignificant, those of the gluttonous kind with the gentle, those of the servile kind with the laborious, those of the responsive kind with the cultured, those of the auspicious kind with the enlightened, and those of the compliant kind with the discerning. All these, having repaid their past debts and regained human form, remain from beginningless time in the deluded reckoning of karma, mutually arising and destroying. Without encountering the Tathagata or hearing the true Dharma, they turn endlessly in the dust and toil of samsara—such beings are called the pitiable.

The fifth is hell. The sutras say: These beings, deluded by their own karma, create the ten habitual causes and receive the six intersecting retributions. What are the ten causes? First, the habit of lust; second, the habit of greed; third, the habit of arrogance; fourth, the habit of anger; fifth, the habit of deceit; sixth, the habit of fraud; seventh, the habit of enmity; eighth, the habit of wrong views; ninth, the habit of oppression; and tenth, the habit of litigation. What are the six retributions? All beings create karma with the six senses, and the evil retributions they incur emerge from the six roots.

First is the retribution of sight, which summons karmic consequences. When this visual karma intersects, at the approach of death, one first sees raging fires filling the ten directions. The deceased’s spirit, borne by smoke, falls and enters the Avici Hell.

Second is the retribution of hearing, which summons karmic consequences. When this auditory karma intersects, at the approach of death, one first sees waves surging and submerging heaven and earth. The deceased’s spirit, descending with the current, enters the Avici Hell.

Third is the retribution of smell, which summons evil consequences. When this olfactory karma intersects, at the approach of death, one first sees poisonous qi filling the distance. The deceased’s spirit leaps from the ground and enters the Avici Hell.

Fourth is the retribution of taste, which summons evil consequences. When this gustatory karma intersects, at the approach of death, one first sees iron nets with raging, blazing flames covering the world. The deceased’s spirit plunges downward, ensnared in the net, head dangling upside down, and enters the Avici Hell.

Fifth is the retribution of touch, which summons evil consequences. When this tactile karma intersects, at the approach of death, one first sees great mountains closing in from all four sides, leaving no path of escape. The deceased’s spirit then sees a great iron city, with fire-serpents, fire-dogs, tigers, wolves, lions, ox-headed jailers, and horse-headed rakshasas, all wielding spears and lances, driving them through the city gates toward the Avici Hell.

Sixth is the retribution of thought, which summons evil consequences. When this mental karma intersects, at the approach of death, one first sees evil winds blowing apart the land. The deceased’s spirit is swept up into the sky, then whirled down by the wind, falling into the Avici Hell.

Within each of these six retributions, two further evil signs are revealed, making this the "ten causes and six effects of hell." In general, all are the deluded fabrications of sentient beings. If sentient beings complete their evil karma in full, they enter the Avīci Hell, suffering boundless torment for boundless kalpas. Each of the six roots generates its own karma, and what they have done—combining both sense-objects and sense-roots—leads them into the eight Avici Hells. If the three karmas of body, speech, and mind commit killing, stealing, and sexual misconduct, they enter the eighteen hells. If the three karmas do not overlap but commit, for instance, one act of killing and one of stealing, they enter the thirty-six hells. If a single root, such as the eye, commits a single transgression, they enter the one hundred and eight hells. Thus, sentient beings, each acting and creating separately, enter corresponding realms in the world, their deluded thoughts arising—yet these are not originally existent.

Sixth is the realm of hungry ghosts. The sutras state: These sentient beings, without having broken the monastic precepts, violated the bodhisattva vows, or destroyed the Buddha’s nirvana, but through other miscellaneous karmas, burn for kalpas. After their sins are exhausted, they take the forms of ghosts. If their original transgression was greed for possessions, upon the exhaustion of their sin, they encounter objects and take form, becoming *guài* ghosts. If their transgression was greed for sensual pleasure, they encounter wind and take form, becoming *bá* ghosts. If their transgression was greed for delusion, they encounter animals and take form, becoming *mèi* ghosts. If their transgression was greed for hatred, they encounter insects and take form, becoming *chóngdú* ghosts. If their transgression was greed for attachment, they encounter decay and take form, becoming *lì* ghosts. If their transgression was greed for arrogance, they encounter qi and take form, becoming hungry ghosts. If their transgression was greed for deception, they encounter darkness and take form, becoming *yǎn* ghosts. If their transgression was greed for clarity, they encounter essence and take form, becoming *wǎngliǎng* ghosts. If their transgression was greed for achievement, they encounter light and take form, becoming servant ghosts. If their transgression was greed for factionalism, they encounter people and take form, becoming messenger ghosts. All these beings, having fallen purely through emotion, are scorched by karmic fire and emerge as ghosts. Among the realms of hungry ghosts, this is the most extreme suffering. Due to their greed, though they conceive desires, they ultimately obtain nothing. Due to their jealousy, when they see others sated, they themselves remain perpetually hungry. Due to their malice, their bodies emit fire, which burns them. Due to their arrogance, their merit is minimal, and even the smallest ghosts overpower them.

Formerly, when the Buddha was in the country of Vaiśālī, he went out at dawn and eighty-four thousand hungry ghosts wailed to him, saying: "In our past lives, though we saw the Buddha, we did not recognize him. Though we heard the Dharma, we did not acknowledge it. Though we saw the Sangha, we did not know them. We did not perform meritorious deeds, nor did we teach others to do so. Seeing others perform merit, we would mock them. Seeing others commit sins, we rejoiced in our hearts. Due to these most evil actions, we have fallen into this realm for seventy thousand years. Whenever we see even a small amount of water, it soon vanishes. When we see a large body of water, it is guarded by yakshas and rakshasas. Even when great rains fall, our bodies feel as though we stand on scorching sand. We beseech your compassion and salvation." The Buddha then taught them to offer water to the Buddha and the Sangha, and thus they were freed from suffering.

When Ānanda was in the city of Kapilavastu, he saw a hungry ghost who called himself *Miànrán* and said to Ānanda: "In three days, you will certainly die and fall into our realm." Ānanda asked: "What can I do to avoid this?" *Miànrán* replied: "There is no way except to offer us food, allowing us to be sated and thus liberated, so that you too may be spared." Ānanda reported this to the Buddha, who said: "If you wish to offer food, first place it in a clean vessel, recite my *dhāraṇī* mantra seven times over it, then take it outside, snap your fingers seven times, and offer it to the ghosts. They will be liberated, and you too will be spared, gaining longevity as well." The mantra was then spoken: *Namo savadhadhakatavaru kṛtī om samvara samvara hūṃ.* Ānanda believed and practiced accordingly, and indeed was spared.

Seventh is the realm of animals. The sutras state: When the karma of ghosts is exhausted, both emotion and thought become empty. Then, in the world, they encounter those to whom they owe karmic debts, and as animal masters, they repay their past obligations. *Wùguài* ghosts, once their retribution for objects is exhausted, are born in the world mostly as owl-like creatures. *Fēngbá* ghosts, once their retribution for wind is exhausted, become omens and all manner of strange creatures. *Chùmèi* ghosts, once their retribution for animals is exhausted, become solitary creatures. *Chónggǔ* ghosts, once their retribution for insects is exhausted, become poisonous creatures. *Shuāilì* ghosts, once their retribution for decay is exhausted, become parasitic creatures. *Shòuqì* ghosts, once their retribution for qi is exhausted, become edible creatures. *Miányōu* ghosts, once their retribution for darkness is exhausted, become creatures used for clothing. *Héjīng* ghosts, once their retribution for harmony is exhausted, become responsive creatures. *Mínglíng* ghosts, once their retribution for clarity is exhausted, become auspicious creatures of all kinds.

Ghosts bound to humans, once their retribution for human death is exhausted, often become creatures of habit. All these are desiccated by the fires of karma, repaying their past debts. Though they become beasts, it is their own deluded karma that draws them. If one awakens to bodhi, these delusions are revealed as fundamentally nonexistent.

It is also said: from these beasts, past debts are repaid. If the repayer exceeds what is owed, these beings may return to human form, only to reclaim the excess. If they possess strength and virtue, they may remain in human form to repay what is due. Those without virtue revert to beasts, settling the remaining debt. Know that whether through money, goods, or labor, repayment continues until the debt is cleared. If, in the interim, one kills or consumes another, even across kalpas as countless as motes of dust, they devour and slay one another in an endless cycle—like a turning wheel, rising and falling without cease.

Have you not heard of Dōudiào, who for his arrogance and love of insults died and took the form of a dog? Or of Li Liùláng, who for lending at usurious rates also died and became a dog?

In praise it is said:
The accumulation of unvirtuous deeds
brings calamity in excess.

The accumulation arises gradually, and the excess is not easily dispelled. Those who delight in oppression and cruelty bring annihilation upon their entire clan. Qin, having long indulged in tyranny, saw Ziying ride the plain carriage. Retribution and recompense stand in opposition—such proofs are not false.

*Here ends Scroll Twenty-Nine of the* **Taishang Ganying Pian** *(The Supreme Scripture on Actions and Responses).*
